1. A 27-year-old woman comes to the physician for a routine examination. She recently immigrated to the USA from Haiti. She feels well and is asymptomatic. She has a 6-year history of type 2 diabetes mellitus. Treatment with metformin was begun 3 weeks ago. She received all recommended vaccinations during childhood, including BCG vaccine 20 years ago. Her pulse is 74/min, respirations are 12/min, and blood pressure is 104/72 mm Hg. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Her fingerstick blood glucose concentration is 168 mg/dl. A PPD skin test produces 12 mm of induration at 48 hours. An x-ray of the chest shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Repeat PPD skin test in 6 months</br>b. Repeat x-ray of the chest in 3 months</br>c. Begin isoniazid therapy</br>d. Begin isoniazid therapy only if the patient becomes symptomatic</br>e. Begin isoniazid, rifampin, and pyrazinamide therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Begin isoniazid (INH) therapy</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 12-mm induration on PPD = positive when the patient has at least one moderate-risk criterion.<br /></br>  – “Recent immigrant (≤ 5 yr) from a high-prevalence country (Haiti)” → ≥ 10 mm is positive.<br /></br>• No symptoms and a normal CXR → latent tuberculosis infection (LTBI), not active disease.<br /></br>• Prior BCG 20 years ago does NOT change interpretation; the CDC treats the test exactly the same.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Stepwise approach to a positive PPD:<br /></br>1. Determine positivity cut-off (5, 10, or 15 mm).<br /></br>2. If positive → rule out active disease with symptom screen &amp; CXR.<br /></br>3. Active disease absent → treat LTBI with INH for 6–9 months (plus pyridoxine).  </p></br><p>Take-home: Any recent immigrant (&lt; 5 yr) from a high-incidence country with ≥10 mm induration and a normal chest x-ray should receive INH to prevent progression to active TB.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Latent TB = contained Mycobacterium tuberculosis within granulomas; patient is asymptomatic &amp; non-infectious.<br /></br>• Risk of reactivation is 5–10 % lifetime, higher in diabetics (she has type 2 DM).<br /></br>• INH kills intracellular/latent bacilli by inhibiting mycolic-acid synthesis, thereby lowering reactivation risk.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Diagnostic Test Pearls<br /></br>1. Latent TB: INH daily for 9 mo (or rifampin for 4 mo if INH intolerant). Always add pyridoxine (vitamin B6).<br /></br>2. Baseline &amp; periodic LFTs in patients with liver disease, alcoholism, or pregnancy; otherwise no routine LFTs needed unless symptoms develop.<br /></br>3. Active TB (symptoms or abnormal CXR): 4-drug regimen (RIPE) for 2 mo → INH/RIF for 4 mo.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Repeat PPD in 6 months<br /></br>   • Unnecessary—current PPD already positive. Repeating will not change management.<br /></br>b. Repeat chest x-ray in 3 months<br /></br>   • No evidence of active disease now; CXR surveillance is not a substitute for chemoprophylaxis.<br /></br>d. Begin INH only if symptoms develop<br /></br>   • Waiting allows preventable progression to contagious disease; prophylaxis is the standard of care.<br /></br>e. Start INH, rifampin, and pyrazinamide (multi-drug regimen)<br /></br>   • Indicated for ACTIVE TB or INH-resistant LTBI, not for uncomplicated latent infection with drug-susceptible strains.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Core Concept<br /></br>Positive PPD ≥10 mm in a recent immigrant with normal CXR = latent TB → start INH (plus B6) now to prevent future reactivation.</p>
2. A previously healthy 7-year-old boy is brought to the physician by his father because of a 1-day history of right hip pain and an associated limp. Five days ago, he fell off his bed while wrestling with his 5-year-old brother. Three weeks ago, he had a cold that resolved within 5 days. He takes no medications. Immunizations are up-to-date. He is at the 50th percentile for height and weight. His temperature is 37.6 C 99.7 F, pulse is 100/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 98/66 mm Hg. He is alert and in no distress. Examination of the hips and knees shows no swelling, warmth, or em,thema. Passive motion of the right hip produces moderate pain. Laboratory studies show:</br>Leukocyte count</br>Segmented neutrophils</br>Bands</br>Eosinophils</br>Lymphocytes</br>Monocytes</br>Erythrocyte sedimentation rate</br>Serum</br>C-reactive protein</br>Antinuclear antibody</br>10,000/mm3</br>52 percent</br>3 percent</br>3 percent</br>37 percent</br>5 percent</br>24 mm/h</br>3000 μg/L N=67-1800</br>negative</br>X-rays of the right hip show no abnormalities. Ultrasonography of the right hip shows mild joint effusion. Needle aspiration of the right hip joint yields clear fluid; a Gram stain of the fluid shows no organisms. Which of the</br>following is the most likely long-term complication of this patient's current condition?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Hip flexion contracture</br>b. Leg length discrepancy</br>c. Pauciarticular arthritis</br>d. Polyarticular arthritis</br>e. There are no long-term complications</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. There are no long-term complications  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age 3–10 yr, acute limp/hip pain → think transient (toxic) synovitis vs septic arthritis, Legg-Calvé-Perthes, SCFE, trauma.<br /></br>• Afebrile (T 37.6 °C), normal WBC (10 000), mildly ↑ESR (24) and ↑CRP (&gt;2 mg/dL) → only 1 Kocher criterion for septic arthritis → probability of septic arthritis &lt;3 %.<br /></br>• Hip ROM elicits pain, ultrasound shows small effusion, clear aspirate, Gram stain negative → favors transient synovitis (sterile inflammatory effusion).<br /></br>• Recent viral URI 3 wk ago and minor trauma 5 d ago are common precipitating events for transient synovitis.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Transient synovitis is the most common cause of acute hip pain in children. It is a self-limited post-infectious or post-traumatic inflammatory response. Symptoms improve within a few days to weeks with NSAIDs and activity limitation. Because the joint is not destroyed and the blood supply to the femoral head is preserved, complications such as growth disturbance or chronic arthritis do not occur. The key Step 2 takeaway: transient synovitis → observe/NSAIDs → complete resolution, no sequelae.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: sterile synovial inflammation, often after viral illness or minor trauma.<br /></br>• Mechanism: cytokine-mediated synovitis → small effusion → pain with motion.<br /></br>• Labs: normal or mildly ↑WBC/ESR/CRP; joint fluid sterile.<br /></br>• Natural history: resolves in &lt;4 weeks; recurrence rare.  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>1. Exclude septic arthritis with Kocher criteria, ultrasound, aspiration.<br /></br>2. NSAIDs (e.g., ibuprofen), rest, limited weight-bearing.<br /></br>3. Most improve within 48–72 h; full recovery by 2–4 wk.<br /></br>4. Follow-up only if pain persists (consider Legg-Calvé-Perthes).  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Hip flexion contracture – Seen in untreated Legg-Calvé-Perthes disease (avascular necrosis of femoral head) or juvenile idiopathic arthritis. Would show months of pain/limp, limited abduction/internal rotation, X-ray changes (flattened femoral head). Our patient’s X-ray is normal and symptoms are acute.  </p></br><p>b. Leg length discrepancy – Occurs with chronic hip disorders that damage the growth plate (e.g., Perthes, SCFE, chronic septic arthritis). Transient synovitis does not affect bone growth.  </p></br><p>c. Pauciarticular arthritis – A subtype of juvenile idiopathic arthritis (≤4 joints, girls, +ANA, chronic &gt;6 wk). Would present with prolonged morning stiffness, not an acute 1-day episode, and ANA often positive (here negative).  </p></br><p>d. Polyarticular arthritis – Involves ≥5 joints, systemic findings/positive RF in older children. Again, time course and single-joint involvement do not fit.  </p></br><p>e. There are no long-term complications – Correct. Transient synovitis resolves completely without residual deformity, growth disturbance, or chronic arthritis.  </p></br><p>Key Takeaway<br /></br>An afebrile child with an acute painful limp, mild inflammatory markers, sterile joint aspirate, and recent viral illness most likely has transient synovitis—a benign, self-limited condition with no expected long-term sequelae.</p>
3. A 37-year-old primigravid woman at 12 weeks' gestation comes to the physician because of a 1-day history of urinary frequency and moderate pain and burning with urination. She has not had fever, abdominal or flank pain, or abnormal vaginal discharge. During the past year, she has had five urinary tract infections that began within 3 days of having sexual intercourse; each episode resolved after 1 day of treatment with ciprofloxacin or trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. She is sexually active with one male partner. Vital signs are within normal limits. Physical examination, including pelvic examination, shows no abnormalities. Urinalysis shows numerous WBCs and bacteria. The patient is prescribed a 7-day course of nitrofurantoin. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management for the remainder of this patient's pregnancy?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Observation only</br>b. Postcoital ciprofloxacin therapy</br>c. Postcoital nitrofurantoin therapy</br>d. Daily ciprofloxacin therapy</br>e. Daily levofloxacin therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>A. Observation only</p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 12-week primigravid → first trimester, fetus highly susceptible to drug toxicities.<br /></br>• Dysuria + frequency, UA with WBCs &amp; bacteria → acute uncomplicated cystitis.<br /></br>• Single episode during pregnancy (her previous infections were before conception).<br /></br>• Treated appropriately with a 7-day course of nitrofurantoin (pregnancy-safe except at term).<br /></br>Key point: In pregnancy, antimicrobial prophylaxis is NOT started for a prior history alone; it is considered only after ≥2 culture-confirmed symptomatic UTIs DURING the pregnancy.</p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Treat the current infection with a pregnancy-safe drug (done).<br /></br>Step 2: Decide whether she needs prophylaxis.<br /></br> • Indications for prophylaxis in pregnancy = recurrent infection during pregnancy (≥2 episodes of cystitis or 1 episode of pyelonephritis).<br /></br> • She has had zero infections in pregnancy so far. Therefore, the correct next step is simply observation (plus a test-of-cure urine culture in 1 wk, which is assumed but not listed).<br /></br>Take-home: History of recurrent pre-pregnancy UTIs does NOT automatically trigger antibiotic prophylaxis while pregnant.</p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>• Pregnancy → progesterone-mediated smooth-muscle relaxation + mechanical compression → urinary stasis &amp; vesicoureteral reflux → ↑ risk of bacteriuria progressing to pyelonephritis.<br /></br>• Recurrent infection threshold is lower in pregnancy because pyelonephritis can lead to preterm labor.<br /></br>• Safe cystitis agents: nitrofurantoin (≤37 wks), amoxicillin-clavulanate, cephalexin, fosfomycin.<br /></br>• Avoid in pregnancy:<br /></br> – Fluoroquinolones (cartilage damage)<br /></br> – TMP-SMX (folate antagonism in 1st tri; kernicterus in 3rd)</p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Postcoital ciprofloxacin therapy<br /></br>• Fluoroquinolone → contraindicated in pregnancy.<br /></br>• Postcoital regimen not recommended in pregnancy; prophylaxis only after recurrent infections during pregnancy.</p></br><p>C. Postcoital nitrofurantoin therapy<br /></br>• Although drug is generally safe early, postcoital prophylaxis is unnecessary because she has not yet shown a pregnancy-related recurrence. Also, nitrofurantoin should be avoided at term (risk of neonatal hemolysis).  </p></br><p>D. Daily ciprofloxacin therapy<br /></br>• Daily fluoroquinolone is both contraindicated (fetal cartilage toxicity) and overly broad; no indication for continuous prophylaxis at this stage.</p></br><p>E. Daily levofloxacin therapy<br /></br>• Same fluoroquinolone class risks as ciprofloxacin; no indication.</p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Treat the current cystitis with a pregnancy-safe antibiotic and observe; initiate prophylactic antibiotics only if she develops two or more symptomatic UTIs later in the pregnancy.</p>
4. An otherwise healthy 12-year-old boy is brought to the emergency department 4 hours after the sudden onset of severe pain in his right testis. There is no history of trauma. Vital signs are within normal limits. On examination, the right testis is palpated in the upper hemiscrotum; it is exquisitely tender and twice the size of the left testis, which is normal. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Results of a complete blood count are within the reference ranges, and urinalysis shows no abnormalities. In addition to administration of morphine, which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Reexamination in 4 hours</br>b. Technetium 99m scan of the right testis</br>c. Intravenous administration of antibiotics</br>d. Reduction of the scrotal mass</br>e. Emergency orchidopexy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Emergency orchidopexy (immediate surgical exploration with detorsion and bilateral fixation)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Sudden onset of severe unilateral testicular pain in a 12-year-old → classic “acute scrotum” presentation.<br /></br>• Testis is “in the upper hemiscrotum,” markedly tender, swollen, twice the size of the contralateral testis → high-riding, enlarged gonad = testicular torsion pattern.<br /></br>• No fever, pyuria, or leukocytosis → argues against infectious causes (epididymitis/orchitis).<br /></br>• Time course: only 4 h since onset. Salvage rates plummet after 6 h, so every minute counts.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Acute scrotal pain in a pubertal boy = torsion until proven otherwise.<br /></br>2. Doppler US or radionuclide scan can confirm, but any diagnostic delay threatens viability.<br /></br>3. Definitive management = immediate surgical exploration, detorsion, and bilateral orchidopexy; contralateral fixation prevents future torsion.<br /></br>Core takeaway: In a time-sensitive surgical emergency, treatment must precede imaging.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Bell-clapper deformity (failure of posterior gubernacular attachment) allows the spermatic cord to twist → venous congestion → arterial occlusion → ischemic necrosis.<br /></br>• Irreversible damage begins ≈4–6 h; near-certain loss after 12 h.<br /></br>Risk factors: adolescence (rapid testicular growth), anatomic deformity, prior self-limited torsion episodes.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatments &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>First-line:<br /></br>• Emergent scrotal exploration → detorsion → bilateral orchidopexy.<br /></br>Adjunct:<br /></br>• Attempt bedside manual detorsion (“open-book” counter-clockwise) only if surgery is not immediately available, but still schedule urgent OR.<br /></br>Diagnostics:<br /></br>• Color Doppler US or technetium-99m scan shows absent perfusion, but reserved for equivocal cases when surgery cannot be expedited.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Re-examination in 4 h<br /></br>   – Delays care; testis may already be non-viable by then.<br /></br>b. Technetium-99m scan<br /></br>   – Accurate but time-consuming; imaging should not postpone surgical detorsion in a classic presentation.<br /></br>c. Intravenous antibiotics<br /></br>   – Would treat epididymitis/orchitis (fever, pyuria, gradual pain, relief with scrotal elevation). None of those findings are present.<br /></br>d. Reduction of the scrotal mass (manual detorsion alone)<br /></br>   – May temporarily relieve pain but is unreliable and must be followed immediately by surgical fixation; therefore not the “most appropriate next step” compared with direct OR.  </p></br><p>Key Difference: All incorrect options either delay definitive treatment or target a different pathology, whereas emergency orchidopexy directly resolves torsion within the critical salvage window.</p>
5. A 56-year-old woman with recurrent ovarian cancer is admitted to the hospital for management of inoperable small-bowel obstruction. Despite undergoing four courses of chemotherapy during the past 5 years, the cancer continues to progress. She currently takes no medications. A nasogastric tube is placed. Her temperature is 37 C 98.6  F, pulse is 80/min, respirations are 12/min, and blood pressure is 120/70 mm Hg. Abdominal examination shows distention and decreased bowel sounds. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Discharge to hospice care</br>b. Family meeting to determine goals of care</br>c. lleostomy</br>d. Intravenous total parenteral nutrition</br>e. Placement of a percutaneous gastrostomy tube</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Discharge to hospice care</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Recurrent ovarian cancer … despite four courses of chemotherapy during the past 5 years, the cancer continues to progress” → End-stage, chemotherapy-refractory disease.<br /></br>• “Inoperable small-bowel obstruction” → No curative surgical option, major marker of terminal course in ovarian cancer.<br /></br>• No medications, stable vitals → Not septic or in acute distress; problem is underlying incurable obstruction.<br /></br>• NG tube already placed → Immediate decompression done; question is what to do next.</p></br><p>These points narrow the differential to palliative vs aggressive interventions. The key phrase “inoperable small-bowel obstruction” in metastatic, refractory ovarian cancer signals a prognosis of weeks to a few months and indicates that further invasive or nutritionally heroic measures add burden without meaningful extension of life or quality.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Determine whether any intervention can alter the disease course or significantly improve quality of life.<br /></br>2. In metastatic, chemotherapy-refractory ovarian cancer with malignant bowel obstruction, median survival ≈ 1–3 months.<br /></br>3. Evidence shows that surgery, TPN, or PEG rarely restore oral intake and are associated with high morbidity.<br /></br>4. Focus should shift from disease-directed care to comfort-focused care (hospice).  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Once a patient has advanced, treatment-refractory malignancy with an inoperable obstruction, the standard of care is to transition to hospice for symptom management rather than pursue further invasive or aggressive therapies.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>• Ovarian cancer commonly spreads intra-peritoneally, encasing and obstructing small bowel loops.<br /></br>• Malignant obstruction reflects widespread peritoneal disease; resection or bypass rarely feasible.<br /></br>• Nutritional interventions (e.g., TPN) do not reverse cachexia because cancer-mediated cytokines drive catabolism.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>First-line palliative measures already initiated: NG tube for decompression, IV fluids, analgesia, antiemetics.<br /></br>Next step:<br /></br>• Hospice enrollment → Home or inpatient services focused on pain control (opioids), antiemetics (ondansetron/haloperidol), anticholinergics for bowel secretions (glycopyrrolate/scopolamine), psychosocial and spiritual support.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>b. Family meeting to determine goals of care<br /></br>• ALWAYS appropriate, but in NBME framing, the vignette gives no indication of decisional conflict; the patient’s disease status clearly meets hospice criteria. Meetings occur within hospice enrollment anyway. The question asks for the next management step—enrolling in hospice is the concrete action.</p></br><p>c. Ileostomy<br /></br>• Surgical diversion requires laparotomy in a hostile abdomen riddled with tumor implants → high morbidity, low success in restoring GI function, no survival benefit.</p></br><p>d. Intravenous total parenteral nutrition<br /></br>• Malignant obstruction + terminal cancer → TPN does not improve survival or quality of life, carries risks (infection, thrombosis, liver dysfunction). Professional guidelines discourage TPN in this setting.</p></br><p>e. Placement of a percutaneous gastrostomy (PEG) tube<br /></br>• PEG decompresses the stomach, not the small bowel; obstruction is distal to the stomach, so PEG provides little symptomatic relief and introduces procedural risk.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>For patients with chemotherapy-refractory ovarian cancer and an inoperable malignant small-bowel obstruction, the evidence-based, patient-centered next step is to transition to hospice care for comfort-focused management rather than pursue further invasive or nutritional interventions.</p>
6. A 30-year-old pnm1grav1d woman at 11 weeks gestation comes to the emergency department because of a 3-day history of increasingly severe m1dhne to nght lower quadrant abdominal pain and increasing vaginal spotting. She also has had pain with sexual intercourse during this time. She has no history of serious illness, and her only medication is a prenatal vitamin. While waiting to be examined, she has the onset of dizziness. Her temperature is 36.7 C 98  F, pulse is 110/min, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 90/50 mm Hg. The uterus is consistent in size with a 10-week gestation and tender to palpation. Tilt test is positive. Transvaginal ultrasonography shows an intrauterine gestational sac to the right of midline and extending into the myometrium. There is free fluid in the cul-de-sac and no adnexal masses. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Ampullar pregnancy</br>b. Complete abortion</br>c. Cornual pregnancy</br>d. Incomplete abortion</br>e. Inevitable abortion</br>F. Missed abortion</br>g. Threatened abortion</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Cornual pregnancy (interstitial ectopic pregnancy)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 11-week pregnancy + acute pelvic pain, vaginal spotting, orthostatic hypotension → ruptured ectopic or hemorrhagic abortion must be high on the differential.<br /></br>• Transvaginal ultrasound: “intrauterine gestational sac to the right of midline and extending into the myometrium.” A true intra-endometrial pregnancy should be completely surrounded by a thick (&gt;5 mm) rim of myometrium; extension into the myometrium at the uterine cornu suggests an interstitial (cornual) implantation.<br /></br>• Free fluid in the cul-de-sac + hemodynamic instability → intraperitoneal bleeding from rupture.<br /></br>• No adnexal mass → rules out the usual tubal (ampullary, isthmic, fimbrial) ectopic location.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Pattern: “eccentric gestational sac at the uterine horn + myometrial thinning” = cornual pregnancy.<br /></br>Cornual pregnancies rupture later (8–16 wk) than other tubal ectopics because the myometrium in the interstitial portion of the tube can initially stretch, so the timing (11 wk) fits.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Know the ultrasound appearance and later presentation of cornual (interstitial) ectopic pregnancies—high rupture mortality because of proximity to uterine and ovarian vessels.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Implantation occurs in the interstitial portion of the fallopian tube that traverses the myometrium.<br /></br>• Surrounding myometrium allows early “intra-uterine–appearing” growth, delaying diagnosis.<br /></br>• When rupture occurs, bleeding is brisk (anastomoses with uterine &amp; ovarian arteries).  </p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• Hemodynamically unstable → immediate laparoscopy/laparotomy with cornual resection or hysterectomy if uncontrollable.<br /></br>• Stable &amp; unruptured: systemic or local methotrexate, uterine artery embolization, or laparoscopic cornuostomy.<br /></br>• Future pregnancies need early ultrasound because recurrence risk is ↑ and uterine rupture during labor is possible after cornu resection.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Ampullary pregnancy<br /></br>   – Most common ectopic site; ultrasound shows an adnexal mass outside the uterus, not an “intrauterine” sac. Would not appear to extend into the myometrium.  </p></br><p>b. Complete abortion<br /></br>   – All products of conception expelled; cervix closed; uterus small/empty on ultrasound. Here, a gestational sac is still visualized.  </p></br><p>d. Incomplete abortion<br /></br>   – Some POC retained; cervical os open; ultrasound shows heterogeneous material within uterine cavity, not a gestational sac in the myometrium.  </p></br><p>e. Inevitable abortion<br /></br>   – Vaginal bleeding + open cervix but intact POC in uterus; ultrasound does not show an eccentric sac nor free intraperitoneal fluid; vital signs usually stable.  </p></br><p>f. Missed abortion<br /></br>   – Fetal demise without expulsion; ultrasound shows gestational sac/fetus without cardiac activity; bleeding minimal and patient hemodynamically stable.  </p></br><p>g. Threatened abortion<br /></br>   – Vaginal bleeding + closed cervix; viable intrauterine pregnancy on ultrasound; no hemodynamic compromise or free intraperitoneal fluid.</p></br><p>Key Differences<br /></br>• Only cornual pregnancy explains: 11-week gestation, eccentric sac within myometrium, free fluid, and shock in the absence of an adnexal mass.</p>
7. A 3402-g 7-lb 8-oz male newborn has severe respiratory distress immediately after delivery. He was born at term following an uncomplicated pregnancy and delivery. He is gasping for air. The trachea is deviated to the right. Breath sounds are markedly decreased over the left hemithorax compared with the right hemithorax. Heart sounds are shifted to the right. The abdomen is markedly scaphoid. This patient is at greatest risk for death from which of the following?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Congenital cardiac disease with low output</br>b. Increased alveolar-arterial oxygen gradient</br>c. Intrapulmonary shunting</br>d. Pulmonary hypoplasia</br>e. Ventilation-perfusion mismatch</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Pulmonary hypoplasia  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Severe respiratory distress immediately after delivery,” “trachea deviated to the right,” “markedly decreased breath sounds over the left hemithorax,” “heart sounds shifted to the right,” and a “markedly scaphoid abdomen.”<br /></br>• Classic pattern for a left-sided congenital diaphragmatic hernia (Bochdalek defect). Herniated bowel fills the left chest, pushes mediastinum rightward, and leaves the abdomen sunken.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Identify the anatomic problem ➔ diaphragmatic hernia.<br /></br>Step 2: Ask what kills these infants ➔ the herniated viscera prevent normal lung development in utero, producing bilateral (but worse ipsilateral) pulmonary hypoplasia.<br /></br>Step 3: Recognize that hypoplastic lungs cannot provide adequate gas exchange even if the bowel is reduced and the airway ventilated. Persistent pulmonary hypertension of the newborn (PPHN) and refractory hypoxemia stem from the structural hypoplasia, but the root cause of mortality is the lack of functional lung tissue itself.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Congenital diaphragmatic hernia is lethal primarily because of pulmonary hypoplasia, not because of a purely post-natal ventilation problem.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Failure of the pleuroperitoneal membrane to close (most often posterolateral on the left).<br /></br>• In-utero bowel herniation compresses the developing lungs → ↓ number and size of airways and alveoli, thickened pulmonary arterioles.<br /></br>• At birth → severe respiratory distress, PPHN, and high O₂ requirement.<br /></br>• Survival hinges on lung size; even ECMO and gentle ventilation cannot compensate for profoundly hypoplastic lungs.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Initial stabilization: </br>1. Endotracheal intubation (avoid bag-mask ventilation which insufflates the bowel).<br /></br>2. NG/OG tube to decompress stomach/bowel.<br /></br>3. Gentle ventilatory strategy ± HFOV; consider inhaled nitric oxide or prostaglandins for PPHN.<br /></br>4. ECMO bridge in severe cases.<br /></br>Definitive repair: Surgical reduction of hernia and patch repair after cardiorespiratory stabilization.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Congenital cardiac disease with low output – CHD can cause neonatal distress, but the vignette provides no murmurs, differential cyanosis, or shock; it explicitly demonstrates mechanical mediastinal shift and scaphoid abdomen typical of a diaphragmatic hernia.</p></br><p>b. Increased alveolar-arterial (A-a) gradient – True in these infants but represents a measurement, not the primary lethal mechanism. The gradient is high because the lungs are hypoplastic.</p></br><p>c. Intrapulmonary shunting – PPHN causes right-to-left shunt through fetal circulatory pathways, contributing to hypoxemia. However, even if shunting is mitigated (e.g., with inhaled NO or ECMO), mortality remains high when lung tissue is inadequate.</p></br><p>e. Ventilation-perfusion mismatch – Present but, again, secondary to the fundamental absence of alveolar units. Correcting V/Q mismatch does not overcome severely underdeveloped lungs.</p></br><p>Key distinction: the above physiologic derangements (b, c, e) are downstream consequences, whereas pulmonary hypoplasia (d) is the underlying structural defect determining prognosis and survival.</p>
8. A 68-year-old woman comes to the office because of a 4-day history of progressive shortness of breath. She has chronic obstructive pulmonary disease, type 2 diabetes mellitus, hypertension, hyperlipidemia, and osteoarthritis. Her medications are metformin, losartan, lovastatin, and acetaminophen. She also uses continuous home oxygen therapy. Her temperature is 37 C 98.6 F_J> ulse is 70/min respirations are 36/min, and blood pressure is 100/84 mm Hg. Pulse oximet [Y on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 82 percent. On pulmonary examination diffuse crackles are heard bilaterally. On cardiac examination, an S 3 gallop is heard. There is 4+ pitting edema of the lower extremities. Which of the following is most likely to confirm the diagnosis?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Chest x-ray</br>b. CT scan of the chest</br>c. Echocardiography</br>d. 12-Lead ECG</br>e. Ventilation-perfusion lung scans</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>Chest x-ray (option A)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Progressive dyspnea (4 days)<br /></br>• Bilateral diffuse crackles → fluid in alveoli/interstitium<br /></br>• S3 gallop + 4+ LE edema → volume overload, elevated left-sided and systemic venous pressures<br /></br>• Hypoxemia despite home O₂<br /></br>Together these findings point toward acute decompensated congestive heart failure (CHF) rather than a primary pulmonary process (eg, COPD flare, PE).</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Suspect CHF from exam (crackles, S3, edema).<br /></br>Step 2: “What bedside/rapid test confirms pulmonary edema due to CHF?” – a portable chest x-ray will demonstrate classic signs (pulmonary vascular redistribution, interstitial edema, Kerley B lines, perihilar “bat-wing” opacities, pleural effusions, cardiomegaly). It is fast, inexpensive, and routinely used to confirm the diagnosis and guide immediate therapy (IV diuretics, nitrates, ± non-invasive ventilation).</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In a patient with symptoms/signs of volume overload, chest x-ray is the quickest way to confirm pulmonary edema from CHF.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Left ventricular systolic or diastolic dysfunction → ↑LV end-diastolic pressure → ↑left atrial &amp; pulmonary venous pressures → transudation of fluid into pulmonary interstitium/alveoli (crackles, hypoxemia). Long-standing pressure/volume overload also causes right-sided congestion (LE edema). An S3 reflects rapid ventricular filling into a dilated LV.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>Immediate work-up: bedside CXR, BNP, basic labs, ECG.<br /></br>Acute therapy: IV loop diuretics (furosemide), nitrates, oxygen, treat precipitating cause. Echocardiography (once patient stabilized) quantifies EF and valve function; not needed to <em>confirm</em> acute pulmonary edema.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. CT scan of the chest – High-resolution CT is excellent for interstitial lung disease, masses, or pulmonary embolism but is unnecessary, slower, and higher-radiation. CHF is usually evident on plain CXR.<br /></br>C. Echocardiography – Defines cardiac structure/function (EF, wall motion, valves) and helps plan long-term management, but it does not <em>confirm</em> that the current dyspnea is due to pulmonary edema; a normal CXR would argue strongly against acute CHF even if echo shows chronic LV dysfunction.<br /></br>D. 12-Lead ECG – Useful to look for ischemia, arrhythmia, or LVH, but ECG findings are nonspecific for pulmonary edema.<br /></br>E. Ventilation-perfusion lung scan – Used to diagnose pulmonary embolism, not CHF. PE would present with sudden pleuritic pain, clear lungs on auscultation, and usually no S3 or massive peripheral edema.</p></br><p>Key differences: Only chest x-ray gives direct visual evidence of fluid overload in the lungs, confirming CHF as the cause of this patient’s acute dyspnea.</p>
9. A 15-year-old boy is brought to the physician because of a 3-day history of fever, sore throat, and fatigue. He has hemophilia A and requires recombinant factor VIII therapy, as needed. His temperature is 38.2 C 100.8 F , pulse is 90/min, respirations are 24/min, and blood pressure is 100/70 mm Hg. Examination shows exudative tonsillopharyngitis and bilateral lymphadenopathy. There is tenderness to palpation of the right upper quadrant of the abdomen. The liver span is 14 cm. The spleen tip is palpated at the left costal margin. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. CT scan of the liver</br>b. Measurement of serum AST and ALT activities</br>c. Measurement of serum ceruloplasmin concentration</br>d. Measurement of serum electrolyte and glucose concentrations</br>e. Ultrasonography of the liver</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Measurement of serum AST and ALT activities  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Fever + exudative tonsillopharyngitis + posterior cervical lymphadenopathy → classic for infectious mononucleosis (Epstein-Barr virus).<br /></br>• Tender hepatomegaly (liver span 14 cm; RUQ pain) and splenomegaly → EBV commonly causes a mild, self-limited hepatitis.<br /></br>• Hemophilia A with recombinant factor VIII: essentially eliminates the historical risk of transfusion-related viral hepatitis, but it reminds the test-taker to think “liver disease.”<br /></br>Key takeaway: The picture is viral pharyngitis (EBV) with possible accompanying hepatitis; first step is to confirm hepatic involvement non-invasively.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Recognize infectious mono pattern.<br /></br>2. Mono frequently produces asymptomatic or minimally symptomatic elevations in AST/ALT and ALP.<br /></br>3. Before pursuing imaging or exotic labs, obtain basic chemistries that directly test the most likely complication—transaminase elevation.<br /></br>Core concept: In a febrile adolescent with mono-like syndrome and RUQ tenderness, the next step is to check liver enzymes to document hepatitis.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>EBV infects B-cells → widespread immune response → cervical LAD, tonsillar exudate, splenic hyperplasia. EBV hepatitis is due to immune-mediated hepatic inflammation; transaminases usually rise &lt;5× normal and normalize within 3–6 weeks. No specific therapy beyond supportive care.</p></br><p>Treatments / Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>Initial evaluation: CBC (atypical lymphocytosis), heterophile (Monospot) or EBV serology, and hepatic panel (AST, ALT, ALP, bilirubin). Management is supportive; monitor LFTs. Avoid contact sports for ≥3 weeks because of splenic rupture risk.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. CT scan of the liver<br /></br>• Imaging is unnecessary for suspected EBV hepatitis; it would not change acute management and exposes the patient to radiation. Reserved for focal lesions, abscess, or trauma.</p></br><p>c. Measurement of serum ceruloplasmin concentration<br /></br>• Screens for Wilson disease (chronic neuro/hepatic disorder) which presents with months–years of symptoms, neurologic findings, Kayser–Fleischer rings—not acute febrile illness with sore throat.</p></br><p>d. Measurement of serum electrolyte and glucose concentrations<br /></br>• Useful in metabolic derangements, DKA, or severe sepsis; does not address the most likely complication (hepatitis) causing the RUQ pain.</p></br><p>e. Ultrasonography of the liver<br /></br>• Helpful for obstructive or structural biliary disease, portal vein thrombosis, hemangiomas; EBV hepatitis is parenchymal and diagnosed biochemically first. Ultrasound would not change management.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Jumping to imaging in any patient with RUQ pain; always ask whether simple labs (transaminases) will give the needed information first.</p>
10. A 12-month-old girl is brought to the physician 6 hours after the onset of swelling of the lips and an itchy rash over her face and hands; she is drooling and has vomited twice during this period. Her mother says that her daughter had a similar rash once before, but it resolved spontaneously within 1 hour. The girl is breast-fed and eats some table foods. Prior to her symptoms toda~, she ate ice cream for the first time. She appears alert and active. Examination shows multiple erythematous macular lesions over the face, neck, hands, and wrists. Bilateral end-expiratory wheezing is heard on auscultation. Which of the following is most likely to confirm the</br>diagnosis?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Measurement of serum lgA concentration</br>b. Rapid streptococcal testing</br>c. Radioallergosorbent testing</br>d. X-ray of the chest</br>e. X-ray of the lateral neck</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>c. Radioallergosorbent testing (RAST) – serum‐specific IgE assay</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Timing/onset: Sudden (within minutes-hours) after ingestion of a new food → favors an IgE-mediated type I hypersensitivity.<br /></br>• Triggers: “Ate ice cream for the first time” (cow-milk protein).<br /></br>• Signs/Sx: Perioral/lip swelling, pruritic erythematous macules (urticaria), drooling from lip/tongue edema, vomiting, bilateral wheezing (bronchospasm) = systemic reaction (early anaphylaxis).<br /></br>• Past h/o a similar self-limited rash implies prior sensitization.<br /></br>Key pattern: First-time exposure in history but not immunologically—she was likely exposed via breast milk or formula earlier, leading to pre-formed IgE; re-exposure now triggers mast-cell degranulation.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Identify urgent syndrome: IgE-mediated food allergy with early anaphylaxis.<br /></br>2. Confirmation test: Demonstrate allergen-specific IgE in the serum or by skin prick. RAST quantitates allergen-specific IgE without risking provocation, making it ideal in infants.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Immediate food allergy is confirmed by demonstrating allergen-specific IgE (skin testing or in-vitro RAST).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Sensitization: First exposure → Th2 cells → class switching → food-specific IgE bound to mast cells/basophils.<br /></br>• Re-exposure: Allergen cross-links surface IgE → histamine, leukotriene, prostaglandin release → urticaria, angioedema, bronchospasm, GI symptoms.<br /></br>Risk factors: Atopy, family history, immature gut barrier in infants.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostics &amp; Treatment (FYI)<br /></br>Immediate management (exam favorite): intramuscular epinephrine, airway support, antihistamines, corticosteroids.<br /></br>Chronic: Strict avoidance, emergency epinephrine autoinjector, consider oral immunotherapy in specialized centers.<br /></br>Diagnostic hierarchy:<br /></br>• First line – Skin prick testing or serum-specific IgE (RAST/ImmunoCAP).<br /></br>• Oral food challenge = gold standard but reserved for equivocal cases under supervision.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Serum IgA concentration<br /></br>   – Screens for selective IgA deficiency (predisposes to recurrent sinopulmonary infections &amp; anaphylaxis to blood products containing IgA). Does not detect food-specific IgE or confirm a milk allergy.</p></br><p>b. Rapid streptococcal test<br /></br>   – Diagnoses group A strep pharyngitis (fever, exudative sore throat). Unrelated to acute urticaria/wheezing after food.</p></br><p>d. Chest X-ray<br /></br>   – Useful for pneumonia, foreign body, lower-airway obstruction. Anaphylaxis causes diffuse bronchospasm without focal radiographic findings.</p></br><p>e. Lateral neck X-ray<br /></br>   – Evaluates epiglottitis or retropharyngeal abscess (toxic, high fever, tripod, stridor). This child is non-toxic, has diffuse wheezing, pruritic rash; drooling is from lip/tongue edema, not supraglottic obstruction.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Drooling can steer examinees toward epiglottitis; always correlate with absence of high fever, stridor, tripod position, and presence of urticaria/wheezing → think allergic angioedema instead.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Acute urticaria, angioedema, GI upset, and wheezing minutes after ingesting a new food = IgE-mediated food allergy. Confirm by demonstrating allergen-specific IgE with a serum RAST assay (or skin prick test).</p>
11. A 37-year-old man comes to the physician because o a 2-week history of fever, progressive fatigue, abdominal pain, nausea, and vomiting. He also has had dark urine and clay-colored stools during this time. Six weeks</br>ago, he traveled to Central America where he ate the local food, including raw shellfish and vegetables. His temperature is 38 C 100.4  F, pulse is 90/min and regular, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is</br>110/85 mm Hg. ElXamination shows scleral icterus. The liver is tender and palpated 6 cm below the right costal margin. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hemoglobin 15 g/dL</br>Mean corpuscular volume 100 μm 3</br>Serum</br>Total bilirubin</br>Alkaline phosphatase</br>AST</br>ALT</br>7.1 mg/dL</br>105 U/L</br>2000 U/L</br>1500 U/L</br>Without treatment, which of the following is the most likely outcome of this patient's condition?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Chronic carrier state</br>b. Chronic hepatitis</br>c. Fulminant hepatitis</br>d. Hepatocellular carcinoma</br>e. Complete resolution of symptoms</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Complete resolution of symptoms  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues: 2-week history of jaundice, very high AST/ALT (&gt;1000 U/L) → acute hepatocellular injury; incubation ≈6 weeks after travel; ate raw shellfish/contaminated produce in Central America; no IV-drug use, transfusions, or sexual risk factors mentioned.<br /></br>• Classic epidemiology for hepatitis A (HAV) (fecal–oral, short incubation, shellfish, travel to endemic areas). HAV (and, on exams, HEV) causes an acute, self-limited hepatitis with NO chronicity.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Identify likely virus → HAV.<br /></br>Step 2: Recall natural history → 99% of immunocompetent adults fully recover within weeks to months, developing lifelong immunity.<br /></br>Step 3: Match with answer choices → “Complete resolution of symptoms.” Core takeaway: HAV produces an acute, self-limited illness; it does not progress to chronic hepatitis, carrier state, cirrhosis, or HCC.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: RNA picornavirus transmitted fecal-orally (contaminated water, food, raw shellfish).<br /></br>• Incubation: ~2–6 weeks.<br /></br>• Mechanism: Viral replication in hepatocytes → immune-mediated cell death → marked transaminase elevation; cholestasis causes dark urine &amp; pale stools.<br /></br>• Immunity: IgM anti-HAV peaks early; class-switch to lifelong protective IgG. Virus cleared completely; no integration into host genome.  </p></br><p>Treatments / Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• Diagnosis: Serum anti-HAV IgM confirms acute infection.<br /></br>• Treatment: Supportive care (hydration, rest, nutrition).<br /></br>• Prevention: Inactivated HAV vaccine (two doses) and immune globulin for post-exposure prophylaxis.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Chronic carrier state – Seen with hepatitis B (HBsAg positive &gt;6 mo) and potentially C; HAV never enters a carrier state.<br /></br>b. Chronic hepatitis – Only HBV, HCV, HDV (with HBV coinfection) cause chronic active hepatitis; HAV infection resolves.<br /></br>c. Fulminant hepatitis – Massive hepatic necrosis within days (↑PT/INR, encephalopathy). Rare with HAV (≈0.1%); more common with HEV in pregnancy. Nothing in vignette (male, stable vitals) suggests fulminant failure.<br /></br>d. Hepatocellular carcinoma – Complication of chronic HBV/HCV cirrhosis; HAV does not persist long enough to induce oncogenesis.<br /></br>e. Complete resolution of symptoms – Expected outcome for acute HAV; matches epidemiology and natural history.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfalls<br /></br>• Confusing HAV (self-limited) with HBV (risk of chronicity).<br /></br>• Assuming all viral hepatitis can become chronic—memorize that HAV and exam-level HEV do not.</p>
12. A 35-year-old woman with systemic lupus erythematosus is brought to the emergency department because of a 3-day history of sharp midchest pain that worsens with inspiration and lying on her back. Her only medication</br>is hydroxychloroquine. She appears mildly uncomfortable. Her temperature is 38.1  C 100.5 F, pulse is 96/min, respirations are 14/min, and blood pressure is 135/87 mm Hg. Examination shows a malar rash. The lungs</br>are clear to auscultation. On cardiac examination, a scratchy, high-pitched sound is heard over the lower left sternal border. There is erythema, swelling, and warmth of the wrists. An ECG is shown. Which of the following</br>is the most appropriate initial pharmacotherapy?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Clopidogrel</br>b. Cyclophosphamide</br>c. Enoxaparin</br>d. Ibuprofen</br>e. Rituximab</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Ibuprofen  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Sharp mid-chest pain that worsens with inspiration and lying on her back” + “scratchy, high-pitched sound over the lower left sternal border” = classic pleuritic chest pain with a pericardial friction rub → acute pericarditis.<br /></br>• Systemic lupus erythematosus (SLE) is a common autoimmune cause of pericarditis.<br /></br>• Low-grade fever (38.1 °C) and joint inflammation further support an SLE flare rather than coronary ischemia or pulmonary embolism.<br /></br>• ECG (not shown) in pericarditis classically shows diffuse, concave (“saddle-shaped”) ST-segment elevations with PR-segment depressions.  </p></br><p>Key pattern: SLE patient + pleuritic positional chest pain + friction rub + diffuse ST ↑/PR ↓ → autoimmune (lupus) acute pericarditis.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Identify pericarditis.<br /></br>Step 2: Determine hemodynamic stability (vital signs stable, no signs of tamponade).<br /></br>Step 3: Choose first-line therapy.<br /></br>• Uncomplicated acute pericarditis (even when autoimmune) is initially treated with a high-dose NSAID (e.g., ibuprofen, indomethacin) plus colchicine unless contraindicated.<br /></br>• Glucocorticoids are reserved for refractory/contraindicated NSAID use or severe CT-disease flares with major organ involvement.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: In acute pericarditis—no matter the etiology—the initial drug of choice is a high-dose NSAID to suppress pericardial inflammation.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Autoimmune pericardial inflammation results from immune-complex deposition in the pericardium.<br /></br>• The inflamed pericardial layers rub during cardiac motion, producing pleuritic pain and a friction rub.<br /></br>• Inflammation generates ST-segment elevations diffusely (all leads except aVR/V1) and PR depression (atrial involvement).  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>First-line<br /></br>1. NSAID (high-dose ibuprofen 600–800 mg q6-8 h or indomethacin)<br /></br>2. Colchicine 0.5–0.6 mg bid × 3 months (↓ recurrence)  </p></br><p>Second-line / special situations<br /></br>• Prednisone or methylprednisolone if NSAIDs contraindicated (e.g., renal insufficiency) or refractory, or if severe SLE organ involvement co-exists.<br /></br>• Pericardiocentesis only if large effusion with tamponade physiology.  </p></br><p>Work-up<br /></br>• ESR/CRP (usually elevated)<br /></br>• Echocardiography to rule out effusion/tamponade<br /></br>• Renal function before high-dose NSAID therapy  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Clopidogrel – Antiplatelet used for ACS or stroke prevention. No evidence of coronary ischemia (positional pain, friction rub, diffuse—not regional—ST changes). Does not treat pericardial inflammation.  </p></br><p>b. Cyclophosphamide – Alkylating agent for severe, life-threatening SLE manifestations (Class III/IV lupus nephritis, CNS lupus, vasculitis). Excessive for isolated pericarditis; significant toxicity (hemorrhagic cystitis, infertility) makes it unsuitable as initial therapy.  </p></br><p>c. Enoxaparin – Anticoagulation for venous/arterial thrombosis or ACS. Not indicated in pericarditis and may precipitate hemorrhagic pericardial effusion. Would be considered only if antiphospholipid-related thrombosis present (not suggested here).  </p></br><p>e. Rituximab – Anti-CD20 monoclonal antibody for refractory, severe SLE or lupus nephritis after failure of cyclophosphamide/mycophenolate. Again, over-treatment for uncomplicated pericarditis.  </p></br><p>Pitfall to avoid: Thinking steroids or cytotoxic agents are always first-line in lupus. Even in lupus, uncomplicated pericarditis follows the same NSAID-first algorithm unless contraindications exist.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Uncomplicated acute pericarditis—whether idiopathic, viral, or autoimmune (SLE)—is initially managed with high-dose NSAIDs (e.g., ibuprofen) plus colchicine.</p>
13. A 70-year-old man is brought to the office by his niece because of a 6-month history of frequent falls. She says he has fallen backward suddenly while walking approximate!~ once monthly during this time. She adds that</br>he has a tendency to look down at his feet while walking. Recently, his speech has become slurred and he has had several choking episodes while eating. He also has had difficulty reading. He has not had</br>light-headedness on standing, difficulty with urination, constipation, changes in mood, or hallucinations. Physical examination shows mild nuchal rigidity and symmetric rigidity of all extremities. There is no tremor. There is</br>impaired visual gaze that is worse when the patient attempts to look down. He has decreased stride length on gait testing and impaired balance while turning. His speech is soft. He has poor enunciation and impaired letter</br>fluency. No other abnormalities are noted. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Cerebellar astrocytoma</br>b. Cervical myelopathy</br>c. Lewy body dementia</br>d. Parkinson disease</br>e. Progressive supranuclear pals~</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Progressive supranuclear palsy (PSP)</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis (key buzzwords)<br /></br>• “70-year-old” → neuro-degenerative time-frame<br /></br>• “Falls backward … once monthly” + “looks down at his feet” → early, unexplained postural instability (especially retropulsion)<br /></br>• “Speech has become slurred … choking while eating” → pseudobulbar/bulbar dysfunction<br /></br>• “Impaired visual gaze that is worse when the patient attempts to look down” → vertical (primarily downward) supranuclear gaze palsy: THE hallmark of PSP<br /></br>• “Symmetric rigidity” and “no tremor” → axial rigidity out of proportion to limb tremor (tremor minimal or absent in PSP)<br /></br>These features together are classic for progressive supranuclear palsy.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach:<br /></br>1. First-year falls + axial rigidity → atypical parkinsonism &gt; idiopathic PD.<br /></br>2. Add vertical gaze palsy → essentially pathognomonic for PSP (Parkinson disease never has a true supranuclear vertical gaze palsy early).<br /></br>3. No hallucinations/cognitive fluctuations → argues against Lewy body dementia.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Early postural instability + vertical gaze palsy + axial rigidity = Progressive supranuclear palsy.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• PSP is a sporadic 4-repeat tauopathy → accumulation of hyper-phosphorylated tau in the brainstem (midbrain tectum, periaqueductal gray) and basal ganglia.<br /></br>• Leads to degeneration of vertical gaze centers (rostral interstitial nucleus of the medial longitudinal fasciculus), globus pallidus, subthalamic nucleus.<br /></br>Clinical triad:<br /></br>1. Early unexplained falls (retropulsion)<br /></br>2. Supranuclear vertical gaze palsy (down &gt; up)<br /></br>3. Axial &gt; appendicular rigidity, bradykinesia, dysarthria/dysphagia<br /></br>Cognitive impairment (frontal executive dysfunction) eventually develops but is usually later.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>• MRI: “hummingbird” or “penguin” midbrain atrophy sign.<br /></br>• Pharmacologic response to levodopa is poor or transient (key distinction from PD).<br /></br>• Supportive care: botulinum toxin for neck dystonia, gait training, swallow evaluation, academic counselling.<br /></br>• Prognosis: median survival ≈ 5–8 yrs.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>a. Cerebellar astrocytoma<br /></br>   – Typically a pediatric tumor; presents with limb ataxia, nystagmus, dysmetria, ICP signs. No vertical gaze palsy or symmetric axial rigidity.</p></br><p>b. Cervical myelopathy<br /></br>   – Would produce upper-motor-neuron signs (hyperreflexia, Babinski), sensory level, possibly Lhermitte sign. Does not explain vertical gaze palsy or speech/choking.</p></br><p>c. Lewy body dementia<br /></br>   – Triad: fluctuating cognition, vivid visual hallucinations, parkinsonism. This vignette lacks hallucinations and cognitive fluctuations; falls occur but vertical gaze palsy is not a feature.</p></br><p>d. Parkinson disease<br /></br>   – Classic features: unilateral onset resting tremor, cogwheel rigidity, bradykinesia, good levodopa response; falls and axial rigidity occur late, vertical gaze is preserved.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Early backward falls + supranuclear vertical (downward) gaze palsy + axial rigidity with minimal tremor = Progressive supranuclear palsy, an atypical parkinsonian tauopathy.</p>
14. A 35-year-old primigravid woman is brought to the emergency department ED by paramedics after a neighbor called emergency services because the patient was being disruptive and combative. En route to the ED,</br>fingerstick blood glucose concentration was 45 mg/dl; intravenous 50 percent dextrose was administered, and her mental status improved. On arrival, she is fully oriented. She has a 10-year history of type 2 diabetes mellitus</br>treated with insulin. She says she is pregnant but does not know when she had her last menstrual period. She has not yet initiated prenatal care. She has smoked two packs of cigarettes daily for 23 years. She does not</br>drink alcohol or use illicit drugs. Vital signs are within normal limits. On examination, the uterus is nontender; fundal height is 2 cm below the umbilicus. Fetal heart rate is 146/min. Pelvic ultrasonography shows a single</br>intrauterine fetus consistent in size with a 17-week gestation; the thighs and legs are fused, and there are two femoral and two tibial bones within a single soft-tissue and muscular compartment. The amniotic fluid index is</br>2 cm N=10-20. Which of the following is the most likely outcome of this patient's pregnancy?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Cerebral palsy</br>b. Macrosomia</br>c. Microcephaly</br>d. Neonatal hypoglycemia</br>e. Stillbirth</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Stillbirth  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues<br /></br>  – Long-standing, insulin-treated type 2 diabetes mellitus (pre-gestational DM).<br /></br>  – 17-week ultrasound: fused thighs/legs with two femurs &amp; tibias in a single compartment → classic description of caudal regression sequence / sirenomelia.<br /></br>  – Amniotic fluid index 2 cm (severe oligohydramnios).<br /></br>  – Heavy cigarette smoking (growth-restriction risk).<br /></br>• Why these clues matter<br /></br>  Maternal pre-gestational diabetes, especially when poorly controlled in the first 4–6 weeks of embryogenesis, dramatically increases the risk of major structural anomalies. The most pathognomonic is caudal regression (sacral agenesis or, in the extreme, sirenomelia with fused lower limbs). Renal agenesis often accompanies sirenomelia → absent fetal urine → severe oligohydramnios → pulmonary hypoplasia and in-utero demise or death shortly after birth.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach:<br /></br>1. Identify maternal condition (long-standing DM).<br /></br>2. Recognize the specific fetal anomaly (caudal regression with oligohydramnios).<br /></br>3. Recall prognosis: Most affected fetuses die in utero (stillbirth) or within hours/days because of renal agenesis &amp; pulmonary hypoplasia.<br /></br>Take-home: Caudal regression sequence in diabetic pregnancies is strongly associated with stillbirth.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Maternal hyperglycemia disrupts mesodermal development of the caudal embryo.<br /></br>• Associated defects: Sacral/lumbar agenesis, lower-limb fusion, genitourinary &amp; gastrointestinal malformations (bilateral renal agenesis in sirenomelia).<br /></br>• Mechanism of death: Renal agenesis → anhydramnios → Potter sequence (pulmonary hypoplasia, limb deformities) → respiratory failure or intrauterine demise.  </p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• Prenatal diagnosis: High-resolution US or fetal MRI.<br /></br>• No curative prenatal therapy; counseling regarding poor prognosis and option for pregnancy termination is standard.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Cerebral palsy – Typically a consequence of perinatal hypoxic-ischemic injury or extreme prematurity, not a direct result of caudal regression or oligohydramnios.<br /></br>b. Macrosomia – Common with diabetic mothers in the absence of severe congenital anomalies. Caudal regression with renal agenesis → often growth restriction, not over-growth, especially with heavy maternal smoking.<br /></br>c. Microcephaly – Seen with congenital infections (Zika, CMV), genetic syndromes, or teratogens like isotretinoin; not characteristic of maternal diabetes–induced caudal regression.<br /></br>d. Neonatal hypoglycemia – Very common in live-born infants of diabetic mothers due to pancreatic β-cell hyperplasia, but requires survival to delivery; most sirenomelia fetuses do not survive to term.<br /></br>e. Stillbirth – Fits the typical natural history of severe caudal regression/sirenomelia with oligohydramnios.  </p></br><p>Core Concept<br /></br>Early, poorly controlled maternal diabetes can cause caudal regression sequence, which is usually lethal in utero or shortly after delivery, making stillbirth the most likely pregnancy outcome.</p>
15. A 92-year-old woman is brought to the emergency department because of a 1-week history of generalized fatigue and weakness. She has been a resident in a skilled nursing care facility for 30 years and is nonambulatory.</br>This morning, she became agitated when the medical staff carefully lifted her arms and legs to move her on the bed. She has intestinal malabsorption syndrome, pulmonary fibrosis, dementia, and global deconditioning.</br>She receives chronic parenteral nutrition. She appears pale and is oriented to person but not to place or time. She can follow simple commands. Palpation of the upper extremities and thighs produces pain. She can flex</br>and extend the elbows and wrists and dorsiflex and plantarflex the ankles and extend the toes bilaterally. Sensation is intact over the extremities. Skeletal survey shows pseudofractures of the femur and proximal ulna</br>bilaterally. Which of the following serum concentrations is most likely to be increased in this patient?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Calcium</br>b. 1,25-Hydroxyvitamin D</br>c. Magnesium</br>d. Parathyroid hormone</br>e. Phosphorus</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Parathyroid hormone  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Chronic parenteral nutrition,” “intestinal malabsorption,” and “pseudofractures (Looser zones)” → classic red flags for vitamin D deficiency → osteomalacia.<br /></br>• Bone pain with gentle lifting, diffuse weakness, and inability to bear weight all fit defective mineralization of osteoid.<br /></br>• Elderly, institutionalized, little sunlight → additional vitamin D risk factors.  </p></br><p>Key diagnostic pattern: malabsorption/TPN + Looser zones + bone pain → vitamin D deficiency → secondary hyperparathyroidism.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Vitamin D deficiency → ↓ intestinal Ca²⁺ &amp; PO₄³⁻ absorption.<br /></br>2. Falling serum Ca²⁺ triggers compensatory ↑ PTH secretion (secondary hyperparathyroidism).<br /></br>3. PTH tries to normalize Ca²⁺ by:<br /></br>   • ↑ bone resorption (worsening pain/pseudofractures).<br /></br>   • ↑ renal Ca²⁺ reabsorption &amp; ↑ PO₄³⁻ wasting (keeping phosphate low).<br /></br>4. Net biochemical picture:<br /></br>   • Ca²⁺: low‐normal or low<br /></br>   • PO₄³⁻: low<br /></br>   • PTH: high (only value guaranteed to be elevated)<br /></br>   • Alk phos: high (not asked)<br /></br>   • 25-OH-vitamin D: low; 1,25-OH₂-vitamin D low-normal (substrate‐limited)</p></br><p>Therefore the serum concentration most likely to be increased is parathyroid hormone.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Any condition causing chronic vitamin D deficiency or resistance → secondary hyperparathyroidism with elevated PTH and low phosphate.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>Vitamin D (cholecalciferol/ergocalciferol) → 25-OH in liver → 1,25-OH₂ in kidney.<br /></br>Deficiency (malabsorption, TPN without supplements, no sunlight) → reduced Ca/PO₄ absorption → inadequate mineralization of osteoid (osteomalacia in adults, rickets in children).<br /></br>Pseudofractures = transverse lucencies at stress points (Looser zones).  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatments / Diagnostics<br /></br>• Labs: ↓ 25-OH-vit D, ↑ PTH, ↓ PO₄, low-normal Ca, ↑ alkaline phosphatase.<br /></br>• Radiology: Looser zones, diffuse osteopenia.<br /></br>• Management: High-dose oral or parenteral vitamin D (cholecalciferol or calcitriol if kidney failure) + Ca²⁺ supplementation; correct phosphate if severely low.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Calcium – Usually decreased or low-normal because vitamin D deficiency impairs GI absorption; would not be elevated.<br /></br>b. 1,25-Hydroxyvitamin D – Depends on substrate; can be low or normal but not elevated in deficiency (kidney cannot make enough without 25-OH substrate).<br /></br>c. Magnesium – Typically normal; may even be low if malnourished but not elevated.<br /></br>e. Phosphorus – PTH-mediated phosphaturia keeps serum PO₄³⁻ low, not high.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Assuming calcium rises whenever PTH rises. In secondary hyperparathyroidism (vit D deficiency or chronic kidney disease), PTH is high but calcium is low-normal because the underlying defect persists.</p>
16. A 4-year-old boy is brought to the emergency department by his mother after he refused to walk or bear weight on his left leg this morning. She says he has been irritable during the past 3 days. He has a 3-month history</br>of episodes of moderate pain in his shoulders and ankles, but the pain has not been as severe as his current symptom. His mother says he bruises easily. He has not had recent trauma or fever. He was born at term by</br>spontaneous vaginal delivery following an uncomplicated pregnancy. He walked at the age of 11 months. He attends preschool. He is at the 70th percentile for height and 60th percentile for weight. Vital signs are within</br>normal limits. There is no gingival bleeding, and the teeth are intact. There is a deep ecchymosis over the anterior aspect of the left thigh; the skin otherwise appears normal. The patient is unable to extend the left knee</br>because of pain. Laboratory studies are most likely to show which of the following sets of findings in this patient?</br>Platelet Partial</br>Count Prothrombin Thromboplastin</br>/mm 3 Time sec Time sec	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. 80,000 12 30</br>b. 80,000 30 80</br>c. 150,000 30 30</br>d. 300,000 12 30</br>e. 300,000 12 80</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. 300,000 /mm³ PT 12 sec (normal) aPTT 80 sec (prolonged)</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “4-year-old boy” + “no recent trauma” → think congenital bleeding disorder.<br /></br>• Recurrent “pain in shoulders and ankles” and now inability to extend the knee with a “deep ecchymosis” → classic hemarthrosis (bleeding into joints).<br /></br>• “Bruises easily,” but NO mucosal / gingival bleeding → points away from platelet disorders.<br /></br>• Vital signs normal, no fever → not septic arthritis.<br /></br>Pattern: Boy + deep-tissue / joint bleeding = Hemophilia (factor VIII or IX deficiency).</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Deep-tissue &amp; joint bleeds → coagulation factor defect, NOT platelet problem.<br /></br>2. Hemophilia affects the intrinsic pathway → isolated prolongation of activated partial thromboplastin time (aPTT).<br /></br>3. Platelet count &amp; function (bleeding time) and prothrombin time (extrinsic pathway) are normal.  </p></br><p>Take-home: Hemarthroses = normal platelets, normal PT, prolonged aPTT.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Hemophilia A (↓factor VIII, X-linked) or Hemophilia B (↓factor IX, X-linked)<br /></br>• Intrinsic pathway factors → measured by aPTT.<br /></br>• Lack of factor → unstable fibrin clot → deep bleeding, hemarthroses, muscle hematomas.<br /></br>• Platelets unaffected, so mucosal bleeding is minimal or absent.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatments &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>Diagnostics:<br /></br>• CBC: normal platelet count.<br /></br>• Coag tests: normal PT, markedly ↑aPTT.<br /></br>• Factor VIII or IX assays confirm diagnosis.</p></br><p>Treatment (USMLE high-yield):<br /></br>• Acute bleed: Recombinant factor VIII (A) or IX (B).<br /></br>• Mild Hemophilia A: Desmopressin (DDAVP) to release vWF &amp; factor VIII.<br /></br>• Adjunct: antifibrinolytics (ε-aminocaproic acid, tranexamic acid).</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. 80 000 /mm³, PT 12, aPTT 30<br /></br> Thrombocytopenia. Would give petechiae, mucosal bleeding (epistaxis, gingival), not isolated hemarthroses.</p></br><p>b. 80 000 /mm³, PT 30, aPTT 80<br /></br> Low platelets + prolonged PT &amp; aPTT → DIC or severe liver disease; patient would be acutely ill with widespread bleeding, not a well-appearing child with chronic joint issues.</p></br><p>c. 150 000 /mm³, PT 30, aPTT 30<br /></br> Isolated prolonged PT → factor VII deficiency or vitamin K antagonism (warfarin). Warfarin toxicity is unlikely in a healthy 4-year-old; also would not selectively cause hemarthroses.</p></br><p>d. 300 000 /mm³, PT 12, aPTT 30<br /></br> Completely normal coagulation panel—cannot explain significant joint bleeding.</p></br><p>e. 300 000 /mm³, PT 12, aPTT 80<br /></br> Matches hemophilia profile -&gt; correct.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Core Concept<br /></br>Deep joint or muscle bleeding in a boy = hemophilia → normal platelets, normal PT, prolonged aPTT.</p>
17. A 37-year-old woman comes to the emergency department 2 hours after cutting her hand while working in the garden. She received a tetanus vaccine 3 months ago. Examination shows a clean wound. After the wound is</br>treated, the patient insists on receiving another tetanus shot. The physician explains that the previous vaccine adequately protects her, but the patient insists that it be repeated. Which of the following is the most appropriate</br>next step?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Administer 0.9 percent saline and tell the patient that it is tetanus toxoid</br>b. Administer tetanus toxoid and bill it to the patient's insurance</br>c. Explain that the patient will have to pay the full fee if she receives the vaccine</br>d. Refuse to administer another vaccine and repeat the explanation</br>e. Tell the patient to find another physician in the emergency department who will administer the vaccine</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>d. Refuse to administer another vaccine and repeat the explanation</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• “Clean wound,” “tetanus vaccine 3 months ago” → Patient is already up-to-date (Td/Tdap booster is recommended every 10 years for clean wounds).<br /></br>• The patient “insists on receiving another tetanus shot” despite adequate counseling → tests physician’s response to an unreasonable request.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step-by-step reasoning:</br>1. Is another dose medically indicated? No. Most recent booster within 5 years (actually ≤10 y) for a clean wound = fully protected.<br /></br>2. Does the request pose potential harm? Yes. Additional doses carry a higher risk of Arthus-type local reactions and serum sickness–like illness.<br /></br>3. Ethical principles involved:</br>   • Non-maleficence (avoid unnecessary risk)<br /></br>   • Beneficence (provide evidence-based care)<br /></br>   • Veracity (truth-telling)<br /></br>   • Autonomy is not absolute—patients cannot demand non-beneficial or harmful interventions.</br>4. Appropriate action: Decline the request, re-educate, document counseling. Hence choice (d).</p></br><p>Pathophysiology / Clinical Review</br>Tetanus immunity: Neutralizing antibody titers remain protective for at least 10 y after a booster. Giving a booster too soon → immune-complex deposition in local tissues (Arthus reaction: severe pain, induration, sometimes necrosis). Therefore extra doses are avoided unless a high-risk dirty wound and &gt;5 y since last booster.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics</br>• Clean minor wound + booster within 10 y → No Td/Tdap, no TIG.<br /></br>• Dirty/contaminated wound → Booster if &gt;5 y; TIG if vaccination status unknown or &lt;3 doses.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Give saline placebo and claim it is tetanus toxoid<br /></br>   – Violates veracity; deceptive practice; destroys trust.</p></br><p>b. Administer tetanus toxoid and bill insurance<br /></br>   – Unnecessary medical treatment (non-beneficial, potential harm) + insurance fraud.</p></br><p>c. Administer vaccine if patient pays out-of-pocket<br /></br>   – Charging does not justify providing non-indicated care; still violates non-maleficence.</p></br><p>e. Tell patient to find another physician who will give it<br /></br>   – Abandonment and shifts unethical practice to a colleague; does not fulfill duty to provide proper care.</p></br><p>Key Takeaway</br>Patient autonomy does not obligate a physician to provide non-indicated, potentially harmful treatment. The correct response is to refuse the request, clearly re-explain the rationale, and document the discussion.</p>
18. A 32-year-old woman is brought to the emergency department because of a 1-hour history of palpitations, tremors, light-headedness, and sweating. She has not had loss of consciousness, chest pain, or shortness of</br>breath. She has had similar episodes in the past, which were relieved by eating and with rest. Her only medication is lorazepam as needed for generalized anxiety disorder. There is no family history of similar episodes.</br>Her temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 124/min, respirations are 24/min, and blood pressure is 138/90 mm Hg. Physical and mental status examinations show no abnormalities. Serum studies show:</br>Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Serum antimicrosomal antibody titer</br>b. Measurement of serum cortisol concentration</br>c. Measurement of serum C peptide concentration</br>d. Measurement of serum thyroxine concentration</br>e. 24-Hour urine collection for measurement of catecholamines</br>F. CT scan of the head</br></br></br><p>Correct answer</br>c. Measurement of serum C-peptide concentration</p></br><p>Vignette analysis</br>• Classic neuro-glycopenic/sympathetic symptoms: “palpitations, tremor, light-headedness, sweating” = autonomic activation provoked by hypoglycemia.<br /></br>• Episodes resolve “by eating and with rest” → strongly suggests low serum glucose is the trigger.<br /></br>• Young woman, no diabetes, no drug history that explains episodic hypoglycemia → think endogenous hyperinsulinemia (insulinoma or surreptitious sulfonylurea use).<br /></br>• Vitals: tachycardic but otherwise normal; exam unrevealing – typical for hypoglycemic episodes outside of actual seizure/coma.  </p></br><p>Clinical critical reasoning</br>Step 1: Confirm Whipple triad (symptoms of hypoglycemia, low plasma glucose, relief with glucose). ED glucose would already be low in this scenario.<br /></br>Step 2: When low glucose is documented, measure insulin AND C-peptide.<br /></br>– High insulin + high C-peptide → endogenous source (insulinoma or sulfonylurea).<br /></br>– High insulin + low/undetectable C-peptide → exogenous insulin administration.<br /></br>Therefore the single best next diagnostic test is a serum C-peptide level obtained at the time of documented hypoglycemia.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In any nondiabetic patient with recurrent fasting/ exertional hypoglycemia, the very next step after confirming low glucose is to check insulin together with C-peptide to distinguish endogenous vs exogenous insulin excess.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology review</br>Insulinoma (β-cell tumor) secretes insulin independent of glucose level. Excess insulin drives glucose into cells, causing recurrent fasting hypoglycemia. Endogenous insulin is secreted as proinsulin, which is cleaved to insulin + C-peptide in equimolar amounts; exogenous insulin lacks C-peptide. Sulfonylureas also increase both insulin and C-peptide. Distinguishing these entities guides further work-up (e.g., sulfonylurea screen, imaging of pancreas).</p></br><p>Treatment / diagnostic cascade</br>1. Document Whipple triad.<br /></br>2. Measure insulin, C-peptide, β-hydroxybutyrate, and screen for oral hypoglycemic agents during an episode or supervised 72-hour fast.<br /></br>3. If endogenous overproduction is confirmed, localize tumor (CT/MRI pancreas, endoscopic US).<br /></br>4. Definitive treatment = surgical enucleation/partial pancreatectomy; diazoxide or octreotide can be temporizing medical therapy.</p></br><p>Incorrect answer analysis</br>a. Serum antimicrosomal (anti-TPO) antibody titer – evaluates for autoimmune thyroid disease. Hyperthyroidism can cause palpitations/tremor but does not produce episodic hypoglycemia relieved by eating; thyroid antibodies give no information on hypoglycemia.</p></br><p>b. Measurement of serum cortisol concentration – Adrenal insufficiency can cause fasting hypoglycemia but usually presents with hypotension, weight loss, hyperpigmentation, hyponatremia, not isolated sympathetic symptoms rapidly relieved by eating.</p></br><p>d. Measurement of serum thyroxine concentration – Same reasoning as (a); thyroid hormones cause chronic, not episodic, adrenergic symptoms and are not linked to relief with food intake.</p></br><p>e. 24-hour urine catecholamines – Screens for pheochromocytoma (paroxysmal HTN, headaches, diaphoresis). Pheochromocytoma episodes are not consistently relieved by eating and would not create documented hypoglycemia.</p></br><p>f.
