CT scan of the head – Appropriate for evaluating seizure or altered-mental-status etiology, not first-line when biochemical hypoglycemia is suspected and needs clarification of insulin source. Imaging of the pancreas, not the brain, follows once endogenous hyperinsulinemia is proven.</p></br><p>Key exam pearl</br>Low glucose + neuro-adrenergic symptoms in a non-diabetic patient → draw insulin AND C-peptide immediately. Elevation of both confirms endogenous insulin excess and directs further work-up for insulinoma or sulfonylurea use.</p>
19. A 24-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 3-day history of progressive right-sided weakness and numbness. One year ago while traveling overseas, she had loss of vision in the left eye that developed</br>gradually during the course of 1 week, persisted for 1 month, and resolved gradually and spontaneously during the following 3 weeks. At its worst, her vision in the left eye was severely blurred and colors appeared</br>washed out. Her vision recovered completely, and she did not see a physician. She has not had similar symptoms since that time. She has no other history of serious illness and takes no medications. Examination shows</br>mild weakness of the right lower aspect of the face. Muscle strength is 4/5 in the right extremities. Sensation to light touch is mildly decreased over the right side of the face and body. Examination shows no abnormalities</br>on the left. An MRI of the brain is shown. Which of the following is the most appropriate pharmacotherapy to decrease the recurrence of similar symptoms in this patient?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Intravenous immune globulin</br>b. Intravenous methylprednisolone</br>c. Oral aspirin</br>d. Plasmapheresis</br>e. Subcutaneous glatiramer</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Subcutaneous glatiramer acetate  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Young woman (24 yrs) with neurologic episodes separated by both time and anatomic location (“dissemination in time and space”):<br /></br>  – 1 yr ago: unilateral optic neuritis (painful vision loss, color desaturation, spontaneous recovery).<br /></br>  – Now: right facial weakness, hemiparesis, hemisensory loss.<br /></br>• Brain MRI (image not shown to you) on NBME questions typically demonstrates multiple, ovoid, periventricular white-matter lesions (“Dawson fingers”) consistent with demyelination.<br /></br>These key features all point to relapsing–remitting multiple sclerosis (RR-MS).</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1 – Identify the disorder: relapsing–remitting MS.<br /></br>Step 2 – Determine what the question is asking: “pharmacotherapy to decrease the recurrence of similar symptoms” = disease-modifying therapy (DMT), not treatment of the current flare.<br /></br>Step 3 – Among available options, first-line DMTs for RR-MS are interferon-β preparations, glatiramer acetate, dimethyl fumarate, or teriflunomide. From the list provided, glatiramer acetate is the only correct choice.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In MS, corticosteroids treat acute relapses; injectable DMTs such as interferon-β or glatiramer acetate are used chronically to reduce relapse frequency and slow disability progression.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>• Autoimmune, T-cell–mediated demyelination of CNS oligodendrocytes → plaques of myelin loss with relative axonal preservation.<br /></br>• Relapsing–remitting course in 85–90 % of patients; each relapse reflects new lesions/inflammation.<br /></br>• Risk factors: female sex, HLA-DRB1, temperate climates (vitamin D deficiency), smoking.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Acute relapse<br /></br>1. IV high-dose methylprednisolone (1 g/d for 3–5 days).<br /></br>2. If steroid-refractory: plasmapheresis.  </p></br><p>Disease-modifying therapy (relapse prevention)<br /></br>First-line injectable agents<br /></br>• Interferon-β-1a, interferon-β-1b.<br /></br>• Glatiramer acetate (20 mg SC daily or 40 mg SC 3×/week).<br /></br>Other oral/IV options for more aggressive disease<br /></br>• Dimethyl fumarate, teriflunomide, fingolimod, natalizumab, ocrelizumab, alemtuzumab, cladribine.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Intravenous immune globulin (IVIG)<br /></br>   – Occasionally used in other autoimmune neuromuscular disorders (e.g., Guillain-Barré, myasthenia gravis), but has no proven benefit as chronic DMT in MS.  </p></br><p>b. Intravenous methylprednisolone<br /></br>   – Appropriate for treating the CURRENT relapse, but it does not prevent future relapses; question explicitly asks about decreasing recurrence.  </p></br><p>c. Oral aspirin<br /></br>   – Antiplatelet agent for vascular events; MS relapses are inflammatory/demyelinating, not thrombotic.  </p></br><p>d. Plasmapheresis<br /></br>   – Rescue therapy for severe steroid-refractory relapses, not maintenance therapy for relapse prevention.  </p></br><p>Key differences: Only glatiramer acetate is validated to reduce relapse rate and is used chronically; the other options are either acute-phase treatments or unrelated to MS.</p>
20. A previously healthy 1-year-old boy is brought to the emergency department because of difficulty breathing for 12 hours. Over the past 2 days, he has had a cough and clear nasal discharge. His temperature is</br>36.5 C 97.7 F, pulse is 180/min, respirations are 60/min, and blood pressure is 70/46 mm Hg. He appears extremely anxious and apprehensive. Examination shows cool, pale, and mottled extremities. Pulses are thready.</br>Scattered wheezes and bilateral basilar crackles are heard on auscultation of the chest; there is a gallop with no murmur. The liver is tender and palpated 6 cm below the right costal margin. Capillary blood gas analysis</br>while breathing supplemental oxygen shows:</br>pH 7.02</br>Pco 2 28 mm Hg</br>Po 2 80 mm Hg</br>An x-ray of the chest shows mild cardiomegaly and hilar infiltrates. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Bronchiolitis</br>b. Dehydration</br>c. Hepatitis</br>d. Myocarditis</br>e. Pneumonia</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Myocarditis  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• URI prodrome (2-day history of cough + clear rhinorrhea) → common trigger for viral myocarditis (Coxsackie-B, adenovirus, parvovirus B19).<br /></br>• Signs of cardiogenic shock: hypotension (70/46 mm Hg), tachycardia (180/min), cool mottled extremities, thready pulses, agitation/anxiety.<br /></br>• Left-sided heart failure findings: bilateral basilar crackles &amp; scattered wheezes, S3 gallop.<br /></br>• Right-sided congestion: tender hepatomegaly (liver 6 cm below costal margin).<br /></br>• Chest-x-ray: mild cardiomegaly + perihilar interstitial infiltrates → pulmonary edema.<br /></br>• Blood-gas: severe metabolic (lactic) acidosis (pH 7.02) with respiratory alkalosis compensation (PCO₂ 28 mm Hg) from hyperventilation caused by hypoperfusion.  </p></br><p>Put together, these point to acute viral myocarditis causing acute biventricular failure and cardiogenic shock.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Recognize shock (vital signs + mottled skin).<br /></br>Step 2: Decide type of shock → presence of crackles, gallop, hepatomegaly, cardiomegaly ⇒ pump failure (cardiogenic), not volume depletion or distributive.<br /></br>Step 3: In an infant after a viral URI, myocarditis is the classic cause of acute pump failure.  </p></br><p>Core concept: Viral myocarditis presents days after URI with acute heart failure and cardiogenic shock; exam shows S3, hepatomegaly, pulmonary congestion, low cardiac output, metabolic acidosis.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Viral invasion of myocytes → lymphocytic infiltrate &amp; myocyte necrosis → decreased contractility → ↓stroke volume → ↓cardiac output → sympathetic activation (tachycardia, vasoconstriction) → cardiogenic shock → pulmonary venous congestion (crackles), systemic venous congestion (hepatomegaly).<br /></br>• Lactic acidosis from tissue hypoperfusion.<br /></br>• Chest radiograph shows cardiomegaly from dilated, poorly contracting ventricles.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Immediate management:<br /></br>1. Stabilize airway &amp; breathing (supplemental O₂, possible intubation).<br /></br>2. Improve circulation: IV fluids cautiously (5–10 mL/kg), then inotropes (milrinone, dobutamine) to augment contractility; consider vasopressors if hypotension persists.<br /></br>3. PICU admission; monitor for arrhythmias.<br /></br>Diagnostics (after stabilization):<br /></br>• Echocardiogram: global hypokinesis, ↓ejection fraction.<br /></br>• Cardiac enzymes often elevated (troponin, CK-MB).<br /></br>• Viral PCR/serologies; endomyocardial biopsy rarely needed.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Bronchiolitis<br /></br>• Also follows URI and causes wheezing, but vitals usually show tachypnea WITHOUT profound hypotension, gallop, hepatomegaly, or metabolic acidosis. Chest x-ray shows hyperinflated lungs, not cardiomegaly.  </p></br><p>b. Dehydration<br /></br>• Would cause tachycardia and possibly hypotension, but no crackles, S3, hepatomegaly, or cardiomegaly. ABG would show metabolic acidosis but chest exam and x-ray would be clear.  </p></br><p>c. Hepatitis<br /></br>• Hepatomegaly possible, but respiratory distress, crackles, gallop, cardiomegaly, and metabolic acidosis would not be typical. Transaminases would be markedly elevated; not provided.  </p></br><p>e. Pneumonia<br /></br>• Fever common; focal crackles or consolidation rather than diffuse crackles + wheezes. No gallop or cardiomegaly. Hypotension less likely in simple pneumonia without sepsis, and ABG would show respiratory acidosis/hypoxemia rather than severe metabolic acidosis.  </p></br><p>Take-Home Message<br /></br>In infants, a sudden onset of cardiogenic shock with pulmonary and systemic congestion a few days after a viral URI is classic for viral myocarditis—recognize the triad of 1) recent viral illness, 2) signs of biventricular failure (S3, crackles, hepatomegaly), and 3) cardiogenic shock.</p>
21. A 28-year-old woman comes to the hysician because of a 4-month histo~ of mild lower abdominal pain that is relieved with defecation. During this time, she also has had nonfatty, nongreasy diarrhea with mucus but no</br>blood. She does not have bowel movements during the night. She is otherwise asymptomatic. She has generalized anxiety disorder treated with citalopram. There is no family history of colon cancer. The patient's vital</br>signs are within normal limits. The abdomen is not distended or tender. There are no masses, and bowel sounds are normal. Results of a complete blood count are within the reference ranges. Results of serum</br>antiendomysial lgA and anti-tissue transglutaminase antibody testing are negative. Tests of the stool for occult blood, ova, parasites, and leukocytes are negative. Sigmoidoscopy shows no abnormalities. Which of the</br>following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Buspirone therapy</br>b. Exploratory laparotomy</br>c. Loperamide therapy</br>d. Mesalamine therapy</br>e. Trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Buspirone therapy  </p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 28-year-old woman with 4 months of:<br /></br>  – Mild, crampy lower-abdomen pain relieved by defecation<br /></br>  – Non-bloody, non-greasy, mucus-containing loose stools<br /></br>  – No nocturnal symptoms, weight loss, fever, or family hx cancer<br /></br>• Work-up completely normal: CBC, celiac serologies, stool O&amp;P/leukocytes/occult blood, and normal sigmoidoscopy.<br /></br>• Past history: generalized anxiety disorder treated with citalopram (an SSRI).  </p></br><p>Key phrases: “diarrhea,” “pain relieved by defecation,” “normal objective work-up” + chronic SSRI use → think of SSRI-induced diarrhea masquerading as IBS-like symptoms.  </p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Rule out organic disease – already done (normal labs + endoscopy; no red-flag features).<br /></br>Step 2: Review medications – SSRIs frequently cause dose-dependent, chronic, watery or mucus-mixed diarrhea due to ↑ serotonergic stimulation of gut motility.<br /></br>Step 3: Management principle – stop/switch the offending drug rather than treat the side effect with antidiarrheals.<br /></br>• Buspirone is a non-serotonergic anxiolytic that effectively treats GAD and carries minimal GI adverse effects. Transitioning from citalopram to buspirone should eliminate the precipitating cause of the diarrhea.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Always review current medications in chronic “functional” GI complaints; remove the culprit before adding symptomatic drugs.  </p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>SSRIs (e.g., citalopram, sertraline, fluoxetine) ↑ serotonin availability in the enteric nervous system, accelerating intestinal transit and increasing secretion → chronic, non-infectious diarrhea with possible mucus (but no inflammatory or malabsorptive findings).  </p></br><p>Buspirone is a partial 5-HT₁A agonist with negligible GI pro-motility activity; therefore, it controls anxiety without the diarrheal side effect.  </p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>1. Discontinue citalopram; initiate buspirone (takes 2–4 weeks for full effect).<br /></br>2. If diarrhea resolves, no further intervention is required.<br /></br>3. If symptoms persist, then consider dietary measures, antidiarrheals (loperamide, diphenoxylate), or formal IBS therapy.  </p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Exploratory laparotomy – Reserved for acute abdomen, obstruction, perforation, or mass; no abdominal tenderness, peritoneal signs, or imaging abnormalities here.  </p></br><p>c. Loperamide therapy – Symptomatic antidiarrheal useful in IBS-D but does not address the root cause (drug-induced diarrhea); first eliminate the offending agent.  </p></br><p>d. Mesalamine therapy – 5-ASA used for ulcerative colitis or Crohn colitis, which would show inflammatory findings (blood, positive stool leukocytes, sigmoidoscopic ulcers) – absent here.  </p></br><p>e. Trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole therapy – Treats bacterial infections such as traveler’s diarrhea or Shigella. Stool studies are negative, and the chronic course without systemic toxicity makes infection unlikely.  </p></br><p>Common pitfall: Jumping to treat presumed IBS without first reviewing medication list for reversible causes of chronic diarrhea. Always think “drug side effect” before labeling a functional disorder.</p>
22. A 6-year-old boy is brought to the emergency department by his parents because of a 2-day history of fever, sore throat, a rapidly spreading rash, and decreased oral intake. During the past 12 hours, he has refused to eat</br>or drink and has not voided. Medical history is remarkable for a 3-day hospitalization at age 2 months for enterovirus meningitis. The patient currently receives no medications and vaccinations are up-to-date. He appears</br>uncomfortable. He is at the 60th percentile for height and 50th percentile for weight; BMI is at the 40th percentile. Temperature is 39.0 C 102.2 F, pulse is 100/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 110/70</br>mm Hg. Pulse oximet [Y on room air shows an oxyJJen saturation of 98 percent. Physical examination discloses oral lesions and dry mucous membranes. There is a blanching rash over the upper and lower extremities and</br>buttocks. The lesions appear as shown in the photographs. Cardiopulmonary examination discloses no abnormalities. Abdominal examination discloses no tenderness or hepatosplenomegaly. Which of the following is the</br>most appropriate initial diagnostic study?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Blood culture</br>b. Platelet count</br>c. Respiratory panel polymerase chain reaction test</br>d. Tzanck test of a skin vesicle</br>e. No further testing is indicated</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. No further testing is indicated  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age &amp; seasonality: 6-year-old school-aged child → peak group for enteroviral exanthems<br /></br>• Prodrome: 2 days of fever, sore throat, ↓ oral intake → typical viral prodrome<br /></br>• Rash description/location: “Blanching rash over the upper and lower extremities and buttocks” + “oral lesions” (pictures show small vesicles with erythematous halos) → classic for hand-foot-mouth disease (HFMD) due to Coxsackie A16 or other non-polio enteroviruses.<br /></br>• Hydration status: dry mucous membranes &amp; no urine for 12 h → tells you severity but not etiology.<br /></br>• Vitals: only fever; otherwise stable (normal BP, HR, O2 sat) → sepsis unlikely.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Pattern recognition: Fever + painful oral vesicles + vesicular/blanching rash on hands/feet/buttocks = HFMD.<br /></br>HFMD is diagnosed clinically; labs or imaging do not alter management. Key pitfall: mistaking vesicular rash for bacterial sepsis or thrombocytopenic purpura—note lesions are blanching, not petechial/purpuric.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Coxsackievirus A16 (most common) or other enteroviruses (A6, A71).<br /></br>• Transmission: fecal-oral, respiratory droplets—late summer/early fall peaks.<br /></br>• Mechanism: Viral replication in oropharynx/GI tract → viremia → vesicular lesions in oral mucosa and acral skin.<br /></br>• Clinical course: 3–7 days fever/sore throat, painful oral ulcers → rash appears 1–2 days later; illness is self-limited (7–10 days). Rare complications: aseptic meningitis, myocarditis, nail shedding.  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• Treatment: Supportive only—hydration (oral or IV), antipyretics (acetaminophen, ibuprofen), topical oral pain relief (lidocaine, “magic mouthwash”).<br /></br>• Diagnostics: None routinely needed. Viral PCR can confirm but is unnecessary, costly, and does not change care.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Blood culture<br /></br>   – Indicated for toxic-appearing child or signs of sepsis/meningococcemia (fever, petechiae/purpura, hypotension). This child is hemodynamically stable and has a blanching, not purpuric, rash.  </p></br><p>b. Platelet count<br /></br>   – Useful if you suspect thrombocytopenic purpura (petechiae, ecchymoses, mucosal bleeding). HFMD lesions are vesicular and blanching, not hemorrhagic, so platelet testing is low yield.  </p></br><p>c. Respiratory panel PCR<br /></br>   – Employed for severe/at-risk cases of influenza, RSV, adenovirus, etc. The presentation is not a primary lower-respiratory illness; result would not change management.  </p></br><p>d. Tzanck test of a skin vesicle<br /></br>   – Detects multinucleated giant cells in HSV or VZV. Varicella lesions appear in crops at different stages, are diffusely distributed (trunk &gt; extremities), and often intensely pruritic; HSV gingivostomatitis is confined to oral mucosa without acral rash. Clinical picture fits HFMD, making Tzanck smear unnecessary.  </p></br><p>Take-home Message<br /></br>Hand-foot-mouth disease is a clinical diagnosis characterized by fever, painful oral ulcers, and vesicular rash on hands, feet, and buttocks. It is self-limited; no lab tests are required—management is entirely supportive.</p>
23. A 16-year-old girl is brought to the office by her mother because of a 1-year history of increasing facial hair growth and acne. Menses have occurred at irregular 60- to 90-day intervals since menarche at the age of12 years. She takes no medications. She is 168 cm 5 ft 6 in tall and weighs 70 kg 155 lb; BMI is 25 kg/m 2. Examination shows acne vulgaris over the forehead and chin. Her Ferriman-Gallwey score for hair growth is</br>10 N<8. Her serum testosterone concentration is 45 ng/dl N<5-40. Results of other serum studies, including thyroid-stimulating hormone, prolactin, and dehydroepiandrosterone sulfate concentrations, are within the</br>reference ranges. Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient's condition?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Adrenal tumor</br>b. Congenital adrenal hyperplasia</br>c. Exogenous exposure to androgens</br>d. Ovarian tumor</br>e. Polycystic ovarian syndrome</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>e. Polycystic ovarian syndrome (PCOS)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis – Key Clues</br>• 16-year-old girl with gradual onset (1-year) of hirsutism and inflammatory acne<br /></br>• Chronic oligomenorrhea: cycles every 60–90 days since menarche<br /></br>• Mildly elevated total testosterone (45 ng/dL; upper limit of normal ≈40 ng/dL)<br /></br>• Normal DHEA-S, TSH, and prolactin → rules out adrenal hyperandrogenism, thyroid disease, and prolactinoma<br /></br>• BMI 25 (overweight) – common in PCOS<br /></br>Collectively these findings point to ovarian androgen excess with chronic anovulation → classic adolescent presentation of PCOS.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Stepwise thought process:</br>1. Hyperandrogenism? YES (hirsutism, acne, ↑testosterone).<br /></br>2. Source adrenal vs ovarian? DHEA-S normal → adrenal causes unlikely; testosterone mildly ↑ → ovarian source.<br /></br>3. Menstrual dysfunction? YES (oligo-ovulation).<br /></br>4. Exclude other mimickers (TSH, prolactin, CAH screen, drug exposure) → all negative.<br /></br>This satisfies NIH diagnostic criteria for PCOS in adolescents (both hyperandrogenism and menstrual irregularity ≥2 yrs post-menarche).</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)</br>• Core defect: insulin resistance → compensatory hyperinsulinemia → ovarian theca-cell stimulation → ↑LH-mediated androgen production (testosterone, androstenedione).<br /></br>• Androgens inhibit granulosa-cell aromatase → impaired follicular maturation → chronic anovulation → irregular menses.<br /></br>• Peripheral conversion of androgens → hirsutism/acne.<br /></br>Risk factors: obesity, family history, metabolic syndrome.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics (Board-relevant)</br>First-line:</br>1. Combined estrogen-progestin oral contraceptive pill (ethinyl estradiol + levonorgestrel, etc.) – suppress LH, ↑SHBG → ↓free testosterone; provides endometrial protection.<br /></br>2. Weight loss &amp; exercise → ↓insulin resistance, restores ovulation.<br /></br>Add-ons:</br>• Spironolactone (antiandrogen) for refractory hirsutism (only with reliable contraception).<br /></br>• Metformin for metabolic derangements or glucose intolerance.<br /></br>Diagnostic adjuncts: pelvic ultrasound (string-of-pearls ovaries) not required if clinical criteria met.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Adrenal tumor<br /></br>– Would produce markedly ↑ DHEA-S (often &gt;700 μg/dL) ± virilization (deep voice, clitoromegaly) over weeks to months. Normal DHEA-S and indolent course make this unlikely.</p></br><p>b. Congenital adrenal hyperplasia (late-onset 21-hydroxylase deficiency)<br /></br>– Presents in adolescence with hirsutism, acne, oligomenorrhea, but typically shows ↑17-hydroxyprogesterone and ↑DHEA-S; may have family history, premature pubarche. Labs here are normal.</p></br><p>c. Exogenous exposure to androgens<br /></br>– Requires history of topical or systemic androgen use (e.g., anabolic steroids) or virilizing medications. No such history; androgen level only mildly elevated.</p></br><p>d. Ovarian tumor (Sertoli-Leydig, theca-cell)<br /></br>– Produces rapidly progressive virilization (clitoromegaly, voice deepening), markedly ↑ testosterone (often &gt;200 ng/dL). Teen’s testosterone just mildly high and course is gradual.</p></br><p>Take-Home Message</br>In adolescent girls, the combination of chronic oligomenorrhea ≥2 yrs post-menarche and evidence of hyperandrogenism with normal adrenal labs is PCOS until proven otherwise.</p>
24. A randomized clinical trial is planned to determine whether a new form of chemotherapy increases the survival time for patients with pancreatic cancer. The investigators intend to randomize 300 patients aged 40 to 70</br>years into two groups. One group will receive the new chemotherapy and the other group will receive standard therapy. Patients will be observed for up to 5 years. Interim analyses will be conducted on an annual basis, and</br>the trial will be terminated if sufficient evidence of efficacy is noted at any of the interim analyses. This interim analysis plan will most likely lead to which of the following?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Decreased chance of a type I error overall</br>b. Decreased chance of a type 11 error overall</br>c. Increased chance of a type I error overall</br>d. Increased chance of a type 11 error overall</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Increased chance of a type I error overall  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Interim analyses will be conducted on an annual basis” and “the trial will be terminated if sufficient evidence of efficacy is noted at any of the interim analyses” are the key phrases.<br /></br>• Each additional “look” at the data is an additional statistical test of the same primary endpoint. Re-testing the same hypothesis multiple times without adequate statistical correction inflates the overall α (family-wise error rate).<br /></br>• The question is asking about the aggregate (overall) error rate for the study, not the error rate at any single analysis.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>• Type I error (α) = concluding a benefit exists when it truly does NOT (false-positive).<br /></br>• Repeated testing of accumulating data increases the probability of finding a “statistically significant” difference just by chance, unless α is adjusted at each interim look (e.g., O’Brien-Fleming, Pocock boundaries).<br /></br>Core takeaway: Interim (sequential) analyses without appropriate α-spending adjustments raise the overall risk of a false-positive conclusion → higher type I error.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>(Brief, because this is a study-design—not disease—question.) No pathophysiology component here.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Not applicable; the stem focuses solely on trial design/statistics.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Decreased chance of a type I error overall<br /></br>• Opposite of what actually happens. Multiple unadjusted looks INCREASE, not decrease, the cumulative α. Only if the investigators pre-specify an α-spending plan or use stricter p-value thresholds at each interim look would the overall type I error be controlled.  </p></br><p>b. Decreased chance of a type II error overall<br /></br>• Type II error (β) = failing to detect a true benefit (false-negative). Interim looks can sometimes lower β (because you might detect a true effect earlier), but they do so at the expense of raising α. The question asks what effect is “most likely;” the dominant, well-known consequence is inflation of type I error.  </p></br><p>d. Increased chance of a type II error overall<br /></br>• Incorrect. Stopping early for benefit cannot increase the probability of missing a real effect; if anything, β tends to decrease or stay the same.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Students often assume “more data looks = more power → lower β,” overlooking the simultaneous inflation of α. On exams, when you see “multiple interim analyses” without mention of alpha-spending correction, think “greater risk of type I error.”</p>
25. Six weeks after undergoing triple coronary artery bypass grafting after suffering an acute myocardial infarction, a 57-year-old man comes to the office with his wife for a follow-up examination. Since the surgery, the patient</br>has had left leg pain and numbness and a 9-kg 20-lb weight loss. He reports that he has not been sleeping well and has had an intermittent cough. His energy has been decreased and he has lost interest in his usual</br>activities. He has not had chest pain. A low-impact exercise program was prescribed at the time of discharge. The patient's wife says that he has not maintained the suggested exercise program, stating that he was too</br>tired. Medications include atenolol, atorvastatin, and 81-mg aspirin daily. He is 173 cm 5 ft 8 in tall and weighs 91 kg 200 lb; 8MI is 30 kg/m 2. Vital signs are temperature 37.0 C 98.6 F, pulse 62/min, respirations</br>14/min, and blood pressure 140/80 mm Hg. Physical examination discloses well-healed surgical incisions. Total serum cholesterol concentration is 180 mg/dl, and fasting blood glucose concentration is 128 mg/dl. Which</br>of the following is the most appropriate change in the patient's therapy?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Change atorvastatin to pravastatin</br>b. Prescribe diltiazem</br>c. Prescribe hydralazine</br>d. Prescribe sertraline</br>E  Prescribe warfarin</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>D. Prescribe sertraline  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “6 weeks after… MI &amp; CABG” → recent major cardiac event (high-risk period for post-MI depression).<br /></br>• “Poor sleep, 9-kg weight loss, ↓ energy, anhedonia (‘lost interest’), ↓ motivation for rehab” → ≥2 weeks of SIGECAPS criteria → major depressive episode.<br /></br>• “No chest pain, vitals stable, on β-blocker + statin + aspirin” → cardiac status currently stable; problem is mood, not ischemia or hemodynamics.<br /></br>• “Has not maintained exercise program” → functional decline secondary to depression.  </p></br><p>Pattern: Post-MI patients frequently develop major depression; untreated depression worsens adherence and increases morbidity &amp; mortality.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1 – Identify primary new problem: classic depressive symptoms beginning after MI.<br /></br>Step 2 – Choose treatment that is both effective for depression and safe in patients with coronary disease.<br /></br>Step 3 – SSRIs (sertraline, escitalopram) have the best evidence and cardiac safety profile (minimal QT-prolongation, no BP/HR effects, safe with aspirin).<br /></br>Therefore prescribe sertraline.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Recognize and treat post-MI major depression with an SSRI that has proven cardiovascular safety; this improves quality of life, rehab participation, and possibly survival.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Post-MI depression prevalence ≥20 %. Mechanisms include inflammatory cytokines, HPA-axis activation, psychosocial stressors, and reduced autonomic variability.<br /></br>• Depression after MI is an independent predictor of recurrent ischemic events and death. Timely SSRI therapy mitigates these risks.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>First-line pharmacotherapy: SSRI (sertraline 25–50 mg daily start; titrate).<br /></br>• Avoid tricyclics/SNRIs early after MI (anticholinergic and α-block effects → tachycardia, arrhythmia, orthostasis).<br /></br>• Psychotherapy (CBT, cardiac rehab) adjunctive.<br /></br>• Screen all post-ACS patients for depression (PHQ-9).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>A. Change atorvastatin to pravastatin<br /></br>• Atorvastatin is appropriate (LDL-lowering, secondary prevention). No statin myopathy symptoms (muscle pain, ↑CK). Pravastatin offers no advantage here; changing would weaken LDL control.  </p></br><p>B. Prescribe diltiazem<br /></br>• Non-DHP CCB provides rate control/angina relief but is contraindicated with β-blocker (atenolol) due to risk of heart block and bradycardia. No angina or arrhythmia present. Does not address depressive symptoms.  </p></br><p>C. Prescribe hydralazine<br /></br>• Used for refractory hypertension or with nitrates in HFrEF. BP 140/80 is acceptable; patient asymptomatic. Hydralazine offers no mortality benefit post-MI and doesn’t treat depression.  </p></br><p>E. Prescribe warfarin<br /></br>• Indicated for AF, LV thrombus, mechanical valves, VTE. Patient has none of these. Routine anticoagulation post-CABG is not recommended; plus warfarin would increase bleeding risk with aspirin.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfalls<br /></br>• Attributing weight loss and fatigue solely to recent surgery or β-blocker side-effects—always screen for depression.<br /></br>• Reaching for TCAs (“good antidepressants”)—they raise mortality post-MI.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>High-Yield Takeaway<br /></br>After an MI, new-onset SIGECAPS symptoms = major depression. Treat with an SSRI proven safe in cardiac patients—sertraline is the NBME/USMLE answer.</p>
26. A 42-year-old primigravid woman at 26 weeks' gestation comes to the physician for a routine prenatal visit. She feels well, and her pregnancy has been uncomplicated. Prior to conception, she used an oral contraceptive</br>and drank two cups of coffee daily. She plans to resume using an oral contraceptive and drinking coffee after delivery. She states that after delivery, she will be taking a 6-month maternity leave from work and plans to</br>breast-feed exclusively during this time. Examination shows a uterus consistent in size with a 26-week gestation. The most appropriate recommendation for the infant during breast-feeding is supplementation with which of</br>the following?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Calcium</br>b. Fluoride</br>c. Folic acid</br>d. Vitamin C</br>e. Vitamin D</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Vitamin D  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Plans to breast-feed exclusively during this time” – the phrase that triggers consideration of routine neonatal supplementation.<br /></br>• Healthy pregnancy, no maternal disease → question is about standard recommendations, not special situations (eg, premature birth, malabsorption).  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Core concept: Human breast milk is nutritionally complete except for two main deficiencies:<br /></br>1. Vitamin D (low concentration in milk and indoor/lower-latitude lifestyles)<br /></br>2. Iron (stores last ≈4 mo; supplementation starts then, not at birth)  </p></br><p>Therefore, all term infants who are exclusively (or mostly) breast-fed should receive oral vitamin D (400 IU/day) beginning in the first few days of life, regardless of maternal diet.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Vitamin D is needed for intestinal calcium &amp; phosphate absorption and bone mineralization.<br /></br>• Breast milk contains only ~25 IU/L; a typical infant would have to drink &gt;4 L daily to meet the 400 IU requirement.<br /></br>• Deficiency → rickets (craniotabes, rachitic rosary, genu varum) and hypocalcemic seizures.<br /></br>• Maternal prenatal vitamins or sunlight exposure rarely provide sufficient neonatal vitamin D.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Recommended supplementation for a healthy term, exclusively breast-fed infant:<br /></br>1. Vitamin D 400 IU orally daily from birth.<br /></br>2. Iron 1 mg/kg/day orally starting at 4 months until solid iron-rich foods are introduced.<br /></br>(No routine calcium, fluoride &lt;6 mo, folic acid, or vitamin C are required.)  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Calcium – Breast milk already contains a highly bioavailable calcium-to-phosphorus ratio; supplementation is unnecessary in term infants.<br /></br>b. Fluoride – Not given until 6 months and only if local water supply &lt;0.3 ppm fluoride. Starting at birth would be premature.<br /></br>c. Folic acid – Maternal folate deficiency affects neural-tube development in utero, not post-natal bone growth; breast milk provides adequate folate.<br /></br>d. Vitamin C – Breast milk supplies sufficient vitamin C; deficiency (scurvy) is extremely rare in exclusively breast-fed infants.  </p></br><p>Take-home Message<br /></br>Exclusively breast-fed infants need oral vitamin D supplementation from birth because breast milk alone cannot meet daily vitamin D requirements, placing infants at risk for rickets and hypocalcemia.</p>
27. 57-year-old man comes to the physician for a routine examination. He feels well. He has mild hypertension and type 2 diabetes mellitus. He underwent an uncomplicated total right knee arthroplasty 2 years ago. Repair</br>of a chipped molar tooth is scheduled in 1 week, and the patient requests antibiotics to prevent infection around his prosthetic joint. General health screening measures are up-to-date. The patient is allergic to penicillin.</br>Current medications are hydrochlorothiazide, enalapril, and insulin. His temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 85/min, respirations are 12/min, and blood pressure is 145/84 mm Hg. Examination shows a well-healed scar</br>over the right knee. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Reassurance</br>b. Oral azithromycin therapy</br>c. Oral clindamycin therapy</br>d. Oral vancomycin therapy</br>e. Intravenous clindamycin therapy</br>F. Intravenous vancomycin therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>A. Reassurance (no antibiotic prophylaxis indicated)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Prosthetic material present: total knee arthroplasty 2 years ago<br /></br>• Planned procedure: routine dental restoration (“repair of a chipped molar”) → transient bacteremia possible<br /></br>• Time since surgery: &gt; 3 months (well past the initial peri-operative period where hematogenous seeding risk is greatest)<br /></br>• No other high-risk features: no prior prosthetic-joint infection, no immunosuppression, no ongoing bacteremia, and no severe uncontrolled diabetes (his DM is treated with insulin, HbA1c not given but patient “feels well”).<br /></br>Key phrase: “patient requests antibiotics to prevent infection around his prosthetic joint” → tests knowledge of current ADA/AAOS guidelines.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Stepwise approach for dental-procedure prophylaxis:</br>1. Ask: Is IE (infective endocarditis) prophylaxis indicated?  ➜ Only for specific cardiac conditions (prosthetic cardiac valve, prior IE, certain congenital heart disease, cardiac transplant with valvulopathy). None apply here.</br>2. Ask: Is prosthetic-joint prophylaxis indicated? ➜ Current consensus (American Dental Association 2021, AAOS) = NO routine antibiotics for dental procedures, regardless of joint age, in otherwise healthy patients.<br /></br>Therefore, reassure; no prescription needed.</p></br><p>Take-home message: Do NOT give antimicrobial prophylaxis for dental work solely to prevent prosthetic-joint infection.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Prosthetic-joint infection typically results from peri-operative contamination or early postoperative bacteremia. Late hematogenous seeding from oral flora is exceedingly uncommon.<br /></br>• Overuse of antibiotics ➜ resistance, C. difficile colitis, adverse drug reactions. Evidence shows no reduction in joint infections with routine dental prophylaxis, hence recommendation against it.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>• First-line prophylaxis for IE (if indicated) would be amoxicillin 2 g PO 30–60 min pre-procedure; alternatives for penicillin allergy are azithromycin or clindamycin.<br /></br>• For prosthetic joints, no prophylaxis unless: 1) active infection elsewhere, 2) immunocompromised with poor glycemic control, 3) surgery &lt; 3 months*, or 4) surgeon/infectious disease specialist specifically recommends after prior joint infection. None of these apply.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Oral azithromycin<br /></br>C. Oral clindamycin<br /></br>D. Oral vancomycin<br /></br>E. Intravenous clindamycin<br /></br>F. Intravenous vancomycin<br /></br>Why wrong: These agents are used as IE prophylaxis alternatives in penicillin-allergic patients or for treating active infections. However, no prophylaxis is indicated here. Giving any antibiotic would be unnecessary exposure and deviates from guidelines. IV preparations (E &amp; F) are especially inappropriate for outpatient dental work; they offer no additional benefit and increase cost, risk of phlebitis, and promote resistance.  </p></br><p>Common pitfall: Confusing IE prophylaxis criteria with prosthetic-joint prophylaxis or assuming all prosthetic material warrants dental antibiotics. Boards expect you to know that prosthetic-joint prophylaxis is not recommended.</p></br><p>*Some orthopedists still prefer prophylaxis &lt; 3 months, but USMLE follows ADA/AAOS guideline: routine prophylaxis not indicated once patient is medically stable.</p>
28. A 52-year-old woman with long-standing alcohol use disorder is brought to the emergency department because of a 2-day history of severe abdominal cramps, fever, nausea, vomiting, and tremulousness. She has drunk</br>1 L of vodka daily during the past week; her last glass of vodka was 2 days ago. On arrival, the patient is mildly confused and is unable to provide further history. She is oriented to person and place but not to time. Her</br>temperature is 37.7 C 99.9 F, pulse is 110/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 152/92 mm Hg. Examination shows marked scleral icterus. Abdominal examination shows tenderness to palpation over the</br>right upper quadrant. The liver span is 14 cm. There is no fluid wave or asterixis. Laboratory studies show:</br>Which of the following is the most appropriate pharmacotherapy?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Diazepam</br>b. Disulfiram</br>c. Fresh frozen plasma</br>d. Lactulose</br>e. Propranolol</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>A. Diazepam  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Last drink 2 days ago,” “tremulousness,” “tachycardia (110/min),” “mild confusion / disoriented to time” = classic constellation of early-to-moderate alcohol-withdrawal symptoms (progressing toward delirium tremens, which usually peaks 48-96 h after cessation).<br /></br>• Fever and hypertension are part of autonomic hyperactivity seen in withdrawal.<br /></br>• RUQ tenderness, scleral icterus, and ↑ liver span point to alcoholic hepatitis, but those findings do not explain the acute tremor, autonomic instability, and temporal relation to cessation of alcohol.<br /></br>Pattern: Recent cessation of heavy alcohol + sympathetic overdrive + tremor → think benzodiazepine treatment.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Recognize time course:<br /></br>   • 6-24 h: tremor, anxiety, diaphoresis<br /></br>   • 12-48 h: seizures, hallucinosis<br /></br>   • 48-96 h: delirium tremens (agitation, fever, HTN, tachycardia, confusion)<br /></br>   The patient is at 48 h—high-risk window for DTs.<br /></br>2. Benzodiazepines (long-acting preferred unless severe liver failure) are first-line because they cross-taper with ethanol’s GABA-A action, preventing seizures and DTs.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Chronic ethanol potentiates GABA (inhibition) and blocks NMDA (excitation). Abrupt cessation unmasks excessive NMDA activity and insufficient GABA tone → CNS hyperexcitability (tremor, seizures, autonomic surge). Benzodiazepines enhance GABA-A receptor activity, re-establishing inhibitory tone.<br /></br>• Alcoholic hepatitis (AST&gt;ALT 2:1, ↑bilirubin) is present but does not acutely threaten life as much as impending DTs; management of withdrawal takes priority.  </p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Immediate:<br /></br>• Benzodiazepine (diazepam, chlordiazepoxide; lorazepam/oxazepam if severe cirrhosis).<br /></br>• IV fluids, thiamine before glucose, electrolytes (Mg2+, K+), nutrition.<br /></br>Long-term:<br /></br>• After stabilization, consider relapse-prevention agents (naltrexone, acamprosate).  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Disulfiram – Aldehyde dehydrogenase inhibitor used only for motivated, sober patients to maintain abstinence; contraindicated during acute use or withdrawal and offers no help against DTs.  </p></br><p>C. Fresh frozen plasma – Replaces clotting factors in coagulopathy/active bleeding. Although alcoholic hepatitis can prolong INR, the stem does not mention bleeding, high INR, or planned invasive procedure. Does not treat withdrawal.  </p></br><p>D. Lactulose – Traps ammonia in the gut for hepatic encephalopathy (AMS, asterixis). The patient lacks asterixis, and the mental status changes match withdrawal timing, not ammonia toxicity. Giving lactulose would not correct the life-threatening problem.  </p></br><p>E. Propranolol – Sometimes used as adjunct for autonomic symptoms but NEVER monotherapy; it can mask vital signs of worsening withdrawal and does not prevent seizures/DTs.  </p></br><p>Take-Home Message<br /></br>In a patient 48–96 h after stopping heavy alcohol who exhibits tremor, confusion, fever, tachycardia, and hypertension, prioritize benzodiazepines (e.g., diazepam) to prevent progression to seizures and delirium tremens.</p>
29. 70-year-old woman is brought to the emergency department from the nursing care facility where she resides, because of a 2-day histo~ of low-grade fever, decreased appetite, and lethargy. She has type 2 diabetes</br>mellitus and cognitive impairment. Her only medication is metformin. The patient is drowsy and responds to questions with inconsistent answers. She is 152 cm 5 ft tall and weighs 64 kg 140 lb; BMI is 27 kg/m ~. He ri</br>temperature is 38.0 C 100.4  F , pulse is 90/min, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 100/60 mm Hg. Cardiopulmonary examination shows no abnormalities. Examination of the left lower extremity shows a</br>rapidly expanding area of red, shiny skin on the shin with a small fluid-filled bulla. The area is exquisitely tender and warm to the touch. In addition to intravenous antibiotic therapy, which of the following is the most</br>appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Admit the patient to the intensive care unit for vasopressor therapy</br>b. Begin surgical exploration in the operating room</br>c. Discharge the patient back to the nursing care facility with a peripherally inserted central catheter for intravenous antibiotic therapy</br>d. Order culture and sensitivity of a tissue sample and await results before further therapy</br>e. Transfer the patient to a negative-pressure room</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection</br>b. Begin surgical exploration in the operating room</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• “Rapidly expanding area of red, shiny skin … with a small fluid-filled bulla” + “exquisitely tender” = classic early cutaneous clues for necrotizing fasciitis.</br>• Pain/tenderness out of proportion to the visual findings and rapid progression over 48 h strongly favor necrotizing soft-tissue infection over simple cellulitis or abscess.</br>• Risk factors: elderly, diabetes, nursing-home resident → impaired immunity and microvascular disease.</br>• Low-grade fever, lethargy, mild hypotension suggest evolving systemic toxicity.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Necrotizing fasciitis is a surgical emergency. Mortality rises dramatically with every hour of delay. The correct initial management sequence is:</br>1. Broad-spectrum IV antibiotics (already initiated in the stem) PLUS</br>2. Immediate operative exploration / debridement to remove necrotic fascia and stop toxin production.</br>Supportive measures (fluids, vasopressors) are adjuncts but cannot control the source of infection.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Suspect necrotizing fasciitis when an acutely ill patient has rapidly progressive, exquisitely painful skin findings (bullae, shiny skin). Definitive therapy is emergent surgical exploration and debridement—do not wait for imaging, cultures, or hemodynamic deterioration.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Etiology: polymicrobial (anaerobes + facultative aerobes) or monomicrobial (group A Strep, S. aureus, Vibrio vulnificus, etc.).</br>• Toxins and bacterial enzymes spread along fascial planes, causing vascular thrombosis → tissue ischemia, necrosis, severe pain, and systemic inflammatory response.</br>• Hallmarks: severe pain, edema beyond erythema, crepitus (if gas-forming), bullae, skin anesthesia late in disease, systemic toxicity.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review</br>1. Immediate broad-spectrum IV antibiotics:</br>   – Piperacillin-tazobactam or meropenem + vancomycin + clindamycin (inhibits streptococcal toxin production).</br>2. Emergent operative exploration/debridement; repeat debridement every 24–48 h until all necrotic tissue is removed; amputation if needed.</br>3. Adjunctive therapy: aggressive IV fluids, vasopressors if septic shock develops, tight glucose control, IVIG for streptococcal toxic shock (select cases).</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Admit to ICU for vasopressor therapy<br /></br>   • Vasopressors treat hypotension after aggressive fluid resuscitation in septic shock. The patient is not yet in shock (BP 100/60) and, crucially, vasopressors alone do not eliminate the source. Surgical debridement cannot be delayed.</p></br><p>c. Discharge with PICC for IV antibiotics<br /></br>   • Outpatient therapy is inappropriate for a rapidly progressive, life-threatening infection requiring emergent surgery and close monitoring.</p></br><p>d. Order culture &amp; sensitivity of tissue and await results<br /></br>   • Waiting for culture data would delay life-saving surgery. Empiric broad-spectrum antibiotics with immediate exploration is the standard of care; cultures can be obtained intra-operatively.</p></br><p>e. Transfer to a negative-pressure room<br /></br>   • Negative-pressure isolation is used for airborne pathogens (e.g., tuberculosis, varicella). Necrotizing fasciitis is not an airborne hazard.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall</br>Students may equate “antibiotics first” with “antibiotics only.” Always pair antibiotics with surgical source control in necrotizing fasciitis.</p>
30. One week after undergoing splenectomy for injuries sustained in a motorcycle collision, a 19-year-old man has a platelet count of 1,250,000/mm 3. Examination shows normal healing wounds. Which of the following is the</br>most appropriate measure to prevent thrombotic events in this patient?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Aspirin therapy</br>b. Heparin therapy</br>c. Periodic platelet pheresis</br>d. Placement of a vena cava filter</br>e. Warfarin therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>A. Aspirin therapy  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “One week after splenectomy” → reactive (postsplenectomy) thrombocytosis is expected.<br /></br>• Platelet count 1,250,000/mm³ (normal 150-400K) → marked thrombocytosis; highest risk of platelet-mediated arterial/venous thrombosis is in the first 2–3 weeks after splenectomy.<br /></br>• “Normal healing wounds” → no active bleeding contraindication to antiplatelet therapy.<br /></br>Key pattern: Young trauma patient + recent splenectomy + extreme thrombocytosis = prophylaxis against platelet-driven thrombosis is required.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-by-step thought process:<br /></br>1. Identify cause: loss of splenic sequestration accelerates thrombopoiesis → transient megathrombocytosis.<br /></br>2. Determine what type of clot to prevent: primarily platelet-rich (arterial) micro- or macro- thrombi.<br /></br>3. Choose agent that specifically targets platelet aggregation without causing major systemic anticoagulation risk in early post-op trauma: low-dose aspirin irreversibly inhibits platelet cyclo-oxygenase-1 → ↓TXA₂.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: High platelet counts after splenectomy are managed prophylactically with low-dose aspirin until the count falls (&lt; 600–700K) to prevent platelet-mediated thrombosis.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Spleen normally removes senescent platelets. After splenectomy, circulating platelet mass abruptly increases; thrombopoietin additionally rises due to inflammatory cytokines from trauma/surgery.<br /></br>• Timeline: Peaks 1–3 weeks post-op, gradually declines over months.<br /></br>• Risk: Venous thrombosis (portal, mesenteric, DVT), arterial events (stroke, MI).<br /></br>• Mechanism: Excess platelets adhere/aggregate, ↑blood viscosity, release pro-coagulant microparticles.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Management algorithm for postsplenectomy thrombocytosis:<br /></br>1. If platelet count &gt;1,000,000/mm³ or symptomatic → low-dose aspirin 81–325 mg daily.<br /></br>2. Cytoreductive therapy (hydroxyurea, anagrelide) rarely needed; platelet-pheresis reserved for acute, life-threatening events.<br /></br>3. Anticoagulation (heparin/warfarin) only if separate venous thromboembolism indication exists.<br /></br>4. Monitor CBC weekly until platelet count &lt;600–700K.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>B. Heparin therapy<br /></br>• Heparin blocks thrombin/factor Xa (anticoagulation), useful for fibrin-rich venous clots or existing DVT/PE.<br /></br>• Over-treats this patient; adds bleeding risk while doing little to curb platelet aggregation driving thrombocytosis-related events. No current DVT/PE.  </p></br><p>C. Periodic platelet-pheresis<br /></br>• Indicated for emergent cytoreduction when counts &gt;1.5–2 million with symptomatic microvascular ischemia or acute stroke/Budd-Chiari.<br /></br>• Invasive, temporary measure; unnecessary when simple oral antiplatelet therapy suffices.  </p></br><p>D. Placement of a vena cava filter<br /></br>• Prevents pulmonary emboli from lower-extremity DVT; does not address arterial events or platelet aggregation; invasive with device-related complications.  </p></br><p>E. Warfarin therapy<br /></br>• Long-term vitamin K antagonist targets coagulation factors II, VII, IX, X; requires days to become therapeutic and frequent INR checks.<br /></br>• Same issues as heparin: treats fibrin-dependent venous thrombosis, not primary platelet hyperactivity. Not indicated without another condition (e.g., atrial fibrillation, DVT, mechanical valve).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Reactive thrombocytosis after splenectomy poses a short-term risk of platelet-mediated thrombosis. First-line prophylaxis is low-dose aspirin to inhibit platelet aggregation until the platelet count normalizes.</p>
31. A 34-year-old woman, gravida 1, para 1, comes to the physician because of a 3-month history of heavy vaginal bleeding with large clots. The bleeding occurs every 10 days and lasts 7 to 10 days. She is not currently</br>bleeding. Before the bleeding began, she had not had a menstrual period for 4 months. She has a history of six episodes of amenorrhea, each lasting 4 months during the past 2 years; previous treatment with oral progestin</br>induced withdrawal bleeding. Her last Pap smear 1 year ago showed no abnormalities. She currently takes no medications. She is 163 cm 5 ft 4 in tall and weighs 86 kg 190 lb; BMI is 33 kg/m 2. Examination shows</br>darkened skin with a velvety texture over the base of the neck, axillae, and perineum. Coarse hair is noted over the upper lip and chin. There is no thyromegaly. No discharge is expressed on palpation of the nipples.</br>Central obesity is noted. Coarse hair extends from the umbilicus to the pubic area; the pubic hair has a diamond-shaped pattern. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Cervical testing for Chlamydia trachomatis and Neisseria gonorrhoeae</br>b. Colposcopy with biopsy</br>c. Endometrial biopsy</br>d. Keeping a menstrual calendar</br>e. Measurement of serum progesterone concentration during luteal phase</br>F. Pap smear</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Endometrial biopsy  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Pattern of bleeding: 4-month stretches of amenorrhea followed by prolonged, heavy, “clotty” bleeding every 10 days → strongly suggests chronic anovulation with episodic breakthrough bleeding.<br /></br>• Risk factors for unopposed estrogen / endometrial hyperplasia: age ≥35? (she is close), obesity (BMI 33), chronic anovulation (oligomenorrhea), polycystic-ovary syndrome stigmata (hirsutism, acanthosis nigricans, central obesity).<br /></br>• No contraceptive or hormonal medication use to explain bleeding.<br /></br>These features meet ACOG criteria for immediate endometrial sampling in a woman &lt;45 y with abnormal uterine bleeding plus obesity and signs of PCOS.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Core takeaway: In abnormal uterine bleeding, the first question is “Could this be endometrial cancer or precancer?” Any woman ≥45 y or &lt;45 y with chronic anovulation, obesity, or failed medical therapy must undergo endometrial evaluation—most quickly and inexpensively done with office endometrial biopsy.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>PCOS → chronic anovulatory cycles → persistent estrogen production without progesterone opposition → proliferative endometrium → hyperplasia ± carcinoma. When the endometrium outgrows its blood supply, sloughing produces heavy, irregular bleeding.  </p></br><p>Treatments / Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>Diagnostic: Office endometrial biopsy (or dilation &amp; curettage with hysteroscopy if biopsy nondiagnostic).<br /></br>Therapy once malignancy excluded: cyclic or continuous progestin, combined OCPs, LNG-IUD, weight loss, or endometrial ablation/hysterectomy if refractory.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Cervical NAAT for C. trachomatis / N. gonorrhoeae<br /></br>   • Indicated in women &lt;25 y or at high STI risk, or with cervicitis/PID. Does not evaluate endometrial pathology causing anovulatory bleeding.  </p></br><p>b. Colposcopy with biopsy<br /></br>   • Performed after abnormal Pap/HPV testing or visible cervical lesion. Patient’s Pap 1 y ago was normal; no cervical mass or abnormal cytology.  </p></br><p>d. Keeping a menstrual calendar<br /></br>   • Helpful for documenting bleeding pattern but does NOT rule out hyperplasia/cancer. It would delay necessary evaluation.  </p></br><p>e. Serum progesterone in luteal phase<br /></br>   • Used to confirm ovulation (progesterone &gt;3 ng/mL at day 21). The clinical picture already demonstrates anovulation; confirming it adds no new management-altering information and again delays ruling out malignancy.  </p></br><p>f. Repeat Pap smear<br /></br>   • Annual cytology alone is unnecessary with a normal test 1 y ago (current guidelines allow 3-year interval for cytology alone in women 21–29 and 3–5 years after 30 with HPV testing). Pap screens for cervical, not endometrial, cancer.  </p></br><p>Key Take-Home<br /></br>Abnormal uterine bleeding in a woman with obesity and clinical PCOS, even if &lt;45 y, mandates endometrial sampling to exclude hyperplasia/carcinoma—choose endometrial biopsy.</p>
32. A 67-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 4-month history of increasingly severe, constant epigastric pain. He says that he feels full after eating only small amounts of food. During the past 6 months, he has</br>had an 8.6-kg 19-lb weight loss. He has osteoarthritis treated with ibuprofen as needed and a 15-year history of type 2 diabetes mellitus treated with insulin. He has smoked two packs of cigarettes daily for 50 years and</br>drinks two beers daily. He is 178 cm 5 ft 10 in tall and now weighs 67 kg 148 lb; BMI is 21 kg/m 2. His pulse is 68/min, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 138/72 mm Hg. Abdominal examination shows</br>epigastric tenderness. Rectal examination shows brown stool. Test of the stool for occult blood is negative. His hemoglobin concentration is 12 g/dl, and mean corpuscular volume is 70 μm 3. Which of the following is the</br>most appropriate initial step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Gastric emptying test</br>b. Abdominal ultrasonography</br>c. Upper gastrointestinal series</br>d. Abdominal CT scan</br>e. Upperendoscopy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Upper endoscopy (esophagogastroduodenoscopy, EGD)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “67-year-old man” + “4-month history” + “8.6-kg [19-lb] weight loss” → alarm-feature, upper-GI red flag.<br /></br>• “Feels full after eating only small amounts” (early satiety) → suggests intraluminal space-occupying lesion (eg, gastric cancer) or gastric outlet obstruction.<br /></br>• “Constant epigastric pain” → upper-GI pathology rather than functional dyspepsia.<br /></br>• Microcytic anemia (Hb 12 g/dL, MCV 70 µm³) → chronic GI blood loss or anemia of chronic disease; occult blood can be intermittently negative.<br /></br>• Significant risk factors: 50-pack-year smoking, daily NSAID use, age &gt;60.</p></br><p>Key pattern: Alarm symptoms + microcytic anemia in an older patient with risk factors ⇒ MUST visualize the upper GI mucosa directly and obtain biopsy.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Guideline-based approach to dyspepsia/epigastric pain:<br /></br>1. Are alarm features present (age ≥60, weight loss, anemia, overt/occult GI bleed, progressive dysphagia, vomiting, strong FH)?  → YES<br /></br>2. If alarm features present → immediate diagnostic EGD with biopsy.<br /></br>3. Non-invasive tests or empiric therapy are reserved only when alarm features are absent.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In an older patient with red-flag symptoms, the first diagnostic step is always direct visualization (upper endoscopy), not imaging or functional studies.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Gastric cancer (especially intestinal-type adenocarcinoma) or complicated peptic ulcer disease can produce:<br /></br>• Mucosal ulceration → chronic occult bleeding → iron-deficiency anemia.<br /></br>• Intraluminal mass/narrowing → early satiety, weight loss.<br /></br>• Epigastric pain that is constant, not meal-related.<br /></br>Risk factors: chronic H. pylori, smoking, chronic NSAID use, age &gt;60.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostic &amp; Treatment Pearls<br /></br>First-line diagnostic test:  Upper endoscopy with multiple biopsies, which:<br /></br>• Confirms/ excludes malignancy, peptic ulcer disease, erosive gastritis.<br /></br>• Can sample H. pylori and direct therapy.  </p></br><p>Subsequent work-up (after EGD, if needed):<br /></br>• CT abdomen/pelvis for staging if cancer found.<br /></br>• Endoscopic ultrasound for depth/lymph-node assessment.<br /></br>Treatment depends on findings (eg, surgical resection + perioperative chemotherapy if resectable gastric cancer; PPI + H. pylori eradication for ulcer).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Gastric emptying test<br /></br>   – Evaluates gastroparesis; indicated for nondiabetic patients with chronic post-prandial fullness after structural lesions have been excluded. Cannot detect malignancy; inappropriate before endoscopy in high-risk patient.</p></br><p>b. Abdominal ultrasonography<br /></br>   – Good for gallstones, hepatobiliary pathology, or large pancreatic masses. Poor sensitivity for gastric/duodenal mucosal lesions; would miss early cancer.</p></br><p>c. Upper gastrointestinal series (barium swallow)<br /></br>   – Can show filling defects/narrowing but lacks ability to biopsy. Current guidelines favor EGD when alarm features are present.</p></br><p>d. Abdominal CT scan<br /></br>   – Useful for staging once malignancy is diagnosed, or for pancreatic pathology. Lower sensitivity than EGD for mucosal disease; exposes patient to radiation. Not first step.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Older patient with alarm symptoms (weight loss, early satiety, microcytic anemia) → jump straight to upper endoscopy to visualize and biopsy potential gastric malignancy.</p>
33. A study is conducted to compare the safety of primary closure of the colon with temporary colostomy in patients with penetrating injuries of the colon. The study is conducted over a 36-month period in a single institution.</br>All patients between the ages of 18 and 65 years admitted with penetrating injuries of the colon are enrolled in the study; they are assigned to one of two study groups, depending on which procedure their surgeon chose,</br>and observed until discharge from the hospital. The complication rate for the 38 patients who underwent primary closure of the colon is 14 percent. The complication rate for the 37 patients who underwent temporary colostomy</br>is 16 percent. The authors conclude that primary closure of colon injuries has the same level of safety as temporary colostomy in adults. Which of the following raises the most concern about this conclusion?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. The age range of the patients is inappropriate</br>b. The duration of follow-up is too short</br>c. The patient groups are not matched</br>d. The study is not double-blind</br>e. The study is not prospective</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. The patient groups are not matched  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “…assigned to one of two study groups, depending on which procedure their surgeon chose” = non-random allocation.<br /></br>• No information is given about whether the two cohorts were comparable (injury severity, comorbidities, hemodynamic stability, associated injuries, timing to operation, etc.).<br /></br>• Result: any difference—or lack of difference—in complications could be due to baseline differences rather than the procedure itself.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Key exam concept: When an intervention is not randomly assigned, selection bias and confounding threaten internal validity. The major threat here is unequal baseline risk that could mask a true difference or create a spurious one. Thus the principal concern is that “the patient groups are not matched.”</p></br><p>Pathophysiology / Study Design Review<br /></br>• Matching or randomization attempts to equalize known and unknown confounders (e.g., worse injuries might preferentially receive colostomy).<br /></br>• Without equivalence, complication rates cannot be confidently attributed to the surgical technique.<br /></br>• The small sample size (38 vs 37) amplifies the impact of any imbalance.</p></br><p>Treatments / Outcomes<br /></br>Complications after colon repair vs colostomy (anastomotic leak, intra-abdominal abscess, stoma complications, wound infection) are influenced by:<br /></br>– Injury grade, fecal contamination<br /></br>– Shock, transfusion volume<br /></br>– Associated visceral injuries<br /></br>If one group systematically has “cleaner” wounds or fewer comorbidities, its lower complication rate could be unrelated to the closure method.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Inappropriate age range<br /></br>• Adults 18-65 encompass the typical trauma population; age-related confounding could be adjusted for statistically. Not the primary flaw.</p></br><p>b. Follow-up too short<br /></br>• Most early postoperative colon injury complications occur during the index hospitalization. Longer follow-up could detect late stoma problems, but that affects only the colostomy group and is a secondary concern compared with baseline imbalance.</p></br><p>d. Not double-blind<br /></br>• Impossible to blind surgeons or patients to the presence of a colostomy. Lack of blinding is less problematic for objective outcomes like infection/leak rates than selection bias from non-matched groups.</p></br><p>e. Not prospective<br /></br>• The investigators enrolled patients as they were admitted and followed them forward—this is a prospective cohort study. The problem is allocation, not temporality.</p></br><p>Take-home Message<br /></br>When treatment choice is left to provider preference, ensure comparability of groups through randomization or meticulous matching/adjustment; otherwise, any conclusions about relative safety or efficacy are unreliable.</p>
34. A 15-month-old boy is admitted to the hospital because of a 2-day history of temperatures to 39.5 C 103.1  F and a swollen red mass on the right side of the neck. He has a 1-year history of failure to thrive, recurrent</br>suppurative skin infections, and two prior episodes of lymphadenitis. At the age of 9 months, he was hospitalized for a lung abscess secondary to Staphylococcus aureus infection. He has had three hospital admissions for</br>two episodes of staphylococcal pneumonia with abscesses and one episode of osteomyelitis. He currently receives no medications. He is at the 3rd percentile for height and 5th percentile for weight. His temperature is</br>39.5 C 103.1  F, pulse is 100/min and regular, respirations are 30/min, and blood pressure is 105/55 mm Hg. Examination shows no dysmorphic features. There is a 3 x 4-cm erythematous, indurated, warm, tender nodule</br>on the right side of the neck. No murmurs are heard on cardiac examination. On abdominal examination, the liver edge is palpated 4 cm below the right costal margin. Which of the following is most likely to confirm the</br>cause of recurrent suppurative infections in this patient?	Correct answer: F</br></br>a. Blood smear</br>b. Complete blood count, including leukocyte differential</br>c. Measurement of serum immunoglobulins and subclasses</br>d. Measurement of serum T lymphocytes</br>e. Measurement of serum total hemolytic complement</br>F. Neutrophil oxidative burst test</br>Correct Answer: F</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>F. Neutrophil oxidative-burst test (eg, dihydrorhodamine-123 flow cytometry or older nitro-blue tetrazolium assay)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Recurrent suppurative skin infections,” “lung abscess,” “osteomyelitis,” “lymphadenitis,” all due to Staphylococcus aureus (catalase-positive) beginning in early infancy → classic red flag for a phagocytic (neutrophil) killing defect rather than an antibody, T-cell, or complement problem.<br /></br>• Abscesses are deep-seated, purulent, and repeatedly involve catalase-positive organisms (Staph, Serratia, Aspergillus, Burkholderia).<br /></br>• No dysmorphic features, normal heart exam, mild hepatomegaly; growth failure from chronic infection.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Pattern recognition: catalase-positive, pus-forming infections + granulomatous/abscess disease in a boy → Chronic Granulomatous Disease (CGD), an X-linked (most cases) or autosomal recessive defect in NADPH oxidase.<br /></br>Core takeaway: CGD is diagnosed by demonstrating absent or markedly reduced oxidative burst in neutrophils. The oxidative-burst test is therefore the single best study to “confirm the cause.”</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• NADPH oxidase generates superoxide → hydrogen peroxide → hypochlorous acid (via MPO) inside the phagolysosome.<br /></br>• Catalase-positive organisms destroy their own H2O2, so CGD phagocytes have nothing to “borrow” and cannot kill these microbes. Result = granuloma formation and recurrent abscesses.<br /></br>• Inheritance: ~70% X-linked, remainder AR.<br /></br>• Clinically appears in first year of life with recurrent staphylococcal/serratia pneumonia, lymphadenitis, skin/liver abscesses, osteomyelitis.  </p></br><p>Treatment / Management<br /></br>1. Lifelong prophylaxis: trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole + itraconazole/posaconazole.<br /></br>2. Interferon-γ to ↓ infection frequency.<br /></br>3. Hematopoietic stem-cell transplant is curative.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>A. Peripheral blood smear<br /></br>   • Helpful for Chediak-Higashi (giant granules) or Pelger-Huët, but smear is normal in CGD.  </p></br><p>B. CBC with differential<br /></br>   • CGD often shows normal WBC count; LAD would show marked neutrophilia but with NON-purulent infections and delayed cord separation—different phenotype.  </p></br><p>C. Serum immunoglobulins &amp; subclasses<br /></br>   • X-linked agammaglobulinemia/hyper-IgM cause otitis, sinusitis, pneumonia with encapsulated organisms, not deep Staph abscesses. Ig levels are normal in CGD.  </p></br><p>D. Serum T-lymphocyte quantitation<br /></br>   • Low T cells → viral/fungal/opportunistic infections (SCID, DiGeorge), not staphylococcal abscesses. T-cell counts are normal in CGD.  </p></br><p>E. Total hemolytic complement (CH50)<br /></br>   • Complement deficiencies cause recurrent Neisseria (C5-C9) or encapsulated bacterial sepsis (C3). They do not explain the hallmark catalase-positive abscesses.  </p></br><p>Bottom line: Recurrent catalase-positive, pus-forming infections in a male infant → think CGD; confirm with a neutrophil oxidative-burst test.</p>
35. A 22-year-old white woman, gravida 3, para 1, aborta 1, at 28 weeks' gestation comes to the office because of an itchy rash on her vulva that she first noticed yesterday. Two days ago at a routine prenatal visit, the patient</br>reported a 2-day history of vaginal discharge. Wet mount preparation of the vaginal discharge disclosed trichomoniasis. Oral metronidazole was prescribed, which the patient began taking yesterday. Medical history is</br>otherwise unremarkable. She has been taking prenatal vitamins. She is employed as an aide in a veterinary office. Vital signs are temperature 37.6 C 99.7  F, pulse 60/min, respirations 15/min, and blood pressure</br>120/76 mm Hg. Physical examination discloses the vulvar findings as shown in the photograph. Fundal height is consistent with a 28-week gestation. In addition to prescribing treatment for the itching, which of the</br>following is the most appropriate recommendation?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. She should avoid sharing towels and linens with household contacts</br>b. She should discontinue the metronidazole</br>c. She should limit avian exposure while at work in order to prevent a recurrence of the rash</br>d. Reassurance onlv: the skin chanaes are normal and associated with oreanancv</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. She should avoid sharing towels and linens with household contacts  </p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Itchy rash on her vulva” that began abruptly and is localized to the hair-bearing skin of the vulva.<br /></br>• Photograph (not shown here) in the original item depicts small tan-brown insects and oval white nits attached to pubic hair shafts—classic for pediculosis pubis (“crabs”).<br /></br>• Works in a veterinary office → possible animal exposure, but pediculosis is most commonly spread by sexual contact or fomites such as bedding and towels.<br /></br>• Timing with oral metronidazole is coincidental; drug eruptions are typically widespread, maculopapular, and not limited to the pubic hair.  </p></br><p>Pattern: Intense pruritus + lice/nits on pubic hair = pediculosis pubis.  </p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Localized pruritus on a hair-bearing surface → think ectoparasite.<br /></br>Step 2: Visualize insects/nits → lice infestation.<br /></br>Step 3: Location (pubic) → Phthirus pubis.<br /></br>Key counseling: Treat the patient (permethrin 1% cream) and decontaminate fomites—wash bedding, clothing, towels in hot water or seal in plastic bags for ≥72 h. Don’t share these items with others until decontaminated.  </p></br><p>Core Takeaway: Pediculosis pubis requires both pharmacologic eradication of the lice and environmental measures (avoid sharing / launder linens) to prevent reinfestation and transmission.  </p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiologic agent: Phthirus pubis louse.<br /></br>• Transmission: Sexual contact or fomite transfer (towels, sheets, clothes).<br /></br>• Pruritus results from hypersensitivity to louse saliva after 3–5 wk incubation.<br /></br>• Physical exam: Moving lice (1–2 mm), nits cemented to hair ≈1 cm from skin.<br /></br>• Pregnancy: Permethrin, pyrethrin-piperonyl butoxide, and mechanical nit removal are safe; avoid lindane in pregnancy because of neurotoxicity.  </p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Highlights<br /></br>1. Topical 1% permethrin cream to entire pubic region; repeat in 7–10 d if needed.<br /></br>2. Decontaminate environment:<br /></br>   • Wash clothing, bedding, towels in ≥55 °C (≥130 °F) water, then hot dryer.<br /></br>   • Items that can’t be washed: seal in plastic bag for ≥3 days.<br /></br>3. Sexual partners within past month should be treated simultaneously.<br /></br>4. No need to discontinue metronidazole; continue treating trichomoniasis.<br /></br>5. Consider STI screening (gonorrhea, chlamydia, syphilis, HIV) because pubic lice often coexist with other STIs.  </p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Discontinue the metronidazole<br /></br>• A drug hypersensitivity would cause a generalized morbilliform rash, not isolated pruritus with visible lice/nits. Metronidazole is safe in the 2nd/3rd trimester.<br /></br>c. Limit avian exposure while at work<br /></br>• Bird (avian) contact is linked to psittacosis, histoplasmosis, or Chlamydia psittaci—not a vulvar rash. No connection to recurrence of lice.<br /></br>d. Reassurance only: normal pregnancy skin changes<br /></br>• Normal pregnancy skin findings (linea nigra, striae, melasma) are non-pruritic or mildly pruritic, diffuse, and do not show insects/nits. Vulvar pediculosis is pathologic and needs treatment plus fomite precautions.  </p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Pediculosis pubis causes intense vulvar itching in pregnancy and is transmitted via sexual contact or contaminated linens. In addition to permethrin therapy, patients must avoid sharing towels and linens and launder them properly to prevent reinfestation and household spread.</p>
36. A 77-year-old man is brought to the physician by his wife because of a 3-week history of confusion, daytime sleepiness, and frequent falls. He appears lethargic but is arousable. Neurologic examination shows no focal</br>findings. On mental status examination, he is oriented to person but not to place or time. Short-term memory is decreased. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Dementia, Alzheimer type</br>b. Multi-infarct vascular dementia</br>c. Parkinson disease with dementia</br>d. Pick disease</br>e. Subdural hematoma</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e.
