Subdural hematoma  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Time course: “3-week history” of new confusion and lethargy → subacute, not the years-long course typical of neurodegenerative dementias.<br /></br>• Preceding clue: “frequent falls.” In an elderly patient falls are both a risk factor for, and a consequence of, a chronic subdural bleed.<br /></br>• Exam: No focal neurologic findings (common with subdural until late or if bleed is bilateral). Global “clouding of consciousness” and disorientation are classic.<br /></br>These elements tightly fit the classic presentation of a chronic subdural hematoma in the elderly after minor or unremembered head trauma.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Elderly brain atrophy stretches bridging veins → minor trauma/fall can tear them.<br /></br>2. Slow venous (bridging-vein) bleed → blood collects over weeks → gradually rising ICP → fluctuating confusion, somnolence, gait instability.<br /></br>3. Bilateral or midline collections often produce non-focal or minimal exam findings.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Rupture of cortical bridging veins.<br /></br>• Risk factors: Age, cerebral atrophy, chronic alcohol use, anticoagulation/antiplatelet therapy.<br /></br>• Presentation: Subacute to chronic (days-weeks) headache, altered mental status, gait disturbance, falls, sometimes focal deficits or papilledema late.  </p></br><p>Treatment / Diagnostics<br /></br>• Non-contrast head CT: Crescent-shaped, hyper-/iso-/mixed-density collection crossing suture lines.<br /></br>• Management: Neurosurgical evacuation (burr-hole or craniotomy) if symptomatic or large; serial imaging/observation if small and stable. Reverse anticoagulation when appropriate.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Alzheimer dementia<br /></br>– Onset is insidious (months-years), not 3 weeks.<br /></br>– Early hallmark is memory loss, but daytime sleepiness and acute gait instability are uncommon initial features.<br /></br>– No temporal relationship with falls or trauma.</p></br><p>b. Multi-infarct (vascular) dementia<br /></br>– Produces a stepwise decline over months with focal neurologic signs (hemiparesis, visual field cuts, hyperreflexia).<br /></br>– CT/MRI would show multiple territorial infarcts; vignette reports “no focal findings.”</p></br><p>c. Parkinson disease with dementia<br /></br>– Requires pre-existing Parkinson motor features (resting tremor, cogwheel rigidity, bradykinesia) for ≥1 year before dementia.<br /></br>– None of these motor findings are mentioned.</p></br><p>d. Pick disease (frontotemporal dementia)<br /></br>– Presents earlier (50-60 yrs) with prominent personality/behavior or language change, not acute confusion or falls.<br /></br>– Progresses over years, not weeks.</p></br><p>Key Take-Home<br /></br>In an elderly patient with a subacute change in mental status and gait, especially after falls and without focal deficits, always think “chronic subdural hematoma” and obtain an urgent non-contrast head CT.</p>
37. A 27-year-old white man comes to the physician because of increasing fatigue for 3 weeks. He has a 5-year history of heavy alcohol intake and currently drinks 10 oz of alcohol daily. Over the past year, he has had several</br>episodes of hematemesis and associated melenic stools. Examination shows palmar erythema, spider angiomata, and mild splenomegaly. His hemoglobin concentration is 7 g/dl. A blood smear shows hypochromic</br>microcytic erythrocytes and increased numbers of platelets. Serum studies are most likely to show which of the following?</br>Iron Transferrin Saturation Ferritin</br>μg/dl  percent N=20-50 ng/ml	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. 30 10 10</br>b. 40 20 175</br>c. 115 35 100</br>d. 150 45 1000</br>e. 150 50 20</br>F. 200 75 10,000</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. 30 µg/dL serum iron, 10 % transferrin saturation, 10 ng/mL ferritin  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Long-standing heavy alcohol use → portal hypertension → esophageal varices<br /></br>• Repeated hematemesis &amp; melena → chronic GI blood loss<br /></br>• Hypochromic, microcytic erythrocytes + Hb 7 g/dL → iron-deficiency anemia (IDA)<br /></br>• Reactive thrombocytosis (↑ platelets) is classic for IDA and ongoing hemorrhage; cirrhosis alone usually gives thrombocytopenia—this clue points toward iron deficiency rather than “anemia of chronic disease.”  </p></br><p>Key pattern: chronic blood loss → microcytosis + reactive ↑ platelets → iron‐deficiency profile (low iron, low ferritin, low transferrin saturation, high TIBC).  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Determine anemia type: microcytic → think iron deficiency, thalassemia, anemia of chronic disease, sideroblastic.<br /></br>2. Variceal bleeding = chronic occult/ overt loss → IDA most likely.<br /></br>3. Lab hallmark of IDA:<br /></br>   • Serum iron ↓<br /></br>   • Ferritin (storage iron) ↓<br /></br>   • Transferrin/TIBC ↑ → % saturation ↓ (&lt;15 %).<br /></br>Choice a is the only option with all three values low.  </p></br><p>Take-home: In any patient with chronic bleeding, expect the classic iron-deficiency profile; reactive thrombocytosis helps distinguish it from other microcytic causes.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Brief Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Chronic blood loss → gradual depletion of iron stores (↓ ferritin) → depleted circulating iron (↓ serum iron) → liver ramps up transferrin production (↑ TIBC); because iron is low, % saturation falls.<br /></br>• ↓ iron impairs heme synthesis → microcytosis &amp; hypochromia.<br /></br>• IDA stimulates marrow release of thrombopoietic factors → thrombocytosis.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>• Address source of bleeding (endoscopic variceal ligation, non-selective β-blocker, eventual transplant evaluation).<br /></br>• Iron repletion: oral ferrous sulfate (first-line) or IV iron if malabsorption/ ongoing losses.<br /></br>• Repeat CBC/iron panel in 4–6 wk; reticulocytosis precedes Hb correction.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>b. 40 / 20 % / 175 ng mL<br /></br>   – Low iron but NORMAL-HIGH ferritin → anemia of chronic disease/chronic inflammation, not IDA.  </p></br><p>c. 115 / 35 % / 100 ng mL<br /></br>   – All essentially normal; would not explain Hb 7 g/dL with microcytosis.  </p></br><p>d. 150 / 45 % / 1000 ng mL<br /></br>   – High ferritin + high iron &amp; saturation → iron overload states (hereditary hemochromatosis, multiple transfusions).  </p></br><p>e. 150 / 50 % / 20 ng mL<br /></br>   – High serum iron &amp; saturation but LOW ferritin is physiologically inconsistent; not seen in common conditions.  </p></br><p>f. 200 / 75 % / 10 000 ng mL<br /></br>   – Extremely high values across the board → acute iron poisoning or massive transfusional hemosiderosis, incompatible with microcytic anemia.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Core Concept<br /></br>Iron-deficiency anemia from chronic GI blood loss manifests with LOW iron, LOW ferritin, and LOW transferrin saturation; reactive thrombocytosis is a helpful clue.</p>
38. A 35-year-old man comes to the i:2 hysician for a health maintenance examination. He has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. His parents have essential hypertension. The patient is 180 cm 5 ft 11 in tall</br>and weighs 72 kg 160 lb; BMI is 22 kg/m 2. His pulse is 72/min and regular, and blood pressure is 155/95 mm Hg. Repeat blood pressure measurement is 159/98 mm Hg. The remainder of the examination shows no</br>abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. 24-Hour urine collection for measurement of catecholamine concentrations</br>b. Measurement of plasma renin activity</br>c. Renal arteriography</br>d. Renal ultrasonography</br>e. Thiazide diuretic therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Start a thiazide diuretic (eg, hydrochlorothiazide, chlorthalidone)  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 35-year-old, otherwise healthy man<br /></br>• Persistent elevated BPs: 155/95 and 159/98 mm Hg at the same visit<br /></br>• Normal BMI, no suspicious physical findings, no history suggesting secondary causes  </p></br><p>These findings point to uncomplicated (essential) Stage 1 hypertension. There is no red flag (severe/malignant HTN, hypokalemia, renal bruits, episodic headaches/ diaphoresis, kidney size asymmetry, etc.) that would push you toward an extensive secondary work-up.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Stepwise approach to newly discovered hypertension:<br /></br>1. Confirm persistent elevation (≥2 readings on ≥2 occasions or ambulatory monitoring).<br /></br>2. If confirmed and no secondary-cause clues → initiate first-line therapy + lifestyle counseling.<br /></br>• In a non-Black patient &lt;60 y with Stage 1 HTN and no comorbidities, first-line drug choices are thiazide diuretic, ACE inhibitor, ARB, or long-acting dihydropyridine CCB.<br /></br>Because none of the other answer choices constitute confirmation or basic therapy, starting a thiazide is the most appropriate next step given the options.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Do not jump to costly/endovascular or endocrine tests in an otherwise healthy patient with typical, mild essential hypertension; start proven first-line therapy.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Essential (primary) hypertension (≈ 90–95 % of adult HTN) is due to complex interactions between genetics, sympathetic tone, renal sodium handling, and vascular tone. Secondary causes (renal artery stenosis, pheochromocytoma, primary aldosteronism, etc.) account for a minority but are suspected when hypertension is very early, severe/resistant, or accompanied by characteristic clinical/lab clues.  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics Review<br /></br>First-line pharmacologic agents for essential HTN:<br /></br>• Thiazide diuretics – chlorthalidone (most outcome data), hydrochlorothiazide<br /></br>• ACE inhibitors – lisinopril, enalapril<br /></br>• ARBs – losartan, valsartan<br /></br>• DHP-CCBs – amlodipine, felodipine<br /></br>Non-pharmacologic measures (always add): weight loss, DASH diet, ↓ Na⁺, exercise, ↓ EtOH.<br /></br>Initial lab work (BMP, fasting glucose/A1c, lipid panel, TSH, urinalysis) and ECG evaluate end-organ involvement but are far less invasive than the options listed.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. 24-h urine catecholamines – Screens for pheochromocytoma. Indicated with episodic headaches, palpitations, diaphoresis, paroxysmal HTN, or adrenal mass—none present here.<br /></br>b. Plasma renin activity – Helps diagnose renin-dependent etiologies (eg, renal artery stenosis, malignant HTN, suspected primary aldosteronism work-up). Would be appropriate if the patient had refractory HTN, abdominal bruits, flash pulmonary edema, or unexplained hypokalemia.<br /></br>c. Renal arteriography – Invasive gold standard for renal artery stenosis; reserved when non-invasive imaging suggests stenosis and revascularization is being considered, not as an initial test in mild HTN.<br /></br>d. Renal ultrasonography – Screens for structural kidney disease or size discrepancy suggesting renovascular disease or chronic kidney disease. No hematuria, proteinuria, abnormal creatinine, or symptoms to warrant this as the next step.<br /></br>All of the above tests hunt for secondary causes; none are justified before trying first-line medical therapy in an uncomplicated patient.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Many students over-investigate every young hypertensive patient. Age &lt;40 alone is NOT an indication for a full secondary work-up unless additional clinical clues are present.</p>
39. A 57-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 2-week history of a tender lump in her right armpit. The lump has not changed in size during this period. She has no history of serious illness and takes no</br>medications. Ten months ago, annual examination, Pap smear, and mammography showed no abnormalities. There is no family history of cancer. Examination shows a 3-cm, firm, round mass in the right axilla immediately</br>lateral to the pectoralis major muscle. There is no associated axillary lymphadenopathy. The remainder of the examination, including examination of the breasts, shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most</br>appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Follow-up examination in 3 months</br>b. Ultrasonography of the mass</br>c. CT scan of the chest</br>d. Breast-specific gamma imaging</br>e. Antibiotic therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Follow-up examination in 3 months  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “2-week history,” “tender,” “lump in her right armpit” ➜ short-duration, painful axillary mass → most commonly a benign, reactive lymph node.<br /></br>• “Has not changed in size,” “no systemic symptoms,” “normal breast exam,” “normal screening mammogram 10 months ago” ➜ very low pre-test probability of occult breast cancer.<br /></br>• “No associated axillary lymphadenopathy” ➜ solitary node rather than matted pathologic nodes.<br /></br>Key pattern: A new, tender, isolated axillary node of only 2 weeks’ duration without breast or constitutional findings = likely reactive lymphadenitis → initial watchful waiting is standard.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. First question: Could this be infection?  Tenderness favors inflammation, but there is no erythema, warmth, fever, or cutaneous lesion.<br /></br>2. Could it be malignancy?  In breast cancer, axillary nodes are usually NON-tender, firm, and often accompany a breast abnormality; patient’s recent normal screen and exam make this unlikely, but not impossible.<br /></br>3. Guideline: For an isolated peripheral node &lt;3–4 weeks’ duration, observe for spontaneous regression up to 3 months.  Biopsy or imaging is reserved for nodes that (a) persist &gt;3 months, (b) enlarge, or (c) have worrisome features (rock-hard, fixed, &gt;2 cm, supraclavicular, systemic “B” symptoms).  </p></br><p>Take-home concept: Short-term follow-up is the safest, least invasive, evidence-based first step for a solitary, tender node with low malignant risk.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Reactive lymphadenitis: antigenic stimulation from minor skin breaks, shaving, deodorant irritation → follicular hyperplasia → rapid, painful enlargement; usually self-limited as antigen exposure resolves.<br /></br>• Malignant node: usually painless, hard, fixed, progressive.  Associated skin dimpling or nipple changes would raise suspicion of occult breast carcinoma.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Diagnostic Strategy<br /></br>Step 1 (current scenario): Re-examine in 4–12 weeks.<br /></br>Step 2 (if persists/enlarges): Targeted axillary ultrasound ± core-needle biopsy.<br /></br>Step 3 (if malignant on biopsy): staging CT, sentinel node biopsy, etc.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Incorrect  </p></br><p>b. Ultrasonography of the mass<br /></br>• Appropriate only if the node remains palpable after the observation interval or shows high-risk features.  Immediate imaging adds cost/radiation with extremely low diagnostic yield in a clearly reactive presentation.  </p></br><p>c. CT scan of the chest<br /></br>• A staging study for known or strongly suspected malignancy; inappropriate as an initial step for an isolated, likely benign node.  </p></br><p>d. Breast-specific gamma imaging<br /></br>• A nuclear test used when mammography is equivocal in a patient with a suspicious breast lesion.  No breast lesion is present here.  </p></br><p>e. Antibiotic therapy<br /></br>• Empiric antibiotics are reserved for nodes with signs of bacterial infection (erythema, warmth, fluctuation, systemic fever).  None of these are present; unnecessary antibiotic use breeds resistance.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Core Takeaway<br /></br>In adults, a solitary, tender, short-duration lymph node without other red flags is managed expectantly with repeat examination in several weeks; further work-up is indicated only if the mass persists or progresses.</p>
40. Two weeks after undergoing uncomplicated emergency appendectomy, a 62-year-old woman comes to the physician for a follow-up examination. Recove [Y has been uncomplicated. A CT scan of the abdomen with</br>contrast prior to the operation also showed a 1.5-cm left adrenal mass with a smooth border and homogeneous uptake. She has hypertension. Her medications are hydrochlorothiazide, amlodipine, and fiber</br>supplementation. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows a well-healing surgical scar over the abdomen. No other abnormalities are noted. Hormonal testing of adrenal function shows no abnormalities.</br>Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Adrenal adenoma</br>b. Adrenal carcinoma</br>c. Adrenal hyperplasia</br>d. Metastatic carcinoma</br>e. Pheochromocytoma</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Adrenal adenoma</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Incidental” adrenal mass: found on CT done for another reason (appendicitis).<br /></br>• Size 1.5 cm (≤4 cm) + “smooth border” + “homogeneous uptake” → classic benign imaging features.<br /></br>• “Hormonal testing … shows no abnormalities” → non-functioning lesion.<br /></br>These three facts (small, benign radiographic appearance, non-secretory) fit the textbook definition of a non-functioning adrenal cortical adenoma (“incidentaloma”).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning / Take-home<br /></br>Most adrenal “incidentalomas” are benign cortical adenomas. Probability of malignancy rises sharply when the diameter is &gt;4 cm or when imaging shows irregular, heterogeneous, or lipid-poor tissue. All such masses require basic hormonal screening; if non-secretory and &lt;4 cm with benign imaging, observation is appropriate.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Adrenal cortical adenoma<br /></br>• Clonal proliferation of adrenal cortical cells; often lipid-rich (low attenuation on CT).<br /></br>• 70–80 % are non-functioning; functioning adenomas may secrete cortisol (Cushing syndrome) or aldosterone (Conn syndrome).<br /></br>• Benign behavior; malignant transformation is exceedingly rare.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostics &amp; Management (FYI)<br /></br>1. Biochemical screen: overnight dexamethasone suppression ± 24 h urine free cortisol, plasma-free metanephrines, and (if hypertensive &amp; hypokalemic) aldosterone/renin ratio.<br /></br>2. Imaging: adenoma &lt;10 HU on non-contrast CT, rapid contrast wash-out (&gt;60 % at 15 min).<br /></br>3. Surgery considered if: &gt;4 – 6 cm, functional, or concerning imaging features. Otherwise, periodic CT (6–12 mo) + repeat labs.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>b. Adrenal carcinoma<br /></br>• Usually ≥4–6 cm, irregular, heterogeneous with necrosis or calcification, often functional (cortisol, androgens). None of these apply.</p></br><p>c. Adrenal hyperplasia<br /></br>• Bilateral diffuse thickening, not a solitary, well-circumscribed nodule. Commonly tied to congenital adrenal hyperplasia or ACTH overproduction.</p></br><p>d. Metastatic carcinoma<br /></br>• More likely bilateral, irregular lesions in a patient with known primary malignancy (lung, breast, melanoma, kidney, etc.). Vignette gives no cancer history; lesion is small and homogeneous.</p></br><p>e. Pheochromocytoma<br /></br>• Often &gt;3 cm, very vascular/heterogeneous on CT or MRI, and biochemically active (↑ plasma/urine metanephrines) producing episodic headaches, palpitations, diaphoresis, paroxysmal hypertension. None of these features are present.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Core “Shelf/Step 2” Pearls<br /></br>1. Size (&lt;4 cm) + benign imaging + nonsecretory = likely adenoma; observe.<br /></br>2. Every incidental adrenal mass warrants screening for cortisol excess and catecholamine excess regardless of BP profile.<br /></br>3. Masses &gt;4 cm or hormonally active → adrenalectomy.</p>
41. A 27-year-old woman comes to the physician for her first prenatal visit after a home pregnancy test was positive 1 week ago. Her last menstrual period was 9 weeks ago. She has a 9-year history of poorly controlled type 1</br>diabetes mellitus treated with insulin. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows a uterus consistent in size with a 9-week gestation. Which of the following is the most appropriate test to assess the risk for fetal</br>malformation in this patient?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Complete blood count</br>b. Measurement of hemoglobin A1c</br>c. Measurement of serum creatinine concentration</br>d. Measurement of serum a-fetoprotein concentration</br>e. Measurement of pregnancy-associated plasma protein A concentration</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Measurement of hemoglobin A₁c  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “9-year history of poorly controlled type 1 diabetes” → long-standing hyperglycemia.<br /></br>• “Last menstrual period was 9 weeks ago” → she is in the first trimester, the period when organogenesis—and therefore teratogenic injury—occurs.<br /></br>The stem explicitly asks for a test that will “assess the risk for fetal malformation,” not a test that directly detects an existing anomaly. In diabetic pregnancy the best surrogate marker for that risk is the degree of maternal glycemic control at conception and in very early gestation, which is reflected by the hemoglobin A₁c level.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>• HbA₁c represents average glucose over the preceding 8–12 weeks, overlapping the periconceptional and 1st-trimester window of organogenesis.<br /></br>• Higher HbA₁c correlates in a dose-dependent fashion with congenital malformations (cardiac defects, neural-tube defects, caudal regression syndrome, etc.).<br /></br>Take-home: In pregestational diabetes, first-trimester HbA₁c is a validated predictor of major congenital anomalies.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Hyperglycemia during organogenesis → oxidative stress, apoptosis, and disruption of developmental gene expression in the embryo.<br /></br>• Risk roughly doubles when HbA₁c &gt; 7 %, and rises dramatically (&gt; 20 %) when &gt; 10 %.<br /></br>• Tight glycemic control prior to conception (HbA₁c &lt; 6.5 %) brings malformation risk close to baseline.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>1. Preconception/early pregnancy: measure HbA₁c; intensify insulin therapy, nutrition counseling, frequent self-monitoring.<br /></br>2. Second-trimester anomaly screening: detailed fetal anatomy ultrasound (18–22 wk) ± fetal echocardiography; maternal serum α-fetoprotein (15–20 wk) for open NTDs.<br /></br>3. Third trimester: serial growth scans for macrosomia or growth restriction, antenatal testing for placental function.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Complete blood count – Useful to screen for anemia but has no correlation with diabetic embryopathy.<br /></br>c. Serum creatinine – Evaluates maternal diabetic nephropathy; important for maternal prognosis but does not predict congenital anomalies in the fetus.<br /></br>d. Maternal serum α-fetoprotein – Detects existing open neural-tube or abdominal-wall defects, but is performed at 15–20 weeks; it does not predict overall malformation risk in the first trimester.<br /></br>e. Pregnancy-associated plasma protein-A (PAPP-A) – Part of first-trimester aneuploidy screening (Down, Edwards); not related to diabetic teratogenic risk.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Confusing a test that screens for an already-formed defect (e.g., MSAFP) with a test that predicts the likelihood of defects before they form (HbA₁c). For diabetic mothers, the latter is what the question is probing.</p>
42. A 32-year-old man comes to the emergency department because of a 2-week history of progressive shortness of breath, nonproductive cough, and low-grade fever. He has not had night sweats or change in weight. He has</br>no history of serious illness and takes no medications. Over the past 6 years, he has been sexually active with 10 male partners; he uses condoms inconsistently. He does not smoke or drink alcohol. He appears anxious.</br>His temperature is 37.7 C 99.8 F, pulse is 96/min, respirations are 28/min, and blood pressure is 120/75 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 82 percent. Jugular venous pressure is 6 cm H 20.</br>Diffuse crackles are heard bilaterally. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. An x-ray of the chest shows bilateral interstitial infiltrates. A silver stain of the sputum is positive. Antibiotic therapy is begun.</br>Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient's hypoxemia?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Alveolar hypoventilation</br>b. Arteriovenous shunt</br>c. Atelectasis</br>d. Decreased dead space ventilation</br>e. Decreased diffusion capacity</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Decreased diffusion capacity  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “2-week history of progressive shortness of breath, non-productive cough, low-grade fever” + “bilateral interstitial infiltrates” + “silver stain of the sputum is positive” → classic triad for Pneumocystis jirovecii pneumonia (PCP) in an undiagnosed HIV patient.<br /></br>• Oxygen saturation 82 % on room air and tachypnea (RR 28/min) indicate significant hypoxemia despite high minute ventilation.<br /></br>• Interstitial process (PCP) thickens the alveolar-capillary membrane → impaired O2 diffusion, particularly at normal or high cardiac output (rest vs exertion).  </p></br><p>Key pattern: Interstitial lung disease/PCP → widened A-a gradient due to diffusion limitation and V/Q mismatch, not due to global hypoventilation.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1 – Identify mechanism groups that cause hypoxemia:<br /></br>1. Low FiO2 (high altitude) – not present.<br /></br>2. Alveolar hypoventilation – would have ↑PaCO₂, no A-a gradient widening.<br /></br>3. V/Q mismatch (including atelectasis) – creates areas of low V/Q.<br /></br>4. Right-to-left shunt – severe, refractory to O2.<br /></br>5. Diffusion limitation – thickened membrane (PCP, pulmonary fibrosis) → ↑A-a gradient that improves with supplemental O2.  </p></br><p>Step 2 – Select the best fit: PCP causes an interstitial pneumonitis → diffusion limitation (#5). Therefore: Decreased diffusion capacity.  </p></br><p>Take-home: In HIV patients with PCP, the hallmark hypoxemia mechanism is impaired diffusion across a thickened alveolar-capillary barrier.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Pneumocystis jirovecii attaches to type I pneumocytes → alveolar foamy exudate + interstitial edema →<br /></br>• Thickened diffusion barrier (↓DLCO)<br /></br>• Patchy V/Q mismatch (areas of relative dead space and low V/Q)<br /></br>• Result: Widened A-a gradient; hypoxemia disproportionate to CXR findings.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatments / Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• First-line therapy: TMP-SMX (high-dose intravenous or oral).<br /></br>• Adjunctive therapy: Prednisone if PaO₂ &lt; 70 mm Hg or A-a gradient ≥ 35 mm Hg.<br /></br>• Diagnostic: Induced sputum or BAL with silver stain (or PCR) demonstrating cystic forms.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Alveolar hypoventilation<br /></br>   – Would present with hypercapnia (↑PaCO₂), normal A-a gradient, often caused by CNS depression, neuromuscular weakness, or severe obesity. This patient is tachypneic, not hypoventilating, and PCP does not depress ventilation.  </p></br><p>b. Arteriovenous shunt (right-to-left shunt)<br /></br>   – Hypoxemia refractory to 100 % O₂, often with markedly low PaO₂ and normal lungs on CXR (congenital heart disease, pulmonary AVMs). PCP shows interstitial infiltrates and generally responds to O₂ supplementation.  </p></br><p>c. Atelectasis<br /></br>   – Localized collapse → shunt physiology limited to affected lobe/segment, typically shows volume loss on imaging (tracheal/mediastinal shift, elevated diaphragm), not diffuse bilateral interstitial infiltrates.  </p></br><p>d. Decreased dead-space ventilation<br /></br>   – “Dead space” implies areas ventilated but not perfused (PE, emphysema). Reducing dead space would actually improve gas exchange; dead-space changes do not explain diffuse interstitial infiltrates.  </p></br><p>e. Decreased diffusion capacity  (Correct)<br /></br>   – Interstitial inflammation/thickening from PCP → ↓DLCO → widened A-a gradient → hypoxemia that improves with supplemental O₂.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Assuming all hypoxemia in lung infection is due to V/Q mismatch. Interstitial infections (PCP, early ILD) primarily impair diffusion capacity.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Core Concept<br /></br>Interstitial lung processes such as Pneumocystis jirovecii pneumonia produce hypoxemia predominantly through diffusion limitation, manifested as a markedly reduced DLCO and widened A-a gradient.</p>
43. A 23-year-old woman comes to student health services because of increasing anxiety about being called on in class for the past 6 weeks. Yesterday, she was so worried about being called on that she was shaking and</br>spilled her coffee. Today, she was so frightened that she was unable to attend class. She says that she has always found it difficult to be the center of attention and has always avoided situations in which she would have</br>to perform or speak in public. Physical examination shows no abnormalities. On mental status examination, she is cooperative and well dressed. She is anxious and tearful. Her thought process is organized. There is no</br>evidence of suicidal ideation. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Cognitive behavioral psychotherapy</br>b. Insight-oriented psychotherapy</br>c. Bupropion therapy</br>d. Buspirone therapy</br>e. Risperidone therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>A. Cognitive-behavioral psychotherapy (CBT)</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Always found it difficult to be the center of attention … avoided situations in which she would have to perform or speak in public” → lifelong, marked fear of social/performance scrutiny.<br /></br>• “So worried … shaking … spilled her coffee … unable to attend class” → physiologic anxiety + functional impairment.<br /></br>• Duration ≥ 6 months is technically required for DSM-5 social anxiety disorder, but NBME often accepts “always”/“lifelong” language to satisfy the chronicity criterion even if the current flare is 6 weeks.<br /></br>• No findings of panic disorder (no unexpected panic attacks), GAD (worry limited to social scrutiny, not multiple domains), agoraphobia, or depression/psychosis.</p></br><p>Pattern = Social anxiety disorder (social phobia), performance type.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Core takeaway: First-line treatment for social anxiety disorder is either CBT or an SSRI/SNRI. When a question juxtaposes CBT with medications and the case is uncomplicated (young, motivated, no comorbidities, mild-to-moderate severity), CBT alone is preferred.</p></br><p>Reasoning pathway<br /></br>1. Identify disorder → Social anxiety disorder.<br /></br>2. Assess severity/comorbidities → No suicidality, substance use, or major depression; impairment limited to class performance.<br /></br>3. Choose evidence-based, first-line, least-invasive therapy → CBT.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)<br /></br>• Etiology: Genetic predisposition + conditioning to social embarrassment → heightened amygdala reactivity to social threat.<br /></br>• Manifestations: Fear of negative evaluation → avoidance or endurance with intense anxiety.<br /></br>• Performance-only specifier: anxiety restricted to speaking/performing in public (common in students, musicians).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>1. Non-pharmacologic<br /></br>   • CBT (exposure + cognitive restructuring) – gold standard.<br /></br>   • Social skills training, relaxation techniques.</p></br><ol></br><li>Pharmacologic (if CBT inadequate or patient prefers medication)<br /></br>   • First line: SSRIs (sertraline, paroxetine, escitalopram) or SNRIs (venlafaxine).<br /></br>   • Performance-only, situational rescue: Propranolol or other non-selective β-blocker 30–60 min before speech.<br /></br>   • Second line: Benzodiazepines (short-term), MAOIs (phenelzine).</li></br></ol></br><p>No routine labs/imaging necessary unless ruling out medical mimickers (thyrotoxicosis, pheochromocytoma).</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Insight-oriented psychotherapy<br /></br>   • Uses free association to uncover unconscious conflicts; helpful for personality disorders, chronic relationship issues, not evidence-based first-line for social anxiety disorder.</p></br><p>C. Bupropion therapy<br /></br>   • Norepinephrine-dopamine reuptake inhibitor effective for depression and smoking cessation; minimal serotonergic activity → not first-line for anxiety disorders and can actually worsen anxiety.</p></br><p>D. Buspirone therapy<br /></br>   • 5-HT1A partial agonist approved for generalized anxiety disorder (worry about multiple domains for ≥ 6 months), not social anxiety disorder; onset 2–4 weeks, no utility for performance-specific fear.</p></br><p>E. Risperidone therapy<br /></br>   • Atypical antipsychotic used for schizophrenia, bipolar mania, severe agitation; high metabolic and extrapyramidal risk; no role as initial treatment for social anxiety disorder.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Students equate any “anxiety” with prescribing an anxiolytic (buspirone, benzodiazepine). Step exams expect recognition that specific phobias and social anxiety respond best to CBT-based exposure therapy as first-line.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Take-Home Message<br /></br>For social anxiety disorder—especially in young, otherwise healthy patients—the most appropriate initial management is cognitive-behavioral psychotherapy focused on graduated exposure and cognitive restructuring.</p>
44. A 27-year-old woman is brought to the emergency department by her sister because of a 3-month history of strange behavior. The sister says the patient intermittently mumbles to herself and has refused to leave the</br>apartment they share. The sister has not seen her eating during this time; yesterday, the patient disposed of all the food in the refrigerator because she insisted it was not safe to eat. She has no history of serious medical</br>or psychiatric illness and takes no medications. She does not drink alcohol or use illicit drugs. She appears distracted. She is 165 cm 5 ft 5 in tall and weighs 51 kg 112 lb; BMI is 19 kg/m 2. Physical examination shows</br>no abnormalities. On mental status examination, she describes her mood as OK. She has a flat affect. Her thought process is well organized and goal directed; there is no loosening of associations. She refuses to</br>answer when asked if she has had auditory hallucinations. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Anorexia nervosa</br>b. Avoidant personality disorder</br>c. Major depressive disorder</br>d. Obsessive-compulsive personality disorder</br>e. Schizophrenia</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Schizophrenia  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 3-month history of “strange behavior,” social withdrawal, refusing to leave apartment → prodromal/negative symptoms.<br /></br>• Refuses to eat because the food “is not safe to eat” → fixed, bizarre delusional belief (= positive symptom).<br /></br>• Intermittently “mumbles to herself,” appears distracted, will not answer questions about voices → strongly suggests auditory hallucinations (another positive symptom).<br /></br>• Flat affect on exam → negative symptom.<br /></br>• No substance use, no medical problems → organic/medication causes unlikely.<br /></br>• BMI 19 (normal) → weight loss not severe and no under-weight criterion for anorexia.  </p></br><p>Putting these findings together gives us ≥2 core psychotic symptoms (delusions + hallucinations) plus a negative symptom, fulfilling the active-phase criteria for a schizophrenia-spectrum disorder. Although the sister dates the problem to 3 months, we often do not know exactly when prodromal symptoms began; with multiple positive and negative findings in an adult and no better explanation, USMLE-style questions default to “schizophrenia” when schizophreniform is not offered.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Establish the presence of psychosis (delusions, hallucinations, disorganized/negative symptoms).<br /></br>2. Exclude secondary causes (drugs, medical illness).<br /></br>3. Consider duration. If options are limited to schizophrenia vs non-psychotic diagnoses, choose schizophrenia when psychotic symptoms last ≥1 month.  </p></br><p>Take-home message: A patient with clear psychotic symptoms, functional decline, and no medical or substance cause is most consistent with schizophrenia on shelf/Step exams when no “schizophreniform disorder” choice is provided.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Thought disorder linked to dopaminergic hyperactivity in mesolimbic pathways (positive symptoms) and hypoactivity in mesocortical pathways (negative symptoms).<br /></br>• Onset typically in young adulthood; stressors or genetic predisposition play roles.  </p></br><p>Treatment / Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• First-line pharmacotherapy: oral atypical antipsychotic (e.g., risperidone, olanzapine, aripiprazole).<br /></br>• If non-adherent: long-acting injectable formulations.<br /></br>• Psychosocial interventions: family education, CBT for psychosis, supported employment.<br /></br>• Rule out drugs, metabolic disorders (basic labs, urine tox) and obtain baseline metabolic profile before starting antipsychotics.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Anorexia nervosa<br /></br>   – Requires BMI &lt; 18.5 or weight &lt; 85 % expected PLUS body-image distortion or intense weight-gain fear. Patient’s BMI is 19 and delusional content is about contamination, not weight or shape.  </p></br><p>b. Avoidant personality disorder<br /></br>   – Characterized by lifelong social inhibition due to fear of rejection, NOT frank psychosis. No hallucinations, delusions, or flat affect are seen.  </p></br><p>c. Major depressive disorder<br /></br>   – Would show ≥2-week period of depressed mood or anhedonia plus vegetative/cognitive symptoms. Patient says mood is “OK,” has flat (not depressed) affect, and psychotic features are not mood-congruent.  </p></br><p>d. Obsessive-compulsive personality disorder<br /></br>   – Involves rigid perfectionism, orderliness, and control; no true obsessions/compulsions or psychosis. Disposal of food here is due to a fixed delusion, not an ego-dystonic obsession.  </p></br><p>Pitfall to avoid: Confusing approximate 3-month timeline with schizophreniform disorder. When that option is absent, schizophrenia is the next best diagnosis on NBME/Step 2 exams.</p>
45. A 67-year-old woman has had recurrent episodes of pain and stiffness in the hands and knees over the past year; the episodes occur almost daily. She has had no fevers or chills. A photograph of the hands is shown.</br>Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Degenerative osteoarthritis</br>b. Hyperparathyroidism</br>c. Polyarticular gout</br>d. Psoriatic arthritis</br>e. Rheumatoid arthritis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>A. Degenerative osteoarthritis  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age: 67 years – OA risk rises steadily with age; inflammatory arthritides (e.g., RA, psoriatic) can start earlier.<br /></br>• Joint distribution: “hands and knees.” In the hands, OA characteristically affects the distal interphalangeal (DIP) and proximal interphalangeal (PIP) joints, plus the 1st carpometacarpal (thumb-base); knees are the most common large joint site.<br /></br>• Chronic, non-systemic course: “recurrent episodes… almost daily… no fevers or chills.” OA is non-inflammatory, so systemic signs (fever, fatigue) are absent.<br /></br>• Photograph (classic on NBME): Heberden nodes (bony enlargement of DIPs) and possibly Bouchard nodes (PIPs) are visible—pathognomonic for OA.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Pattern recognition: Elderly patient + weight-bearing and DIP/PIP involvement + bony swellings + absence of systemic/inflammatory findings → degenerative OA.<br /></br>Core takeaway: The combination of age, mechanical joint pain, and characteristic bony nodules in the DIPs/PIPs clinches the diagnosis of osteoarthritis.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Progressive cartilage degeneration → subchondral bone remodeling and osteophyte formation.<br /></br>• Risk factors: Age, female sex (hand OA), repetitive joint use, obesity (knee/hip), prior joint trauma.<br /></br>• Mechanism of symptoms: Loss of articular cartilage causes joint-space narrowing, subchondral sclerosis, and osteophytes that manifest as Heberden (DIP) and Bouchard (PIP) nodes.<br /></br>• Clinical manifestations:<br /></br>  – Pain worsens with use, improves with rest (“evening” pain).<br /></br>  – Morning stiffness &lt;30 min.<br /></br>  – Crepitus, decreased range of motion.<br /></br>  – No extra-articular or systemic features.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Diagnosis is clinical; plain radiographs show joint-space narrowing, osteophytes, subchondral sclerosis/cysts.<br /></br>Management (step-wise):<br /></br>1. Non-pharmacologic: Weight loss, exercise/PT, assistive devices.<br /></br>2. Topical NSAIDs (diclofenac gel), topical capsaicin, oral NSAIDs (naproxen, ibuprofen) if no contraindication.<br /></br>3. Intra-articular corticosteroid or hyaluronic acid injections for flares.<br /></br>4. Surgical joint replacement for end-stage disease (e.g., total knee/hip arthroplasty).</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Hyperparathyroidism<br /></br>• Skeletal findings include subperiosteal bone resorption (especially radial aspect of middle phalanges), Brown tumors, “salt-and-pepper” skull—not Heberden/Bouchard nodes.<br /></br>• Presents with hypercalcemia symptoms (stones, bones, groans) rather than isolated mechanical joint pain.  </p></br><p>C. Polyarticular gout<br /></br>• Gouty tophi can appear on fingers, but gout classically starts as acute, episodic mono-/oligo-arthritis (1st MTP).<br /></br>• Severe inflammatory pain, redness, swelling; serum urate often elevated; tophi are chalky/whitish, not bony hard nodes at DIP.<br /></br>• Photograph in gout would show erythematous, swollen joints or whitish nodules, not hard bony enlargement.  </p></br><p>D. Psoriatic arthritis<br /></br>• Usually affects younger‐to‐middle-aged adults with known psoriasis (nail pitting, plaques).<br /></br>• Dactylitis (“sausage digits”), enthesitis, asymmetric oligoarthritis, sacroiliitis.<br /></br>• X-ray: “Pencil-in-cup” deformities, not osteophytes at DIP/PIP alone.<br /></br>• May involve DIPs, but inflammatory (warm, swollen) and accompanied by skin lesions.  </p></br><p>E. Rheumatoid arthritis<br /></br>• Symmetric inflammatory polyarthritis involving MCPs, PIPs; spares DIPs.<br /></br>• Morning stiffness &gt;1 h, systemic symptoms (fatigue, low-grade fever), rheumatoid nodules.<br /></br>• Radiographs: periarticular osteopenia, marginal erosions—not bony hypertrophy/osteophytes.<br /></br>• Photograph would show ulnar deviation or swan-neck/boutonnière deformities, not isolated Heberden nodes.</p></br><p>Key Differences Summarized<br /></br>• OA: Elderly, mechanical pain, DIP/PIP nodes (osteophytes), no systemic symptoms.<br /></br>• RA/Psoriatic: Inflammatory, symmetric with stiffness &gt;1 h, systemic/skin findings.<br /></br>• Gout: Acute, intensely inflammatory attacks, 1st MTP common.<br /></br>• Hyperparathyroidism: Metabolic bone disease with hypercalcemia signs, distinct radiographic features.</p>
46. A 47-year-old man is admitted to the hospital for repair of a recurrent right inguinal hernia. One year ago, he underwent bilateral inguinal hernia repair. He has hypertension well controlled with atenolol. Cardiopulmonary</br>examination shows no abnormalities. There are well-healed surgical scars over the inguinal area bilaterally. The testes are descended, and a mass is palpated in the right groin on Valsalva maneuver. During the operation,</br>the surgeon operates on the left groin. Which of the following is the most effective process to prevent this type of error in the future?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Having the patient's chart available in the operating room</br>b. Presence of the surgeon in the operating room at the time of anesthesia induction</br>c. Preoperative marking of the operative site by the patient</br>d. Preoperative identification of the operative site by the nurse</br>e. Preoperative marking of the operative site by the surgeon with the patient's assistance</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Preoperative marking of the operative site by the surgeon with the patient’s assistance</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key phrase: “the surgeon operates on the left groin” – classic example of wrong-site surgery.<br /></br>• The stem asks for the single “most effective process” to prevent this specific sentinel event in the future.<br /></br>• This immediately calls to mind the Joint Commission’s Universal Protocol, especially the requirement for surgeon-performed site marking before the patient is taken to the OR.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Wrong-site, wrong-procedure, and wrong-patient surgeries are rare but devastating, entirely preventable errors. Evidence shows the greatest reduction in these events comes from:<br /></br>1) Pre-procedure verification of the planned operation.<br /></br>2) Site marking by the operating surgeon while the awake patient actively confirms the correct site.<br /></br>3) A “time-out” immediately before incision with the entire team.<br /></br>The surgeon’s personal site marking—initials or “YES” with a surgical-grade marker—creates direct accountability, eliminates ambiguity, and allows the patient to participate. This intervention is superior to charts, nurses, or the patient alone because it links the person actually performing the incision (the surgeon) with the verified, conscious patient.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Not applicable; the question tests patient-safety systems rather than disease mechanisms, but the core concept is prevention of “never events” through standardized human-factor engineering.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Universal Protocol (safety “treatment”) components:<br /></br>• Pre-procedure verification checklist (ID band, consent, imaging, implants, lab results).<br /></br>• Site marking by the surgeon with patient involvement before sedation.<br /></br>• Final “time-out” (correct patient, procedure, site, positioning, implants, antibiotics) with all team members, including anesthesia and nursing, verbally agreeing.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Having the patient’s chart available in the operating room<br /></br>   – Necessary but insufficient. Charts are often present yet wrong-site events still occur. They lack direct, visible confirmation on the patient’s body.</p></br><p>b. Presence of the surgeon in the OR at the time of anesthesia induction<br /></br>   – The surgeon can still operate on the wrong side once the patient is draped. Presence alone does not guarantee verification.</p></br><p>c. Preoperative marking of the operative site by the patient<br /></br>   – Patients may mis-mark, have limited mobility, or the mark may wash off. Joint Commission specifically requires the mark be placed by the licensed independent practitioner who will perform the operation.</p></br><p>d. Preoperative identification of the operative site by the nurse<br /></br>   – Nursing verification is a valuable cross-check but does not replace surgeon responsibility. Wrong-site cases have occurred despite nurse documentation.</p></br><p>Take-home Message<br /></br>The single most effective safeguard against wrong-site surgery is: the operating surgeon personally marks the correct site while the awake patient confirms it, followed by a team time-out.</p>
47. A previously healthy 3-year-old boy is brought to the physician by his mother because of a 3-week history of foul-smelling progressive diarrhea and gas. He has not had fever. He began attending day care 2 months ago.</br>Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows dry mucous membranes and mild abdominal distention. Which of the following is the most likely causal organism?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Entamoeba histolytica</br>b. Escherichia coli</br>c. Giardia lamblia</br>d. Salmonella enteritidis</br>e. Shigella sonnei</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Giardia lamblia  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “3-year-old” + “began attending day care”: day-care centers are a classic exposure for Giardia (fecal–oral spread from contaminated hands, toys, water).<br /></br>• “3-week history” of “foul-smelling progressive diarrhea and gas”: greasy, malodorous, chronically progressive diarrhea with excessive flatus is textbook for giardiasis (fat malabsorption → steatorrhea).<br /></br>• “No fever”: most invasive bacterial enteritides (Shigella, Salmonella, EHEC) and Entamoeba histolytica usually produce fever and/or dysentery (bloody diarrhea). Afebrile course supports a non-invasive protozoan such as Giardia.<br /></br>• “Dry mucous membranes” &amp; “mild abdominal distention”: signs of dehydration plus bloating from malabsorption—again typical of Giardia.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Pattern: daycare/stream water + afebrile, foul-smelling, fatty stools → think Giardia.<br /></br>Pitfall: confusing with Entamoeba histolytica; however, E. histolytica causes bloody stools and right-upper-quadrant pain (liver abscess risk), not foul steatorrhea.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Giardia lamblia is a flagellated protozoan.<br /></br>• Transmission: cysts in fecally contaminated water, food, or person-to-person in daycare.<br /></br>• Trophozoites adhere to duodenal/jejunal mucosa → brush-border damage → fat malabsorption → greasy, bulky stools, bloating, weight loss.<br /></br>• No mucosal invasion → minimal/no fever, no blood.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Diagnosis: stool antigen assay (most sensitive) or stool ova &amp; parasite exam showing pear-shaped trophozoites with ventral sucking disk.<br /></br>First-line therapy: Metronidazole 5-7 days (alternatives: tinidazole, nitazoxanide).<br /></br>Preventive: hand hygiene, safe water (boil/filter).</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Entamoeba histolytica<br /></br>   – Presentation: bloody diarrhea, abdominal pain, possible fever, liver abscess (“anchovy-paste” pus).<br /></br>   – Invasive ulceration of colon; not foul steatorrhea.  </p></br><p>b. Escherichia coli<br /></br>   – Enterotoxigenic (ETEC) = watery traveler’s diarrhea; Enterohemorrhagic (EHEC) = bloody diarrhea ± HUS; both usually shorter course (days), not chronic steatorrhea, and often associated with contaminated food, not daycare.  </p></br><p>d. Salmonella enteritidis<br /></br>   – Inflammatory diarrhea, often febrile, may have vomiting; duration typically &lt;1 week; acquired from poultry/eggs, not classically daycare.  </p></br><p>e. Shigella sonnei<br /></br>   – High fever, crampy abdominal pain, watery → bloody/mucoid diarrhea; seizures possible in young children; usually acute, not 3-week foul steatorrhea.  </p></br><p>Take-Home Message<br /></br>Chronic foul-smelling, greasy diarrhea without fever in a daycare child = Giardia lamblia infection; think “G = Greasy, Giardia, day-care Gatherings.”</p>
48. A 12-year-old girl is brought to the physician by her mother because of a 1-month history of a lump in her right breast. The lump is not painful, and she has not had nipple discharge. She has no history of serious illness</br>and takes no medications. She is at the 25th percentile for height and 50th percentile for weight and BMI. Examination shows no axillary lymphadenopathy. There is a 1 x 1-cm, smooth, rubbery, mobile mass beneath the</br>areola in the right breast. Examination of the left breast shows no abnormalities. There are no skin changes or nipple discharge. Breast development is sexual maturity rating SMR stage 2 on the right and SMR stage 1</br>on the left. Pubic hair development is SMR stage 2. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Follow-up examination in 6 months</br>b. Measurement of serum prolactin concentration</br>c. Ultrasonography of the right breast</br>d. CT scan of the head</br>e. Fine-needle aspiration of the mass</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>A. Follow-up examination in 6 months  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age 12, Tanner (SMR) 2 pubic hair = early puberty.<br /></br>• “1-cm smooth, rubbery, mobile mass beneath the areola” = classic description of a unilateral breast bud.<br /></br>• Unilateral breast development (stage 2 on right, 1 on left) is very common in the first 6–12 months of puberty.<br /></br>• No red-flag findings: no pain, discharge, skin changes, axillary nodes, rapid growth, or size &gt;2 cm.<br /></br>These clues point to physiologic pubertal breast development rather than a pathologic lesion.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Stepwise approach to a breast lump in an adolescent:<br /></br>1. Is she in puberty? Yes (Tanner 2).<br /></br>2. Is the lump subareolar, &lt;2 cm, rubbery, mobile, non-tender? Yes → most likely normal breast bud.<br /></br>3. Any worrisome features requiring imaging/biopsy? None.<br /></br>Therefore reassurance and re-exam after puberty has progressed is appropriate. Core takeaway: In early-pubertal girls, unilateral asymmetric breast budding is physiologic; observe, don’t over-test.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Estrogen at the onset of puberty stimulates ductal and stromal proliferation. This process can begin unilaterally and may be palpable as a small firm “breast bud.” As the contralateral breast catches up, symmetry returns. The gland is mobile because it is not fixed to surrounding tissue, and the overlying skin remains normal.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Management:<br /></br>• Reassurance + scheduled follow-up (typically 4–6 months, or sooner if rapid growth/pain develops).<br /></br>• Imaging (ultrasound) reserved for masses that are enlarging, &gt;2 cm, eccentric to areola, fixed, or associated with adenopathy/skin changes.<br /></br>• Biopsy/FNA only if imaging is suspicious or there is high clinical concern. No medications are indicated.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Serum prolactin – Indicated for galactorrhea or amenorrhea suggesting pituitary disease; this patient has no nipple discharge or menstrual abnormalities.<br /></br>C. Ultrasonography – First-line imaging for suspicious or persistent breast masses, but not needed for a classic breast bud (&lt;2 cm, central, early puberty, benign exam).<br /></br>D. CT scan of the head – Used to evaluate a pituitary tumor when hyperprolactinemia symptoms are present; not relevant here.<br /></br>E. Fine-needle aspiration – Invasive; reserved for solid masses with concerning features or persistent growth despite observation. It is unnecessary and potentially harmful in a normal developing breast bud.</p></br><p>Bottom line: In an early-pubertal girl with a small, subareolar, mobile mass and no worrisome features, simple reassurance and re-examination in about 6 months is the correct, high-value care.</p>
49. A 42-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 6-month history of cough productive of thick, white, mucopurulent sputum. The cough frequently keeps him awake at night. He has not had fever or chills but has</br>had an unintentional 4.5-kg 10-lb weight loss during the past 3 years. Each winter during the past 15 years, he has had recurrent episodes of bronchitis treated with antibiotic therapy. During childhood, he had four</br>episodes of double pneumonia. He emigrated from the Philippines 10 years ago. He smoked one-half pack of cigarettes daily for 5 years but quit 15 years ago. He is not in distress. He is 183 cm 6 ft tall and weighs</br>68 kg 150 lb; BMI is 20 kg/m 2. His pulse is 68/min, respirations are 14/min, and blood pressure is 132/81 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 95 percent. On pulmonary examination, coarse</br>rhonchi are heard best at the left lung base. There is bilateral inspiratory and faint expiratory wheezing in both lung bases. Cardiac examination shows no abnormalities. A chest x-ray is shown. Which of the following is</br>the greatest predisposing risk factor for this patient's condition?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Sex</br>b. History of respiratory tract infections</br>c. Low BMI</br>d. Smoking history</br>e. Travel history</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>B. History of respiratory tract infections  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “6-month history of cough productive of thick, white, mucopurulent sputum … cough frequently keeps him awake at night” → chronic, large-volume, purulent sputum = classic for bronchiectasis.<br /></br>• “Recurrent episodes of bronchitis every winter for 15 years” + “four episodes of double pneumonia in childhood” → repeated infections strong clue.<br /></br>• Auscultation: “coarse rhonchi … bilateral inspiratory and faint expiratory wheezing” → airflow obstruction with retained secretions.<br /></br>• CXR (not shown to us on the exam) in bronchiectasis typically demonstrates tram-track or ring-shadow (“signet-ring”) opacities.<br /></br>Pattern recognition: chronic purulent cough + recurrent infections + characteristic imaging = bronchiectasis.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Bronchiectasis develops after a cycle of infection → inflammation → destruction &amp; permanent dilation of bronchi → impaired mucociliary clearance → more infection. The single most important factor that sets this vicious cycle in motion is severe or repeated lower-respiratory infections, especially in childhood.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Recurrent or severe pulmonary infections are the greatest predisposing factor for non-CF bronchiectasis in adults.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>1. Etiologies<br /></br>   • Post-infectious (≈50% worldwide): bacterial pneumonias, pertussis, measles, TB.<br /></br>   • Cystic fibrosis, primary ciliary dyskinesia, immunodeficiency, allergic bronchopulmonary aspergillosis, obstruction (tumor, foreign body).<br /></br>2. Mechanism<br /></br>   Infection/inflammation damages elastic &amp; muscular components of bronchi → dilated, thick-walled bronchi that pool secretions.<br /></br>3. Clinical manifestations<br /></br>   Persistent productive cough, large amounts of purulent sputum, hemoptysis, crackles/rhonchi, wheeze, and recurrent exacerbations.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics (high-yield)<br /></br>• High-resolution CT = test of choice.<br /></br>• Airway clearance: chest physiotherapy, hypertonic saline, oscillatory PEP devices.<br /></br>• Exacerbations: culture-directed antibiotics (e.g., levofloxacin, piperacillin-tazobactam).<br /></br>• Maintenance: macrolides (azithromycin) for anti-inflammatory &amp; antimicrobial effect.<br /></br>• Vaccinations (influenza, pneumococcal), bronchodilators if coexistent obstructive disease.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>A. Sex<br /></br>  – Bronchiectasis is slightly more common in women, but sex alone is not a major causal factor. The question asks for the “greatest predisposing risk factor”; past infections have far stronger evidence.  </p></br><p>C. Low BMI<br /></br>  – Low BMI can be a consequence of chronic lung disease (weight loss from increased work of breathing) rather than a cause. It does not initiate bronchiectasis.  </p></br><p>D. Smoking history<br /></br>  – Only 5 pack-years, remote; smoking is tightly linked to chronic bronchitis and COPD, not to the airway wall destruction seen in bronchiectasis. Most patients with bronchiectasis are never-smokers.  </p></br><p>E. Travel history (Philippines)<br /></br>  – TB can cause focal bronchiectasis, but the stem emphasizes lifelong, diffuse recurrent infections, not a single cavitary TB focus. His emigration 10 years ago is less compelling than infections dating back to childhood.  </p></br><p>Key difference: only one option (history of recurrent respiratory infections) directly initiates the pathophysiologic cascade leading to diffuse bronchiectasis.</p>
50. A previously healthy 22-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 2-month history of intermittent moderate headache. She is otherwise asymptomatic. She takes no medications. She has smoked one pack of</br>cigarettes daily for 4 years. Her temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 64/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 156/90 mm Hg. Cardiopulmonary examination shows no abnormalities. On abdominal</br>examination, a bruit is heard in the left upper quadrant. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Serum studies show:</br>Which of the following additional serum findings is most likely to be increased in this patient?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Angiotensin 11 concentration</br>b. Angiotensin-converting enzyme activity</br>c. Angiotensinogen concentration</br>d. Brain natriuretic peptide concentration</br>e. Prostaglandin E2 concentration</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Angiotensin II concentration ↑</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Young woman (22 y) with new-onset hypertension → primary essential HTN is uncommon at this age, so think secondary causes.<br /></br>• LUQ abdominal bruit → classically points to renal artery bruit from renal artery stenosis.<br /></br>• Otherwise healthy, minimal cardiovascular findings → favors fibromuscular dysplasia (young female, “string-of-beads” renal artery).  </p></br><p>Key pattern: Young woman + HTN + flank/upper-abdominal bruit = renal artery stenosis → activates the renin-angiotensin-aldosterone system (RAAS).</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Renal artery stenosis ↓ renal perfusion pressure → JG cells release renin → renin converts angiotensinogen → angiotensin I → ACE converts to angiotensin II.<br /></br>Angiotensin II:<br /></br>• Potent systemic vasoconstrictor (raises BP).<br /></br>• Stimulates aldosterone secretion (↑ Na⁺/water retention).<br /></br>Therefore serum angiotensin II (and renin, aldosterone) are all increased.</p></br><p>Take-home concept: Secondary hyperaldosteronism from renovascular HTN = ↑ renin → ↑ angiotensin II → ↑ aldosterone.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Etiology:<br /></br>• In older men → atherosclerotic plaque.<br /></br>• In young women → fibromuscular dysplasia (medial fibroplasia → “string-of-beads”).<br /></br>Mechanism: Reduced perfusion pressure sensed by afferent arteriole → activates RAAS.<br /></br>Clinical manifestations: Resistant HTN, abdominal/flank bruit, possible hypokalemic alkalosis if severe.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatments / Diagnostics (high-yield)<br /></br>Diagnostic tests:<br /></br>• Duplex renal Doppler ultrasound (screen).<br /></br>• CT or MR angiography, or catheter-based angiography (gold standard).<br /></br>Management:<br /></br>• Percutaneous transluminal angioplasty with or without stent (especially FMD).<br /></br>• ACE inhibitors/ARBs (contraindicated if bilateral stenosis or solitary kidney).<br /></br>• Smoking cessation (disease progression).</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) activity – Usually unchanged. ACE is constitutively produced by pulmonary endothelial cells; its activity is not the rate-limiting step in RAAS activation during renovascular HTN.<br /></br>c. Angiotensinogen concentration – Synthesized by the liver; levels rise with corticosteroids, estrogens, inflammation, but not directly with renal hypoperfusion.<br /></br>d. Brain natriuretic peptide (BNP) concentration – Elevated in ventricular volume/pressure overload (e.g., heart failure). Patient has no dyspnea, edema, S3, or other HF signs.<br /></br>e. Prostaglandin E2 concentration – Kidney produces PGE₂ for afferent arteriolar dilation; systemic PGE₂ is not typically elevated in renal artery stenosis and may actually fall with NSAID use, not relevant here.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Renal artery stenosis → ↑ renin → ↑ angiotensin II (correct answer) → secondary hyperaldosteronism and hypertension.</p>
51. A 57-year-old man, who is an accountant, comes to the physician because of progressive shortness of breath during the past 6 months. He says he has difficulty breathing after walking more than one city block and when</br>walking up one flight of stairs. He reports that during these episodes, he feels that he needs to breathe rapidly. He also has had a dry cough. He has not had chest pain, diaphoresis, nocturnal symptoms, or worsening of the</br>symptoms when lying down. He has a 5-year history of hypertension treated with enalapril and hydrochlorothiazide. He has never smoked cigarettes. His temperature is 37 C 98.6  F, pulse is 89/min, respirations are</br>18/min, and blood pressure is 145/82 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 90 percent. Crackles are heard at both lung bases. A high-resolution CT scan of the chest shows a reticulogranular</br>appearance at the bases bilaterally with minimal fibrosis. Pulmonary function tests show moderately severe decreased diffusion capacity. Examination of bronchoalveolar lavage fluid shows many segmented neutrophils that</br>are negative for bacteria, fungi, and acid-fast bacilli. In addition to supplemental oxygen, administration of which of the following is the most appropriate next step?	Correct answer: F</br></br>a. Albuterol</br>b. Aminophylline</br>c. Colchicine</br>d. Cyclophosphamide</br>e. Cyclosporine</br>F. Prednisone</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>F. Prednisone  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Progressive exertional dyspnea + chronic non-productive cough → think interstitial lung disease (ILD).<br /></br>• Bibasilar “crackles” (Velcro-like) + high-resolution CT (HRCT) showing bilateral basilar reticulogranular infiltrates with early/minimal fibrosis → classic for idiopathic pulmonary fibrosis (IPF, a usual interstitial pneumonia pattern).<br /></br>• PFT: markedly ↓ DLCO and a restrictive pattern are typical for IPF.<br /></br>• BAL: Numerous neutrophils, but cultures negative → sterile “alveolitis” frequently seen in IPF (neutrophil-predominant BAL differentiates it from eosinophilic or sarcoid processes).<br /></br>These data together point to idiopathic pulmonary fibrosis.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Goals in IPF: 1) slow or blunt the inflammatory alveolar injury that leads to fibrosis, 2) improve oxygenation.  Supplemental O₂ addresses hypoxemia; the next pharmacologic step is an anti-inflammatory agent.  Corticosteroids (e.g., prednisone) have historically been first-line to reduce the neutrophilic/alveolar inflammation that precedes irreversible fibrosis.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: For newly diagnosed idiopathic pulmonary fibrosis, the traditional NBME-tested therapy after oxygen is systemic glucocorticoids (prednisone).</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Repeated, unknown epithelial injury → aberrant wound healing → fibroblast proliferation &amp; collagen deposition.<br /></br>• Early phase: inflammatory alveolitis (neutrophils on BAL) → potentially steroid-responsive.<br /></br>• Late phase: dense fibrosis, honeycombing → poor steroid response, irreversible loss of lung compliance.<br /></br>• Clinical manifestations: progressive DOE, dry cough, bibasilar fine crackles, clubbing (late), restrictive PFT, ↓ DLCO.</p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>First-line/early treatment (NBME tradition):<br /></br>1. Supplemental O₂ for hypoxemia.<br /></br>2. Oral prednisone (0.5–1 mg/kg/day) ± a steroid-sparing agent (azathioprine) in some protocols.<br /></br>More current (but not reflected in many NBME questions): antifibrotic agents nintedanib or pirfenidone for chronic IPF.<br /></br>Other supportive measures: pulmonary rehab, vaccinations, eventual lung transplant in advanced disease.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>A. Albuterol – A short-acting β₂-agonist relieves bronchospasm in obstructive diseases (asthma, COPD).  IPF is restrictive; no wheezing or reversible airway obstruction.<br /></br>B. Aminophylline – Methylxanthine bronchodilator; same rationale as albuterol—ineffective for restrictive parenchymal disease.<br /></br>C. Colchicine – Occasionally studied but not proven; not standard or first-line and far less effective than corticosteroids for inflammatory alveolitis.<br /></br>D. Cyclophosphamide – Cytotoxic immunosuppressant reserved for severe connective-tissue–related ILD or steroid-refractory vasculitic lung disease; significant toxicity, not initial therapy for idiopathic disease.<br /></br>E. Cyclosporine – Calcineurin inhibitor sometimes used in steroid-refractory sarcoidosis or certain CTD-ILDs; no role as first-line in idiopathic pulmonary fibrosis.</p></br><p>Key Differences: Only prednisone directly targets the early inflammatory component characteristic of IPF and is the accepted NBME answer for first pharmacologic management beyond oxygen.</p>
52. A 57-year-old man comes to the physician because of severe right shoulder pain since having a generalized tonic-clonic seizure 3 days ago. He first noted the pain in the emergency department, where he was brought after</br>the seizure. He reports no other history of shoulder pain or injury. He has a 40-year history of a seizure disorder treated with phenytoin; his only other medication is acetaminophen for shoulder pain. On examination, passive</br>and active range of motion of the right shoulder elicits pain. External rotation of the right shoulder is limited to 10 degrees. An AP x-ray of the shoulder obtained in the emergency department 3 days ago showed no</br>abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Observation only</br>b. Lateral x-ray of the right shoulder _______,</br>c. Arthrography</br>d. Physical therapy</br>e. MRI of the right shoulder</br>F. Electromyography and nerve conduction studies</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Obtain a lateral (axillary or scapular-Y) x-ray of the right shoulder  </p></br><hr /></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues<br /></br>  – “Severe right shoulder pain since having a generalized tonic-clonic seizure” – seizures and electrical shocks are classic precipitants of posterior shoulder dislocation.<br /></br>  – “External rotation … limited to 10°” – patients with posterior dislocation hold the arm adducted and internally rotated; they cannot externally rotate.<br /></br>  – “AP x-ray … showed no abnormalities” – posterior dislocations are frequently missed on the anteroposterior view; additional orthogonal views are mandatory if clinical suspicion remains high.  </p></br><hr /></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Pattern recognition: Shoulder pain after a tonic-clonic seizure + markedly decreased external rotation = posterior shoulder dislocation until proven otherwise.<br /></br>Pitfall: Relying on a normal AP film—up to 50% of posterior dislocations are not seen on that view.<br /></br>Next step: Obtain an axillary or scapular-Y (lateral) view to visualize the glenohumeral relationship.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Always get orthogonal (“two-view”) imaging of the shoulder when the mechanism or exam suggests dislocation, especially posterior dislocation after seizure/electric shock.  </p></br><hr /></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Posterior shoulder dislocation<br /></br>• Etiology: Violent internal rotation/adduction produced by seizures or electrical injury; less commonly trauma (dashboard injury).<br /></br>• Anatomy: Humeral head is displaced posteriorly behind the glenoid.<br /></br>• Clinical findings: Arm held in adduction/internal rotation, flattened anterior shoulder, prominent coracoid, severe pain, inability to externally rotate or abduct.<br /></br>• Imaging:<br /></br>  – AP: May appear “normal” or show light-bulb sign (humeral head internally rotated).<br /></br>  – Axillary or scapular-Y: Confirms posterior displacement.  </p></br><hr /></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Diagnostic hierarchy<br /></br>1. Confirm diagnosis with proper x-ray views (axillary or scapular-Y).<br /></br>2. Once confirmed: closed reduction under conscious sedation, post-reduction neurovascular exam and repeat films, followed by sling and early physical therapy.<br /></br>3. Persistent instability or large reverse Hill-Sachs lesions → orthopedic referral for surgical management.  </p></br><hr /></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Observation only<br /></br>   – Inappropriate; missing a posterior dislocation risks avascular necrosis and chronic instability.  </p></br><p>c. Arthrography<br /></br>   – Used for labral or rotator cuff pathology when routine x-ray is normal and MRI is contraindicated; not first-line when dislocation is suspected.  </p></br><p>d. Physical therapy<br /></br>   – Therapy without a definite diagnosis could worsen an unreduced dislocation and delay definitive care.  </p></br><p>e. MRI of the right shoulder<br /></br>   – Excellent for soft-tissue injuries but unnecessary before obtaining proper plain films; more costly and less readily available in the acute setting.  </p></br><p>f. Electromyography and nerve conduction studies<br /></br>   – Evaluate peripheral nerve injury, not indicated for acute shoulder trauma with suspected dislocation.  </p></br><hr /></br><p>High-Yield Take-Home Message<br /></br>After seizures or electrical shocks, always suspect posterior shoulder dislocation. A normal AP film does NOT rule it out—order an axillary or scapular-Y lateral x-ray to confirm the diagnosis.</p>
53. A 72-year-old man with hypertension comes to the office because of a 7-month history of postvoid dribbling and urinary incontinence associated with urgent, unexpected sensations of the need to urinate. His medications</br>are lisinopril and aspirin. His blood pressure is 160/80 mm Hg. Examination shows normal external genitalia and a moderately enlarged, firm prostate. His postvoid residual volume is 200 ml. Which of the following is the</br>most likely cause of this patient's urinary incontinence?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Bladder outlet obstruction</br>b. Detrusor instability</br>c. Detrusor underactivity</br>d. Functional incontinence</br>e. Urinary tract infection</br>F. Weak external urethral sphincter</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>A. Bladder outlet obstruction (most commonly from benign prostatic hyperplasia, BPH)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “72-year-old man” – advancing age → strong risk factor for BPH.<br /></br>• “Moderately enlarged, firm prostate” – classic physical finding of BPH.<br /></br>• “Post-void dribbling” + “postvoid residual 200 mL” – incomplete bladder emptying; overflow component. Normal PVR is &lt;50 mL (definitely &lt;100 mL).<br /></br>• “Urgent, unexpected sensations of the need to urinate” – urgency arises because chronic retention stretches the detrusor, triggering uninhibited contractions.<br /></br>Together, these data point to mechanical obstruction of the urethra by the prostate → overflow with secondary urgency.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (why A is correct)<br /></br>1. BPH narrows the prostatic urethra → high outlet resistance.<br /></br>2. Patient voids incompletely, leaving a large residual volume (200 mL).<br /></br>3. Bladder chronically overdistended → intermittent leakage (post-void dribble) and reflex detrusor contractions perceived as urgency.<br /></br>Core takeaway: In an older man with enlarged prostate + high PVR, the most likely etiology of mixed urgency/overflow incontinence is bladder outlet obstruction from BPH.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Hyperplasia of periurethral (transitional zone) prostate glands driven by DHT.<br /></br>• Obstruction → ↑ bladder pressure → detrusor muscle hypertrophy → eventual decompensation → overflow incontinence.<br /></br>• Secondary detrusor overactivity causes urgency symptoms.  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>1. First line: α1-blockers (tamsulosin, terazosin) to relax smooth muscle.<br /></br>2. 5-α-reductase inhibitors (finasteride, dutasteride) shrink prostate over months.<br /></br>3. Refractory cases: TURP, laser ablation.<br /></br>4. Acute retention/overflow: prompt bladder catheterization.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Detrusor instability (overactive bladder)<br /></br>• Produces urgency, frequency, small-volume leakage, but PVR is low (&lt;50 mL).<br /></br>• No post-void dribbling; prostate exam often normal.</p></br><p>C.
