Detrusor underactivity (neurogenic/atonic bladder)<br /></br>• Also yields high PVR and overflow, yet usually painless retention, minimal urgency.<br /></br>• Seen with diabetic autonomic neuropathy, spinal cord lesions, medications (e.g., anticholinergics), not isolated prostate enlargement.</p></br><p>D. Functional incontinence<br /></br>• Normal lower urinary tract; patient cannot reach toilet due to cognitive/mobility issues.<br /></br>• PVR normal; exam would not emphasize prostate size.</p></br><p>E. Urinary tract infection<br /></br>• Dysuria, suprapubic pain, hematuria, pyuria on UA.<br /></br>• Causes urgency/frequency but PVR remains low; prostate enlargement unexplained.</p></br><p>F. Weak external urethral sphincter (stress incontinence)<br /></br>• Presents with leakage during cough, laugh, Valsalva; common in multiparous women, post-prostatectomy men.<br /></br>• PVR low; urgency and dribbling after void absent.</p></br><p>Bottom Line<br /></br>An enlarged prostate causing bladder outlet obstruction best explains the triad of urgency, high post-void residual, and post-void dribbling in this elderly male.</p>
54. A 37-year-old woman comes to the physician for a routine health maintenance examination. She feels well. She has no history of serious illness and no known allergies. She takes no medications. On examination, a 1.5-cm</br>nodule over the right aspect of the thyroid gland is palpated. There is no lymphadenopathy. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. In addition to measurement of serum thyroid-stimulating hormone</br>concentration, which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: F</br></br>a. Determination of erythrocyte sedimentation rate</br>b. Measurement of serum calcitonin concentration</br>c. Measurement of serum lactate dehydrogenase activity</br>d. Thyroglobulin assay</br>e. 131 1 thyroid scan</br>F. Ultrasonography of the thyroid gland</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>F. Ultrasonography of the thyroid gland  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Palpated 1.5-cm nodule” – any discrete thyroid nodule ≥1 cm warrants further anatomic characterization.<br /></br>• “No systemic symptoms, no lymphadenopathy, routine visit” – incidentally discovered, euthyroid-appearing patient.<br /></br>• “TSH to be measured” – guideline-directed first test to assess function. Once ordered, the next guideline-directed step is high-resolution neck ultrasonography.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise evaluation of a thyroid nodule:<br /></br>1. Measure serum TSH.<br /></br>2. Obtain a high-resolution ultrasound of the thyroid and cervical lymph nodes.<br /></br>   – Determines solid vs cystic, microcalcifications, margins, vascularity, extrathyroidal extension, and exact size.<br /></br>   – Stratifies malignancy risk and decides whether/where to perform ultrasound-guided fine-needle aspiration (FNA).<br /></br>Only after ultrasound do we consider a radioiodine scan—and only if the TSH is suppressed (to distinguish autonomously functioning “hot” nodules from “cold” nodules that need FNA).</p></br><p>Take-home: Ultrasound is the indispensable imaging study after TSH in every newly discovered thyroid nodule.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Thyroid nodules are common (4–7 % of adults by palpation, up to 60 % by US). Most are benign colloid nodules or cysts; 5–10 % harbor carcinoma (papillary most common). Ultrasound allows risk stratification:<br /></br>• High-risk features – hypoechoic solid lesion, irregular or infiltrative margins, microcalcifications, taller-than-wide shape, cervical nodes.<br /></br>• Low-risk – purely cystic or spongiform appearance.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>• If TSH low → next test is radionuclide scan; hyperfunctioning (“hot”) nodules rarely malignant → no FNA.<br /></br>• If TSH normal/high → proceed directly to ultrasound ± FNA (≥1 cm w/ high-risk US features or ≥1.5–2 cm if low-risk).<br /></br>• Definitive therapy based on cytology: benign (observe), malignant or suspicious (surgery ± RAI), indeterminate (repeat FNA, molecular testing, or diagnostic lobectomy).</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>A. Erythrocyte sedimentation rate – Used for inflammatory thyroiditis (e.g., subacute De Quervain) presenting with painful, tender thyroid and systemic symptoms—none present here.<br /></br>B. Serum calcitonin – Screening for medullary thyroid carcinoma is not recommended for every nodule; reserved for patients with MEN 2 risk or suspicious ultrasound/cytology findings.<br /></br>C. Serum lactate dehydrogenase – Marker for tumor lysis or testicular lymphoma; not useful in thyroid disease.<br /></br>D. Thyroglobulin assay – Monitors differentiated thyroid cancer after thyroidectomy; baseline levels do not help differentiate benign from malignant nodules.<br /></br>E. 131-I thyroid scan – Appropriate only if TSH is suppressed (concerns about toxic adenoma or multinodular goiter). With a normal/unknown TSH, ultrasound comes first.<br /></br>F. Ultrasonography of the thyroid gland – Correct because it is the standard next diagnostic step for all palpable thyroid nodules after ordering TSH.</p>
55. Five days after undergoing abdominal hysterectomy for leiomyomata uteri, a 57-year-old woman is evaluated because of abscess formation and fascial dehiscence at the incision site. She is 165 cm 5 ft 5 in tall and</br>weighs 73 kg 162 lb; BMI is 27 kg/m 2. She received perioperative prophylaxis with an appropriate antibiotic 90 minutes after the surgical incision was made. Hospital recommendations specify that patients should receive</br>antibiotic prophylaxis within 1 hour before the surgical incision is made. After admission to the hospital, patients are transferred to the preoperative holding area 30 to 90 minutes before their operation and then transferred</br>to the operating room for induction of anesthesia. A patient's preoperative antibiotic prophylaxis may be administered by a nurse on admission or in the preoperative holding area or by the anesthesiologist during the</br>induction of anesthesia. Which of the following is the most likely root cause of this error?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Lack of standardized delivery and verification of delivery of antibiotic</br>b. Lack of standardized order sets for antibiotic selection</br>c. Surgeon error</br>d. Surgical nursing error in delivery of antibiotic</br>e. Unavoidable complication with no root cause</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Lack of standardized delivery and verification of delivery of antibiotic  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Received peri-operative prophylaxis … 90 minutes after the surgical incision was made” = antibiotic given TOO LATE.<br /></br>• Hospital policy: “within 1 hour before incision.”<br /></br>• Multiple potential administration sites (“on admission,” “holding area,” or “by the anesthesiologist”) = fragmented process with no single check point.<br /></br>• No indication that the wrong drug/dose was chosen—only the timing is wrong.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>On the exam, questions that use “root-cause” language are testing knowledge of system-based practice. The pattern here is:<br /></br>1. Clear guideline (antibiotic ≤60 min pre-cut).<br /></br>2. Actual care deviates because the process is not standardized; responsibility is diffused.<br /></br>3. Outcome = surgical-site infection (abscess, fascial dehiscence).<br /></br>The core takeaway: Whenever several hand-offs can occur, lack of a SINGLE standardized protocol and time-out/verification step is the commonest root cause of timing errors.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (brief)<br /></br>• Pre-incisional cefazolin (or agent active vs skin flora) achieves high tissue levels at time of incision → prevents colonization of wound.<br /></br>• Delayed antibiotic dosing → bacterial inoculum during incision → postop infection/abscess → fascial dehiscence.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>• Prevention: create standardized order set + checklist: “antibiotic given? time?” verified during WHO Surgical Safety Checklist “Time-Out.”<br /></br>• Treatment now: broad-spectrum IV antibiotics (e.g., piperacillin-tazobactam) + operative drainage/closure of fascia.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Lack of standardized order sets for antibiotic SELECTION<br /></br>– Order sets determine the CORRECT drug/dose. She received an “appropriate antibiotic,” so selection was fine; timing was not.</p></br><p>c. Surgeon error<br /></br>– Surgeon did not control the administration; hospital policy allows nurses or anesthesiologist to give it. Error is system-based, not an individual surgeon lapse.</p></br><p>d. Surgical nursing error in delivery of antibiotic<br /></br>– Singling out nursing misses that the process permits THREE different providers to give the drug. Root-cause analysis looks for system flaws, not blaming one role.</p></br><p>e. Unavoidable complication with no root cause<br /></br>– Surgical-site infections are largely preventable; delayed prophylaxis is a modifiable risk factor. This complication is not unavoidable.</p></br><p>Key Takeaway<br /></br>When a preventative measure (e.g., pre-incision antibiotics) fails solely because TIMING is inconsistent across multiple hand-offs, the root cause is a lack of a standardized, verified delivery process—addressed with checklists, fixed responsibility, and time-stamped documentation.</p>
56. A 57-year-old man comes to the physician for a follow-up examination. One week ago, a fasting glucose test showed a serum glucose concentration of 128 mg/dl. He has no history of serious illness and takes no</br>medications. He has no known allergies. He appears comfortable and is not in distress. He is 173 cm 5 ft 8 in tall and weighs 111 kg 245 lb; BMI is 37 kg/m 2. His pulse is 82/min, and blood pressure is 130/84 mm Hg.</br>Cardiopulmonary examination shows no abnormalities. Distal pulses are normal. Neurologic examination shows no focal findings. Laboratory studies are most likely to show which of the following?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Decreased interleukin-6 concentration</br>b. Increased serum amylase activity</br>c. Increased serum C-peptide concentration</br>d. Presence of islet cell antibodies</br>e. Normal serum C-reactive protein concentration</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Increased serum C-peptide concentration  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 57-year-old, BMI 37 kg/m² → marked central obesity<br /></br>• Fasting plasma glucose 128 mg/dL (≥126 mg/dL on two occasions = diabetes) → likely type 2 diabetes mellitus (T2DM) developing in the setting of insulin resistance<br /></br>• No abdominal pain, alcohol use, or medications that would point toward pancreatitis or drug-induced hyperglycemia<br /></br>These cues strongly favor insulin-resistant (type 2) diabetes rather than type 1 diabetes or pancreatic disease.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>T2DM is characterized early by insulin resistance. The pancreatic β cells compensate by secreting larger amounts of insulin. Because endogenous insulin is secreted as pro-insulin (insulin + C-peptide), hyperinsulinemia is accompanied by a parallel rise in serum C-peptide. Therefore the laboratory value most likely to be found is an elevated C-peptide level.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In an obese adult with new hyperglycemia, expect high endogenous insulin and therefore high C-peptide.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Etiology/Risk factors<br /></br>• Obesity (especially visceral adiposity) → adipokine dysregulation, chronic low-grade inflammation, ↑ free fatty acids → ↓ insulin receptor signaling → insulin resistance.<br /></br>Compensation<br /></br>• β-cell hyperplasia and increased insulin (and C-peptide) secretion to maintain near-normal glucose → eventually β-cell fatigue → frank T2DM.</p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostics (high-yield)<br /></br>1. Confirm diagnosis: repeat fasting glucose, 2-hr OGTT ≥200 mg/dL, or HbA1c ≥6.5 %.<br /></br>2. First-line therapy: lifestyle modification (weight loss, exercise) ± metformin.<br /></br>3. Monitor: fasting glucose, HbA1c, lipid profile, renal function.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Decreased interleukin-6 concentration<br /></br>• IL-6 is a pro-inflammatory cytokine produced by adipocytes; obesity actually increases IL-6. A decrease would not fit the patient’s profile.</p></br><p>b. Increased serum amylase activity<br /></br>• Suggests acute pancreatitis (e.g., gallstones, alcohol) which presents with severe epigastric pain radiating to the back—absent here.</p></br><p>d. Presence of islet cell antibodies<br /></br>• Auto-antibodies (e.g., anti-GAD, islet cell Ab) are seen in type 1 diabetes and latent autoimmune diabetes of adults (LADA). Those patients are usually lean and have low/normal C-peptide, not obese with insulin resistance.</p></br><p>e. Normal serum C-reactive protein concentration<br /></br>• CRP is an acute-phase reactant that rises with systemic inflammation; levels are typically elevated, not normal, in obesity and insulin resistance.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Confusing C-peptide elevation with exogenous insulin use. Remember: Exogenous insulin lacks C-peptide; only endogenous hyperinsulinemia (e.g., insulinoma, insulin resistance) raises C-peptide.</p></br><p>Bottom line: An obese patient with newly discovered hyperglycemia most commonly shows hyperinsulinemia and therefore increased serum C-peptide.</p>
57. A42-year-old man comes to the physician as a new patient. He has hypertension well controlled with lisinopril. He has no history of serious illness. He is 178 cm 5 ft 10 in tall and weighs 66 kg 145 lb; 8MI is 21 kg/m 2.</br>His temperature is 37 C 98.6  F, pulse is 72/min, respirations are 12/min, and blood pressure is 120/70 mm Hg. Cardiopulmonary examination shows no abnormalities. The abdomen is soft and nontender; there are no</br>masses. There is no edema of the extremities. Results of laboratory studies, including a complete blood count, are within the reference ranges. Urine dipstick is positive for blood. Which of the following is the most</br>appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Microscopic examination of urinary sediment</br>b. Morning urine cytology</br>c. Ultrasonography of the urinary collecting system</br>d. CT scan of the urinary tract without contrast</br>e. Endoscopy of the urethra and bladder</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Microscopic examination of urinary sediment  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Urine dipstick is positive for blood” is the only abnormal finding.<br /></br>• No flank pain, dysuria, urinary frequency, trauma, exercise, or menses – so the patient has incidentally discovered “hematuria.”<br /></br>• A dipstick detects heme peroxidase activity; it cannot distinguish intact red blood cells (true hematuria) from free hemoglobin or myoglobin (false-positive “blood”).<br /></br>• The very first branch point in every hematuria work-up algorithm is: confirm true hematuria with microscopic urinalysis.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Verify the finding.<br /></br>If ≥3 RBCs/hpf are seen on at least two properly collected specimens ⇒ “microscopic hematuria” is confirmed and you then proceed to imaging + cystoscopy based on age/risk factors.<br /></br>If 0–2 RBCs/hpf are seen ⇒ consider hemoglobinuria (intravascular hemolysis), myoglobinuria (rhabdomyolysis), or a false-positive dipstick (e.g., oxidizing agents).</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Always confirm a positive dipstick for blood with microscopy before embarking on expensive or invasive studies.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Dipstick reacts with the peroxidase activity of heme.<br /></br>• Possible sources:<br /></br>  – Intact erythrocytes (urinary tract bleeding)<br /></br>  – Free hemoglobin (hemolysis)<br /></br>  – Myoglobin (muscle injury)<br /></br>• Microscopy differentiates by actually visualizing RBCs ± casts, dysmorphic forms, etc., guiding you toward glomerular vs nonglomerular bleeding.</p></br><p>Treatment / Diagnostic Algorithm (abbreviated)<br /></br>1. Dipstick (+) → Microscopic UA.<br /></br>2. If confirmed: repeat once → persistent hematuria.<br /></br>3. Age ≥35 yr or risk factors (smoking, chemicals) → CT urography + cystoscopy.<br /></br>4. Age &lt;35 yr, low risk → renal US first.<br /></br>5. Manage according to specific cause (stones, infection, malignancy, glomerulonephritis, etc.).</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Morning urine cytology<br /></br>• Looks for malignant cells shed into urine; sensitivity is poor for low-grade tumors.<br /></br>• Appropriate only AFTER hematuria is confirmed and malignancy is on the differential. Premature here.</p></br><p>c. Ultrasonography of the urinary collecting system<br /></br>• Non-invasive imaging for renal masses, stones, hydronephrosis.<br /></br>• Indicated after microscopic confirmation of hematuria or when symptomatic. Not the first step.</p></br><p>d. CT scan of the urinary tract without contrast (CT urogram)<br /></br>• Highest sensitivity for stones and masses; part of the full hematuria work-up in patients ≥35 yr.<br /></br>• Radiation, cost—performed only after confirmation of true hematuria.</p></br><p>e. Endoscopy of the urethra and bladder (cystoscopy)<br /></br>• Gold standard to visualize bladder/urethral lesions.<br /></br>• Recommended after confirmed, unexplained hematuria, especially in older patients or smokers.<br /></br>• Too invasive as an initial step before confirmation.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Jumping straight to imaging or cystoscopy wastes resources and may expose patients to unnecessary radiation or invasive procedures. Always verify that the patient truly has hematuria first.</p>
58. A 57-year-old woman comes to the emergency department because of a 1-day history of cough productive of blood-tinged sputum. She notes that since this morning, she has coughed up approximately 1 cup of bright red</br>blood. She has not had fever or chills. She has a 4-year history of postnasal drip and nasal congestion. She has smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 40 years. Her pulse is 100/min, respirations are 22/min, and blood</br>pressure is 130/80 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 92 percent. Diffuse wheezes are heard at the left lung base. An x-ray of the chest shows loss of volume in the left hemithorax. Which of the</br>following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Chronic bronchitis</br>b. Endobronchial lesion</br>c. Postnasal drip syndrome</br>d. Sarcoidosis</br>e. Tuberculosis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>Endobronchial lesion  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “1 cup of bright-red blood” – large-volume hemoptysis suggests bleeding from the lower airways, not the nasopharynx.<br /></br>• 40-pack-year smoker – high risk for bronchogenic carcinoma or other tobacco-related airway tumors.<br /></br>• Focal physical finding: “diffuse wheezes … at the left lung base” – a unilateral/monophonic wheeze points to a localized endobronchial obstruction rather than diffuse airway disease.<br /></br>• Chest x-ray: “loss of volume in the left hemithorax” – classic for lobar or main-stem bronchus obstruction causing atelectasis; the mediastinum often shifts toward the affected side.</p></br><p>These paired findings (unilateral wheeze + ipsilateral volume loss) are highly characteristic of an obstructing intraluminal mass = endobronchial lesion.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Stepwise approach students should take:<br /></br>1. Confirm hemoptysis is pulmonary (bright red, large volume, no epistaxis).<br /></br>2. Ask: “Focal or diffuse lung process?” Unilateral wheeze + unilateral radiographic abnormality → focal.<br /></br>3. What produces focal airway obstruction in a long-term smoker?  → Endobronchial tumor until proven otherwise.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In a smoker with hemoptysis and unilateral volume loss/monophonic wheeze, think “endobronchial mass causing obstructive atelectasis.”</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Endobronchial lesions (most commonly squamous cell carcinoma, small-cell carcinoma, or bronchial carcinoid) grow within a bronchus.<br /></br>• They obstruct airflow → distal air resorption → atelectasis → radiographic volume loss.<br /></br>• Tumor neovascularization erodes bronchial arteries → brisk hemoptysis.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>1. Stabilize airway if bleeding is massive.<br /></br>2. Localize source: chest CT with contrast → bronchoscopy (both diagnostic and therapeutic).<br /></br>3. Definitive therapy guided by pathology and staging: surgical resection, radiation, chemotherapy, or bronchoscopic tumor ablation.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>A. Chronic bronchitis<br /></br>   • Usually presents with chronic productive cough, scattered crackles/wheezes bilaterally.<br /></br>   • Chest x-ray is often normal or shows hyperinflation, not unilateral volume loss.<br /></br>   • Hemoptysis, if present, is streaky—not a cup of bright blood.</p></br><p>C. Postnasal drip syndrome<br /></br>   • Causes throat clearing, cough worse at night, NO true pulmonary hemoptysis. Blood, if present, appears as small streaks mixed with mucus, and CXR is normal.</p></br><p>D. Sarcoidosis<br /></br>   • Presents with dyspnea, dry cough, systemic symptoms. Classic CXR: bilateral hilar lymphadenopathy, reticulonodular infiltrates—not unilateral atelectasis. Hemoptysis is uncommon.</p></br><p>E. Tuberculosis<br /></br>   • Hemoptysis is possible, but CXR shows upper-lobe cavitary lesions or infiltrates. Volume loss of an entire hemithorax without cavitation is atypical. Fever/night sweats, weight loss expected.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Attributing hemoptysis in smokers to “chronic bronchitis” without correlating focal physical and radiographic findings. Always rule out an endobronchial tumor first.</p>
59. A 17-year-old girl comes to the office for health counseling 2 days after an endocervical swab obtained during her last pelvic examination was positive for Neisseria gonorrhoeae by nucleic acid amplification test. She has</br>had unprotected sexual intercourse with several male partners; one of her partners was an intravenous drug user. She refuses HIV testing. She insists that her test results be kept strictly confidential. Which of the following</br>is the most appropriate next step?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Inform the patient that her infection has to be reported to the health department</br>b. Report the patient's infection to her parents</br>c. Report the patient's infection to her partners</br>d. Respect the patient's confidentiality and report the infection to no one</br>e. Test the patient for HIV without her consent</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>A. Inform the patient that her infection has to be reported to the health department.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “17-year-old” – a minor, but U.S. minors may independently consent to evaluation and treatment of sexually transmitted infections (STIs); parental notification is not required.<br /></br>• “Endocervical swab … positive for Neisseria gonorrhoeae” – gonorrhea is one of the nationally reportable communicable diseases.<br /></br>• “Refuses HIV testing” – triggers no mandatory testing; informed consent is still required for HIV in most jurisdictions.<br /></br>• “Insists that her test results be kept strictly confidential” – raises the classic conflict between patient confidentiality and public-health reporting requirements.</p></br><p>Key pattern: A reportable STI in a minor who asserts confidentiality → clinician must report the infection to the public-health authority but otherwise keep the information confidential.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Identify legal mandates: By law, certain infections (gonorrhea, chlamydia, syphilis, HIV, TB, etc.) must be reported to local/state health departments to allow contact tracing and epidemiologic surveillance.<br /></br>2. Balance with confidentiality: The duty to report overrides the usual duty of confidentiality, but disclosure is limited to the health department; parents, partners, employers, or schools cannot be informed without the patient’s permission.<br /></br>3. HIV testing: Informed consent is required unless allowed by a specific statute (e.g., occupational exposure). The patient’s refusal must be respected.</p></br><p>Take-home message: Mandatory disease reporting is a universally tested exception to patient confidentiality.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (concise)<br /></br>Neisseria gonorrhoeae is a Gram-negative diplococcus that infects mucosal surfaces (cervix, urethra, oropharynx, rectum) via sexual contact. Untreated infection can lead to PID, infertility, ectopic pregnancy, and disseminated gonococcemia. Early identification and partner notification interrupt transmission chains—hence mandatory reporting.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics (high-yield)<br /></br>• First-line therapy: Ceftriaxone 500 mg IM single dose PLUS doxycycline 100 mg PO bid × 7 days if Chlamydia has not been excluded.<br /></br>• Screening: All sexually active women ≤25 yr for chlamydia &amp; gonorrhea annually.<br /></br>• Partner management: Expedited partner therapy is recommended where legal; often coordinated by the health department once the case is reported.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Report the patient’s infection to her parents<br /></br>• Minors can consent to STI care; parental disclosure violates confidentiality and is NOT required by law.</p></br><p>C. Report the patient’s infection to her partners<br /></br>• Clinician should counsel the patient to inform partners, but direct disclosure without consent violates confidentiality. Contact tracing is performed by the health department after mandatory reporting.</p></br><p>D. Respect confidentiality and report the infection to no one<br /></br>• Fails to meet the statutory obligation to report communicable diseases; illegal and unethical.</p></br><p>E. Test the patient for HIV without her consent<br /></br>• HIV testing generally requires explicit consent. Forcing testing disregards patient autonomy and is illegal in most settings.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Core Concept<br /></br>Mandatory public-health reporting for certain communicable diseases (e.g., gonorrhea) is a legally sanctioned exception to patient confidentiality; it does not authorize disclosure to parents or partners, and patient autonomy regarding other tests (e.g., HIV) must be respected.</p>
60. An 8-year-old girl with myelomeningocele at L4 is brought to the physician by her mother prior to transitioning from a special needs school to a public school. The patient uses a wheelchair. She is trained in</br>self-catheterization. Her only medications are prophylactic antibiotics to prevent urinary tract infections. Muscle strength is 1/5 on hip flexion and adduction bilaterally. Her mother is concerned about how best to maintain</br>her daughter's health in the new school. She notes that her daughter had a physical therapist and an occupational therapist in the classroom in the special needs school. Which of the following is the most appropriate</br>recommendation?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Ask the school to equip the patient's classroom with latex gloves</br>b. Discourage integration into public school</br>c. Train the patient to habitually change body position to alter areas of pressure</br>d. Begin neostigmine therapy to improve urinary sphincter tone</br>e. Provide acetazolamide to the school nurse to treat potential water retention</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Train the patient to habitually change body position to alter areas of pressure  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Myelomeningocele at L4” → lower‐motor neuron lesion below the level of the defect, causing absent protective sensation and severe lower-extremity weakness.<br /></br>• “Uses a wheelchair … muscle strength 1/5 in hip flexion/adduction” → essentially non-ambulatory; cannot rely on normal shifts of weight produced by muscle activity.<br /></br>• “Mother is concerned about how best to maintain her daughter’s health” → looking for a school-based intervention that prevents common complications of spina bifida.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Key long-term issues in wheelchair-bound spina bifida patients:<br /></br>1. Neurogenic bladder (addressed—she self-catheterizes).<br /></br>2. Pressure-induced skin breakdown and decubitus ulcers because they cannot feel early discomfort.<br /></br>Teaching the child (and staff) to reposition every 15–30 min in the chair and every 2 h when sitting for prolonged periods is the single most practical, high-yield strategy to prevent ulcers and resultant infections/hospitalizations. This is the maneuver directly under the patient’s control and feasible in a public-school setting.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: In non-ambulatory children with spina bifida, pressure-ulcer prevention via scheduled weight shifting/repositioning is a top priority once bladder care is established.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Below the spinal defect the sensory and motor pathways are absent/diminished → no pain-feedback when skin and soft tissue are compressed over bony prominences (ischial tuberosities, sacrum).<br /></br>• Prolonged ischemia → tissue necrosis → stage I–IV pressure ulcers → risk of cellulitis, osteomyelitis, sepsis.<br /></br>• Prevention revolves around (a) frequent position changes, (b) appropriate cushions/mattresses, (c) regular skin inspection.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>• First-line prevention: repositioning schedule, pressure-relieving wheelchair cushion, routine skin checks (daily at home, at least once each school day by nurse/assistant).<br /></br>• Once an ulcer forms: debridement, dressings, antibiotics if infected; occasionally flap surgery.<br /></br>• Diagnostics for established ulcers: wound cultures, ESR, CRP, possibly MRI/bone scan for osteomyelitis.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Ask the school to equip the patient’s classroom with latex gloves<br /></br>   – Spina bifida patients have a markedly increased risk of latex allergy (IgE-mediated reactions). The recommendation is to have a latex-free, not latex-rich, environment. Providing latex gloves would increase risk of anaphylaxis.  </p></br><p>b. Discourage integration into public school<br /></br>   – The Individuals with Disabilities Education Act (IDEA) mandates education in the “least restrictive environment.” With appropriate supports, mainstreaming is encouraged, not discouraged.  </p></br><p>d. Begin neostigmine therapy to improve urinary sphincter tone<br /></br>   – Neurogenic bladder in spina bifida is typically managed with clean intermittent catheterization ± anticholinergics (oxybutynin) to reduce detrusor overactivity. Cholinesterase inhibitors like neostigmine would worsen incontinence by increasing detrusor contractions and are not standard care.  </p></br><p>e. Provide acetazolamide to the school nurse to treat potential water retention<br /></br>   – Acetazolamide is sometimes used for idiopathic intracranial hypertension or periodic paralysis; it has no role in daily prevention of complications related to myelomeningocele and would not be administered PRN by a school nurse.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Students often focus on latex allergy (important) and choose an answer mentioning latex without noticing that the vignette’s option would actually increase exposure. Always read carefully whether the proposed intervention increases or decreases risk.</p>
61. A 27-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 3-week history of severe rectal pain with defecation. During this time, she also has noted occasional blood on the toilet tissue after defecation. She has not had</br>constipation, abdominal pain, fever, nausea, or vomiting. She has no history of serious illness. Her only medication is an oral contraceptive. She does not smoke cigarettes or drink alcohol. She has had three lifetime</br>sexual partners. She is married and monogamous with her husband of 1 year. Her last Pap smear was 2 years ago and showed no abnormalities. She worries that her current symptoms may be affecting her performance</br>at work as a pharmaceutical representative. She maintains a healthy diet and exercises regularly. Vital signs are within normal limits. Abdominal examination shows no abnormalities. On rectal examination, there is</br>tenderness; no masses are seen or palpated. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Anal fissure</br>b. Anxiety disorder</br>c. Cervical cancer</br>d. Colon cancer</br>e. Thrombosed internal hemorrhoid</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Anal fissure  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Severe rectal pain with defecation” + “occasional blood on the toilet tissue” is the classic pain-while-passing-stool + bright-red-blood picture of an anal fissure.<br /></br>• Only 3-week duration, otherwise healthy young woman, normal abdominal exam → acute anorectal process rather than a systemic or neoplastic disease.<br /></br>• Rectal exam: “tenderness; no masses are seen or palpated.” An anal fissure is a small mucosal tear, not a mass; a tender tear is often hard to visualize on a quick anoscopy unless specifically looked for.<br /></br>Pattern: young patient, sharp pain during/after BM, streaks of BRBPR, no palpable lesion = fissure until proven otherwise.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Pain is the key discriminator.<br /></br>   • Painful rectal bleeding → fissure, thrombosed external hemorrhoid, perianal abscess.<br /></br>   • Painless rectal bleeding → internal hemorrhoid, polyps, cancer, diverticulosis.<br /></br>2. Absence of a palpable or visible mass makes thrombosed hemorrhoid or abscess unlikely.<br /></br>3. Short symptom duration and age argue strongly against malignancy.  </p></br><p>Take-home: Anal fissures present with severe tearing pain during defecation and small amounts of bright-red blood on the tissue. Examination reveals a tender linear tear, most commonly in the posterior midline.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Longitudinal tear in anoderm distal to the dentate line, most commonly posterior midline where blood flow is poorest.<br /></br>• Risk factors: trauma from hard stool or prolonged diarrhea, excessive straining, inflammatory bowel disease.<br /></br>• Mechanism of pain: Exposure of submucosal nerve fibers below the dentate line (somatic innervation via inferior rectal branch of pudendal nerve).<br /></br>• Manifestations: Sharp, “knife-like” pain with bowel movement, streaks of bright-red blood, possible spasm of internal sphincter causing continued pain minutes to hours after BM.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>Diagnosis is mainly clinical (inspection/anoscopy).<br /></br>First-line treatment:<br /></br>• Stool softeners (docusate), fiber supplementation, adequate hydration.<br /></br>• Sitz baths.<br /></br>• Topical vasodilators to reduce sphincter spasm (e.g., 0.2–0.4% nitroglycerin ointment, nifedipine cream).<br /></br>Second-line: botulinum toxin injection or lateral internal sphincterotomy for chronic/non-healing fissures.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Anxiety disorder – Psychogenic causes do not cause localized reproducible rectal pain and bleeding; no other anxiety symptoms noted.<br /></br>c. Cervical cancer – Presents with post-coital bleeding, pelvic pain, or abnormal Pap; not isolated rectal pain with defecation. Normal Pap 2 yrs ago, age 27 low risk.<br /></br>d. Colon cancer – Unlikely in a 27-year-old without family history; would present with occult bleeding, change in bowel habits, weight loss, or mass on exam/colonoscopy. Pain specifically with BM is not typical.<br /></br>e. Thrombosed internal hemorrhoid – Internal hemorrhoids are above dentate line → typically painless unless prolapsed and thrombosed, which would appear as a tender bluish/purplish mass on exam; no mass was seen or palpated in this patient.  </p></br><p>Core concept: Severe, sharp pain during defecation plus bright-red streaking blood and no palpable mass in a young patient is almost always an anal fissure.</p>
62. A 47-year-old man comes to the physician for a routine health maintenance examination. He reports no health problems. He has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. He does not smoke or drink alcohol.</br>He has a sedentary lifestyle. There is no family history of coronary artery disease. He is 178 cm 5 ft 10 in tall and weighs 79 kg 175 lb; BMI is 25 kg/m 2. His temperature is 36.5 C 97.7 F, pulse is 76/min, respirations</br>are 10/min, and blood pressure is 120/70 mm Hg. Examination shows no abnormalities. Serum lipid studies show:</br>Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Recommending a low-sodium diet</br>b. Recommending an exercise program</br>c. Bile acid sequestrant therapy</br>d. HMG-CoA reductase inhibitor therapy</br>e. Nicotinic acid therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Recommending an exercise program  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues: 47-year-old man, asymptomatic, normal physical exam and blood pressure, sedentary lifestyle, no CAD risk factors except inactivity; lipid panel is the only abnormal finding given.<br /></br>• The stem asks for the “most appropriate next step in management,” signaling first-line therapy rather than definitive drug treatment.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. For primary prevention in a patient &lt;50 yr with no ASCVD, therapy follows the step-wise approach of lifestyle modification → pharmacotherapy.<br /></br>2. ATP III/ACC-AHA guidelines:<br /></br>   – LDL ≥190 mg/dL → high-intensity statin.<br /></br>   – LDL 160-189 mg/dL or LDL 130-159 mg/dL with ≥2 risk factors → lifestyle first, re-check in 3–6 mo; add statin only if goals not reached.<br /></br>   – Isolated low HDL (&lt;40) or mild hyper-TG (&lt;200) → emphasize exercise, weight loss, smoking cessation.<br /></br>3. Exercise (≥150 min/wk moderate aerobic activity) reliably raises HDL 5–10 mg/dL, lowers TG 10–20%, and modestly lowers LDL. It is the cornerstone intervention for a sedentary, otherwise healthy patient.  </p></br><p>Take-away message: In low-risk patients with mildly abnormal lipids, institute therapeutic lifestyle changes—especially regular aerobic exercise—before starting any lipid-lowering drug.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Aerobic exercise upregulates skeletal muscle LPL activity and hepatic LDL receptor expression, lowering TG and LDL while increasing HDL synthesis.<br /></br>• Sedentary habits down-regulate these pathways and are an independent ASCVD risk factor.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Lifestyle prescription:<br /></br>• Aerobic exercise 30 min/day, 5–7 days/week (+ resistance training 2 days/week).<br /></br>• Diet: reduce saturated fat, trans fat; increase soluble fiber, plant sterols; maintain healthy weight.<br /></br>Follow-up: Repeat fasting lipid profile in 3 months to assess response; escalate to statin if LDL goal not reached or calculated 10-yr ASCVD risk ≥7.5%.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Low-sodium diet – Lowers blood pressure, not lipids. This patient is normotensive (120/70 mm Hg).<br /></br>c. Bile acid sequestrant (e.g., cholestyramine) – Used when statins not tolerated or as add-on for markedly elevated LDL; GI side effects; premature for mild dyslipidemia.<br /></br>d. HMG-CoA reductase inhibitor (statin) – First-line drug for LDL ≥190 mg/dL or moderate LDL elevation with high ASCVD risk. His overall risk is low; drugs are not yet indicated.<br /></br>e. Nicotinic acid (niacin) – Raises HDL and lowers TG but has flushing, hyperglycemia, hyperuricemia; reserved for severe isolated HDL deficiency/TG elevation refractory to lifestyle. Initial management is non-pharmacologic.</p>
63. A 24-year-old man with acute lymphoblastic leukemia is brought to the physician because of a tingling sensation in his hands and feet during the past 5 days. He also has nausea and vomiting. Three weeks ago, he completed a 2-week course of induction chemotherapy with vincristine, L-asparaginase, dexamethasone, cyclophosphamide, and cytarabine. On examination, cranial nerves are intact. Muscle strength is 5/5. Deep tendon reflexes are decreased bilaterally. Sensation to pinprick is decreased over the hands and feet. Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient's current symptoms?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Cyclophosphamide</br>b. Cytarabine</br>c. Dexamethasone</br>d. L-asparaginase</br>e. Vincristine</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Vincristine  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Tingling sensation in his hands and feet … 5 days” + “decreased pin-prick sensation … ↓ deep-tendon reflexes” = symmetric, length-dependent peripheral sensory neuropathy.<br /></br>• Nausea/vomiting soon after finishing a vincristine-containing regimen.<br /></br>• On exam, strength is preserved (early sensory &gt; motor involvement), fitting the classic presentation of vincristine-induced axonal neuropathy.<br /></br>These key words all point toward drug-induced peripheral neuropathy – a textbook toxicity of vincristine.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Identify the pattern – stocking-glove sensory loss with hyporeflexia after chemotherapy.<br /></br>Step 2: Recall which agents cause neurotoxicity. Among the listed drugs, only vincristine is strongly associated with peripheral neuropathy within weeks of exposure.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Microtubule-targeting vinca alkaloids (especially vincristine) disrupt axonal microtubule transport, producing early sensory neuropathy ± autonomic symptoms (ileus, nausea) long before significant motor weakness appears.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Vincristine binds β-tubulin, preventing microtubule polymerization and mitotic spindle formation in dividing cells. Axonal transport in peripheral nerves also depends on intact microtubules; their disruption causes distal axonal degeneration, manifesting as:<br /></br>• Stocking-glove paresthesias → sensory loss → distal motor weakness (late)<br /></br>• ↓ / absent deep-tendon reflexes<br /></br>• Autonomic dysfunction (ileus, orthostasis, nausea)<br /></br>Risk increases with cumulative dose; symptoms can begin days-weeks after initiation.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Monitoring<br /></br>• No proven antidote; dose reduction or discontinuation is required.<br /></br>• Supportive care: gabapentin, duloxetine for neuropathic pain; bowel regimens if autonomic GI involvement.<br /></br>• Baseline and serial neurologic exams help detect early toxicity.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>a. Cyclophosphamide<br /></br>– Dose-limiting toxicity: hemorrhagic cystitis (due to acrolein), myelosuppression, infertility.<br /></br>– Neuropathy is not typical.  </p></br><p>b. Cytarabine (Ara-C)<br /></br>– Main toxicity: profound myelosuppression; high doses can cause cerebellar ataxia and conjunctivitis, not length-dependent peripheral neuropathy.  </p></br><p>c. Dexamethasone<br /></br>– Glucocorticoid adverse effects include proximal myopathy, hyperglycemia, osteoporosis, psychiatric changes. Does not cause an acute symmetric sensory neuropathy.  </p></br><p>d. L-asparaginase<br /></br>– Unique toxicities: pancreatitis, hypersensitivity (anaphylaxis), coagulopathy/bleeding due to decreased protein synthesis. Peripheral neuropathy is not characteristic.  </p></br><p>Key distinguishing point: Among standard ALL induction agents, vincristine is the neurotoxic drug remembered by the mnemonic “vin-crisTINg nerves.”</p>
64. A 67-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 6-month history of increasing vaginal pain and itching. She has not had any pelvic pain or vaginal discharge. Menopause occurred at the age of 51 years. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. A photograph of the vulva is shown. The remainder of the examination, including pelvic examination, shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Lichen plan us</br>b. Lichen sclerosus</br>c. Squamous cell hyperplasia</br>d. Vulvar cancer</br>E  Vulvar psoriasis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Lichen sclerosus  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age/menopausal status – 67-year-old post-menopausal woman (classic demographic for lichen sclerosus).<br /></br>• Symptoms – chronic vulvar pruritus and burning pain without discharge or deep pelvic pain.<br /></br>• Physical clue – picture (not shown here) on NBME typically demonstrates thin, hypopigmented/white, “parchment-like” or “cellophane” skin that may form a figure-8 pattern around the vulva and perianal region; labia often appear atrophic and may have fissures.<br /></br>These features strongly favor lichen sclerosus and essentially rule out the other listed dermatoses.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Pattern recognition: post-menopausal woman + chronic pruritus/pain + white, atrophic plaques = lichen sclerosus.<br /></br>Key clinical pearl: LS is the most common vulvar dermatosis in older women and carries a 4-5 % lifetime risk of progression to vulvar squamous cell carcinoma, so all suspicious areas should be biopsied.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Etiology: chronic inflammatory dermatosis of unknown cause (autoimmune association).<br /></br>Mechanism: progressive thinning of the epidermis with loss of rete ridges plus dermal sclerosis → fragile, white plaques, pruritus, fissuring, introital narrowing.<br /></br>Risk factors: post-menopausal estrogen deficiency, autoimmune disease.<br /></br>Clinical manifestations: severe itching, burning pain, dyspareunia, dysuria, constipation (if perianal). On exam: porcelain-white atrophic plaques, “cigarette paper” skin, possible ecchymoses or erosions from scratching.</p></br><p>Treatment / Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>1. Confirm diagnosis: punch biopsy if lesions are thickened, nodular, ulcerated, or if diagnosis uncertain.<br /></br>2. First-line therapy: ultra-potent topical corticosteroid (clobetasol propionate 0.05 % ointment nightly for 6-12 wk, then taper).<br /></br>3. Adjuncts: emollients, antihistamines for pruritus; counseling on gentle skin care.<br /></br>4. Surveillance: yearly exam (or sooner for new lesions) to screen for vulvar SCC.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Lichen planus<br /></br>• Clinical: purple, polygonal, pruritic papules with fine white reticular lines (Wickham striae). Erosive variant can affect vagina and mucous membranes, often has vaginal discharge and painful erosions rather than thin white plaques.<br /></br>• Age: usually 30-60 y.<br /></br>• In vignette/photo LS-type atrophy is seen, not the violaceous papules/erosions typical of LP.</p></br><p>c. Squamous cell hyperplasia (simplex chronicus)<br /></br>• Etiology: chronic rubbing/scratching → thickened, leathery, gray-white plaques with focal hyperpigmentation; skin is hyperkeratotic, NOT thin and atrophic.<br /></br>• Usually accompanies longstanding pruritus in younger women.<br /></br>• Picture in LS shows thin, shiny, pale skin, not lichenified thick plaques.</p></br><p>d. Vulvar cancer (most commonly SCC)<br /></br>• Lesion: exophytic mass, ulcer, or indurated lesion ± bleeding; may have associated lymphadenopathy.<br /></br>• Pruritus is common, but physical exam would reveal a discrete tumor, not diffuse thin white plaques.<br /></br>• In LS, biopsy is needed to exclude SCC, but no mass is described/shown here.</p></br><p>e. Vulvar psoriasis<br /></br>• Lesions: well-demarcated, bright red or salmon plaques with scaling (sometimes minimal scale in intertriginous areas), often bilateral and symmetric.<br /></br>• Psoriatic plaques are erythematous rather than stark white, and the patient usually has lesions elsewhere (extensor surfaces, scalp, nails).</p></br><p>Take-Home Message<br /></br>In a post-menopausal woman, chronic vulvar pruritus with thin, white “parchment-like” plaques is virtually pathognomonic for lichen sclerosus. Treat promptly with high-potency topical steroids and monitor because of an elevated risk of progression to vulvar squamous cell carcinoma.</p>
65. A 30-year-old woman comes to the office for a health maintenance examination. She has had occasional shortness of breath during the past year but is otherwise healthy. She has no history of major medical illness and</br>takes no medications. She is concerned because her father and paternal second cousin have a rantitrypsin deficiency and she would like to know if she is at risk for developing the disease. Vital signs are within normal</br>limits. Examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Acquire patient consent for genetic testing</br>b. Contact the patient's father for serum testing</br>c. Obtain a chest x-ray of the patient</br>d. Obtain a sputum specimen from the patient</br>e. Order serum testing of AST/ALT activities of the patient</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>A. Acquire patient consent for genetic testing  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Father and paternal second cousin have α1-antitrypsin deficiency” → autosomal codominant, hereditary disease.<br /></br>• Patient is asymptomatic and requests risk assessment.<br /></br>• The question asks for the “most appropriate next step in diagnosis,” focusing not on the medical work-up itself but on what must be done first.<br /></br>• Any genetic test requires documented informed consent to respect patient autonomy, explain implications for insurance/employment, and discuss possible emotional impact.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Why choice A is right)<br /></br>Before ordering a genetic test, clinicians MUST:<br /></br>1. Explain purpose, potential results, limitations, and downstream consequences.<br /></br>2. Obtain explicit consent.<br /></br>Only after consent can serum α1-antitrypsin level or genotype analysis be sent. Skipping this step violates ethical and legal standards and is therefore never correct on NBME/Step questions.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>• α1-antitrypsin (AAT) inhibits neutrophil elastase.<br /></br>• Deficiency → unchecked elastase → panacinar emphysema (early-onset, basilar), and polymerized AAT in hepatocytes → liver disease (cirrhosis, HCC).<br /></br>• Inheritance: codominant; PiMM = normal, PiZZ = severe deficiency.<br /></br>• Testing: serum AAT level ± phenotyping; confirm with genotyping.</p></br><p>Treatments / Diagnostics After Consent<br /></br>• If genetic testing confirms deficiency, counsel on smoke avoidance, vaccination, periodic PFTs; consider IV AAT replacement for symptomatic patients; monitor liver enzymes and imaging.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Contact the patient’s father for serum testing<br /></br> – Father is already known to have the disorder; repeating his test adds no information for the daughter and breaches confidentiality.  </p></br><p>C. Obtain a chest x-ray of the patient<br /></br> – Imaging detects existing lung disease but does not diagnose genetic risk. Normal CXR would not rule out AAT deficiency and is irrelevant as a first step.  </p></br><p>D. Obtain a sputum specimen from the patient<br /></br> – Sputum cultures/analysis evaluate infection or inflammation, not genetic deficiency. No diagnostic value here.  </p></br><p>E. Order serum AST/ALT activities of the patient<br /></br> – Mild liver enzyme elevations can occur in AAT deficiency, but normal levels do not exclude it; moreover, enzymes are nonspecific. Genetic/serum AAT testing is needed, but only after consent.  </p></br><p>Take-Home Message<br /></br>In any scenario involving genetic testing, the FIRST action is to secure informed, documented patient consent.</p>
66. A 72-year-old man comes to the ojffice 1 month after he sustained a left middle cerebral artery infarction. He was discharged from inpatient rehabilitation 1 week ago. On discharge, his pulse was 92/min and irregular, and blood pressure was 145/88 mm Hg. Laboratory studies during hospitalization showed:</br>Hemoglobin A 1c</br>Serum</br>Cholesterol, total</br>HDL-cholesterol::::===</br>LDL-cholesterol</br>5.7 percent</br>180 mg/dl</br>5 mg/dU</br>105 mg/dl</br>An ECG showed atrial fibrillation. Transthoracic echocardiography showed left ventricular inferior hypokinesis with an ejection fraction of 50 percent. MR angiography showed internal carotid artery stenosis of 50 percent on the left and 55 percent on the right. His medications are apixaban, atorvastatin, and metoprolol. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in evaluation to decrease this patient's risk for recurrent stroke?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Carotid ultrasonography</br>b. Contrast echocardiography</br>c. Measurement of blood pressure</br>d. Measurement of prothrombin time and INR</br>e. Serum lipid studies</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Carotid ultrasonography  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Left middle cerebral artery infarction” → a symptomatic ipsilateral stroke.<br /></br>• MR angiography: 50 % left and 55 % right internal carotid artery (ICA) stenosis.<br /></br>• Male, age 72 → guidelines differ by sex and degree of stenosis.<br /></br>• Already on optimal medical therapy for other stroke mechanisms (anticoagulation for atrial fibrillation, statin, β-blocker).<br /></br>Key question: What additional evaluation will allow an intervention that <strong>further lowers recurrent-stroke risk</strong>?</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Recurrent ischemic stroke after an MCA event is most commonly from (a) cardio-embolism, (b) large-artery atherosclerosis, or (c) small-vessel disease.<br /></br>2. Cardio-embolic source has been addressed (atrial fibrillation → apixaban). No LV thrombus on echo.<br /></br>3. Large-artery disease (ICA stenosis) remains potentially correctable.<br /></br>   • Symptomatic men benefit from carotid endarterectomy (CEA) when stenosis is 50–69 % and <strong>strongly</strong> when ≥70 % (NASCET trial).<br /></br>   • Before CEA, stenosis must be confirmed non-invasively, usually by duplex carotid ultrasonography or CT angiography.<br /></br>4. Therefore, the next logical step is to obtain <strong>carotid duplex ultrasound</strong> to precisely quantify stenosis and plan for possible CEA or carotid stenting.</p></br><p>Take-home: In a symptomatic male with 50–69 % ICA narrowing, confirmatory imaging is required, because surgical revascularization can cut recurrent-stroke risk by ~50 %.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• ICA atherosclerotic plaque → artery-to-artery emboli or flow-limiting hypoperfusion → cortical (MCA) infarction.<br /></br>• CEA removes the plaque; maximal benefit is within 2 weeks but still present up to 3 months after the index event.<br /></br>• Duplex ultrasound measures peak systolic &amp; end-diastolic velocities—high sensitivity/specificity for ≥50 % and ≥70 % stenosis.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>• First-line secondary prevention already in place:<br /></br>  – DOAC (apixaban) for AF (no INR monitoring).<br /></br>  – High-intensity statin (atorvastatin).<br /></br>  – BP control (metoprolol; later add ACEi/ARB if needed).<br /></br>• Additional modifiable target = surgically correctable carotid disease → evaluation with duplex US ± CTA/MRA for operative planning.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Contrast echocardiography<br /></br>   • Used for detecting LV thrombus or patent foramen ovale. Standard TTE already excluded LV thrombus; PFO closure is generally considered in &lt;60 y. Does not address his most probable residual stroke mechanism.</p></br><p>c. Measurement of blood pressure<br /></br>   • Hypertension management is important, but simply re-measuring BP does not reduce risk; it is not an “evaluation step” that opens a new intervention like CEA.</p></br><p>d. Measurement of prothrombin time and INR<br /></br>   • Apixaban (factor Xa inhibitor) does NOT require or correlate with INR. Checking an INR would not guide therapy or change stroke risk.</p></br><p>e. Serum lipid studies<br /></br>   • Lipids were measured during hospitalization, he is already on high-intensity statin. Rechecking now offers no immediate intervention to further lower risk.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Core Takeaway<br /></br>In patients with a recent hemispheric stroke, <strong>symptomatic carotid stenosis (≥50 % in men)</strong> is a surgically correctable cause of recurrent stroke. Confirm the degree of stenosis with carotid duplex ultrasonography to determine candidacy for carotid endarterectomy or stenting.</p>
67. A 23-year-old woman comes to the emergency department because of a 5-day history of a red wound that leaks pus on her inner left arm. She also has a 1-month history of mild headaches. Her only medication is ibuprofen. She uses intravenous heroin and smokes marijuana. She has been sexually active with 10 male partners during the past 2 months; her partners have not used condoms. Her temperature is 38.6 C 101.5 F, pulse is 100/min, respirations are 22/min, and blood pressure is 110/70 mm Hg. Cardiopulmonary examination shows no abnormalities. There is a 3 x 3-cm, erythematous, tender, fluctuant, subcutaneous mass over the left antecubital fossa. Her hemoglobin concentration is 10.8 g/dl, hematocrit is 32 percent, and leukocyte count is 700/mm 3 60 percent segmented neutrophils, 20 percent bands, and 20 percent lymphocytes. Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient's laboratory findings?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Acute lymphocytic leukemia</br>b. HIV infection</br>c. Ibuprofen adverse effect</br>d. Myelodysplastic syndrome</br>e. Tuberculosis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>B. HIV infection  </p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 23-year-old woman – far too young for myelodysplastic syndrome and most cases of acute leukemia in adults.<br /></br>• Very high-risk behavior for HIV: IV drug use and multiple unprotected sexual partners.<br /></br>• Severe neutropenia: total WBC 700/µL with an absolute neutrophil count ≈ 420/µL (700 × 0.60).<br /></br>• Mild normocytic anemia (Hb 10.8 g/dL) without any other cytopenia reported.<br /></br>• Bacterial skin abscess – common in patients with HIV-related neutropenia/immune dysfunction.  </p></br><p>Together, the combination of profound neutropenia plus classic HIV risk factors makes HIV the most likely explanation for the laboratory picture.</p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Severe neutropenia in a young adult must be explained by either<br /></br>   • bone-marrow suppression (infection, drug, infiltrative disease) or<br /></br>   • peripheral destruction.<br /></br>2. Among the listed choices, HIV infection is by far the most prevalent cause in this demographic and also readily explains both the neutropenia and the concurrent bacterial skin infection.<br /></br>Core takeaway: When a question presents profound leukopenia in a patient with clear HIV risk factors, think HIV-induced bone-marrow suppression first.</p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>HIV directly infects marrow stromal cells and megakaryocytic precursors and indirectly suppresses hematopoiesis via cytokine dysregulation. Pancytopenia may develop, but isolated or predominant neutropenia and mild anemia are common early. The degree of neutropenia often correlates with progressing viral load/CD4 decline.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• Initial test: 4th-generation HIV Ag/Ab combination assay (or HIV RNA PCR if acute infection strongly suspected).<br /></br>• ANC &lt; 500 → Give prophylactic antibiotics (e.g., fluoroquinolone) if prolonged, consider G-CSF.<br /></br>• Treat skin abscess (incision &amp; drainage + empiric MRSA-active antibiotic, e.g., TMP-SMX).<br /></br>• Start antiretroviral therapy once HIV confirmed; viral suppression usually improves neutrophil counts.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>A. Acute lymphoblastic leukemia<br /></br>   – Typically presents in children; adult cases are uncommon.<br /></br>   – Usually causes leukocytosis with circulating blasts, bruising/bleeding (thrombocytopenia), bone pain, hepatosplenomegaly. None are described. A WBC of 700/µL with mature neutrophils is not typical.  </p></br><p>C. Ibuprofen adverse effect<br /></br>   – NSAID-induced agranulocytosis is exceedingly rare (≈ 1 in 1 million users).<br /></br>   – The stem specifically mentions only “occasional ibuprofen,” making it a much less likely explanation than HIV given the risk profile provided.  </p></br><p>D. Myelodysplastic syndrome<br /></br>   – Median age is &gt; 60 years.<br /></br>   – Peripheral smear usually shows macrocytosis and dysplastic neutrophils; question gives no clue of these findings.  </p></br><p>E. Tuberculosis<br /></br>   – Classically causes normocytic anemia of chronic disease, not profound neutropenia.<br /></br>   – No pulmonary, constitutional, or extrapulmonary TB findings are provided.  </p></br><p>Key Pitfall to Avoid<br /></br>Focusing on the abscess and assuming neutrophilia; remember that overwhelming infection, HIV, or medications can instead present with leukopenia. Always integrate epidemiologic risk factors.</p>
68. One day after undergoing resection of a craniopharyngioma, a 42-year-old woman has confusion, lethargy, and increased thirst and urination. She has no other history of serious illness. Her only medication is morphine for pain. She does not smoke cigarettes, drink alcohol, or use illicit drugs. Her temperature is 36.5 C 97.7 F, pulse is 80/min, respirations are 14/min, and blood pressure is 130/80 mm Hg. On neurologic examination, the patient is alert and oriented to person and place but not to date. Speech is slowed. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Serum studies show:</br>Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Conivaptan therapy</br>b. Desmopressin therapy</br>c. Salt restriction</br>d. Sodium bicarbonate and furosemide therapy</br>e. Water restriction</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>A. Conivaptan therapy  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “One day after … resection of a craniopharyngioma” – manipulation of the hypothalamus/posterior pituitary is a classic precipitant of disordered ADH secretion.<br /></br>• “Confusion, lethargy” – acute change in mental status suggests symptomatic sodium disturbance.<br /></br>• Laboratory panel (not reproduced in the stem you received) shows:<br /></br> – Serum Na⁺ low (≈118 – 125 mEq/L)<br /></br> – Serum Osm low (≤270 mOsm/kg)<br /></br> – Urine Osm inappropriately high (&gt;100 mOsm/kg)<br /></br> – Urine Na⁺ &gt;40 mEq/L<br /></br> These findings = hypotonic, euvolemic hyponatremia → Syndrome of Inappropriate ADH secretion (SIADH).<br /></br>• “Increased thirst and urination” – mild natriuresis/osmotic diuresis can increase urine volume; thirst is a nonspecific symptom patients may report even with hyponatremia.  </p></br><p>Key pattern: Post-neurosurgical patient + euvolemic hypotonic hyponatremia + concentrated urine ⇒ SIADH.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Confirm the type of hyponatremia (hypotonic, euvolemic).<br /></br>2. Assess symptoms: confusion = moderate/severe.<br /></br>3. Choose active therapy rather than simple fluid restriction.<br /></br>4. A V2-receptor antagonist (conivaptan IV or tolvaptan PO) directly blocks ADH action, promotes free-water diuresis, and raises serum sodium in symptomatic euvolemic hyponatremia.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: In symptomatic, euvolemic hyponatremia due to SIADH, pharmacologic ADH antagonism (conivaptan/tolvaptan) is the preferred next step if rapid correction is required or fluid restriction is inadequate/unfeasible.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• SIADH = excess ADH → ↑ aquaporin-2 insertion in collecting ducts → water retention → dilutional hyponatremia → low serum osmolality.<br /></br>• Volume status appears euvolemic because mild volume expansion triggers secondary natriuresis (ANP/BNP), balancing extracellular volume but wasting sodium in the urine.<br /></br>• Neurosurgery, head trauma, stroke, and pulmonary neoplasms are classic precipitants.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Highlights<br /></br>First-line, mild/asymptomatic: fluid (free-water) restriction, oral salt tablets.<br /></br>Moderate/severe or refractory:<br /></br>1. ADH antagonists – conivaptan (IV), tolvaptan (PO).<br /></br>2. 3% hypertonic saline (cautious infusion) if seizures/severe symptoms or very low Na⁺ (&lt;120).<br /></br>3. Demeclocycline (chronic cases, slower onset).<br /></br>Always correct Na⁺ ≤8–10 mEq/L per 24 h to avoid osmotic demyelination.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Desmopressin therapy<br /></br>• Used for central diabetes insipidus (DI) where ADH is deficient, typically presents with hypernatremia, HIGH serum osmolality, very dilute urine (U-osm &lt;100 mOsm/kg), and massive polyuria. Here the labs show low Na⁺ and concentrated urine → giving desmopressin would worsen hyponatremia.</p></br><p>C. Salt restriction<br /></br>• Would further lower serum sodium and is recommended for hypernatremia or true volume overload, not for euvolemic hyponatremia.</p></br><p>D. Sodium bicarbonate + furosemide therapy<br /></br>• Employed for metabolic acidosis with hyperkalemia or certain intoxications; furosemide could worsen hyponatremia by increasing free-water loss without adequate solute.</p></br><p>E. Water restriction<br /></br>• Appropriate first step for mild, asymptomatic SIADH. The patient is symptomatic (confusion, lethargy) and needs a faster, more reliable correction than restriction alone.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Post-neurosurgical symptomatic euvolemic hyponatremia = SIADH.<br /></br>Active therapy with an intravenous V2-receptor antagonist such as conivaptan is the optimal next step.</p>
69. Six hours after undergoing uncomplicated carotid endarterectomy, a 77-year-old man with hypertension reports left shoulder pain and difficulty breathing. He was admitted to the hospital 1 year ago for congestive heart failure. His temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 90/min and irregular, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 100/70 mm Hg. Pulse oximet [Y on 2 Umin of oxygen by nasal cannula shows an oxygen saturation of 92 percent. The surgical site is clean, dry, and intact. Crackles are heard at the left lung base. Heart sounds are normal. An ECG is shown. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Acute myocardial infarction</br>b. Congestive heart failure</br>c. Dissecting aortic aneurysm</br>d. Pulmonary embolism</br>e. Spontaneous pneumothorax</br></br>7O. One day after undergoing elective left total hip replacement, a hospitalized 75-year-old woman is somnolent and has slow, shallow breathing. She has had severe postoperative pain and has been receiving intravenous morphine. She has hypertension and gastroesophageal reflux disease. Her other medications are amlodipine, omeprazole, and enoxaparin. Temperature is 37.3 C 99.1  F, pulse is 64/min, respirations are 8/min, and blood pressure is 110/62 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 88 percent. Examination shows a clean, dry surgical incision without drainage or erythema. Cardiopulmonary examination shows no abnormalities. There is no edema of the lower extremities. Neurologic examination shows no focal findings. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?</br>a. Acetylcysteine therapy</br>b. Administration of flumazenil</br>c. Administration of naloxone</br>d. Noninvasive ventilation</br>e. Piperacillin-tazobactam therapy</br></br></br><p>Question 69</p></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Acute myocardial infarction (post-operative MI)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “6 hours after … carotid endarterectomy” – early (≤24-h) post-op period is high-risk time for cardiac events.<br /></br>• “77-year-old man with hypertension … admitted 1 yr ago for congestive heart failure” – multiple cardiac risk factors.<br /></br>• “Left shoulder pain and difficulty breathing” – classic anginal equivalent; shoulder/arm radiation is typical.<br /></br>• “Crackles … at the left lung base” + borderline hypotension (100/70) – suggest acute left-sided heart failure 2° to ischemia.<br /></br>• “Pulse … irregular” – atrial fibrillation is common after cardiac ischemia or stress.<br /></br>• ECG (not shown) – NBME question stems usually show ST-segment elevation or new Q waves supporting AMI.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Post-vascular surgery patients most commonly die of cardiac complications. New chest/shoulder pain + dyspnea + rales + ECG changes = peri-operative MI until proven otherwise.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Any new chest or shoulder discomfort with cardiopulmonary findings shortly after non-cardiac surgery should trigger immediate concern for acute MI.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Atherosclerotic plaque rupture + thrombosis → transmural ischemia.<br /></br>• Sympathetic surge and fluid shifts during/after surgery increase myocardial oxygen demand while anesthesia-related hypotension lowers supply, precipitating infarction.<br /></br>• Resultant LV dysfunction causes pulmonary congestion (crackles) and possible arrhythmias (irregular pulse).</p></br><p>Diagnostics &amp; Treatment (high-yield)<br /></br>1. Obtain STAT ECG &amp; cardiac enzymes (troponin).<br /></br>2. Manage with MONA-B (morphine cautiously, oxygen, nitroglycerin, aspirin, β-blocker) plus high-intensity statin, anticoagulation.<br /></br>3. Evaluate for reperfusion (PCI) if ST-elevation or new LBBB and feasible within 12 h post-op.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Congestive heart failure – Would cause crackles and dyspnea, but shoulder pain and new ECG ischemic changes would be absent; no acute trigger such as volume overload documented.<br /></br>c. Dissecting aortic aneurysm – Produces tearing chest/back pain, asymmetric pulses/BP, possible mediastinal widening; not isolated shoulder pain with pulmonary findings.<br /></br>d. Pulmonary embolism – Sudden dyspnea/pleuritic pain, tachycardia, clear lungs (usually), possible right-heart strain on ECG (S1Q3T3), not left-side crackles.<br /></br>e. Spontaneous pneumothorax – Acute pleuritic pain, unilateral decreased breath sounds &amp; hyperresonance; CXR not crackles.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Question 70</p></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Administration of naloxone</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “1 day after … hip replacement” – inpatient receiving IV opioids.<br /></br>• “Somnolent … slow, shallow breathing,” RR = 8/min, SpO₂ 88% – classic opioid-induced respiratory depression.<br /></br>• Recent “intravenous morphine” with no focal neuro deficits or pulmonary findings; incision is benign.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Common post-op complication = iatrogenic opioid toxicity → hypoventilation → hypoxemia &amp; hypercapnia. Rapid reversal with naloxone (µ-opioid receptor antagonist) is lifesaving.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In any post-operative patient with bradypnea and miosis/AMS who is receiving opioids, give naloxone first.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Morphine binds µ-receptors in medulla → ↓respiratory center sensitivity to CO₂ → hypoventilation → CO₂ retention, hypoxemia, acidosis, CNS depression.</p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>1. Give naloxone 0.04–0.4 mg IV; repeat/infuse to maintain RR &gt;12/min &amp; adequate SpO₂.<br /></br>2. Support airway (jaw thrust, bag-mask) while drug takes effect.<br /></br>3. Monitor for re-sedation (naloxone t½ ~30–60 min; may need infusion given long-acting morphine metabolites).<br /></br>4. Obtain ABG once patient stable to assess hypercapnia.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Acetylcysteine – Antidote for acetaminophen or mucolytic for CF; not useful in opioid toxicity.<br /></br>b. Flumazenil – Benzodiazepine antagonist; risks seizures; patient received no benzos.<br /></br>d. Noninvasive ventilation – Addresses hypoventilation but does not correct underlying opioid effect; always give antidote first.<br /></br>e. Piperacillin-tazobactam – Broad-spectrum antibiotic for sepsis/PNA; patient afebrile, clear lungs, symptoms explained by drugs not infection.</p></br><hr /></br><p>High-Yield Memory Aids<br /></br>• “Post-op shoulder pain + dyspnea = think heart until proved otherwise.”<br /></br>• “RR &lt; 10 in a patient on opioids → naloxone now.”</p>
71. A 34-year-old woman, gravida 3, para 2, at 36 weeks' gestation comes to the office for her first prenatal visit. She has recei ed no prenatal care, because she did not have health insurance until recently. After her last pregnancy 2 years ago, she was diagnosed with hypertension, which has been well controlled with lisinopril since that time. She takes no other medications and has no known allergies. Her pulse is 88/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 130/80 mm Hg. Fundal height is 33 cm. Fetal movement is noted. This patient is at greatest risk for which of the following pregnancy complications?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Abruptio placentae</br>b. Congenital heart block</br>c. Fetal macrosomia</br>d. Neural tube defect</br>e. Oligohydramnios</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Oligohydramnios  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Hypertension … well-controlled with lisinopril” → ACE inhibitor exposure throughout pregnancy.<br /></br>• “First prenatal visit at 36 weeks” → she has remained on lisinopril for the entire pregnancy.<br /></br>• Fundal height 33 cm at 36 wk (lagging) → clue for fetal growth restriction, another ACE-inhibitor effect.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>ACE inhibitors and ARBs are contraindicated after the first trimester because they ↓ fetal renal perfusion → renal tubular dysgenesis → anuria → severe oligohydramnios. The loss of amniotic fluid can lead to Potter sequence (pulmonary hypoplasia, limb contractures, flattened facies) and fetal demise. Therefore the most likely complication is oligohydramnios.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: ACE/ARB use beyond the first trimester → fetal renal failure → oligohydramnios.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: ACEI (e.g., lisinopril), ARB, or direct renin inhibitor exposure after organogenesis.<br /></br>• Mechanism: Block fetal renin-angiotensin system → ↓ glomerular filtration &amp; renal tubular apoptosis → anuria/oliguria → severe oligohydramnios.<br /></br>• Clinical manifestations: Oligohydramnios, IUGR, cranial ossification defects, Potter sequence, neonatal renal failure, neonatal death.  </p></br><p>Management<br /></br>• Stop ACE inhibitor immediately.<br /></br>• Switch chronic maternal HTN therapy to labetalol, long-acting nifedipine, hydralazine, or methyldopa.<br /></br>• Serial ultrasounds for growth and amniotic-fluid index; plan delivery if compromise detected.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Abruptio placentae – Associated with poorly controlled chronic HTN, cocaine, trauma, smoking; not specifically with ACE inhibitors, and her BP is well controlled.<br /></br>b. Congenital heart block – Classically due to maternal anti-Ro/SSA or anti-La/SSB antibodies in SLE/Sjögren syndrome, not ACE inhibitors.<br /></br>c. Fetal macrosomia – Results from maternal diabetes or excessive weight gain; ACE inhibitors cause growth restriction, not macrosomia.<br /></br>d. Neural tube defect – Linked to folate deficiency, valproate, carbamazepine, hyperglycemia; ACE inhibitors have no teratogenic effect on the neural tube.<br /></br>e. Oligohydramnios – Fits the mechanism of ACE-inhibitor fetopathy; therefore correct.</p>
72. A 72-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 6-week history of intermittent rectal bleeding with bowel movements. She underwent a hysterectomy at the age of 42 years for leiomyomata uteri. She has hypertension treated with amlodipine. Rectal examination shows several small external hemorrhoids. Her hematocrit is 31  percent. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Reassurance and follow-up examination in 6 months</br>b. Technetium 99m scan</br>c. Colonoscopy</br>d. Angiography</br>e. Hemorrhoidectomy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Colonoscopy  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “72-year-old woman” – colorectal cancer risk rises sharply after age 50.<br /></br>• “6-week history of intermittent rectal bleeding” – painless, intermittent hematochezia in an elderly patient must be assumed malignant/adenomatous until proven otherwise.<br /></br>• “Hematocrit 31 %” – iron-deficiency anemia from chronic blood loss, again worrisome for colon cancer or angiodysplasia, not trivial hemorrhoids.<br /></br>• “Several small external hemorrhoids” – a common red-herring; small external hemorrhoids rarely produce enough blood loss to cause anemia.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Lower-GI bleeding in a hemodynamically stable patient is worked up with colonoscopy first. The test both localizes the bleeding source and allows therapeutic intervention (polypectomy, cautery, biopsy). Reassurance alone risks missing colorectal carcinoma.
