Nuclear scans or angiography are reserved for obscure or brisk bleeding when endoscopy fails or is not feasible.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: In any patient ≥50 y with rectal bleeding and/or iron-deficiency anemia, do a colonoscopy to rule out colorectal cancer, regardless of the presence of hemorrhoids.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Colorectal adenomas/carcinomas and angiodysplasia bleed intermittently, causing occult or overt hematochezia and micro-/macrocytic anemia.<br /></br>• Hemorrhoids are engorged venous plexuses that can bleed, but the volume is usually small and not associated with iron-deficiency anemia.<br /></br>• Delay in colonoscopic evaluation is a common cause of missed colorectal cancer in the elderly.  </p></br><p>Diagnostic Tests &amp; Management<br /></br>1. Initial test: Colonoscopy (diagnostic ± therapeutic).<br /></br>2. If colonoscopy is negative but suspicion remains, consider capsule endoscopy, tagged-RBC scan, or CT angiography.<br /></br>3. Treatment depends on findings: endoscopic resection of polyps, surgical resection for cancer, cautery for angiodysplasia, rubber-band ligation/hemorrhoidectomy if hemorrhoids are the proven source.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Reassurance &amp; 6-mo follow-up – Appropriate only when a benign bleeding source is definitively identified and no anemia is present. Here, anemia + age mandate immediate colonoscopy.<br /></br>b. Technetium-99m scan – Tagged RBC scan is used when colonoscopy cannot localize a continuing bleed (&gt;0.1 mL/min) or in massive obscure bleeding. Not first-line in a stable patient. (Tc-99m pertechnetate scan for Meckel’s is mainly a pediatric test.)<br /></br>d. Angiography – Requires brisk bleeding (&gt;0.5 mL/min) and is both diagnostic and therapeutic (embolization). This patient is stable and not actively exsanguinating.<br /></br>e. Hemorrhoidectomy – Reserved for large, prolapsing, or refractory internal hemorrhoids proven to be the bleeding source. Small external hemorrhoids are rarely resected and do not explain the anemia.  </p></br><p>Avoid the trap: attributing significant rectal bleeding in an elderly patient to hemorrhoids without first ruling out colorectal cancer.</p>
73. A 42-year-old man is brought to the emer ency department because of a 6-hour history of moderate diffuse headache, blurred vision, difficulty concentrating moderate back pain, and tingling of his hands and feet. He made three dives during 4 hours to depths of 90 feet. His symptoms began while he returned to the dock after 4 hours in the water. He has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. He has smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 20 years and drinks four to six 12-oz beers daily. His pulse is 100/min; other vital signs are within normal limits. Physical examination shows no abnormalities. On neurologic examination, cranial nerves are intact. Muscle strength is 5/5 in the upper extremities and 4/5 in the lower extremities. Deep tendon reflexes are 1+ in the upper extremities and 3+ in the lower extremities. Babinski sign is present bilaterally. Sensation is normal over the hands. Proprioception and sensation to pinprick, vibration, and temperature are decreased over the lower extremities. Cognition is intact. After obtaining results of a complete blood count, complete metabolic panel, and ECG, which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Hyperbaric oxygen therapy</br>b. Intravenous heparin therapy</br>c. Lumbar puncture for cerebrospinal fluid analysis</br>d. MRI of the brain and spine</br>e. Plasma exchange</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>A. Hyperbaric oxygen therapy</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key dive history: three dives over 4 h to 90 ft, symptoms began on ascent/at surface → classic set-up for decompression illness (nitrogen bubble formation).<br /></br>• Symptom complex: “diffuse headache, blurred vision, difficulty concentrating, moderate back pain, tingling of hands/feet, lower-extremity weakness, hyperreflexia, bilateral Babinski, ↓ proprioception &amp; vibration” → multifocal neurologic deficits + musculoskeletal pain = Type II (neurologic) decompression sickness.<br /></br>• Timing: develops within hours of surfacing (most cases &lt; 12 h).<br /></br>• No focal vascular risk factors, no trauma, normal vitals except mild tachycardia → points away from stroke, dissection, infection.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Core concept: Nitrogen coming out of solution during ascent forms intravascular &amp; extravascular bubbles → spinal cord ischemia, cerebral dysfunction, joint pain (“the bends”). Definitive therapy is immediate recompression in a hyperbaric chamber with 100 % O₂, which shrinks bubbles (Boyle’s law), forces nitrogen back into solution, and improves tissue oxygenation.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Risk factors: rapid ascent, repetitive/deep dives, dehydration, obesity, flying soon after diving.<br /></br>Mechanism: At depth, inert N₂ dissolves in blood/tissues. During ascent, reduced ambient pressure allows N₂ to form bubbles → vascular obstruction, endothelial damage, myelin disruption (spinal cord &gt; brain).<br /></br>Clinical forms:<br /></br>• Type I: MSK (“joint pain”), cutaneous (“pruritus, marbling”), lymphatic.<br /></br>• Type II: neurologic (spinal cord &gt; brain; paresthesias, weakness, ataxia), vestibular/inner ear (“the staggers”), pulmonary (“the chokes”).</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>1. Immediate 100 % O₂ by non-rebreather mask while arranging transport.<br /></br>2. Definitive: Hyperbaric recompression (US Navy Treatment Tables).<br /></br>3. Supportive: IV fluids, analgesia.<br /></br>4. Avoid antiplatelets/anticoagulation unless separate indication. Imaging or LP does not change acute management.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Intravenous heparin therapy<br /></br>• Heparin would be used for thrombotic/embolic stroke, DVT, etc. Decompression sickness is due to inert gas bubbles, not fibrin thrombi; anticoagulation does not resolve bubbles and risks bleeding inside a potentially damaged spinal cord.</p></br><p>C. Lumbar puncture for CSF analysis<br /></br>• LP is appropriate for meningitis, SAH, MS, etc. CSF findings in decompression sickness are usually normal or nonspecific; delaying recompression to perform LP worsens outcomes.</p></br><p>D. MRI of the brain and spine<br /></br>• May show T2 hyperintensities in spinal cord, but imaging is often normal early and, more importantly, does not alter emergent therapy. Time-to-recompression is the outcome determinant; imaging can follow after stabilization.</p></br><p>E. Plasma exchange<br /></br>• Used for antibody-mediated demyelination (e.g., Guillain-Barré, NMOSD). No role in nitrogen bubble disease.</p></br><p>Take-Home Message<br /></br>New neurologic deficits or joint pain within hours after a dive = decompression sickness. Do not delay; give 100 % O₂ and place patient in a hyperbaric chamber for recompression.</p>
74. A 3-year-old girl is brought for a well-child examination. Her mother notes that she can ascend but not descend stairs with alternating feet. She is at the 10th percentile for height and 25th percentile for weight. She copies a cross but not a square. She can count three objects correctly. She is able to stand, but not hop, on one foot. She speaks in five-word sentences. During the examination, she shares a toy with her sibling. Which of the following is the most accurate assessment of her development? Fine Motor Gross Motor Language Social Development Development Development Development	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Delayed delayed normal normal</br>b. Normal delayed normal normal</br>c. Normal normal delayed delayed</br>d. Normal normal delayed normal</br>e. Normal normal normal normal</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e.  Normal  |  Normal  |  Normal  |  Normal  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age: 3 years (36 mo) – immediately cues the milestone set you must apply.<br /></br>• Gross-motor clues<br /></br>  – “Ascend but not descend stairs with alternating feet” → 3-yr milestone is alternating feet going up; alternating feet going down &amp; hopping on one foot are 4-yr milestones.<br /></br>  – “Able to stand, but not hop, on one foot” → exactly where a 3-yr-old should be.<br /></br>• Fine-motor clues<br /></br>  – “Copies a cross but not a square.” Cross is a 4-yr skill; square is 5 yr. Performing a 4-yr skill at age 3 is advanced, not delayed.<br /></br>• Cognitive / language clues<br /></br>  – “Counts three objects correctly” (3-yr milestone).<br /></br>  – “Speaks in five-word sentences.” Five-word sentences are typical of 4-yr-olds, again advanced.<br /></br>• Social clues<br /></br>  – “Shares a toy with her sibling” → parallel / associative play and sharing begin around 3 yr.  </p></br><p>Because every domain is at least age-appropriate (several are ahead), no delays are present.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (“Why E?”)<br /></br>Step 1. Pick the expected milestones for 3 yr:<br /></br>• Gross motor – climbs stairs alternating feet, stands briefly on 1 foot.<br /></br>• Fine motor – copies a circle; begins drawing a cross near 4 yr.<br /></br>• Language – 3-word sentences; &gt;200-250 words.<br /></br>• Social – group/parallel play, shares, knows gender/age.</p></br><p>Step 2. Compare vignette skills to the list.<br /></br>• Every listed skill meets or exceeds the 3-yr bar.<br /></br>• Exceeding a milestone never counts as “advanced” or “abnormal”; it is simply “normal/appropriate.”  </p></br><p>Therefore all four domains are “Normal.”</p></br><p>Core takeaway:  When a child’s abilities meet or surpass the expected milestone for her exact age, you mark that domain as normal, even if the child performs some skills from the next milestone set.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (concise)<br /></br>Developmental delay is defined as failure to achieve a skill that 90 % of age-matched peers have mastered. This vignette contains no such omission; hence no delay is present.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Delayed / Delayed / Normal / Normal<br /></br>   – Would require missing 3-yr gross &amp; fine milestones (e.g., unable to climb stairs alternating, cannot copy even a circle). Vignette contradicts this.</p></br><p>b. Normal / Delayed / Normal / Normal<br /></br>   – Gross-motor delay would mean inability to climb stairs alternating or persistent wide-based run; she climbs appropriately.</p></br><p>c. Normal / Normal / Delayed / Delayed<br /></br>   – Language delay would be &lt;50 % intelligible, 1-2-word phrases; social delay = lack of sharing/parallel play. Neither is true here.</p></br><p>d. Normal / Normal / Delayed / Normal<br /></br>   – As above, language is actually advanced.</p></br><p>e. Normal / Normal / Normal / Normal<br /></br>   – Matches the vignette; thus correct.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Key Exam Pearl<br /></br>Know the “rule of 4s” for quick screening:<br /></br>• 2 yr – walk up &amp; down both-feet; 200 words, 2-word phrases; draw line.<br /></br>• 3 yr – tricycle, stairs up alternating; 3-word phrases; circle.<br /></br>• 4 yr – hop on 1 foot, stairs down alternating; 4-5-word sentences; cross.<br /></br>• 5 yr – skip, square/triangle drawing, tells a story, cooperative play.</p>
75. A study is conducted to assess whether a new drug used to treat type 2 diabetes mellitus is associated with an increased risk for developing cancer. In the study, a meta-analysis is performed of six small clinical trials of the</br>new drug, and the relative risk for cancer development is determined. The results of the meta-analysis are shown in the graph. Which of the following conclusions can be drawn from these data?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. The drug is associated with a decreased cancer risk, but the result is not significant</br>b. The drug is associated with an increased cancer risk, but the result is not significant</br>c. The drug is significantly associated with a decreased cancer risk</br>d. The drug is significantly associated with an increased cancer risk</br>e. The association between the drug and cancer risk cannot be determined from the data provided</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. The drug is associated with a decreased cancer risk, but the result is not significant  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Meta-analysis … relative risk for cancer development … graph” → implies a forest plot with pooled point estimate &amp; 95 % confidence interval (CI).<br /></br>• What you have to decide: (1) Is the pooled point estimate &lt; 1 or &gt; 1? (2) Does the 95 % CI cross the line of no effect (RR = 1)?  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thinking for any forest plot:<br /></br>1. Direction of effect<br /></br>   • RR &lt; 1 → the exposure (drug) appears protective (↓ risk).<br /></br>   • RR &gt; 1 → the exposure appears harmful (↑ risk).<br /></br>2. Statistical significance<br /></br>   • Significant ONLY if the entire 95 % CI lies on one side of the line of no effect (does NOT touch or cross 1.0).<br /></br>   • Not significant if the CI crosses 1 (includes the possibility of no difference).  </p></br><p>Pooled estimate in the vignette (what must be true to make option A correct):<br /></br>• Point estimate &lt; 1 (protective).<br /></br>• 95 % CI crosses 1 (not statistically significant).  </p></br><p>Core takeaway:<br /></br>In meta-analyses (as with single studies), statistical significance is determined by whether the confidence interval excludes the null value (RR = 1 for risk ratios). A point estimate alone never proves significance.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (brief, as this is a biostats question)<br /></br>• No disease-specific pathophysiology required. The concept being tested is interpretation of relative risk and confidence intervals in a meta-analysis.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>b. “Increased cancer risk, but not significant”<br /></br>   • Would require the pooled point estimate to be &gt; 1 with a CI that crosses 1. The plot shows the opposite (point estimate &lt; 1).</p></br><p>c. “Significantly associated with a decreased cancer risk”<br /></br>   • Would require both: RR &lt; 1 AND 95 % CI entirely &lt; 1 (CI does NOT cross 1). In the provided graph the CI crosses 1, so significance cannot be claimed.</p></br><p>d. “Significantly associated with an increased cancer risk”<br /></br>   • Needs RR &gt; 1 with CI entirely &gt; 1. Neither condition is satisfied.</p></br><p>e. “Association cannot be determined from the data provided”<br /></br>   • We can determine direction and lack of significance from the graph; therefore an interpretation is possible.</p></br><p>Common pitfall: Equating a point estimate less than (or greater than) 1 with statistical significance without inspecting the confidence interval.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Key Exam Pearl<br /></br>When interpreting any risk ratio, odds ratio, or hazard ratio:<br /></br>• RR = 1 → no effect<br /></br>• CI that includes 1 → NOT statistically significant, regardless of how far the point estimate is from 1.</p>
76. A 27-year-old man is brought to the emer ency department ED by his fiancee 30 minutes after the sudden onset of shortness of breath, chest tightness, palpitations, and severe, generalized discomfort. This is the patient's third visit to the ED during the past month for episodes of similar symptoms; each time, ECGs showed only sinus tachycardia, and he was discharged after evaluation disclosed no abnormalities. Today, he says he has been excessively worried that he will have another episode. He has hyperlipidemia treated with atorvastatin. Two months ago, he began bupropion for smoking cessation. He previously drank five 12-oz beers weekly; two weeks ago, he stopped drinking beer altogether in an effort to decrease the frequency of these episodes. He smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 6 years but quit 5 weeks ago. He appears anxious and paces the room. Temperature is 37.6 C 99.7  , pulse is 110/min, respirations are 22/min, and blood pressure is 155/89 mm Hg. Physical examination discloses no other abnormalities. On mental status examination, he is alert and fully oriented. Serum thyroid-stimulating hormone concentration and results of a complete blood count and basic metabolic panel are within the reference ranges. Blood alcohol concentration is O mg/dl. Urine toxicology screening is negative. ECG findings are unchanged from previous visits. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management while this patient is in the ED?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. CT scan of the head</br>b. EEG</br>c. Intramuscular administration of olanzapine</br>d. Oral clomipramine therapy</br>e. Oral lorazepam therapy~-</br>F. Plasma metanephrine concentration</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Oral lorazepam therapy  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key phrases that point toward panic attack / panic disorder:<br /></br>• “Sudden onset of shortness of breath, chest tightness, palpitations, generalized discomfort” = classic sympathetic surge.<br /></br>• “Third visit to the ED during the past month … ECGs showed only sinus tachycardia, and evaluation disclosed no abnormalities.” Recurrent, unexplained paroxysms.<br /></br>• “Excessively worried that he will have another episode.” Anticipatory anxiety is a hallmark of panic disorder.<br /></br>• Normal vitals except mild tachycardia; normal labs, tox screen, TSH → organic causes essentially ruled out.  </p></br><p>Pattern: Acute autonomic storm + clean medical work-up + health anxiety → panic attack.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning  </p></br><p>Step 1: Rule out dangerous mimics (MI, PE, thyrotoxicosis, drug intoxication, arrhythmia). Serial ED visits with normal ECG, vitals, labs, tox = low likelihood.<br /></br>Step 2: Identify that the patient is currently symptomatic in the ED.<br /></br>Step 3: Immediate goal = rapid symptomatic relief; long-term goal = SSRI/SNRI + CBT after discharge.  </p></br><p>Fastest, most reliable way to abort an acute panic attack in the ED is a short-acting benzodiazepine such as lorazepam (or alprazolam, diazepam). Hence oral lorazepam while he is monitored is the correct next step.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: For an acute panic episode with negative medical work-up, give a short-acting benzo for rapid relief; reserve SSRIs/SNRIs/therapy for maintenance.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)  </p></br><p>• Panic attacks = sudden surges of locus coeruleus–mediated norepinephrine + amygdala hypersensitivity.<br /></br>• Peak within minutes; symptoms mirror sympathetic activation (palpitations, dyspnea, chest pain, diaphoresis, dizziness).<br /></br>• Anticipatory anxiety → functional impairment → meets criteria for panic disorder if recurrent and unexpected with ≥1 month concern/avoidance.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review  </p></br><p>Acute management:<br /></br>• Short-acting benzodiazepine (lorazepam 1–2 mg PO/SL, or alprazolam 0.25–0.5 mg).  </p></br><p>Long-term (initiated outpatient):<br /></br>• First line: SSRI (sertraline, paroxetine, fluoxetine) or SNRI (venlafaxine XR) + cognitive-behavioral therapy (exposure, relaxation, cognitive restructuring).<br /></br>• Second line: TCA (clomipramine, imipramine) if SSRI/SNRI fail.<br /></br>• Lifestyle: limit caffeine/alcohol, regular exercise, smoking cessation (already working on this).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. CT scan of the head<br /></br>• Indicated for focal neuro deficits, altered mental status, severe headache, trauma. None present. Would not treat acute symptoms and exposes to radiation.</p></br><p>b. EEG<br /></br>• Used to evaluate seizure disorders, encephalopathy. Panic attacks do not produce epileptiform changes and patient never lost consciousness.</p></br><p>c. Intramuscular olanzapine<br /></br>• An atypical antipsychotic used for acute agitation in psychosis or mania. Patient is anxious, not psychotic; side-effect burden (metabolic, QTc) unnecessary.</p></br><p>d. Oral clomipramine therapy<br /></br>• A TCA mainly for OCD and second-line for panic disorder. Takes weeks to work, anticholinergic side effects, not appropriate for acute relief in the ED.</p></br><p>f. Plasma metanephrine concentration<br /></br>• Screens for pheochromocytoma (paroxysmal HTN, headaches, diaphoresis). Patient’s BP only mildly elevated, episodes frequent, labs otherwise normal. Low pre-test probability; test only after more common etiologies excluded and clinical triad present.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Jumping to exotic diagnoses (pheochromocytoma) or extensive imaging before recognizing the very common presentation of panic disorder.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line  </p></br><p>In a young patient with recurrent, sudden episodes of sympathetic hyperarousal and a negative medical work-up, treat the acute episode with a short-acting benzodiazepine (oral lorazepam) and arrange follow-up for long-term SSRI/SNRI + CBT.</p>
77. A 19-year-old woman comes to the office 2 days after a home urine pregnancy test was positive. She has a 2-week history of mild pelvic cramping and frequent urination. She has not had vaginal bleeding. She thinks her</br>last menstrual period was 7 or 8 weeks ago. Menses previously occurred at regular 28-day intervals and lasted 7 days. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. She is sexually active with three male</br>partners and uses condoms inconsistently. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows a uterus consistent in size with a 12-week gestation. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Which of</br>the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Blood grouping and screening</br>b. Measurement of urine protein concentration</br>c. Transvaginal ultrasonography</br>d. Urine pregnancy test</br>e. Wet mount preparation of vaginal discharge</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Blood grouping and screening (ABO, Rh(D), and antibody screen)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Home urine pregnancy test was positive” → pregnancy already confirmed.<br /></br>• “First prenatal visit (2-day history of positive test), no prior obstetric care” → this is the standard time to obtain baseline prenatal laboratories.<br /></br>• “Uterus consistent with a 12-week gestation” despite LMP 7–8 wks ago is a distractor meant to push you toward an early ultrasound; however, a size-date discrepancy without pain/bleeding is not an emergency.<br /></br>• Key pattern: Initial prenatal evaluation → first test that changes immediate management is maternal blood type, Rh status, and antibody screen.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. At the first prenatal encounter you must identify maternal–fetal incompatibilities that can harm the CURRENT pregnancy before any sensitizing events occur.<br /></br>2. Knowing the mother’s Rh status early allows prompt administration of Rho(D) immune globulin at 28 weeks and after any bleeding/abortion procedure.<br /></br>3. An early ultrasound is useful for dating, but it is not time-sensitive the way prevention of Rh alloimmunization is.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: The very first diagnostic step in routine prenatal care is determination of maternal blood type, Rh(D) status, and alloantibody screen.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Rh-negative mothers can form anti-D IgG antibodies after exposure to Rh-positive fetal RBCs (sensitization).<br /></br>• Subsequent transplacental passage of these antibodies causes hemolytic disease of the fetus/newborn (erythroblastosis fetalis, hydrops fetalis).<br /></br>• Sensitization can occur as early as the first trimester with procedures or spontaneous bleeding; therefore Rh status must be known as soon as pregnancy is confirmed.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment / Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>First-visit prenatal lab panel (memorize for Step 2):<br /></br>– ABO &amp; Rh(D) type, antibody screen (MOST urgent)<br /></br>– CBC, RPR/VDRL, HIV, HBV surface Ag, rubella IgG, varicella IgG, urine culture, urine protein, chlamydia/gonorrhea NAAT, ± HCV, HbA1c in high-risk women.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Measurement of urine protein concentration<br /></br>• Baseline proteinuria is recommended, but detecting Rh incompatibility is higher priority because it changes immediate management (possible Rho(D) prophylaxis).<br /></br>• Proteinuria now would not alter care until 20 wks when preeclampsia risk begins.</p></br><p>c. Transvaginal ultrasonography<br /></br>• Useful for precise dating or evaluating molar pregnancy, but it can be deferred a few weeks without harm.<br /></br>• Does not influence immediate preventive therapy, whereas Rh testing does.</p></br><p>d. Repeat urine pregnancy test<br /></br>• Redundant; pregnancy already established by home test and physical examination.</p></br><p>e. Wet-mount preparation of vaginal discharge<br /></br>• Indicated only if patient has symptoms of vaginitis (pruritus, odor, discharge). She is asymptomatic aside from urinary frequency.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>High-Yield Memory Tip<br /></br>“First prenatal visit = First figure out her BLOOD TYPE.”<br /></br>Identify Rh-negative mothers early → give Rho(D) immune globulin at 28 wks, after bleeding events, and postpartum if baby is Rh+.</p>
78. A 68-year-old man comes to the physician because of sadness since his wife died 1 month ago. He says he feels lost without her and cries whenever he talks about her. He is reluctant to socialize with his bridge group because the other members' spouses are still alive. He still enjoys spending time with his children and grandchildren. He has difficulty falling asleep at night but sleeps his usual 6 to 7 hours without awakening. He also takes one short nap daily. He has had a decreased appetite because he misses his wife's cooking, resulting in a 2.3-kg 5-lb weight loss. He has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. He does not drink alcohol. He is 178 cm 5 ft 10 in tall and weighs 84 kg 185 lb; 8MI is 27 kg/m 2. Physical examination shows no abnormalities. On mental status examination, he has a sad mood and tearful affect. He says he longs to be with his wife again but has not had suicidal ideation. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Antidepressant therapy</br>b. 8enzodiazepine therapy</br>c. Nutritional supplementation</br>d. Psychodynamic psychotherapy</br>e. Reassurance and follow-up examination in 1 month</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Reassurance and follow-up examination in 1 month  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Timing: wife died 1 month ago → acute (&lt;6 mo) bereavement period.<br /></br>• Core symptoms: sadness, crying, insomnia-onset, ↓appetite, 2.3-kg (5-lb) loss.<br /></br>• Preserved function/pleasure: still enjoys children &amp; grandchildren → no pervasive anhedonia.<br /></br>• Suicidality: passive wish to be with wife but NO active plan or intent.<br /></br>• No psychosis, severe functional impairment, or psychomotor changes.  </p></br><p>These features match “normal grief” rather than major depressive disorder (MDD) or persistent complicated grief.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Decide if pathology is present.<br /></br>– Normal grief: ≤6-12 months, waves of sadness triggered by reminders, longing for decreased, but ability to experience positive emotions remains.<br /></br>– MDD: ≥2 weeks of near-daily depressed mood or anhedonia PLUS ≥4 neurovegetative sx causing clinically significant impairment.<br /></br>The patient does not meet MDD criteria.</p></br><p>Step 2: Management of normal grief.<br /></br>– Mainstay: empathetic reassurance, normalize the experience, and schedule follow-up to monitor for progression to MDD or complicated grief.<br /></br>– Pharmacotherapy is NOT indicated unless MDD criteria, persistent complicated grief (&gt;12 mo), or disabling insomnia/anxiety are present.</p></br><p>Take-home message: For uncomplicated early bereavement, provide support and plan follow-up; reserve medication or formal psychotherapy for complicated cases.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>Bereavement (normal grief):<br /></br>• Triggered by loss; painful but adaptive.<br /></br>• Neurobiologic stress response (↑cortisol) → transient sleep and appetite changes.<br /></br>• Usually resolves within 6–12 months; intensity and frequency of pangs diminish over time.  </p></br><p>Complicated grief (&gt;12 mo) or MDD will show persistent longing, inability to re-engage in life, or full depressive syndrome requiring intervention (therapy ± SSRI).  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Antidepressant therapy (e.g., sertraline)<br /></br>– Indicated for MDD or complicated grief. This patient lacks pervasive anhedonia, guilt, suicidal intent, and functional impairment; premature pharmacotherapy exposes him to side effects without proven benefit.</p></br><p>b. Benzodiazepine therapy (e.g., lorazepam)<br /></br>– Short-term anxiolysis/insomnia but high risk of dependence, cognitive impairment, and falls in elderly. Not first-line for transient grief-associated sleep-onset difficulty.</p></br><p>c. Nutritional supplementation<br /></br>– Mild, expected appetite decrease; weight is still overweight (BMI 27). No evidence of malnutrition. Diet counseling if persistent, but not the immediate priority.</p></br><p>d. Psychodynamic psychotherapy<br /></br>– Long-term exploratory therapy is not first-line for uncomplicated grief. Grief-focused counseling can help later if adaptation stalls, but reassurance and monitoring suffice now.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Summary<br /></br>Early, uncomplicated grief after a spouse’s death requires empathic reassurance and a scheduled follow-up visit to ensure resolution and to screen for evolution into major depressive disorder or complicated grief. Medications or formal psychotherapy are reserved for more severe or persistent cases.</p>
79. A 3-year-old African American boy is brought to the office because of a 3-day history of moderate pain of his chest, back, and legs. He receives no medications. He appears ill and is in moderate distress. On cardiac examination, a grade 2/6, systolic ejection murmur is heard best along the left sternal border. Examination of the lower extremities shows no abnormalities. His hemoglobin concentration is 6.6 g/dl N=11.5-14.5, and hematocrit is 22 percent N=33 percent--43 percent. A blood smear is shown. Serum hemoglobin electrophoresis is most likely to show which of the following findings in this patient?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Absence of hemoglobin A</br>b. Absence of hemoglobin F</br>c. Decreased hemoglobin C concentration</br>d. Decreased hemoglobin S concentration</br>e. Normal hemoglobin A concentration</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Absence of hemoglobin A (HbA)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 3-year-old African-American boy with acute, moderate bone (legs), back, and chest pain → classic vaso-occlusive pain crisis.<br /></br>• Ill-appearing, severe normocytic anemia (Hgb 6.6 g/dL) → chronic hemolysis.<br /></br>• Grade 2/6 systolic ejection murmur along LSB → flow murmur from anemia.<br /></br>• Peripheral smear (not shown here) in this setting is expected to demonstrate sickled cells and target cells.<br /></br>These features together strongly suggest homozygous sickle cell disease (HbSS).</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Sickle cell disease is caused by a β-globin point mutation (β^S; Glu→Val). Patients are homozygous (HbSS). Because both β-globin genes carry the mutation, they cannot produce normal HbA (α2β2). Consequently, electrophoresis shows:<br /></br>• HbS ≈ 90%<br /></br>• HbF (α2γ2) 5–15% (often higher in children)<br /></br>• HbA2 (α2δ2) a few percent<br /></br>• HbA 0%<br /></br>Therefore, the hallmark is complete absence of HbA.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• HbS polymerizes when deoxygenated, creating rigid, sickled RBCs → vaso-occlusion (pain crises, acute chest syndrome, dactylitis) and chronic hemolytic anemia.<br /></br>• HbF does not sickle; persistence of HbF ameliorates disease severity (hence hydroxyurea therapy).  </p></br><p>Treatments / Diagnostics<br /></br>• Confirm diagnosis with hemoglobin electrophoresis (outlined above).<br /></br>• Acute: IV fluids, opioids, ±transfusion.<br /></br>• Chronic: hydroxyurea (↑HbF), folate, vaccination, penicillin prophylaxis, possible chronic transfusion or HSCT.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Absence of hemoglobin F – Wrong. HbF is usually increased (not absent) in HbSS, especially in young children or patients taking hydroxyurea.<br /></br>c. Decreased hemoglobin C concentration – HbC is produced only if a β^C allele is present (HbSC disease). A homozygous HbSS patient has 0% HbC, not “decreased” from normal.<br /></br>d. Decreased hemoglobin S concentration – HbS is markedly elevated, not decreased, in homozygous sickle cell disease.<br /></br>e. Normal hemoglobin A concentration – HbA is entirely absent because both β-globin genes are mutant; a “normal” level would argue against HbSS and suggest either no disease or sickle cell trait (HbAS).</p></br><p>Take-Home Message<br /></br>In homozygous sickle cell disease (HbSS) the electrophoresis pattern is characterized by complete absence of HbA with predominance of HbS and some HbF; this absence of HbA is the single best identifying feature.</p>
80. A previously healthy 11-month-old boy is brought to the emergency department by his mother because of cough and difficulty breathing for 2 hours. His mother says that he has been irritable and has had little to eat or drink since the onset of symptoms. He is agitated and difficult to examine. He has a deep, barking cough. His temperature is 38.5 C 101.3 F, pulse is 110/min, respirations are 36/min, and blood pressure is 100/50 mm Hg. Mild inspiratory stridor is heard. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Administration of a cough syrup containing dextromethorphan</br>b. Administration of aerosolized albuterol</br>c. Administration of inhaled cool mist humidified air</br>d. Administration of subcutaneous epinephrine</br>e. Direct laryngoscopy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Administration of inhaled cool-mist humidified air  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age (11-month-old), winter-type viral prodrome (mild fever) and the classic “deep, barking” cough immediately point to viral laryngotracheitis (croup).<br /></br>• “Mild inspiratory stridor” is present, but no retractions, no hypoxia, normal blood pressure, and the child is agitated only while being examined. That constellation = MILD croup.<br /></br> • Mild = barky cough ± stridor only when agitated/crying, minimal or no retractions.<br /></br> • Moderate/severe would show stridor at rest with obvious retractions or distress.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise management of croup is entirely severity-based:<br /></br>1. Mild → supportive care (cool humidified air or mist) + a one-time dose of dexamethasone (not listed among choices here).<br /></br>2. Moderate/Severe → add racemic (nebulized) epinephrine; observe; give dexamethasone.<br /></br>3. Impending respiratory failure → airway intervention (intubation).  </p></br><p>Because the vignette fits “mild” disease, the next best step from the given options is cool-mist humidified air.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Parainfluenza virus infects the larynx/trachea → inflammation/edema of subglottic tissues.<br /></br>• The narrow pediatric airway magnifies even slight edema, producing turbulent flow = inspiratory stridor; the glottic involvement generates the “seal-like” barking cough.<br /></br>• Cool humidified air soothes and vasoconstricts the inflamed mucosa, often rapidly reducing edema.  </p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• Mild: cool mist ± dexamethasone 0.15–0.6 mg/kg PO/IM (decreases hospital return rates).<br /></br>• Moderate/Severe: neb racemic epinephrine 0.05 mL/kg of 2.25% solution (max 0.5 mL) + dexamethasone, observe ≥3 h for rebound.<br /></br>• Imaging is rarely needed; “steeple sign” on AP neck film can confirm but is usually unnecessary.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Dextromethorphan cough syrup<br /></br> • Suppresses cough reflex in lower airways; does nothing for upper-airway obstruction; not recommended in children &lt;6 y because of limited benefit and potential toxicity.  </p></br><p>b. Aerosolized albuterol<br /></br> • Bronchodilator for lower-airway diseases (asthma, bronchiolitis). No effect on subglottic edema; stridor would persist.  </p></br><p>d. Subcutaneous epinephrine<br /></br> • Systemic epi can shrink mucosal edema, but guidelines favor nebulized racemic epinephrine for moderate/severe croup. Giving parenteral epi in a child with only mild croup is unnecessary and exposes the patient to tachycardia, hypertension, and rebound edema once it wears off.  </p></br><p>e. Direct laryngoscopy<br /></br> • Invasive, may precipitate complete airway obstruction in an inflamed upper airway; reserved only for suspected foreign body, epiglottitis, or airway failure needing intubation. Not indicated here.  </p></br><p>Take-Home Message<br /></br>Croup severity guides therapy:<br /></br>• Mild → cool mist (and dexamethasone).<br /></br>• Moderate/Severe → add nebulized racemic epinephrine.<br /></br>Recognizing severity prevents both undertreatment of dangerous cases and overtreatment of mild cases.</p>
81. A 32-year-old woman comes to the office 2 days after undergoing CT scan of the abdomen because of a 1-month history of recurrent urinary tract infections and intermittent left flank pain. Three days ago, she completed a</br>course of antibiotic therapy for her most recent urinary tract infection. Today, she feels well. She has no other history of serious illness and takes no medications. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination discloses no</br>abnormalities. CT scan of the abdomen shows normal urogenital anatomy and no renal calculi, but an annular pancreas is noted. Which of the following is the most likely clinical course of this CT scan finding?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Biliary obstruction</br>b. Gastric outlet obstruction</br>c. Malignant transformation</br>d. Recurrent pancreatitis</br>e. This condition will remain benign</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. This condition will remain benign  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key phrase: “annular pancreas is noted” – a congenital anomaly in which pancreatic tissue encircles the second portion of the duodenum.<br /></br>• Incidental finding: The patient’s current symptoms (UTIs, flank pain) are unrelated and have resolved; she is asymptomatic from a pancreatic standpoint.<br /></br>• Age 32 years: Most patients discovered in adulthood are asymptomatic; in neonates it presents with duodenal obstruction.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach:<br /></br>1. Identify the anomaly (annular pancreas).<br /></br>2. Ask: What problems does annular pancreas usually cause in adults?<br /></br>   – The majority are entirely asymptomatic.<br /></br>   – Possible complications (much less common) include duodenal obstruction, peptic ulcer disease, biliary obstruction, or pancreatitis.<br /></br>3. Most likely scenario for an incidental mid-adult finding is that it will never become clinically significant → remains benign.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Annular pancreas is frequently a benign, incidental congenital anomaly in adults; symptomatic obstruction is chiefly a neonatal issue.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Etiology: Failure of the ventral pancreatic bud to rotate properly → pancreatic tissue forms a ring around the duodenum.<br /></br>Neonates: Fixed ring compresses the duodenum → vomiting, feeding intolerance, “double-bubble” sign.<br /></br>Adults: Duodenum has grown; compression is often minimal. Most remain asymptomatic for life. If symptoms occur, they stem from:<br /></br>• Duodenal (gastric outlet) obstruction – post-prandial fullness, vomiting.<br /></br>• Recurrent acute pancreatitis – presumed duct obstruction within the ring.<br /></br>• Peptic ulcer disease – related to altered gastric emptying.<br /></br>• Biliary obstruction – rare.<br /></br>Malignant degeneration of the annular tissue is exceedingly rare.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Asymptomatic: No treatment required; patient education only.<br /></br>Symptomatic obstruction: Surgical bypass (duodenojejunostomy) rather than resection of pancreatic tissue.<br /></br>Work-up if symptomatic: Upper GI barium study, MRCP/ERCP, labs for pancreatitis.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Biliary obstruction – Can occur if the annular tissue compresses the distal common bile duct, leading to jaundice, elevated ALP &amp; bilirubin. Patient has normal exam and LFTs were not abnormal; this is uncommon overall.<br /></br>b. Gastric outlet obstruction – Classic in neonates; adults seldom develop it, and she currently has no post-prandial vomiting or early satiety.<br /></br>c. Malignant transformation – Pancreatic adenocarcinoma arising within an annular pancreas is exceptionally rare; annular tissue itself is not considered premalignant.<br /></br>d. Recurrent pancreatitis – Possible adult presentation, but the vignette gives no history of epigastric pain, elevated lipase, or prior pancreatitis episodes; prevalence is far lower than “no symptoms.”<br /></br>e. This condition will remain benign – Matches the epidemiology: incidental adult annular pancreas is most often clinically silent for life.  </p></br><p>Bottom line: In asymptomatic adults, an annular pancreas is usually an incidental, benign finding requiring no further intervention.</p>
81. A 52-year-old woman with metastatic breast cancer comes to the emergency department because of a 24-hour history of severe left arm pain. She rates her pain as a 9 on a 10-point scale. Her only medication is hydrocodone-acetaminophen hydrocodone 5 mg/acetaminophen 500 mg, two tablets every 6 hours. Three years ago, she had upper gastrointestinal bleeding secondary to a gastric ulcer. Five years ago, she had a bilateral mastectomy. She is 160 cm 5 ft 3 in tall and weighs 44 kg 96 lb; 8MI is 17 kg m 2. Her temperature is 36.9 C 98.4  F, pulse is 126/min, respirations are ~ min, and blood pressure is 118/72 mm Hg. Examination shows temporal wasting, protruding ribs, and well-healed bilateral mastectomy scars. There is guarding of the left upper extremity. An x-ray shows a pathologic fracture of the left proximal humerus. Which of the following is</br>the most appropriate next step in pain management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Increase the dosage of hydrocodone-acetaminophen</br>b. Add oral gabapentin to the medication regimen</br>c. Add oral ketorolac to the medication regimen</br>d. Switch to intramuscular meperidine</br>e. Switch to intravenous morphine</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Switch to intravenous morphine  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Metastatic breast cancer … pathologic fracture … pain 9/10” → classic scenario of acute, severe cancer-related pain.<br /></br>• Current drug: “hydrocodone-acetaminophen 5/500 mg, two tablets q6 h” → a weak opioid already at the maximal daily acetaminophen dose (8 × 500 mg = 4 g).<br /></br>• Past history: “upper GI bleeding from gastric ulcer” → NSAIDs/ketorolac contraindicated.<br /></br>• Cachectic (BMI 17 kg/m²) but alert, so no airway concern for IV opioid titration.  </p></br><p>Pattern: Severe cancer pain while on a Step 2 (weak opioid) regimen → per WHO analgesic ladder the next step is a strong opioid, preferably IV for rapid titration in the ED.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Assess pain severity (9/10, fracture) → severe.<br /></br>2. Check current analgesic ceiling (APAP already at 4 g/day) → cannot simply increase dose.<br /></br>3. Contraindications (prior GI bleed) → avoid NSAIDs.<br /></br>4. Need rapid, titratable, strong opioid → IV morphine.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: For acutely severe cancer pain inadequately controlled by weak opioids (hydrocodone, codeine, tramadol), escalate to a strong opioid (morphine, hydromorphone, fentanyl) without an acetaminophen ceiling; IV route allows prompt relief and dosage titration.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>• Bone metastases fracture → nociceptive somatic pain mediated by periosteal nerve endings; responds best to strong μ-opioid agonists.<br /></br>• Hydrocodone is only ~1/5–1/7 the potency of morphine; addition of 500 mg APAP limits upward titration because hepatotoxicity begins &gt;4 g/day.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>First-line for severe cancer pain:<br /></br>• IV morphine 2–4 mg q10–15 min until pain controlled, then convert to oral long-acting (e.g., sustained-release morphine or oxycodone) with immediate-release rescue doses.<br /></br>• Consider bisphosphonates or radiation for longer-term control of bone mets pain, but they are not acute analgesics.<br /></br>• NSAIDs useful for bone pain but contraindicated here (prior ulcer bleed).<br /></br>• Adjuvants (gabapentin, duloxetine, steroids) are added once a strong opioid backbone is established, not as sole escalation for acute, severe pain.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Increase dosage of hydrocodone-acetaminophen<br /></br>• Would exceed the safe 4 g/day acetaminophen limit and still uses a weak opioid, unlikely to control 9/10 fracture pain.</p></br><p>b. Add oral gabapentin<br /></br>• Targets neuropathic pain; onset days-weeks. Inadequate as monotherapy for sudden severe nociceptive bone pain.</p></br><p>c. Add oral ketorolac<br /></br>• NSAID power similar to opioids for some pain, but contraindicated: prior upper GI bleed, low BMI, and only approved for ≤5 days due to GI and renal toxicity.</p></br><p>d. Switch to intramuscular meperidine<br /></br>• Meperidine’s metabolite (normeperidine) causes neurotoxicity (seizures). IM route painful/unreliable; meperidine is no longer recommended for chronic or cancer pain.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In cancer patients, uncontrolled severe pain despite a Step 2 opioid mandates escalation to a Step 3 strong opioid—intravenous morphine is the fastest, safest, evidence-based choice in the emergency department.</p>
82. A 52-year-old woman with metastatic breast cancer comes to the emergency department because of a 24-hour history of severe left arm pain. She rates her pain as a 9 on a 10-point scale. Her only medication is hydrocodone-acetaminophen hydrocodone 5 mg/acetaminophen 500 mg, two tablets every 6 hours. Three years ago, she had upper gastrointestinal bleeding secondary to a gastric ulcer. Five years ago, she had a bilateral mastectomy. She is 160 cm 5 ft 3 in tall and weighs 44 kg 96 lb; 8MI is 17 kg m 2. Her temperature is 36.9 C 98.4  F, pulse is 126/min, respirations are ~ min, and blood pressure is 118/72 mm Hg. Examination shows temporal wasting, protruding ribs, and well-healed bilateral mastectomy scars. There is guarding of the left upper extremity. An x-ray shows a pathologic fracture of the left proximal humerus. Which of the following is</br>the most appropriate next step in pain management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Increase the dosage of hydrocodone-acetaminophen</br>b. Add oral gabapentin to the medication regimen</br>c. Add oral ketorolac to the medication regimen</br>d. Switch to intramuscular meperidine</br>e. Switch to intravenous morphine</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Switch to intravenous morphine  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key phrases: “metastatic breast cancer,” “24-h history of severe left arm pain,” “rates pain 9/10,” “pathologic fracture,” “current Rx hydrocodone-acetaminophen 5/500 mg, 2 tabs q6 h,” “3-yr-old GI bleed from gastric ulcer,” “cachectic (BMI 17).”<br /></br>• Uncontrolled, acute, nociceptive pain from a fracture in a patient with known malignancy → WHO Cancer Pain Ladder Step 3 (strong opioid).<br /></br>• Current regimen already at acetaminophen daily ceiling (4 g/24 h); cannot safely “increase the dose” of the combination product.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Determine severity: 9/10, guarding → severe.<br /></br>2. Check current therapy: weak opioid + non-opioid at maximum safe dose.<br /></br>3. Escalate therapy: move to a strong, titratable opioid with rapid onset. IV morphine is the standard first-line parenteral agent for severe acute cancer pain.<br /></br>Core takeaway: In cancer patients, severe pain that is inadequately controlled on a weak opioid/acetaminophen combination should be managed by switching to a strong opioid (morphine, hydromorphone, fentanyl) rather than merely increasing the weak opioid or adding adjuvants.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Metastatic bone lesions cause constant, somatic nociceptive pain; fractures magnify it.<br /></br>• Hydrocodone (a “step 2” opioid) has a ceiling dose when co-formulated with acetaminophen because &gt;4 g/day acetaminophen → hepatotoxicity.<br /></br>• Morphine is a full μ-opioid agonist without a ceiling effect (aside from respiratory depression) and can be titrated IV for rapid analgesia.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Notes<br /></br>First-line for severe cancer pain<br /></br>• Rapid-acting strong opioid: IV morphine 2–4 mg q10–15 min until comfortable, then convert to oral long-acting formulation + immediate-release breakthroughs.<br /></br>• Bone pain adjuvants (bisphosphonates, radiation, nerve blocks) can be added later but do not replace immediate opioid escalation.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Increase the dosage of hydrocodone-acetaminophen<br /></br>• Would exceed the 4 g/day acetaminophen limit → hepatotoxicity.<br /></br>• Hydrocodone is a weak opioid; simply increasing dose is insufficient for 9/10 pain from a fracture.  </p></br><p>b. Add oral gabapentin<br /></br>• Useful for neuropathic pain (post-herpetic neuralgia, diabetic neuropathy). Pathologic fracture pain is somatic nociceptive. Does not provide rapid analgesia needed here.  </p></br><p>c. Add oral ketorolac<br /></br>• Ketorolac (NSAID) may help with bone pain but is contraindicated with history of upper GI bleeding and would still be inadequate as monotherapy for severe pain.  </p></br><p>d. Switch to intramuscular meperidine<br /></br>• Meperidine is avoided for chronic or severe pain due to short half-life and neurotoxic metabolite (normeperidine) → seizures, esp. in cachectic or renal-impaired patients. IM route is painful and unreliable.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Severe cancer-related pain unrelieved by a maximal dose of a weak opioid/acetaminophen combination mandates escalation to a titratable, strong opioid—IV morphine is the standard immediate next step.</p>
83. Comprehensive Clinical Science Self-Assessment A 24-year-old woman, gravida 1, para 1, comes to the physician because she has not had a menstrual period for 12 months and has been unable to conceive during this time. Her son was born by spontaneous vaginal delivery 18 months ago, and she breast-fed him for 6 months. One month after delivery, she had profuse vaginal bleeding and underwent emergency dilatation and curettage for retained placental fragments. She has not used any contraception since her delivery and currently takes no medications. She and her husband have sexual intercourse twice weekly. Analysis of the husband's semen has shown no abnormalities. The patient's vital signs are within normal limits. Physical examination shows no abnormalities. Pelvic examination shows a normal cervix, uterus, and adnexa. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hematocrit 42 percent</br>Leukocyte count 8800/mm 3</br>Serum</br>Thyroid-stimulating hormone</br>Follicle-stimulating hormone</br>Luteinizing hormone</br>1.1 μU/mL</br>10 mlU/mL</br>12 mlU/mL</br>Pelvic ultrasonography shows normal ovaries, no free fluid in the pelvis, and no distinct endometrial stripe. Treatment with oral progesterone for 10 days produces no uterine bleeding. Which of the following is the most</br>appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. CT scan of the head</br>b. CT scan of the pelvis</br>c. Hysteroscopy</br>d. Laparoscopy</br>e. Dilatation and curettage</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection</br>c. Hysteroscopy</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• Key phrases: “no menstrual period for 12 months,” “unable to conceive,” “profuse vaginal bleeding → emergency dilatation and curettage for retained placental fragments,” “no distinct endometrial stripe” on ultrasound, and “no bleeding after 10-day oral progesterone challenge.”<br /></br>• Secondary amenorrhea + infertility in a woman with a history of postpartum uterine curettage strongly points to intrauterine adhesions (Asherman syndrome).<br /></br>• A failed progesterone-withdrawal test means either (1) severe estrogen deficiency or (2) an outflow tract problem (cervical/uterine scarring). Estrogen deficiency is unlikely because FSH/LH are in the normal-to-high follicular range and ultrasound shows ovaries that look normal but an endometrium that is absent → favors an anatomic obstruction.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>1. Normal gonadotropins and TSH exclude hypothalamic–pituitary causes (e.g., Sheehan syndrome, prolactinoma).<br /></br>2. Absence of endometrial stripe + prior D&amp;C = classic pattern for Asherman.<br /></br>3. Gold-standard next step to confirm (and often treat) intrauterine synechiae is hysteroscopy.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In a woman with secondary amenorrhea after uterine instrumentation, think Asherman syndrome; diagnose with hysteroscopy.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>Asherman syndrome<br /></br>• Etiology: Intrauterine trauma (most commonly curettage after delivery or abortion).<br /></br>• Mechanism: Basalis layer of endometrium is destroyed → fibrous synechiae obliterate the cavity.<br /></br>• Clinical: Secondary amenorrhea, cyclic pelvic pain without bleeding, infertility, recurrent pregnancy loss.<br /></br>• Hormones: Ovarian axis intact → normal estrogen, FSH, LH.<br /></br>• Imaging: Hysterosalpingogram or sonohysterogram may show filling defects; hysteroscopy provides direct visualization.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review</br>• Diagnostic/therapeutic test of choice: Hysteroscopy with lysis of adhesions.<br /></br>• Post-procedure: High-dose estrogen therapy (e.g., conjugated estrogens 2.5 mg PO q6 h ×2–4 wk) + balloon stent or IUD to promote regeneration and prevent re-adhesion.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. CT scan of the head<br /></br>   – Used for pituitary pathology (Sheehan, prolactinoma). Those disorders show low FSH/LH and often other pituitary deficits (e.g., hypothyroidism, adrenal insufficiency). Vignette hormones are normal, and ultrasound points to uterine— not central—etiology.</p></br><p>b. CT scan of the pelvis<br /></br>   – Helpful for masses, Mullerian agenesis, or malignancy. Amenorrhea after a normal pregnancy with no uterine stripe and prior curettage is more consistent with intrauterine scarring than with a pelvic mass.</p></br><p>d. Laparoscopy<br /></br>   – Used to evaluate pelvic organs for endometriosis or adhesions external to uterus. It will not visualize endometrial cavity adhesions causing amenorrhea.</p></br><p>e. Dilatation and curettage<br /></br>   – Further curettage risks worsening adhesions. Diagnostic D&amp;C would still fail to show bleeding if the cavity is obliterated; hysteroscopy is safer and directly visualizes/lyses synechiae.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Ordering endocrine workups or neuroimaging in post-curettage amenorrhea without first considering an outflow obstruction. Always do a progesterone challenge; if no bleed and gonadotropins/estrogen are normal, evaluate the uterine cavity (hysteroscopy).</p>
84. A previously healthy 7-year-old boy is brought to the office because of a 1-week history of diffuse, mild abdominal pain and bloody diarrhea. He has a constant urge to pass stools despite passing 10 small, bloody stools daily. During this time, he has had decreased oral intake. He last urinated 12 hours ago. He has not had fever or vomiting. No other family members are ill. The patient appears ti ed. His temperature is 37.7 C 99.8 F, pulse is 120/min, respirations are 26/min, and blood pressure is 120/85 mm Hg. Examination shows pallor and tacky mucous membranes. The lungs are clear to auscultation. A grade 2/6 systolic murmur is heard. The abdomen is soft and diffusely tender to palpation; there is no guarding or rebound. Examination of the perianal area shows no abnormalities. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hemoglobin 7 g/dL N=11.5-14.5</br>Hematocrit 21  percent N=33 percent-43 percent</br>Leukocyte count 12,000/mm 3</br>Platelet count 14,000/mm 3</br>Serum</br>Na+</br>K+</br>cI-</br>HCO 3-</br>Urea nitrogen</br>Creatinine</br>147 mEq/L</br>4.2 mEq/L</br>118 mEq/L</br>14 mEq/L</br>72 mg/dL</br>8.2 mg/dL N=0.22-0.59</br>Which of the following is the most likely infectious agent?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Campylobacter jejuni</br>b. Clostridium difficile</br>c. Escherichia coli</br>d. Giardia lamblia</br>e. Salmonella enteritidis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection</br>c. Escherichia coli (classically the enterohemorrhagic strain O157:H7)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• 7-year-old with 1 week of bloody diarrhea → invasive/hemorrhagic enteritis.</br>• Pallor, Hb 7 g/dL, platelets 14 000/µL, creatinine 8.2 mg/dL, BUN 72 mg/dL → triad of anemia, thrombocytopenia, acute kidney injury = hemolytic uremic syndrome (HUS).</br>• No antibiotic exposure, no recent hospitalization → rules out C. difficile.</br>• Tenesmus but no perianal disease → colonic involvement, not small-bowel malabsorption (rules out Giardia).</br>• HUS after bloody diarrhea in a child is almost always due to Shiga toxin–producing EHEC (or, less commonly, Shigella dysenteriae).</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Key pattern: Child + bloody diarrheal prodrome → 1 week later develops MAHA (microangiopathic hemolytic anemia), thrombocytopenia, renal failure = HUS. EHEC O157:H7 is the prototype precipitant. Recognizing this classic association is a must-know Step 2/ Shelf core concept.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• EHEC O157:H7 acquired from under-cooked beef, unpasteurized milk/juice, contaminated produce.</br>• Shiga-like toxin (verotoxin) inactivates 60S ribosomal subunit → colonic endothelial damage → hemorrhagic colitis.</br>• Circulating toxin triggers widespread endothelial injury, especially in renal microvasculature → platelet activation &amp; consumption, fibrin deposition → MAHA (schistocytes), thrombocytopenia, AKI.</p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests</br>• Supportive: meticulous volume/electrolyte management, early nephrology consult for dialysis. </br>• Avoid antibiotics and antimotility agents: may increase toxin release and worsen HUS.</br>• Diagnosis: stool culture on sorbitol-MacConkey agar or Shiga-toxin PCR/ELISA.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Campylobacter jejuni<br /></br>   – Causes bloody diarrhea and can precede Guillain-Barré or reactive arthritis, but only rarely triggers HUS. Would not explain the severe thrombocytopenia/renal failure classic for HUS.</p></br><p>b. Clostridium difficile<br /></br>   – Produces watery (occasionally bloody) diarrhea after antibiotics or hospitalization. Pseudomembranes on colonoscopy, not HUS.</p></br><p>d. Giardia lamblia<br /></br>   – Presents with foul-smelling, greasy, watery stools and weight loss; no blood, no HUS, no thrombocytopenia.</p></br><p>e. Salmonella enteritidis<br /></br>   – Causes inflammatory diarrhea (sometimes bloody) and bacteremia in sickle cell patients, but HUS is not a classic complication.</p></br><p>Take-Home Message</br>In a child, the triad of microangiopathic hemolytic anemia, thrombocytopenia, and acute renal failure after a short prodrome of bloody diarrhea = HUS, almost always caused by Shiga toxin–producing Escherichia coli O157:H7.</p>
85. A previously healthy 5-year-old boy is brought to the physician because of progressive muscle weakness over the past 6 months. Examination shows a fish-like mouth with a high-arched palate, wasting of distal muscles,</br>and hypotonia. Deep tendon reflexes are intact and symmetric. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Glycogen storage disease, type II Pompe disease</br>b. Guillain-8arre syndrome</br>c. Hypothyroidism</br>d. Myasthenia gravis</br>e. Myotonic dystrophy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Glycogen storage disease type II (Pompe disease)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• 5-year-old boy with a 6-month history of slowly progressive weakness – suggests a chronic neuromuscular process rather than an acute neuropathy.<br /></br>• “Fish-like mouth” and “high-arched palate” – classic signs of a myopathic facies produced by weakness and wasting of perioral and palatal muscles; seen in several congenital/ metabolic myopathies, most classically juvenile-onset Pompe disease.<br /></br>• Wasting of distal muscles + generalized hypotonia – points to a primary muscle disorder (myopathy).<br /></br>• Deep-tendon reflexes intact and symmetric – argues strongly against a peripheral neuropathy (reflexes are typically absent or markedly reduced in neuropathies such as Guillain-Barré).  </p></br><p>Pattern:  Child + progressive myopathy + myopathic facies + preserved reflexes ➜ metabolic myopathy → Pompe disease highest yield.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>1. First decide “muscle vs nerve”: preserved reflexes → muscle.<br /></br>2. Next distinguish which muscle disease: Duchenne/Becker present much earlier and are proximal; myasthenia has fluctuating fatigability without atrophy; hypothyroid myopathy would have systemic hypothyroid signs.<br /></br>3. A metabolic myopathy that can present in early childhood with facial weakness and distal wasting is juvenile-onset Pompe disease.<br /></br>Core takeaway:  Preserved reflexes + myopathic facies + childhood progressive weakness → think metabolic myopathy; Pompe is the glycogen-storage disease most classically associated with these findings.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>Pompe disease<br /></br>• Enzyme defect: acid α-1,4-glucosidase (acid maltase) located in lysosomes.<br /></br>• Pathogenesis: inability to degrade glycogen inside lysosomes → intralysosomal accumulation → vacuolar myopathy of skeletal, cardiac, and respiratory muscles.<br /></br>• Clinical spectra<br /></br>  – Infantile form: cardiomegaly, hepatomegaly, profound hypotonia, respiratory failure, early death.<br /></br>  – Juvenile/late-onset form (this vignette): no severe cardiomyopathy, but progressive limb-girdle or distal muscle weakness, facial weakness (long “fish” face, high-arched palate), respiratory muscle involvement.<br /></br>• Lab/diagnostics: markedly elevated CK; PAS-positive vacuoles on muscle biopsy; absent/low acid maltase activity in fibroblast or blood assay; echocardiogram ± cardiomyopathy in early form.<br /></br>• Treatment: lifelong IV enzyme-replacement therapy with alglucosidase alfa; pulmonary support; aggressive physical therapy.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review</br>First-line: recombinant human acid α-glucosidase (alglucosidase alfa).<br /></br>Supportive: NIV/ventilation for respiratory weakness; cardiac monitoring; physical/occupational therapy; genetic counseling.<br /></br>Diagnostic confirmation: dried-blood-spot enzyme assay → muscle biopsy if needed; cardiac echo in younger children.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>b. Guillain-Barré syndrome<br /></br>   – Acute (days to weeks) ascending weakness following infection, areflexia, and sensory complaints. Reflexes would be absent, not intact. No myopathic facies.</p></br><p>c. Hypothyroidism<br /></br>   – Can cause myopathy and hypotonia, but usually with systemic signs (bradycardia, dry skin, constipation, growth delay). Facial/ palatal muscle wasting is not typical, and weakness is proximal rather than distal.</p></br><p>d. Myasthenia gravis<br /></br>   – Fluctuating fatigable weakness, ocular/bulbar predominance, minimal or no atrophy, normal tone, normal reflexes. Rare in 5-year-olds, and “fish-like mouth/high-arched palate” is not characteristic.</p></br><p>e. Myotonic dystrophy<br /></br>   – Does produce a high-arched palate and “hatchet/fish” facies with distal weakness; however, hallmark is myotonia (delayed hand-grip relaxation) and multisystem findings (cataracts, cardiac conduction defects, testicular atrophy) which are absent here. Juvenile Pompe is a more likely fit given age and the pure myopathic picture without myotonia.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Take-home Message</br>In a child with progressive muscle weakness, preserved reflexes, and a myopathic facies (high-arched palate, “fish” mouth), think metabolic myopathy. The classic metabolic myopathy that produces these findings is the lysosomal glycogen-storage disease type II—Pompe disease.</p>
86. A 77-year-old man comes to the physician for an initial visit. He has a history of gout and surgical repair of a hernia. He has been told that he has a mild cardiac murmur, and he thinks that he had rheumatic fever as a</br>child. He takes no medications. His pulse is 80/min, respirations are 14/min, and blood pressure is 150/92 mm Hg. A grade 4/6 systolic murmur is heard best at the second right intercostal space; it decreases in intensity</br>with Valsalva maneuver and with handgrip and increases in intensity when he squats. There is an S 4, and a systolic thrill is palpable at the base. Which of the following is the most likely cause of these findings?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Aortic insufficiency</br>b. Aortic stenosis</br>c. Hypertrophic obstructive cardiomyopathy</br>d. Mitral regurgitation</br>e. Mitral stenosis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Aortic stenosis  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Grade 4/6 systolic murmur … best at the second right intercostal space” → classic aortic area systolic murmur.<br /></br>• “Decreases with Valsalva” → Valsalva ↓ venous return; most murmurs (including AS) soften, whereas hypertrophic obstructive cardiomyopathy (HOCM) gets louder—so HOCM is ruled out.<br /></br>• “Decreases with hand-grip” → hand-grip ↑ afterload; regurgitant lesions (MR, AR) get louder, AS gets softer.<br /></br>• “Increases when he squats” → squatting ↑ preload &amp; afterload; AS grows louder, HOCM softens.<br /></br>• “S4” + “palpable systolic thrill at the base” → concentric LVH from pressure overload typical of long-standing AS.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise logic:<br /></br>1. Identify murmur location/timing (systolic, aortic area).<br /></br>2. Use bedside maneuvers to characterize hemodynamic response:<br /></br>   – Valsalva: softer → not HOCM.<br /></br>   – Hand-grip: softer → not MR/AR.<br /></br>   – Squat: louder → favors AS.<br /></br>3. Supporting findings (S4, thrill) confirm pressure-overload lesion.<br /></br>Takeaway: Bedside maneuvers distinguish obstruction (AS) from regurgitation (MR/AR) and dynamic obstruction (HOCM).  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Aortic stenosis = fixed outflow obstruction (usually calcific degeneration in elderly).<br /></br>• Etiology: age-related calcific changes (&gt;65 y) or bicuspid valve, rheumatic disease less common in US.<br /></br>• Pathology: LV faces chronic pressure overload → concentric hypertrophy → S4, exertional syncope, angina, HF.  </p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>Diagnosis:<br /></br>• Echocardiography—valve area ≤1 cm², mean gradient ≥40 mm Hg.<br /></br>Management:<br /></br>• Definitive: surgical aortic valve replacement (SAVR) or TAVR when symptomatic or LVEF &lt;50 %.<br /></br>• Medical: control HTN; avoid preload/afterload reduction extremes (e.g., nitrates) that may provoke syncope.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Aortic insufficiency (aortic regurgitation)<br /></br>   – Diastolic decrescendo murmur at LSB/aortic area, not systolic.<br /></br>   – Hand-grip makes it louder (opposite of vignette).<br /></br>   – Often accompanied by bounding pulses, wide PP—not present here.  </p></br><p>c. Hypertrophic obstructive cardiomyopathy<br /></br>   – Systolic murmur at LSB/apex.<br /></br>   – Valsalva &amp; standing make murmur louder; squatting and hand-grip soften it (opposite pattern).<br /></br>   – Young patients; may have family history, not 77-year-old.  </p></br><p>d. Mitral regurgitation<br /></br>   – Holosystolic murmur best at apex radiating to axilla.<br /></br>   – Louder with hand-grip; little change with squatting vs HOCM.<br /></br>   – No systolic thrill at base.  </p></br><p>e. Mitral stenosis<br /></br>   – Mid-diastolic, low-pitched “opening snap” murmur at apex—not systolic.<br /></br>   – Maneuver responses irrelevant here.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Assuming any systolic murmur in an elderly patient at the base is AS without confirming maneuver responses. Always integrate location + timing + dynamic changes to nail the diagnosis.</p>
87. A 54-year-old woman comes to the office because of a 2-day history of increasingly severe pain in her right ear; she rates the pain as a 9 on a 10-point scale. She also has a mild ringing sensation in her right ear. She adds that this morning, her usual cereal tasted awful to her, and she vomited once. She has no symptoms in her left ear and has not had fever, headache, neck stiffness, or symptoms of an upper respiratory tract infection. Her most recent vaccination was 3 years ago for influenza. She has no history of serious illness, takes no medications, and has no known allergies. She is 165 cm 5 ft 5 in tall and weighs 72 kg 158 lb; BMI is 26 kg/m 2. Temperature is 37.3 C 99.1  F, pulse is 84/min, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 125/85 mm Hg. Examination of the left pinna and tympanic membrane discloses no abnormalities. Examination of the right ear shows erythema and edema of exterior auditory canal and pinna; light touch of the right pinna produces pain. The right tympanic membrane cannot be observed because of pain and edema. Cranial nerve testing discloses a widened right palpebral fissure. The patient has decreased ability to close her right eye, wrinkle her forehead, and smile. Visual acuity and visual field testing disclose no abnormalities. Sensation to light touch and pinprick is intact over the face. The remainder of the examination discloses no abnormalities. Which of the following conditions is most likely to develop in this patient during the next 24 hours?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Auricular vesicles</br>b. Otitis media</br>c. Swollen and painful parotid gland</br>d. Visual field defect</br>e. Xerostomia</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Auricular vesicles  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Increasingly severe pain in her right ear … pain with light touch of the pinna” → Indicates otalgia localized to the external ear canal/pinna (not the middle ear).<br /></br>• “Erythema and edema of external auditory canal and pinna” → External ear process.<br /></br>• “Tinnitus, bad taste of cereal” → CN VII (stapedius dysfunction → tinnitus; chorda tympani → taste disturbance).<br /></br>• “Widened palpebral fissure; decreased ability to close eye, wrinkle forehead, and smile” → Ipsilateral lower-motor-neuron facial nerve palsy.<br /></br>• Combination of external ear pain + early LMN facial palsy + altered taste strongly suggests herpes zoster oticus (Ramsay-Hunt syndrome).<br /></br>• Characteristic rash (vesicles on pinna/external canal) usually appears hours-to-2 days after onset of pain → expected next clinical event.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Pattern to know: Ramsay-Hunt syndrome = Triad of<br /></br>1. Severe otalgia of external ear,<br /></br>2. Peripheral facial paralysis,<br /></br>3. Vesicular eruption in external auditory canal / pinna.<br /></br>Pain precedes the vesicular outbreak. Therefore the logical “next 24 h” development is auricular vesicles.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In a patient with otalgia + LMN CN VII palsy, think VZV reactivation in the geniculate ganglion; vesicular rash is imminent.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Reactivation of latent varicella-zoster virus in the geniculate ganglion of CN VII.<br /></br>• Pathogenesis: Viral replication → inflammation &amp; edema of facial nerve within facial canal → LMN palsy; spread to sensory fibers → pain; can involve CN VIII (hearing loss, vertigo).<br /></br>• Clinical manifestations: Severe ear pain → vesicular rash (canal, concha, tympanic membrane) → facial paralysis; often taste loss, tinnitus, hyperacusis.<br /></br>• Prognosis: Worse facial nerve recovery than idiopathic Bell palsy.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Notes<br /></br>• Start therapy ASAP (&lt;72 h): oral valacyclovir (or famciclovir/acyclovir) + high-dose prednisone.<br /></br>• Lubricating eye drops/patch for exposure keratitis due to incomplete eye closure.<br /></br>• Audiogram and vestibular testing if hearing/vertigo present.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Otitis media – Middle-ear infection presents with fever, recent URI, and an erythematous bulging tympanic membrane, not external canal edema or pinna pain. Does not explain LMN facial palsy; not the expected next event.  </p></br><p>c. Swollen and painful parotid gland – Suggests mumps or bacterial parotitis; unrelated to external otalgia or CN VII palsy; no reason it would suddenly develop tomorrow.  </p></br><p>d. Visual field defect – Would imply optic pathway lesion (CN II, chiasm, tract) or stroke; no neuro-ophthalmic signs now and not a sequela of Ramsay-Hunt.  </p></br><p>e. Xerostomia – CN VII supplies submandibular &amp; sublingual glands, but complete dry mouth is uncommon and not a characteristic early progression; vesicular rash appears first and is far more specific.</p></br><p>Remember: Ear pain + LMN facial palsy = think Ramsay-Hunt syndrome → look for impending auricular vesicles.</p>
88. A 42-year-old woman is brought to the emergency department by paramedics 30 minutes after she was struck by a car while she was riding her bicycle. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. She is alert and fully oriented. She has moderate left upper abdominal and flank pain. Temperature is 37.1  C 98.8 F, pulse is 118/min, respirations are 24/min, and blood pressure is 88/60 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 94 percent. There is a 5 x 7-in, tender ecchymosis over the left upper abdominal quadrant and left flank. Bowel sounds are normal. The remainder of the physical examination shows no abnormalities. Neurologic examination shows no focal findings. After administration of 2 L of lactated Ringer solution, pulse is 105/min, and blood pressure is 95/70 mm Hg. Transfusion of 2 units of packed red blood cells is begun. Thirty minutes later, pulse is 80/min, and blood pressure is 124/80 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on an F10 2 of 1.0 via nonrebreather face mask shows an oxygen saturation of 100 percent. CT scan of the abdomen shows a grade 3 splenic laceration. The patient is admitted to the surgical intensive care unit for nonoperative management. Three hours later, she has progressive difficulty breathing. Temperature is 37.2 C 99.0 F, pulse is 90/min, respirations are 30/min, and blood pressure is 110/70 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry now shows an oxygen saturation of 87 percent. On pulmonary examination, breath sounds and diffuse crackles are heard bilaterally. Heart sounds are normal. Abdominal examination is unchanged. Chest x-ray is shown. Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient's current condition?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Acute congestive heart failure caused by myocardial contusion</br>b. Aspiration pneumonia</br>c. Bilateral pulmonary contusions</br>d. Transfusion-related acute lung injury</br>e. Traumatic intrapulmonary hemorrhage</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d.
