Transfusion-related acute lung injury (TRALI)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• “2 units of packed red blood cells … Thirty minutes later …” -– temporal link to transfusion.<br /></br>• Rapid onset (&lt; 6 h) of dyspnea, tachypnea (RR 30), hypoxemia (SpO₂ 87 % on 100 % FiO₂) and new bilateral crackles.<br /></br>• CXR (not shown to you, but described in stem as abnormal) – assumed new, diffuse bilateral infiltrates.<br /></br>• Stable blood pressure (110/70) and no volume overload signs → argues against cardiogenic edema.<br /></br>The classic NBME pattern: healthy trauma patient + small-volume transfusion → sudden, non-cardiogenic pulmonary edema.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>1. Ask “When did symptoms start?”  ➜ Within 6 h of transfusion → think TRALI or TACO.<br /></br>2. Look for volume overload or heart failure clues (hypertension, JVD, S3, peripheral edema, large transfusion) → absent here → favors TRALI.<br /></br>3. Consider direct lung trauma (contusions, hemorrhage) → no chest trauma described, normal initial CXR.<br /></br>Core takeaway: TRALI = acute, non-cardiogenic pulmonary edema that begins during/within 6 h of any blood product transfusion, caused by donor anti-leukocyte antibodies that activate recipient neutrophils in pulmonary microvasculature.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Donor anti-HLA or anti-neutrophil Abs bind recipient WBCs → neutrophil activation → capillary endothelial damage → massive capillary leak → pulmonary edema with normal LV filling pressure.<br /></br>• Incidence highest with plasma-rich products (FFP, platelets) but can occur after only 1–2 units PRBCs if plasma fraction high.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment / Diagnostics</br>• Immediate: Stop transfusion, supportive care (high-flow O₂, possible mechanical ventilation).<br /></br>• No role for diuretics (non-cardiogenic edema).<br /></br>• Future: Use leukoreduced or male-only plasma products to prevent recurrence.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Acute congestive heart failure from myocardial contusion<br /></br>   – Requires significant blunt chest trauma (sternal fracture, seat-belt sign). Vignette describes isolated abdominal/flank trauma. CHF would show JVD, S3, elevated BP or persistent hypotension refractory to fluids, and cardiogenic pulmonary edema (↑ PCWP). Not present.  </p></br><p>b. Aspiration pneumonia<br /></br>   – Usually unilateral (dependent lobe) infiltrate, develops over 12-48 h with fever, leukocytosis; not bilaterally diffuse, and not so soon after trauma/transfusion.  </p></br><p>c. Bilateral pulmonary contusions<br /></br>   – Need major thoracic blunt injury; respiratory decline often within first 24 h, but CXR abnormalities are typically evident from the start or progress gradually. No chest wall tenderness, normal initial chest exam/CXR.  </p></br><p>e. Traumatic intrapulmonary hemorrhage<br /></br>   – Also requires direct lung injury; presents immediately after trauma, not hours later, and again would necessitate chest impact.  </p></br><p>Key Differentiator: Only TRALI fits the classic “new bilateral pulmonary edema within 6 h of transfusion in a patient without signs of circulatory overload.”</p>
89. A 12-year-old girl is brought to the emergency department by paramedics 10 minutes after she was stabbed in the chest with a steak knife by another girl after school. The patient's medical history is unknown. She is gasping for breath but able to say her name. Her temperature is 37.0 C 98.6 F, pulse is 140/min, respirations are 36/min, and blood ressure is 80 mm H . Pulse oximet [Y on an F10 ~ of 1.0 by nonrebreather face mask shows an oxygen saturation of 86 percent. Examination discloses a 2-cm stab wound to the left of the lower sternum. Jugular venous pressure is 12 cm H 20 N=S--9. Breath sounds are equal bilaterally. Heart sounds are decreased. The abdomen is soft, nondistended, and nontender. Pulses are decreased in all extremities. Pericardiocentesis yields 30 ml of non-clotting blood. In addition to intravenous fluid resuscitation and typing and crossmatching of the patient's blood, which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Chest x-ray</br>b. CT angiography of the chest</br>c. Sternotomy</br>d. Transesophageal echocardiography</br>e. Tube thoracostomy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Sternotomy  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Stab wound to the left of the lower sternum” → classic location for penetrating cardiac injury.<br /></br>• Beck triad of tamponade: hypotension (BP 80 mm Hg), elevated JVP (12 cm H₂O), and muffled heart sounds.<br /></br>• “Pulses are decreased in all extremities” + tachycardia = obstructive shock.<br /></br>• Pericardiocentesis produced “30 mL of non-clotting blood” → confirms hemopericardium from cardiac penetration.<br /></br>• Despite the tap, the patient remains profoundly unstable (SBP 80, SpO₂ 86%), meaning the tamponade or active cardiac bleeding has not been definitively controlled.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Algorithm for penetrating chest trauma with tamponade:<br /></br>1. Rapid bedside confirmation (FAST or emergent pericardiocentesis).<br /></br>2. If hemodynamically stable after the tap, obtain imaging (echo, CT) and plan elective repair.<br /></br>3. If still unstable or recurrent tamponade → immediate operative exploration (median sternotomy or left anterolateral thoracotomy in extremis).<br /></br>This patient is still in shock → go straight to the OR for sternotomy and cardiac repair.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Pericardiocentesis is only a temporizing maneuver. Definitive management of penetrating cardiac injury with ongoing instability is emergent surgical exploration via sternotomy.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Penetrating injury → blood accumulates in pericardial sac → intrapericardial pressure rises → diastolic ventricular filling impaired → ↓SV → obstructive shock. Removing a small volume of blood may transiently drop pericardial pressure, but continued bleeding through the cardiac wound rapidly re-accumulates. Only suturing the myocardial laceration and wide pericardial drainage definitively resolves the problem.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Definitive therapy:<br /></br>• Median sternotomy (preferred) or resuscitative thoracotomy to:<br /></br>  – Open the pericardium widely.<br /></br>  – Identify and suture the cardiac laceration.<br /></br>  – Place pericardial/mediastinal drains.<br /></br>Adjuncts: massive transfusion protocol, vasopressors only as bridge, cardiopulmonary bypass rarely required.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Chest x-ray – Too slow and nondiagnostic for tamponade; CXR often normal in tamponade and does nothing to treat the bleeding.<br /></br>b. CT angiography of the chest – Requires hemodynamic stability and time in scanner; patient is crashing.<br /></br>d. Transesophageal echocardiography – Good diagnostic tool but delays definitive care; diagnosis is already confirmed by bloody pericardiocentesis.<br /></br>e. Tube thoracostomy – Treats pneumothorax/hemothorax, not tamponade. Breath sounds are equal and no signs of pleural blood or air.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Believing that once you tap the pericardium the problem is solved. In penetrating injury, tamponade almost always recurs unless the cardiac hole is surgically closed.</p>
90. A 68-year-old woman comes to the office for a routine examination. She says she has felt well. Medical history is remarkable for hypertension and type 2 diabetes mellitus. Medications are hydrochlorothiazide,</br>atorvastatin, and insulin. Pulse is 70/min, respirations are 14/min, and blood pressure is 130/60 mm Hg. Physical examination discloses a 2-cm mass in the right groin below the inguinal ligament; the mass is mildly tender</br>to palpation and does not reduce with attempts at manual reduction. The mass is not fluctuant. The patient says she has noticed this abnormality during the past several weeks but has not been bothered by it. The</br>remainder of the physical examination discloses no abnormalities. Fasting serum glucose concentration obtained in preparation for today's visit is within the reference range. Which of the following is the most appropriate</br>recommendation at this time?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. CT scan of the pelvis</br>b. Elective operative repair within the next week</br>c. Use of a hernia belt</br>d. Reevaluation in the office if symptoms develop</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. CT scan of the pelvis  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “2-cm mass in the right groin BELOW the inguinal ligament” → classic anatomic location of a femoral hernia but can also be lymphadenopathy, femoral aneurysm, saphenous varix, etc.<br /></br>• “Mildly tender … does NOT reduce with attempts at manual reduction” → non-reducible (incarcerated) process; however, no signs of bowel obstruction, strangulation, or skin compromise.<br /></br>• No systemic or obstructive symptoms → patient is currently stable.  </p></br><p>Key pattern: Older woman + groin mass below inguinal ligament + non-reducible ⇒ think femoral hernia but confirm with imaging before committing to surgery because other groin pathologies can mimic this exam finding.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Decide whether urgent operation is mandatory now.<br /></br>• Urgent/ emergent repair is indicated if there is evidence of strangulation (severe pain, bowel obstruction, peritonitis, skin discoloration) – not present here.  </p></br><p>Step 2: Ask, “Do we have enough certainty that this is a femoral hernia to schedule elective repair?”<br /></br>• Physical exam alone has limited positive predictive value for femoral hernia (can be confused with lymph nodes, lipoma, femoral artery aneurysm).<br /></br>• Pre-operative cross-sectional imaging (CT or US) is recommended when the diagnosis is not absolutely clear, especially in women where femoral hernias are common.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: A non-reducible but otherwise asymptomatic groin mass first requires diagnostic confirmation. CT pelvis with contrast is the highest-yield study; it delineates hernia type, sac contents, and presence/absence of strangulation so the surgeon can plan timing and approach of repair.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (Femoral Hernia vs Mimics)<br /></br>• Femoral hernia: Protrusion through the femoral canal (medial to femoral vein, below inguinal ligament). Higher incarceration/strangulation rate because of rigid boundaries of the canal. Most common in older multiparous women.<br /></br>• Lymphadenopathy: Usually multiple nodes, firm, rubbery, may be related to infection/malignancy.<br /></br>• Femoral artery aneurysm: Pulsatile, bruit present, risks bleeding/thrombosis.<br /></br>• Saphenous varix: Compressible, “bag of worms,” increases with standing.<br /></br>CT differentiates all of the above reliably.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Management Pearls<br /></br>1. If CT confirms femoral hernia without strangulation → elective repair (often within days to weeks).<br /></br>2. If CT shows strangulation or compromised bowel → urgent operative repair (same admission).<br /></br>3. Observation or truss is reserved for large, soft, reducible INGUINAL hernias in poor surgical candidates, never for suspected femoral hernias.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Incorrect  </p></br><p>b. Elective operative repair within the next week<br /></br>• Appropriate only after the diagnosis of femoral hernia is established. Skipping imaging risks operating on a non-hernia lesion (e.g., lymph node, aneurysm).  </p></br><p>c. Use of a hernia belt<br /></br>• Truss/belt is used for reducible inguinal hernias in patients unfit for surgery. This mass is non-reducible and likely femoral—a truss is ineffective and potentially dangerous.  </p></br><p>d. Reevaluation in the office if symptoms develop<br /></br>• Femoral hernias carry up to a 40% lifetime risk of strangulation. “Watchful waiting” is acceptable for minimally symptomatic INGUINAL hernias in men, but NOT for femoral hernias or any non-reducible groin mass.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>A non-reducible groin mass below the inguinal ligament in an older woman is most suspicious for a femoral hernia. Before planning surgery, obtain a CT scan of the pelvis to confirm the diagnosis, delineate sac contents, and rule out strangulation or mimicking lesions.</p>
91. An 18-year-old woman comes to the physician to request a prescription for an oral contraceptive. She has been sexually active since the age of 17 years and has never used contraception. Menarche was at the age of 12 years. Menses occur at regular 29-day intervals. Her last menstrual period was 2 weeks ago. She has received all appropriate childhood immunizations but none since the age of 6 years. She has smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 1 year. Physical examination, including pelvic examination, shows no abnormalities. In addition to recommending condom use by her partners, which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Inform the patient's parents about her request</br>b. Obtain serum rubella antibody titer</br>c. Perform a Pap smear</br>d. Prescribe an oral contraceptive</br>e. Refuse to prescribe an oral contraceptive until she stops smoking</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Prescribe an oral contraceptive</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “18-year-old woman … sexually active … requests an oral contraceptive” → adult patient (≥18 yr) with capacity to consent; wants pregnancy prevention.<br /></br>• “Smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 1 year” → smoking history, but age &lt; 35 yr.<br /></br>• “Last menstrual period 2 weeks ago … pelvic examination normal” → no current pregnancy, no structural abnormality.<br /></br>Key point: No absolute or relative contraindication to a combined estrogen-progestin pill is present.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1 – Check absolute contraindications to combined OCPs (e.g., pregnancy, estrogen-dependent cancer, history of thromboembolism, migraine with aura, uncontrolled HTN, liver disease, smoking ≥15 cig/day AND age ≥35). None apply.<br /></br>Step 2 – Verify need for additional screening before prescribing (Pap start at 21 yr; STI screening if indicated, but not listed in answer set).<br /></br>Step 3 – Provide contraception and counsel on dual protection (condoms).<br /></br>Take-home message: Women &lt; 35 yr who smoke can still receive combined OCPs; age, not just smoking status, determines the contraindication.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Estrogen in combined OCPs increases hepatic production of clotting factors → pro-thrombotic. Smoking amplifies endothelial injury and platelet activation. The risk becomes clinically significant when the two risks converge heavily (≥35 yr + heavy smoking), hence the guideline cutoff.</p></br><p>Treatments / Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>• First-line reversible contraception includes combined OCPs, progestin-only pills, patch, ring, depot medroxyprogesterone, LARC (IUD or implant).<br /></br>• Patient counseling: correct use, possible side effects (breakthrough bleeding, nausea, breast tenderness), non-contraceptive benefits (lighter menses, less dysmenorrhea, acne improvement), warning signs of thrombosis, importance of smoking cessation, condom use for STI prevention.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Inform the patient’s parents about her request<br /></br>   – She is an adult (18 yr); HIPAA protects her privacy. Parental notification without consent breaches confidentiality.</p></br><p>b. Obtain serum rubella antibody titer<br /></br>   – Rubella immunity is usually documented in childhood; titer is recommended in preconception counseling, not as a prerequisite to contraception.</p></br><p>c. Perform a Pap smear<br /></br>   – Cervical cancer screening begins at age 21 irrespective of sexual activity; earlier screening does not reduce mortality and may cause unnecessary interventions.</p></br><p>e. Refuse to prescribe an oral contraceptive until she stops smoking<br /></br>   – Smoking is not a contraindication in women &lt; 35 yr. Refusal would deny effective contraception and increase unintended pregnancy risk. Instead, provide OCPs and counsel on quitting.</p></br><p>Core Concept<br /></br>Contraindications to combined estrogen-progestin contraceptives hinge on thrombotic risk. Smoking alone becomes a contraindication only once a woman reaches 35 years or smokes heavily in combination with other risk factors.</p>
92. A 77-year-old man with chronic obstructive pulmonary disease COPD comes to the physician for a follow-up examination. He had bronchitis 3 years ago treated with amoxicillin and prednisone. He has not been hospitalized during the past 3 years. He has a history of shortness of breath and cough but says these symptoms have subsided since he quit smoking cigarettes 5 years ago. His only medication is inhaled ipratropium. Pulmonary function testing shows an FVC of 85 percent of predicted and FEV 1 of 75 percent of predicted. The patient says he heard about a new inhaled medication, called BreatheEZ, for management of COPD and asks if he would benefit from this medication. The physician recalls a recent randomized controlled trial evaluating the efficacy of BreatheEZ in 7500 patients with COPD. Patients were randomized to receive BreatheEZ or placebo and observed for 4 years.</br>Data show:</br>Age, years + SD</br>FVC,  percent of predicted</br>FEV 1,  percent of predicted</br>Current smoker, percent of patients</br>Former smoker,  percent of patients</br>Number of COPD exacerbations during 12 months prior</br>to study</br>Medications at baseline  percent of patients</br>Inhaled corticosteroid</br>Long-acting J3-adrenergic agonist</br>Inhaled anticholinergic</br>Bronchodilator</br>Oral corticosteroid</br>~3 Medications</br>BreatheEZ</br>65 + 7.2</br>64</br>31</br>54</br>46</br>3</br>75</br>69</br>50</br>24</br>8</br>40</br>Placebo</br>65 + 7.4</br>66</br>30</br>50</br>50</br>3</br>77</br>67</br>54</br>22</br>9</br>43</br>Analysis shows a mortality rate of 12.2 percent for the patients receiving BreatheEZ and 15.4 percent for patients receiving placebo hazard ratio of 0. 79, 95 percent confidence interval of 0.63-0.98. Based on these data, which of the</br>following is the most appropriate action regarding BreatheEZ for this patient?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Do not prescribe BreatheEZ because the medication is not efficacious</br>b. Do not prescribe BreatheEZ because the results are invalid as a result of differences between the placebo group and the treatment group</br>c. Do not prescribe BreatheEZ because the results are relevant only for younger patients</br>d. Do not prescribe BreatheEZ because the results apply only to patients with more severe COPD</br>e. Prescribe BreatheEZ</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Do not prescribe BreatheEZ because the results apply only to patients with more-severe COPD</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 77-year-old man with COPD whose spirometry shows FVC 85 % predicted and FEV₁ 75 % predicted → GOLD stage I (mild) disease.<br /></br>• He is clinically stable, on only ipratropium, and has had no recent exacerbations or hospitalisations.<br /></br>These data clearly place him at the “mild COPD” end of the spectrum.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Ask, “Does the trial show benefit?” → Yes. HR 0.79 (95 % CI 0.63-0.98) shows a statistically significant ↓21 % mortality.<br /></br>2. Ask, “Can I apply the result to THIS patient?” → Check external validity:<br /></br>   – Trial population FEV₁ ≈ 30 % predicted, FVC ≈ 65 % predicted → severe airflow limitation.<br /></br>   – High exacerbation burden (mean 3/yr) and heavy medication use.<br /></br>   – Patient in vignette FEV₁ 75 %, FVC 85 %, zero recent exacerbations, minimal therapy.<br /></br>   → He is dissimilar to the study population; benefit cannot automatically be extrapolated.</p></br><p>Take-away message: A study can be internally valid (efficacious in those studied) yet not externally valid for a given patient if that patient differs materially from the study cohort.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• COPD severity is gauged chiefly by FEV₁:<br /></br>  – Mild (GOLD I) FEV₁ ≥ 80 %<br /></br>  – Moderate (GOLD II) 50-79 %<br /></br>  – Severe (GOLD III) 30-49 %<br /></br>  – Very severe (GOLD IV) &lt; 30 % or &lt; 50 % + chronic respiratory failure.<br /></br>• Mortality benefit from pharmacologic agents is best documented in patients with moderate-to-severe disease (eg, long-acting β-agonists, long-acting muscarinic antagonists, inhaled corticosteroids in select groups). Evidence in very mild COPD is scant.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Current evidence-based escalation for mild COPD:<br /></br>1. Smoking cessation (already accomplished).<br /></br>2. Short-acting bronchodilator PRN (eg, albuterol) or short-acting anticholinergic (ipratropium).<br /></br>3. Add long-acting bronchodilator(s) only if symptoms or exacerbations increase.<br /></br>BreatheEZ would have to show benefit in mild COPD to justify routine use—those data are absent.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. “Not efficacious” – Wrong. HR 0.79 with 95 % CI that does not cross 1.0 proves efficacy in the studied group.<br /></br>b. “Groups differed at baseline” – Wrong. Baseline characteristics (age, FEV₁, smoking status, medication use) are well-matched; internal validity is sound.<br /></br>c. “Relevant only for younger patients” – Wrong. Mean age 65 ± 7 yr; this patient (77 yr) is older but outside-age generalisation is less of a concern than disease-severity mismatch.<br /></br>e. “Prescribe BreatheEZ” – Wrong. Efficacy in severe COPD does not guarantee usefulness (or cost-effectiveness/side-effect profile) in mild COPD; external validity lacking.</p></br><p>Bottom line: The trial supports BreatheEZ for patients with severe COPD (FEV₁ ~30 % predicted). It should not be prescribed to this patient with mild disease because the evidence is not generalisable to him.</p>
93. A 44-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 4-month history of dryness of her mouth. During this time, she often has consumed liquids to aid in swallowing food. She has not had a sore throat or fever. She has not sustained head or neck trauma. She has rheumatoid arthritis. Her medications are low-dose prednisone and ibuprofen. Vital signs are within normal limits. Skin turgor is normal. Examination shows erythematous, dry oral mucosa and atrophy of the filiform papillae on the dorsum of the tongue. Results of serum anti-Ro and anti-La antibody assays are positive. The patient is given water to drink. Which of the following is the most appropriate pharmacotherapy?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Amitriptyline</br>b. Cyclophosphamide</br>c. Hydrochlorothiazide</br>d. Nifedipine</br>e. Pilocarpine</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Pilocarpine  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 4-month history of “dryness of her mouth” and need to sip liquids to swallow → xerostomia.<br /></br>• Physical exam: erythematous, dry oral mucosa; atrophy of filiform papillae (classic oral finding in Sjögren).<br /></br>• Positive anti-Ro (SSA) and anti-La (SSB) antibodies → highly specific for Sjögren syndrome.<br /></br>• Coexisting rheumatoid arthritis (another autoimmune condition) further supports secondary Sjögren.<br /></br>Key pattern: middle-aged woman + autoimmune disease + xerostomia + anti-Ro/La antibodies = Sjögren syndrome.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Main complaint is symptomatic oral dryness. First-line symptomatic therapy is a muscarinic (M3) receptor agonist that stimulates residual salivary (and lacrimal) gland secretion. Pilocarpine and cevimeline are the two drugs routinely used; pilocarpine is listed in the answer choices, so choose it.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In Sjögren syndrome, treat xerostomia with a muscarinic agonist (pilocarpine/cevimeline) that increases exocrine gland flow.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Autoimmune lymphocytic infiltration (CD4+ T-cells, B-cells) of salivary and lacrimal glands → glandular destruction → ↓ saliva and tears (sicca).<br /></br>• Anti-Ro/La antibodies are directed against ribonucleoproteins; thought to contribute to gland injury.<br /></br>• Disease associations: rheumatoid arthritis, SLE, systemic sclerosis, thyroid disease, NHL (parotid enlargement).  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Symptomatic:<br /></br>1. Pilocarpine 5 mg PO QID or cevimeline 30 mg PO TID → M3 agonists → ↑ saliva &amp; tears.<br /></br>2. Artificial tears/saliva, sugar-free gum or lozenges.<br /></br>3. Good dental hygiene (↑ risk of caries).  </p></br><p>Disease-modifying / extraglandular:<br /></br>• Hydroxychloroquine for arthralgias, mild systemic disease.<br /></br>• Methotrexate, azathioprine, rituximab for severe extraglandular involvement.<br /></br>• Topical cyclosporine eye drops for keratoconjunctivitis sicca.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Amitriptyline – Tricyclic antidepressant with strong anticholinergic (anti-M) activity; would WORSEN xerostomia. Used for neuropathic pain, depression. Contraindicated here.  </p></br><p>b. Cyclophosphamide – Alkylating immunosuppressant reserved for severe, life-threatening systemic manifestations (e.g., vasculitis, glomerulonephritis). Not indicated for isolated dry mouth and carries risk of hemorrhagic cystitis, infertility, malignancy.  </p></br><p>c. Hydrochlorothiazide – Thiazide diuretic; causes diuresis and commonly induces dry mouth as an adverse effect. No role in Sjögren treatment.  </p></br><p>d. Nifedipine – Dihydropyridine calcium-channel blocker; sometimes used for Raynaud phenomenon (which can co-occur with Sjögren) but does nothing for salivary secretion.  </p></br><p>e. Pilocarpine – Direct cholinergic (M3) agonist → ↑ exocrine gland secretions → relieves xerostomia and keratoconjunctivitis sicca. First-line symptomatic therapy.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Mistaking immunosuppressive therapy as the first step. In Sjögren, start with symptomatic secretagogue (pilocarpine/cevimeline); reserve systemic immunosuppression for serious extraglandular disease.</p></br><p>High-Yield Mnemonic: “Pilo-saliva”: Pilocarpine Pulls out saliva and tears.</p>
94. A 27-year-old woman, gravida 2, para 1, at 12 weeks' gestation comes to the physician for her first prenatal visit. Eighteen months ago, her first pregnancy ended in preterm low-transverse cesarean delivery at 32 weeks' gestation because of fetal bradycardia following the onset of contractions. A Pap smear 16 m nths ago showed a low-grade squamous intraepithelial lesion; she has not had a Pap smear since then. Three years ago, she underwent loop electrosurgical excision of the cervical transformation zone for cervical intraepithelial neoplasia CIN 3. She has a 10-year history of cocaine use but has not used cocaine during the past 12 months. She used cocaine intermittently during her first pregnancy. She smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 10 years but quit 6 months ago. She is 163 cm 5 ft 4 in tall. She weighed 49 kg 108 lb prior to her pregnanc~t BMI was 18 kg/m ~. She has had a 1.8-kg 4-lb weight gain during her pregnancy. Examination shows a uterus consistent in size with a 12-week gestation. The fetal heart rate is reassuring. This patient is at greatest risk for which of the following complications during pregnancy?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Abruptio placentae</br>b. Fetal growth restriction</br>c. Placenta previa</br>d. Preterm delivery</br>e. Uterine rupture</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Preterm delivery  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Prior spontaneous preterm birth: delivered at 32 weeks in the first pregnancy → single strongest predictor of another preterm birth<br /></br>• Cervical surgery (loop electrosurgical excision procedure, LEEP) → shortens/weakens cervix → cervical insufficiency → additional preterm-birth risk<br /></br>• No current hypertension, cocaine use, or placenta previa; prior cesarean is low-transverse, not classical.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1 – Recognize the dominant risk factor: a history of spontaneous preterm birth (&lt;37 weeks) confers a recurrence risk of ≈20-30 % (vs ≈8-10 % baseline).<br /></br>Step 2 – Note additive risk: previous LEEP further elevates the chance of cervical shortening and second-trimester loss/preterm labor.<br /></br>Take-home message: A prior spontaneous preterm delivery is the MOST powerful risk factor for future preterm delivery; management in the current pregnancy includes serial cervical-length measurements and 17-α-hydroxyprogesterone injections.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Spontaneous preterm labor results from premature activation of the maternal-fetal inflammatory cascade, cervical insufficiency, or uterine over-distention. Any structural cervical compromise (eg, conization/LEEP) lowers the threshold for dilation under contractile forces.  </p></br><p>Treatments / Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• Start 17-α-hydroxyprogesterone IM weekly from 16–36 weeks (for patients with prior spontaneous preterm birth).<br /></br>• Transvaginal cervical-length ultrasound at 16-24 weeks; if &lt;25 mm, place a cerclage.<br /></br>• Counsel on smoking avoidance and adequate weight gain.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Abruptio placentae<br /></br>   – Major risks: chronic hypertension, preeclampsia, cocaine use DURING pregnancy, prior abruption, abdominal trauma.<br /></br>   – This patient quit cocaine 12 months ago and lacks other major precipitants. Incidence (~1 %) is lower than her recurrent preterm-birth risk.  </p></br><p>b. Fetal growth restriction<br /></br>   – Risks: maternal hypertension, smoking during pregnancy, underweight BMI &lt;18.5, placental insufficiency.<br /></br>   – Her prepregnancy BMI is 18 (borderline), but she quit smoking; recurrence risk of preterm delivery still exceeds the probability of FGR.  </p></br><p>c. Placenta previa<br /></br>   – Risks: prior placenta previa, multiple cesareans, multiple gestations, maternal age &gt;35, smoking.<br /></br>   – One prior low-transverse cesarean confers a prevalence ≈1 %; much lower than 20-30 % recurrence risk of preterm birth.  </p></br><p>e. Uterine rupture<br /></br>   – Relevant only with prior classical (vertical) uterine incision, multiple prior cesareans, or uterine surgery.<br /></br>   – Low-transverse incision has &lt;1 % risk, and rupture occurs intrapartum, not during early pregnancy.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Students frequently over-weight any history of cesarean section and assume placenta-related complications. Remember: prior LOW-TRANSVERSE cesarean mainly affects mode of delivery (trial of labor vs repeat C-section) but does not markedly increase antepartum morbidity. The far more predictive variable here is the prior spontaneous preterm birth.</p>
95. A 13-year-old girl is brought to the physician by her parents because of changes in behavior during the past 6 months. Her parents say that during this time she has been hostile and unpleasant, frequently crying and screaming about being treated unfairly no matter what they do. She also has alienated her friends. Her grades have declined from mostly B's to C's and D's, and she has stopped participating in sports. The patient says she is tired and does not sleep; her parents report she is sleeping more than usual. They add that she has been eating more, resulting in a 10-kg 22-lb. weight gain, which bothers her. They say she was a worrier when she was younger, but her worrying has decreased during the years. The patient is at the 45th percentile for height and 60th percentile for weight. Vital signs are within normal limits. Physical examination shows no abnormalities. On mental status examination, she describes her mood as okay but appears dysphoric and has an irritable affect. She looks at her hands and responds slowly using monosyllabic words. She says, I don't think I'm depressed. I'm bored and angry because no one likes me. I used to want to be an actress, but nothing ever works out for me. I could do it if my parents would let me. Her thought process is goal directed. She says that adults, especially her parents, do not understand her and make unreasonable demands. There is no evidence of psychosis. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Bipolar disorder</br>b. Borderline personality disorder</br>c. Generalized anxiety disorder</br>d. Impulse control disorder</br>e. Major depressive disorder</br>F. Oppositional defiant disorder</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection</br>e. Major depressive disorder (unipolar depression)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• Duration: 6-month history of symptoms → meets ≥ 2-week minimum for a major depressive episode.<br /></br>• Mood/affect: “Hostile,” “unpleasant,” cries often, appears dysphoric and irritable. Irritability is a common depressive equivalent in children/adolescents.<br /></br>• Neurovegetative changes: hypersomnia (parents), hyperphagia with 10-kg weight gain, psychomotor slowing (stares at hands, monosyllabic replies), fatigue.<br /></br>• Anhedonia &amp; hopelessness: Stopped sports, grades falling, “nothing ever works out for me,” pessimistic about the future.<br /></br>• No evidence of mania (no elevated/expansive mood, no decreased need for sleep, no flight of ideas, no risk-taking).<br /></br>• No psychosis.<br /></br>Pattern recognition: depressive episode with atypical features (mood reactivity, hypersomnia, hyperphagia, leaden paralysis–type psychomotor slowing, rejection sensitivity).</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step 1: Identify core mood disturbance → persistent irritability/dysphoria.<br /></br>Step 2: Look for ≥ 5 SIGECAPS symptoms lasting ≥ 2 weeks → Sleep ↑, Interest ↓, Guilt/worthlessness, Energy ↓, Concentration ↓ (grades), Appetite ↑/weight ↑, Psychomotor slowing, Suicidal ideation (not mentioned, but five criteria met).<br /></br>Step 3: Exclude bipolar spectrum → none of the required manic/hypomanic features.<br /></br>Take-home: In teenagers, major depressive disorder often presents as irritability rather than overt sadness and can mimic conduct or oppositional problems.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Multifactorial: genetic vulnerability (altered serotonergic, noradrenergic, dopaminergic signaling), environmental stressors (school/social difficulties), and developmental factors (puberty).<br /></br>• Atypical subtype characterized by mood reactivity and reversed vegetative signs (↑ sleep, ↑ appetite).  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review</br>1. Assess safety (suicidal thoughts/plan).<br /></br>2. First-line therapy:<br /></br>   – Psychotherapy: CBT and/or interpersonal therapy.<br /></br>   – SSRIs (fluoxetine is FDA-approved starting at age 8; escitalopram from age 12).<br /></br>3. If partial response, combination therapy or switch to another SSRI/SNRI.<br /></br>4. Monitor weight, sleep, school functioning, and always screen for emergent manic symptoms when starting antidepressants.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Bipolar disorder: Requires at least 1 manic/hypomanic episode—elevated/expansive mood, decreased need for sleep, increased goal-directed activity, grandiosity, etc.—absent here. The patient’s hypersomnia and psychomotor slowing are opposite of mania.<br /></br>b. Borderline personality disorder: Diagnosis reserved for ≥ 18 y. Would show chronic interpersonal instability, identity disturbance, impulsivity, self-harm. Vegetative depression signs less prominent.<br /></br>c. Generalized anxiety disorder: Persistent, excessive worry about multiple domains for ≥ 6 months with restlessness, muscle tension, sleep disturbance. Her historic “worry” has actually decreased, and neurovegetative depression signs dominate.<br /></br>d. Impulse-control disorder: (e.g., intermittent explosive disorder, kleptomania) characterized by recurrent irresistible impulses; does not explain hypersomnia, weight gain, anhedonia.<br /></br>f. Oppositional defiant disorder: Pattern of angry/irritable mood and vindictiveness toward authority figures beginning in childhood, but lacks depressive neurovegetative symptoms and global functional decline seen here.</p></br><p>Key takeaway: In adolescents, irritability plus atypical neurovegetative changes should trigger consideration of major depressive disorder, not a behavioral or bipolar diagnosis.</p>
96. A 15-year-old boy is brought to the office because of a 2-month history of increasingly frequent left lower thigh pain. The pain began after the patient injured his leg during a soccer game. He has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. Examination shows moderate tenderness of the lateral distal aspect of the left femur. His serum alkaline phosphatase activity is 550 U/L N=130-525, and serum lactate dehydrogenase activity is 360 U/L N=120-330. An x-ray of the left thigh is shown. In addition to an MRI of the left femur, which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Biopsy of the affected area</br>b. CT scan of the thorax</br>c. Gene expression profile</br>d. PET scan of the skeleton</br>e. Three-phase bone scan</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>A. Biopsy of the affected area  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>Key clues that point to a primary malignant bone tumor (most classically osteosarcoma):<br /></br>• Age 15 years (adolescence = rapid bone growth)<br /></br>• Location = distal femur metaphysis (“lateral distal aspect of the femur”)<br /></br>• Chronic, progressively worsening pain not explained by minor trauma<br /></br>• Elevated alkaline phosphatase &amp; LDH → high osteoblastic activity/turnover<br /></br>• X-ray (sunburst pattern/Codman triangle; not shown in text but typical on NBME)  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise work-up of a suspected primary bone malignancy:<br /></br>1. Plain radiograph to detect a suspicious lesion (already done).<br /></br>2. MRI of the entire involved bone to define local extent and neurovascular involvement (ordered).<br /></br>3. Tissue diagnosis with image-guided core needle or open biopsy.<br /></br>   – Histology is mandatory before any treatment or formal staging can begin.  </p></br><p>Therefore the next diagnostic step, after MRI, is biopsy of the lesion.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Imaging can suggest malignancy, but you must obtain tissue before labeling a bone lesion cancer and before undertaking definitive therapy or full metastatic staging.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (Osteosarcoma)<br /></br>• Malignant osteoblasts produce osteoid.<br /></br>• Peak incidence 10-20 y; metaphyses of long bones (distal femur &gt; proximal tibia &gt; proximal humerus).<br /></br>• Risk factors: familial RB, Li-Fraumeni (TP53), Paget disease, radiation.<br /></br>• Radiograph: mixed lytic–sclerotic lesion, periosteal elevation (Codman triangle), sunburst spicules.<br /></br>• Labs: ↑ALP, sometimes ↑LDH; inflammatory markers usually normal.<br /></br>Treatment (after biopsy-confirmed diagnosis &amp; staging):<br /></br>1. Neoadjuvant multi-agent chemotherapy (high-dose methotrexate, doxorubicin, cisplatin).<br /></br>2. Limb-salvage wide resection ± endoprosthesis (or amputation if unresectable).<br /></br>3. Adjuvant chemotherapy. Prognosis depends on presence of metastases (lungs most common).</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. CT scan of the thorax<br /></br>• Purpose: STAGING for pulmonary metastases after diagnosis is established.<br /></br>• Does not provide histology; cannot replace biopsy for initial diagnostic confirmation.</p></br><p>C. Gene expression profile<br /></br>• Relevant for certain tumors (e.g., predicting prognosis in breast CA) but not needed for initial diagnosis of bone tumors; research, not standard of care.</p></br><p>D. PET scan of the skeleton (or total-body PET/CT)<br /></br>• Detects bone and soft-tissue metastases. Like CT chest, it is a staging, not diagnostic, study.</p></br><p>E. Three-phase bone scan<br /></br>• Sensitive for skeletal metastases and occult lesions, but nonspecific and still requires biopsy for diagnosis. Used after confirmation or when radiographs are normal but suspicion remains high (e.g., stress fracture vs osteomyelitis), which is not the case here.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Jumping straight to metastatic work-up without first confirming the histologic diagnosis. Always “look, localize, then biopsy” before staging or treating a suspected primary bone cancer.</p>
97. A previously healthy 18-month-old boy is brought to the emergency department because of a 6-day history of fever. His temperature is 38.5 C 101.3 F pulse is 100/min, respirations are 24/min, and blood pressure is 95/60 mm Hg. Examination shows cracked, red lips and palmar erythema and edema. Cervical lymph nodes are enlarged. There is pharyngeal erythema and bilateral conjunctivitis without injection adjacent to the corneal limbus. Rapid streptococcal testing is negative. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hematocrit</br>Leukocyte count</br>Segmented neutrophils</br>Lymphocytes</br>Monocytes</br>Platelet count</br>Erythrocyte sedimentation rate</br>In addition to intravenous immune globulin, which of the following is the most appropriate pharmacotherapy?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Oral ibuprofen every 6 hours as needed to bring fever below 38.1  C 100.6 F</br>b. Oral high-dose aspirin therapy</br>c. 5-Day course of oral prednisone</br>d. 10-Day course of oral amoxicillin</br>e. 8-Week course of daily, low-dose acetaminophen therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>b. Oral high-dose aspirin therapy</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• 18-month-old, &gt;5 days of fever.<br /></br>• Mucocutaneous changes: cracked, red lips; bilateral non-exudative conjunctivitis sparing the limbus; “strawberry” pharyngeal erythema.<br /></br>• Extremity findings: palmar erythema/edema.<br /></br>• Cervical lymphadenopathy.<br /></br>Pattern = 5 days of fever + ≥4 of the “CRASH” features (Conjunctivitis, Rash, Adenopathy, Strawberry tongue/lip changes, Hand/foot changes) → Classic Kawasaki disease.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Kawasaki disease requires:</br>1. Intravenous immune globulin (IVIG) – decreases coronary artery aneurysm risk.</br>2. Aspirin – the only time aspirin is used in a child with viral-like illness because the thrombotic risk outweighs Reye risk.</br>   • Start high-dose (30–50 mg/kg/day divided q6h) for anti-inflammatory &amp; fever control.</br>   • After the child is afebrile for 48-72 h, switch to low-dose (3–5 mg/kg/day) for 6–8 weeks for antiplatelet effect.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Kawasaki therapy = IVIG + aspirin (high dose, then low dose).</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>Medium-vessel necrotizing vasculitis (pan-vasculitis) predominantly of the coronary arteries. Immune activation → endothelial injury → aneurysm formation. Platelet activation and inflammation cause high risk of thrombosis and myocardial infarction in toddlers.</p></br><p>Treatments / Diagnostic Tests</br>First line:</br>• IVIG (2 g/kg once)</br>• High-dose aspirin as above</p></br><p>Follow-up:</br>• Baseline and repeat echocardiography (2 and 6–8 weeks) to monitor coronary arteries.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Ibuprofen PRN antipyretic<br /></br> – NSAID will suppress fever but provides no antiplatelet effect; does not lower coronary aneurysm risk. Aspirin is uniquely indicated.</p></br><p>c. 5-day oral prednisone<br /></br> – Corticosteroids are second-line for IVIG-resistant Kawasaki or high-risk cases, not part of initial standard therapy.</p></br><p>d. 10-day oral amoxicillin<br /></br> – Appropriate for strep pharyngitis or otitis media; the rapid strep test is negative and the presentation is vasculitis, not bacterial infection.</p></br><p>e. 8-week daily low-dose acetaminophen<br /></br> – Lacks anti-inflammatory and antiplatelet properties; does not alter vascular complications.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Choosing “ibuprofen” or “acetaminophen” because of concern for Reye syndrome. Remember: Kawasaki is the one pediatric febrile illness where aspirin is not only permitted but required.</p>
98. A 35-year-old man, who works as an orthopaedic surgeon, comes to the office because of a 1-day history of severe low back pain after lifting heavy furniture. He has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. He requests a prescription for oxycodone because he is scheduled to give a lecture at a national meeting tomorrow and is worried he will be in too much pain to participate without medication. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management by the physician?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Obtain an MRI of the spine</br>b. Order urine toxicology screening</br>c. Prescribe one tablet of oxycodone</br>d. Report the patient to the state medical board</br>e. Screen the patient for substance use disorder</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Screen the patient for substance-use disorder  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “1-day history” of mechanical low-back pain after lifting a heavy object → common, self-limited musculoskeletal strain; no red-flag features (fever, trauma, neurologic deficit, weight loss, cancer history).<br /></br>• Requests a specific opioid (oxycodone) for a short-term goal (“national lecture tomorrow”) → classic potential drug-seeking behavior.<br /></br>• Works as a physician (orthopaedic surgeon) → access to controlled substances and high professional stakes if impaired, but also higher risk for untreated occupational stress leading to misuse.<br /></br>• Normal exam and vitals → pain is not emergent; conservative therapy (NSAID, heat, activity) is first-line.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Address possible opioid misuse before prescribing.<br /></br>Step 2: Initial approach in suspected substance-use disorder is screening with targeted, non-judgmental questions about frequency, quantity, cravings, control, consequences, and attempts to cut down.<br /></br>Step 3: Further actions (toxicology testing, referral, reporting to the medical board) depend on information obtained from that screen.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: In any patient—especially a colleague—who asks for a specific opioid without clear indication, the first obligation is to assess for substance-use disorder, not to prescribe, image, or immediately report.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (opioid use disorder)<br /></br>• Chronic exposure can lead to neuroadaptive changes in μ-opioid receptors → tolerance, dependence, withdrawal.<br /></br>• Risk factors: health-care workers (easy access), acute injury, psychiatric comorbidity, high-stress jobs.<br /></br>• DSM-5 criteria revolve around impaired control, social impairment, risky use, and pharmacologic criteria (tolerance/withdrawal).  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>1. Screening: Brief validated tools (e.g., Opioid Risk Tool, Substance Use Brief Screen), direct interview.<br /></br>2. Diagnostic confirmation: DSM-5 criteria; urine drug screen AFTER history if suspicion persists.<br /></br>3. Treatment: Medication-assisted therapy (buprenorphine-naloxone, methadone, naltrexone), psychotherapy, physician health programs.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Obtain an MRI of the spine<br /></br>   • Imaging is not indicated in acute (&lt;6 weeks) low-back pain without neurologic deficits or red-flag signs. Would not address opioid-seeking issue.  </p></br><p>b. Order urine toxicology screening<br /></br>   • May be appropriate later, but professional guidelines recommend starting with a verbal screen/history. Jumping straight to a drug test without consent can undermine rapport and violate privacy.  </p></br><p>c. Prescribe one tablet of oxycodone<br /></br>   • Opioids are NOT first-line for acute mechanical back pain and prescribing without assessment risks fueling misuse.  </p></br><p>d. Report the patient to the state medical board<br /></br>   • Reporting is mandated only when a physician’s impairment endangers patients and the physician refuses help. You need evidence first; screening is the necessary initial step.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfalls<br /></br>• Equating any request for opioids with immediate mandatory reporting.<br /></br>• Ordering expensive imaging in uncomplicated back pain.<br /></br>• Believing that “just one pill” is harmless in potential misuse.  </p></br><p>High-Yield Memory Aid<br /></br>“Suspected opioid misuse? 1) SCREEN, 2) CONFIRM, 3) TREAT/REFER, 4) REPORT if necessary.”</p>
99. A 37-year-old woman comes to the physician in January for immunizations 6 weeks before beginning her new job as a nurse's aide in a skilled nursing care facility. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. She has no known allergies. Four years ago, she completed the hepatitis 8 vaccine series and received a tetanus booster. She lives with her husband and two healthy school-aged children. Examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is most appropriate to administer at this visit?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Hepatitis 8 booster</br>b. Hepatitis 8 immune globulin</br>c. Inactivated influenza virus vaccine</br>d. Pneu mococcal polysaccharide vaccine, 23-valent</br>e. No immunizations are indicated at this time</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>c. Inactivated influenza virus vaccine</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• “January” + “beginning her new job as a nurse’s aide” → She will be a health-care worker with direct patient contact during peak influenza season.</br>• “Completed the hepatitis B vaccine series 4 years ago” → Full series confers long-lasting immunity; no routine boosters are recommended in immunocompetent adults.</br>• “Tetanus booster 4 years ago” → Next Td/Tdap not due until 10 years after the last dose.</br>• Age 37, no chronic illnesses → Does not meet criteria for adult pneumococcal vaccination.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Annual influenza vaccination is recommended for (1) every person ≥6 months old and (2) especially for health-care personnel and those working with high-risk populations (e.g., nursing-home residents). Because she is &lt;50 years old and not pregnant, the injected trivalent/quadrivalent inactivated vaccine is preferred; the live-attenuated intranasal formulation is generally avoided in health-care settings due to the theoretical risk of viral shedding to vulnerable patients.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Inactivated influenza vaccine is the one immunization virtually every adult needs every year, and it is especially mandatory for health-care workers.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>Influenza viruses undergo frequent antigenic drift (minor mutations in hemagglutinin and neuraminidase), so circulating strains change year to year. Immunity from prior vaccination or natural infection wanes and may not protect against new strains, necessitating yearly vaccination. The inactivated (killed) vaccine induces primarily humoral (IgG) antibody responses against hemagglutinin; these antibodies neutralize the virus and prevent entry into respiratory epithelial cells.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review</br>• Vaccine types: </br>  – Inactivated (IM) – recommended for all adults, safe in pregnancy and the immunocompromised.</br>  – Live attenuated (intranasal) – for healthy, non-pregnant persons 2–49 yr; not for health-care workers caring for severely immunocompromised patients.</br>• Timing: Give ideally in September–October but continue to vaccinate throughout the winter if it has not yet been given.</br>No other routine tests are needed before vaccination.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Hepatitis B booster<br /></br>   – A properly completed 3-dose series provides long-term (&gt;20 yr, possibly lifelong) protection in immunocompetent adults; boosters are not recommended unless serologic testing shows inadequate response (anti-HBs &lt;10 mIU/mL) in high-risk groups such as hemodialysis patients.</p></br><p>b. Hepatitis B immune globulin<br /></br>   – Used for post-exposure prophylaxis (e.g., needle stick in non-immune person, newborn of HBsAg-positive mother). She has no exposure and is already immune.</p></br><p>d. Pneumococcal polysaccharide vaccine (PPSV23)<br /></br>   – Indicated routinely at age ≥65 or earlier in adults with chronic heart, lung, liver disease, diabetes, alcoholism, CSF leak, cochlear implant, asplenia, or immunocompromise. A healthy 37-year-old does not qualify.</p></br><p>e. No immunizations are indicated at this time<br /></br>   – Incorrect because annual influenza vaccination is always indicated; her occupation further mandates it.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall</br>Students often overlook the universal annual influenza recommendation, thinking vaccines are “up to date” after reviewing childhood and booster schedules. Remember: Influenza is the evergreen answer for “Which vaccine does every adult need this year?”</p>
100. A 23-year-old man is brought to the emergency department after driving his automobile into a bridge abutment. Paramedics state that at the scene his b ood pressure was 100/80 mm Hg, that he was alert, and that the steering wheel was broken. On examination in the emergency department now, he is stuporous, cyanotic, and hypotensive, and has distended neck veins. Breath sounds are absent bilaterally. The trachea is midline. Which of the following is the most appropriate initial management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Determine arterial blood gas values</br>b. Do pericardiocentesis</br>c. Insert needles into the left and right sides of the chest</br>d. Order x-rays of the chest</br>e. Order ECG</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>c. Insert needles into the left and right sides of the chest (bilateral emergency needle decompression)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis – Key Words/Phrases<br /></br>• “Motor-vehicle crash, steering wheel was broken” → high-energy blunt chest trauma.<br /></br>• “Now stuporous, cyanotic, hypotensive, distended neck veins” → obstructive shock.<br /></br>• “Breath sounds are absent bilaterally” → lungs not ventilating; think pneumothorax or massive hemothorax.<br /></br>• “Trachea is midline” → rules out a unilateral tension pneumo but is perfectly compatible with a bilateral tension pneumothorax (equal pressure on both sides keeps mediastinum centered).<br /></br>Taken together: obstructive shock + bilateral absent breath sounds = bilateral tension pneumothorax until proven otherwise.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>• In any suspected tension pneumothorax, treatment always precedes diagnostics – do not wait for CXR, ABG, or CT.<br /></br>• Needle decompression (14-gauge; 2nd ICS mid-clavicular or 5th ICS anterior axillary) converts a tension pneumo to a simple pneumo, relieving the obstructive physiology and restoring venous return.<br /></br>• Because breath sounds are absent on both sides and the patient is crashing, needles go in both sides immediately.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In a hemodynamically unstable trauma patient with absent breath sounds, treat presumed (bi)lateral tension pneumothorax first; never delay for tests.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Tension pneumothorax = progressive intrapleural air accumulation under positive pressure → ipsilateral lung collapse + mediastinal shift/compression of great veins → ↓ venous return, obstructive shock, JVD, hypoxia. If bilateral, mediastinum may remain midline but venous return is still impeded. Death is from cardiovascular collapse, not hypoxia alone.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>1. Immediate: Needle thoracostomy (18- or 14-g large-bore needle).<br /></br>2. Definitive: Follow with large-caliber chest tubes (tube thoracostomy) in each hemithorax.<br /></br>3. After stabilization: CXR to confirm placement, evaluate for additional injuries; ABG to assess oxygenation/ventilation.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Options Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Determine arterial blood gas values<br /></br>   • ABG helps quantify hypoxemia/ventilation but provides no therapeutic benefit and wastes precious minutes; tension pneumothorax kills within seconds-minutes.</p></br><p>b. Do pericardiocentesis<br /></br>   • Cardiac tamponade (hypotension + JVD + muffled heart sounds, normal breath sounds) would not cause absent breath sounds bilaterally. Breath sounds distinguish between tamponade and pneumo.</p></br><p>d. Order x-rays of the chest<br /></br>   • Classic test-taking trap. A CXR is diagnostic but must never precede life-saving decompression in a crashing patient.</p></br><p>e. Order ECG<br /></br>   • Helpful for arrhythmia or myocardial injury, but irrelevant in immediate management of obstructive shock from tension pneumothorax.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Believing that tracheal deviation must be present to diagnose a tension pneumothorax. With bilateral tension or early/partial tension, the mediastinum may remain midline—do not let the absence of deviation delay treatment.</p></br><p>Key Exam Pearls<br /></br>• “Absent breath sounds + hypotension + JVD = tension pneumothorax until proven otherwise.”<br /></br>• Treatment, not diagnosis, comes first.</p>
101. A 62-year-old man with large cell non-Hodgkin lymphoma is brought to the emergency department because of a 3-day history of fatigue, headache, dizziness, and facial flushing that is exacerbated by bending forward. His only medication is omeprazole. On arrival, he appears slightly cyanotic. His temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 96/min, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 140/90 mm Hg. Examination shows brawny edema of the neck and face. The veins over the anterior chest and shoulders are dilated. Breath sounds are normal. Heart sounds are distant. A CT scan of the chest shows clea progression in the size and distribution of mediastinal and abdominal lymphadenopathy; the inferior vena cava is posteriorly displaced. Intravenous hydrocortisone therapy is started. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Tissue plasminogen activator therapy</br>b. Plasmapheresis</br>c. Carotid stent placement</br>d. Radiation therapy</br>e. Thoracotomy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Radiation therapy  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 62-year-old man with known large-cell non-Hodgkin lymphoma → highly radiosensitive malignancy.<br /></br>• “Fatigue, headache, dizziness, facial flushing that is worse when bending forward,” “brawny edema of the neck and face,” and “dilated veins over the anterior chest and shoulders” → classic constellation of superior vena cava (SVC) syndrome.<br /></br>• CT: marked progression of mediastinal lymphadenopathy with posterior displacement of the inferior vena cava (ie, bulky mediastinal nodes compressing the SVC). No mention of intraluminal clot.<br /></br>• IV hydrocortisone has been started → temporary medical measure to shrink lymphoid tissue; definitive rapid relief still required.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Key question: What is the quickest, most effective way to relieve an extrinsic malignant SVC obstruction in a patient with a radiosensitive tumor (lymphoma)?<br /></br>• Lymphomas respond dramatically within 24–48 h to external-beam radiation.<br /></br>• Endovascular stenting is an alternative for non-radiosensitive tumors (eg, lung cancer) or when tissue diagnosis is still pending, but not listed here.<br /></br>Therefore, immediate mediastinal radiation is the correct next step.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: In malignant SVC syndrome due to a radiosensitive tumor (lymphoma, germ-cell tumor, small-cell lung cancer), urgent external-beam radiation is the treatment of choice once steroids have been given.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>1. Extrinsic compression of the SVC → ↑ venous pressure in the head, neck, and upper extremities.<br /></br>2. Impaired venous return worsens with positions that increase central venous pressure (bending, lying flat).<br /></br>3. Lymphomas proliferate rapidly; glucocorticoids cause tumor lysis and edema reduction, but radiation provides swift debulking by inducing DNA breaks and apoptosis in malignant lymphocytes.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>First-line (lymphoma)<br /></br>• IV dexamethasone or hydrocortisone: decreases tumor-associated edema.<br /></br>• Urgent mediastinal radiation therapy: rapid symptom relief (hours to days).<br /></br>• Subsequent systemic chemotherapy tailored to histology.  </p></br><p>Alternative / adjunctive measures<br /></br>• Endovascular SVC stent + anticoagulation: radiosurgery-resistant tumors, need for immediate patency, or when radiation is contraindicated.<br /></br>• Thrombolysis (tPA): only if imaging confirms intraluminal thrombosis (catheter-associated).<br /></br>• Surgical bypass (thoracotomy) is rarely necessary today.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Tissue plasminogen activator therapy<br /></br>   – Appropriate for acute thrombotic SVC obstruction (central line–induced clot). CT instead shows external compression; no clot reported.  </p></br><p>b. Plasmapheresis<br /></br>   – Used for hyperviscosity (Waldenström macroglobulinemia, multiple myeloma) producing headache, blurry vision, mucosal bleeding—patients lack facial plethora from positional venous congestion.  </p></br><p>c. Carotid stent placement<br /></br>   – Treats carotid artery stenosis to prevent stroke; unrelated to venous obstruction signs.  </p></br><p>e. Thoracotomy<br /></br>   – Open surgical resection/bypass reserved for benign causes or tumors unresponsive to chemo/radiation; far more invasive and not first-line.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Confusing SVC compression (extrinsic) with thrombotic occlusion; always correlate imaging. Lymphoma = radiosensitive → think “steroids + radiation.”</p>
102. A 42-year-old woman comes to the physician because of increasingly severe right heel pain and swelling since she stepped off balance onto the foot 4 weeks a o; the pain increases with weight-bearing and decreases with rest. She has systemic lupus erythematosus and chronic renal failure. She receives hemodialysis. Her only medication is hydroxychloroquine. Vital signs are within normal limits. The skin over the lower extremities is intact. Dorsalis pedis pulses are 1+ bilaterally. The posterior tuberosity of the right calcaneus is tender to palpation. Motor examination shows no abnormalities. There is pes planus bilaterally. Sensation to light touch is intact over the lower extremities. An x-ray of the right ankle is shown. Serum studies are most likely to show an increased concentration of which of the following in this patient?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Calcitonin</br>b. 1,25-Dihydroxyvitamin D</br>c. 25-Hydroxyvitamin D</br>d. Ionized calcium</br>e. Parathyroid hormone</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Parathyroid hormone (PTH)</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Chronic renal failure … receives hemodialysis” → think renal osteodystrophy (secondary hyperparathyroidism).<br /></br>• “Heel pain, tenderness over posterior calcaneus” with x-ray change → subperiosteal bone resorption/cystic changes typical of hyperparathyroid bone disease.<br /></br>• No glucocorticoids (rules out avascular necrosis); intact sensation &amp; pulses argue against neuropathic/vascular ulcer.<br /></br>Key pattern: Chronic kidney disease + bone pain/fracture-like lesion = ↑PTH.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Failing kidneys cannot excrete phosphate → hyperphosphatemia.<br /></br>2. Phosphate binds serum Ca²⁺ → ↓ionized Ca²⁺.<br /></br>3. Kidneys also cannot 1-α-hydroxylate 25-OH-vitamin D → ↓1,25-OH₂-D → further ↓Ca²⁺ absorption.<br /></br>4. Low Ca²⁺ chronically stimulates the parathyroid glands → markedly ↑PTH (secondary hyperparathyroidism).<br /></br>5. PTH causes subperiosteal cortical bone resorption, classically at phalanges and calcaneus → pain &amp; x-ray abnormalities.</p></br><p>Take-home: In CKD the lab hallmark is high PTH with low/normal Ca²⁺ and low 1,25-OH₂-vitamin D.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Phosphate retention + loss of renal 1-α hydroxylase activity.<br /></br>• Mechanism: Chronic hypocalcemic stimulus → parathyroid hyperplasia → excessive PTH → osteitis fibrosa cystica (brown tumors, bone pain, fractures).<br /></br>• Clinical manifestations: Bone pain, muscle weakness, vascular/soft-tissue calcifications.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>First-line therapy for secondary hyperparathyroidism in CKD:<br /></br>• Dietary phosphate restriction + oral phosphate binders (sevelamer, calcium acetate).<br /></br>• Active vitamin D analogs (calcitriol) or calcimimetics (cinacalcet).<br /></br>• Refractory cases → surgical subtotal parathyroidectomy.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Calcitonin – Secreted by thyroid C-cells, rises with medullary thyroid carcinoma or exogenous use; not elevated in CKD-related bone disease.<br /></br>b. 1,25-Dihydroxyvitamin D – LOW in CKD because kidneys can’t perform 1-α hydroxylation.<br /></br>c. 25-Hydroxyvitamin D – Storage form made in liver; usually normal in CKD (unless malnutrition/sunlight deficiency).<br /></br>d. Ionized calcium – Tends to be low or low-normal (consumed by high phosphate &amp; low vitamin D); it is not elevated.<br /></br>e. Parathyroid hormone – Correct. Compensatory rise is the defining laboratory abnormality in secondary hyperparathyroidism.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Core concept: Chronic kidney disease → phosphate retention &amp; loss of active vitamin D → hypocalcemia → secondary hyperparathyroidism → ↑PTH with bone resorption visible on imaging.</p>
103. A 72-year-old woman comes to the physician for a routine follow-up examination. She has type 2 diabetes mellitus, hypertension, chronic kidney disease, and osteoarthritis of the knees. She says that she feels well except for mild swelling of her feet, which has made her shoes feel tighter. Her medications are lisinopril, naproxen, acetaminophen, and metformin. She is 170 cm 5 ft 7 in tall and weighs 81 kg 178 lb; BMI is 28 kg/m 2. She has gained 2.3 kg 5 lb since her last examination 1 month ago. Her temperature is 36.8 C 98.2 F, pulse is 72/min, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 112/62 mm Hg. Cardiopulmonary examination shows no abnormalities. There is 1+ pitting edema of the ankles. Her hemoglobin A 1c is 6.1  percent, and serum creatinine concentration is 2.1 mg/dl, unchanged from last year. Metformin therapy is discontinued. Which of the following is the most effective measure to improve this patient's renal function?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Add furosemide to the medication regimen</br>b. Add hydrochlorothiazide to the medication regimen</br>c. Discontinue acetaminophen</br>d. Discontinue lisinopril</br>e. Discontinue naproxen</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Discontinue naproxen  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Chronic kidney disease: baseline creatinine 2.1 mg/dL, long-standing diabetes and hypertension<br /></br>• New findings: +2.3 kg (5 lb) weight gain, 1+ ankle edema, “shoes feel tighter”<br /></br>• Medication list: lisinopril (ACE-I), naproxen (NSAID), metformin, acetaminophen  </p></br><p>Key phrase: “mild swelling of her feet” in a patient on an NSAID who already has CKD. The question asks for the measure that will “improve this patient’s renal function,” not just symptomatic edema relief.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. What is worsening renal perfusion?<br /></br>   • ACE-I → efferent arteriole dilation (lowers intraglomerular pressure) – actually renoprotective long-term in diabetic nephropathy.<br /></br>   • NSAID → block prostaglandin synthesis → afferent arteriole vasoconstriction → ↓ renal blood flow/GFR.<br /></br>   • The combination (ACE-I + NSAID ± diuretic) is the classic “triple whammy” for prerenal azotemia/CKD progression.  </p></br><ol></br><li>Removing the offending hemodynamic stressor (NSAID) reverses or slows the decline in GFR best. Loop/thiazide diuretics would help edema but not the underlying GFR drop; stopping lisinopril would remove a renal-protective drug.</li></br></ol></br><p>Take-home: In CKD, avoid chronic NSAIDs because they acutely decrease GFR and chronically accelerate interstitial nephritis and papillary necrosis.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• NSAIDs inhibit COX-1/2 → ↓ renal vasodilatory prostaglandins (PGE2, PGI2) → afferent arteriole constriction → ↓ glomerular perfusion pressure → reversible fall in GFR; chronic use causes chronic interstitial nephritis and papillary necrosis.<br /></br>• ACE inhibitors dilate the efferent arteriole → lower intraglomerular pressure, slow proteinuric kidney disease progression.<br /></br>• In diabetics, ACE/ARB therapy is first-line nephroprotection unless contraindicated.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>First-line for edema with CKD: sodium restriction ± loop diuretic if symptomatic; but priority is removing nephrotoxins (NSAIDs, IV contrast, certain antibiotics).<br /></br>Monitoring: repeat serum creatinine 1–2 wks after stopping NSAID; UA/proteinuria quantification; BP control.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Add furosemide – Helps ankle edema but does not improve (and can transiently worsen) renal perfusion; does nothing for NSAID-mediated vasoconstriction.<br /></br>b. Add hydrochlorothiazide – Similar to above; weaker diuretic, no effect on NSAID-induced GFR drop.<br /></br>c. Discontinue acetaminophen – Therapeutic acetaminophen is NOT nephrotoxic; only very rare with massive chronic abuse. No benefit for renal function.<br /></br>d. Discontinue lisinopril – Would raise intraglomerular pressure short-term, but sacrifices long-term diabetic nephropathy protection; overall harms kidney.<br /></br>e. Discontinue naproxen – Removes the afferent vasoconstrictor, improves renal blood flow, and halts NSAID-induced chronic injury. (Correct).</p>
104. A 52-year-old woman who has a 20-year history of type 2 diabetes mellitus comes to the physician because of a 1-day history of double vision that resolves when she closes either eye. She has no other symptoms. Her only medication is metformin. Vital signs are within normal limits. She is unable to abduct her left eye. Visual acuity is 20/20 in both eyes. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is</br>the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Mononeuropathy</br>b. Multiple sclerosis</br>c. Myasthenia gravis</br>d. Temporal arteritis</br>e. Vertebrobasilar infarction</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Mononeuropathy (isolated diabetic cranial nerve palsy)</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “20-year history of type 2 diabetes mellitus” – longstanding diabetes predisposes to micro-vascular nerve ischemia.<br /></br>• “1-day history of double vision that resolves when she closes either eye” – true binocular diplopia (requires both eyes open), pointing to an extra-ocular muscle/cranial nerve problem.<br /></br>• “Unable to abduct her left eye” – classic finding of a left abducens (CN VI) palsy.<br /></br>• “Visual acuity 20/20 … remainder of the examination normal” – no optic neuropathy, no other focal deficits → isolated nerve involvement.</p></br><p>These four clues fit the pattern of a diabetic ischemic mononeuropathy of CN VI.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Identify the pattern – acute painless binocular diplopia + isolated failure of abduction = CN VI palsy.<br /></br>Step 2: Ask “Why CN VI?” – common causes are micro-vascular ischemia (diabetes, HTN), increased ICP, or brain-stem lesions.<br /></br>Step 3: Diabetes + no other neurologic signs → micro-vascular mononeuropathy most likely.</p></br><p>Core concept: Long-standing diabetes can cause an acute, isolated cranial nerve III, IV, or VI palsy via ischemia of the vasa nervorum; CN VI is the most frequent.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Chronic hyperglycemia → non-enzymatic glycosylation → hyaline arteriolosclerosis of small vessels (vasa nervorum) supplying cranial nerves.<br /></br>• Focal ischemia → demyelination/axon loss limited to one nerve → abrupt onset palsy.<br /></br>• Pupillary fibers (in CN III palsies) and other cranial nerves are usually spared because the ischemia is patchy and central.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>• Most diabetic CN VI palsies improve spontaneously within 6–12 weeks.<br /></br>• Supportive care: eye patch or prism glasses for diplopia; optimize glycemic control, blood pressure, lipids.<br /></br>• Imaging is not mandatory when presentation is classic, but MRI is obtained if atypical features (pain, multiple nerves, progressive course) are present.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>b. Multiple sclerosis<br /></br>   – Typically affects women 20–40 y. Presents with relapsing neurologic deficits (optic neuritis, INO, sensory loss). Would expect additional focal signs and MRI plaques; isolated CN VI palsy without other findings is unusual, especially at age 52.</p></br><p>c. Myasthenia gravis<br /></br>   – Causes fatigable ptosis and diplopia but eye movement limitation is variable and not restricted to a single direction/nerve. Pupils normal (like here), yet examination often shows fluctuating weakness, worsening later in the day, limb or bulbar involvement.</p></br><p>d. Temporal arteritis (giant-cell arteritis)<br /></br>   – Age &gt;50 but usually presents with new severe headache, scalp tenderness, jaw claudication, elevated ESR. Vision loss is from ischemic optic neuropathy, not an isolated abducens palsy.</p></br><p>e. Vertebrobasilar infarction<br /></br>   – Brain-stem stroke would produce crossed findings (e.g., CN deficits plus contralateral motor/sensory loss), ataxia, vertigo. An isolated CN VI palsy with a completely normal neurologic exam elsewhere is atypical.</p></br><p>Key takeaway: In a diabetic patient, a sudden painless diplopia from a single extra-ocular muscle deficit = diabetic ischemic cranial mononeuropathy until proven otherwise.</p>
105. A 57-year-old woman comes to the physician for evaluation 2 weeks prior to an operation for varicose veins. Two months ago, she received the diagnosis of Graves disease, and propylthiouracil therapy was begun at that time. She has hypertension controlled with hydrochlorothiazide. She also has seasonal rhinitis and takes over-the-counter decongestants as needed. She occasionally takes acetaminophen for headache. Her temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 76/min , respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 134/82 mm Hg. Examination of the lower extremities shows extensive varicose veins along the inner aspect of the left calf. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hematocrit</br>Leukocyte count</br>Segmented neutrophils</br>Bands</br>Eosinophils</br>Lymphocytes</br>Monocytes</br>Serum</br>Na+</br>K+</br>c1-</br>HC03</br>-</br>Urea nitrogen</br>Creatinine</br>41 percent</br>1000/mm 3</br>25 percent</br>2 percent</br>6 percent</br>59 percent</br>8 percent</br>138 mEq/L</br>3.7 mEq/L</br>103 mEq/L</br>24 mEq/L</br>16 mg/dL</br>1 mg/dL</br>Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Obtain blood cultures</br>b. Begin oral broad-spectrum antibiotic therapy</br>c. Begin oral prednisone therapy</br>d. Discontinue hydrochlorothiazide</br>e. Discontinue propylthiouracil</br>F. Begin intravenous methylprednisolone therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct answer selection<br /></br>e. Discontinue propylthiouracil</p></br><p>Vignette analysis<br /></br>• “2 months ago … Graves disease … propylthiouracil therapy was begun” – recent exposure to an antithyroid thionamide.<br /></br>• “Leukocyte count 1,000/mm³” with “segmented neutrophils 25%, bands 2%” → ANC ≈ 270/​mm³ (severe neutropenia/agranulocytosis; ANC &lt; 500).<br /></br>• Afebrile, normal vitals, no infectious symptoms → incidental lab finding during pre-op evaluation.<br /></br>Key pattern: A patient on PTU or methimazole who develops sudden, severe neutropenia = classic drug-induced agranulocytosis.</p></br><p>Clinical critical reasoning<br /></br>1. Thionamides (propylthiouracil, methimazole) can cause idiosyncratic, immune-mediated destruction of granulocytes within the first 90 days of therapy.<br /></br>2. The first and most important step is to stop the offending drug immediately to prevent further marrow suppression; spontaneous recovery usually follows.<br /></br>3.
