Empiric antibiotics or blood cultures are reserved if the patient is febrile or shows signs of infection—neither is present here.<br /></br>Take-home: Any patient on PTU/methimazole with ANC &lt; 500 → discontinue the drug immediately.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology review<br /></br>• Mechanism: Thionamides can trigger anti-granulocyte antibodies → peripheral destruction and marrow aplasia.<br /></br>• Onset: Sudden, usually within the first 3 months of therapy; not dose-dependent.<br /></br>• Clinical manifestations: Fever, sore throat, or asymptomatic leukopenia discovered incidentally.<br /></br>• Prognosis: ANC recovers in 1–2 weeks after drug cessation; give G-CSF if profoundly neutropenic or infected.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; diagnostic tests<br /></br>• Immediate discontinuation of thionamide.<br /></br>• Counsel patient to seek care if fever or sore throat develops in the future.<br /></br>• Alternate Graves therapy options: radioactive iodine ablation or subtotal thyroidectomy; or switch to methimazole only after hematologic recovery if absolutely necessary (cross-reactivity risk 50%).<br /></br>• No role for steroids, hydrochlorothiazide withdrawal, or routine antibiotics in an afebrile patient.</p></br><p>Incorrect answer analysis<br /></br>a. Obtain blood cultures – indicated when infection is suspected (fever, chills, hypotension). This patient is asymptomatic; cultures do not address the underlying cause (drug-induced agranulocytosis).<br /></br>b. Begin oral broad-spectrum antibiotic therapy – prophylactic antibiotics are not recommended in asymptomatic neutropenia; reserve for febrile neutropenia.<br /></br>c. Begin oral prednisone therapy – glucocorticoids do not reverse drug-induced agranulocytosis and carry immunosuppressive risk.<br /></br>d. Discontinue hydrochlorothiazide – thiazides can cause hyponatremia or hyperuricemia, not isolated agranulocytosis in this context; electrolytes are normal.<br /></br>f. Begin intravenous methylprednisolone therapy – same reasoning as option c; unnecessary and potentially harmful.</p></br><p>Core concept<br /></br>Thionamides can acutely wipe out neutrophils. The moment you see a very low ANC in a patient on PTU/methimazole, stop the drug first—everything else is secondary.</p>
106. Four days after admission to the hospital because of abdominal pain and dehydration, a 52-year-old man with metastatic colon cancer is demanding additional medication to relieve his pain. On admission, treatment with intravenous crystalloid and acetaminophen was begun. On hospital day 2, his pain increased in severity, and he was switched to intravenous hydromorphone, which provided adequate analgesia. This morning, in preparation for discharge, he was switched to oral hydromorphone at the same dose. Eight hours later, he said the pain relief was inadequate, but the oral medication was maintained. Since then, he anxiously has been anticipating each dose of oral hydromorphone by watching the clock. He has no history of substance use disorder. On mental status examination, he has an anxious mood and is preoccupied with receiving pain medication. Which of the following is the most likely explanation for this patient's inadequate pain relief?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Development of tolerance to hydromorphone</br>b. Increased volume of distribution of hydromorphone</br>c. Loss of placebo effect from the injection</br>d. Lower potency of oral hydromorphone</br>e. Pain disorder</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Lower potency (↓ oral bioavailability) of oral hydromorphone  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Adequate analgesia on IV hydromorphone (hospital day 2 → morning of day 4).<br /></br>• Same milligram dose simply switched from IV to PO.<br /></br>• Eight hours later the patient reports inadequate relief and is “clock-watching” for the next dose → classic sign of undertreatment, not addiction or psychopathology.<br /></br>• No Hx of substance use disorder; pain is cancer-related.<br /></br>Key phrase: “at the same dose” after route change – invites you to think about an equianalgesic conversion error.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1  Identify the cause of the sudden loss of effect: only variable changed = route of administration.<br /></br>Step 2  Recall that most opioids have much lower oral bioavailability due to extensive first-pass hepatic metabolism.<br /></br>Step 3  Conclude that the same milligram quantity delivers a smaller active systemic dose when taken orally → undertreatment.<br /></br>Take-home: Always perform IV-to-PO equianalgesic calculations; oral doses are generally several-fold higher than IV doses to achieve the same analgesia.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Hydromorphone (a μ-opioid receptor agonist) undergoes significant first-pass metabolism in the liver. IV bioavailability = 100 %. Oral bioavailability ≈ 30–35 %. Therefore, an oral dose must be roughly 3–4 × the IV dose to produce equivalent plasma levels and receptor occupancy. Failure to account for this pharmacokinetic difference results in inadequate analgesia.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Diagnostic Pearl<br /></br>Equianalgesic conversion tables:<br /></br>• IV hydromorphone 1 mg ≈ oral hydromorphone 4 mg (approximate).<br /></br>• When switching routes, calculate the new dose, then usually reduce by 25–50 % to account for incomplete cross-tolerance if therapy has been &gt; 1 week.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Development of tolerance to hydromorphone<br /></br>– Clinically meaningful tolerance to analgesic effects typically develops over days–weeks, not within hours of a route change. The patient was on the drug for only 2 days IV.<br /></br>b. Increased volume of distribution of hydromorphone<br /></br>– Vd is drug-specific and patient-specific (age, obesity) but does not suddenly increase when switching from IV to PO and would not explain abrupt loss of analgesia.<br /></br>c. Loss of placebo effect from the injection<br /></br>– Placebo effect can slightly modify pain perception but cannot account for a several-fold drop in opioid potency. Also, cancer patients have organic pain.<br /></br>e. Pain disorder<br /></br>– Somatic symptom/pain disorder is a diagnosis of exclusion and unlikely in a patient with documented metastatic colon cancer and prior objective response to opioids.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Core Takeaway<br /></br>When converting an opioid from IV to oral administration you must adjust for markedly reduced oral bioavailability; failing to do so leads to undertreatment, clock-watching, and anxiety that can be mistaken for drug-seeking behavior.</p>
107. Ten days after undergoing vagotomy, pyloroplasty, and oversewing for a bleeding duodenal ulcer, a 67-year-old man is brought to the emergency department because of fever, difficulty breathing, and left shoulder pain. He was previously feeling well and tolerating a normal diet. His only medication is oxycodone with acetaminophen. His temperature is 38.5 C 101.3 F, respirations are 34/min, and blood pressure is 140/85 mm Hg. Breath sounds are decreased over the left lung base. Abdominal examination shows tenderness over the leffl: upper quadrant; the incision site is healing well. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. An x-ray of the chest shows a raised left hemidiaphragm. Which of the following is the most likely cause of these current findings?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Diaphragm injury</br>b. Left lobar collapse</br>c. Spleen injury</br>d. Subphrenic abscess</br>e. Vagus nerve injury</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Subphrenic abscess  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Ten days after … vagotomy, pyloroplasty, and oversewing for a bleeding duodenal ulcer” → recent upper abdominal surgery (high-risk period for postoperative intra-abdominal abscesses; typically develop 1–3 wk post-op).<br /></br>• “Fever, difficulty breathing, and left shoulder pain” → classic triad for a subphrenic abscess (fever + diaphragmatic irritation causing referred shoulder pain via phrenic nerve + respiratory symptoms).<br /></br>• “Breath sounds decreased over the left lung base” and “x-ray … raised left hemidiaphragm” → splinting/elevation of diaphragm from fluid-filled abscess underneath.<br /></br>• “Left upper-quadrant tenderness” but well-healed incision → deep, not superficial, source of infection.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise logic:<br /></br>1. Post-op abdominal patient with new fever → think infectious complication.<br /></br>2. Location-specific clues (LUQ tenderness, shoulder pain, elevated hemidiaphragm) localize infection to subphrenic space.<br /></br>3. Timing (≈10 d) fits abscess formation; other listed complications (atelectasis, splenic bleed, diaphragmatic laceration) occur immediately or within first 48 h and typically do not cause fever this late.<br /></br>Take-home: In any post-laparotomy patient who develops late fever + shoulder pain + diaphragmatic elevation, suspect a subphrenic abscess.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: contamination of the subphrenic potential space during upper GI surgery or from leakage of gastric/duodenal contents.<br /></br>• Abscess produces localized inflammation → diaphragmatic irritation (phrenic nerve C3–C5) → referred ipsilateral shoulder pain.<br /></br>• Mass effect elevates hemidiaphragm, limits lung expansion → basilar atelectasis and ↓ breath sounds, tachypnea.  </p></br><p>Diagnostics<br /></br>• Upright CXR: elevated hemidiaphragm ± air-fluid level under diaphragm.<br /></br>• Best test: contrast-enhanced CT abdomen (or ultrasound) to localize and size the abscess.  </p></br><p>Treatment<br /></br>1. Broad-spectrum IV antibiotics covering enteric Gram-negatives and anaerobes (e.g., piperacillin-tazobactam).<br /></br>2. Definitive: percutaneous CT-guided drainage; open surgical drainage if necessary.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Diaphragm injury<br /></br>   – Would present immediately after surgery with respiratory distress or bowel sounds in chest, not delayed fever.<br /></br>   – CXR would show herniated abdominal contents, not just elevation.<br /></br>b. Left lobar collapse (atelectasis)<br /></br>   – Common within first 24–48 h post-op due to mucus plugging.<br /></br>   – Produces dyspnea/tachypnea but rarely high fever after POD 2.<br /></br>   – CXR shows homogeneous opacity of the lobe, not an elevated diaphragm.<br /></br>c. Spleen injury<br /></br>   – Causes acute intra-abdominal hemorrhage: hypotension, tachycardia, dropping Hgb, left shoulder (Kehr) pain within hours of surgery.<br /></br>   – Fever uncommon unless secondary infection of hematoma; timing and vitals don’t fit.<br /></br>e. Vagus nerve injury<br /></br>   – Intentional truncal vagotomy already performed; vagal injury leads to delayed gastric emptying or diarrhea, not infection, fever, or diaphragmatic elevation.  </p></br><p>Core Concept<br /></br>Postoperative subphrenic abscess should be suspected 7–21 days after upper abdominal surgery when a patient develops fever, shoulder pain, LUQ tenderness, and an elevated hemidiaphragm with basilar lung findings.</p>
108. A 65-year-old man comes to the office for a routine examination. He sustained an ST-elevation myocardial infarction 1 year ago with subsequent revascularization and stent placement. The patient says he has felt well since his coronary procedure, and he reports no exercise intolerance, chest pain, shortness of breath, or other symptoms. He also has coronary artery disease, hypertension, and hyperlipidemia. Medications are clopidogrel, lisinopril, metoprolol, and atorvastatin. The patient smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 45 years but quit after his myocardial infarction. He drinks one glass of wine nightly and does not use illicit drugs. He does yard work and walks for 20 minutes three times weekly. He is 180 cm 5 ft 11 in tall and weighs 104 kg 229 lb; 8MI is 32 kg/m 2. Vital signs are normal. Physical examination discloses no abnormalities. Repeat ECG is unchanged from his last ECG. Which of the following is the most appropriate study for disease surveillance?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Cardiac CT angiography</br>b. Echocardiography</br>c. Left-heart catheterization</br>d. Radionuclide myocardial perfusion imaging</br>e. No additional studies are indicated</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. No additional studies are indicated  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key phrases: “1 year ago ST-elevation myocardial infarction with stent placement,” “has felt well since,” “no exercise intolerance, chest pain, shortness of breath, or other symptoms,” “normal vital signs, normal physical exam, unchanged ECG.”<br /></br>• These indicate an asymptomatic, clinically stable, post-PCI patient who is already on optimal, guideline-directed medical therapy and secondary-prevention lifestyle measures.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>• After successful revascularization, the goal is aggressive risk-factor modification and surveillance for new symptoms—not routine anatomic or functional testing.<br /></br>• The ACC/AHA guidelines explicitly state that in an asymptomatic patient &gt;5 days post-MI/PCI, routine stress testing, echocardiography, CTA, or invasive angiography provides no outcome benefit and may lead to unnecessary procedures.<br /></br>Take-home message: In the absence of new or worsening symptoms or evidence of left-ventricular dysfunction, no additional cardiac imaging is warranted.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (concise)<br /></br>• Post-MI patients are at lifelong risk of recurrent ischemia due to progression of atherosclerosis or in-stent restenosis; however, the first clinical sign is usually new exertional symptoms.<br /></br>• Imaging studies are reserved for:<br /></br>  – New or worsening angina equivalents<br /></br>  – Heart-failure symptoms (echo)<br /></br>  – Arrhythmias or unexplained syncope<br /></br>  – Pre-operative cardiac risk stratification in selected cases  </p></br><p>Treatments / Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Ongoing management:<br /></br>1. Dual antiplatelet therapy (clopidogrel + aspirin) for 12 mo; thereafter at least aspirin lifelong.<br /></br>2. Statin therapy high-intensity (atorvastatin 40–80 mg).<br /></br>3. β-blocker, ACE inhibitor/ARB, ± eplerenone if LV EF ≤40%.<br /></br>4. Lifestyle: smoking cessation, weight control, exercise, BP, lipids, HbA1c.<br /></br>No scheduled imaging is part of surveillance.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Cardiac CT angiography<br /></br>   • Non-invasive way to visualize coronary anatomy; used for atypical chest pain with low–intermediate pre-test probability. Not indicated in a symptom-free post-PCI patient; radiation/contrast exposure without benefit.  </p></br><p>b. Echocardiography<br /></br>   • Appropriate if heart-failure symptoms, new murmur, or EF documentation needed (e.g., ≤3–6 mo post-MI in patients not previously imaged). This patient is asymptomatic, had revascularization 1 yr ago, and presumably had an echo then; no interval change expected.  </p></br><p>c. Left-heart catheterization<br /></br>   • Invasive angiography is indicated for refractory angina, high-risk noninvasive test, or acute coronary syndrome. Routine “surveillance cath” after stent has no mortality benefit and carries bleeding/contrast-induced nephropathy risks.  </p></br><p>d. Radionuclide myocardial perfusion imaging (stress test)<br /></br>   • Indicated for risk stratification in symptomatic patients or before high-risk non-cardiac surgery in patients with poor functional capacity. Routine annual stress testing in asymptomatic post-MI patients does not improve outcomes and may generate false-positives leading to unnecessary angiography.  </p></br><p>Common pitfall: Ordering “just to be safe” imaging in well-controlled, asymptomatic cardiovascular patients. Exams test your knowledge that more testing ≠ better care; adhere to evidence-based guidelines.</p>
109. A 57-year-old woman with unresectable pancreatic cancer is admitted to the hospital because of a 3-month history of constant severe abdominal pain that radiates to her back. The pain has not decreased despite an 8- week course of palliative chemotherapy and radiation therapy and treatment with transdermal fentanyl and oral oxycodone every 6 hours for breakthrough pain. She rates the pain as an 8 on a 10-point scale. She says that the pain limits her daily activities and forces her to remain bedridden for 10 to 12 hours daily. She is able to eat but has occasional nausea. Her temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 98/min, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 112/65 mm Hg. Examination shows mild scleral icterus. A 6-cm tender mass is palpated in the epigastrium. A CT scan of the abdomen is shown. Which of the following is the most appropriate next</br>step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Celiac plexus blockade</br>b. Dorsal rhizotomy</br>c. lntercostal nerve blockade</br>d. lntrathecal neurolysis</br>e. Sympathectomy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>A. Celiac plexus blockade  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key words: “unresectable pancreatic cancer,” “constant severe abdominal pain that radiates to her back,” “unresponsive to high-dose opioids (transdermal fentanyl + scheduled oxycodone),” “limits daily activities.”<br /></br>• Pattern: Upper abdominal visceral pain from pancreatic malignancy classically radiates straight through to the back and is mediated by afferent fibers that travel with the sympathetic chain to the celiac plexus.<br /></br>• Why now? When opioid-refractory visceral pain occurs in pancreatic (or other upper abdominal) cancer, guidelines recommend moving to a celiac plexus block/neurolysis as the next step before considering more destructive or extensive procedures.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Confirm pain is visceral, not somatic or neuropathic → location (mid-epigastric), radiation (back), deep/constant.<br /></br>2. Maximize systemic pharmacologic therapy → she already takes around-the-clock fentanyl plus breakthrough oxycodone. (Adjuvant agents such as gabapentin/duloxetine help neuropathic pain, not visceral, and are less effective here.)<br /></br>3. Next escalation in WHO cancer-pain ladder for opioid-refractory visceral pain from pancreas, liver, stomach → image-guided celiac plexus block or neurolytic injection.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Celiac plexus neurolysis is the evidence-based, organ-specific intervention that can markedly reduce pancreatic-cancer visceral pain and opioid requirements.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Pancreatic head/body tumors irritate the rich sympathetic/parasympathetic plexus surrounding the celiac trunk.<br /></br>• Pain signals ascend via greater &amp; lesser splanchnic nerves to the celiac plexus at T12–L2 before entering the spinal cord.<br /></br>• Ethanol or phenol injected under CT/US guidance destroys these post-ganglionic sympathetic fibers, interrupting pain transmission while sparing motor function.<br /></br>• Response rate ≈70–90 %; duration months; significant opioid-sparing.</p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>Procedure: Percutaneous or endoscopic-ultrasound-guided celiac plexus block → diagnostic local anesthetic + steroid; if relief, proceed to neurolytic phenol/ethanol injection.<br /></br>Adverse effects: transient hypotension, diarrhea (loss of sympathetic tone).<br /></br>Other options only considered if block fails → intrathecal pump, neurolysis, rhizotomy.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Dorsal rhizotomy<br /></br>• Surgical severing of dorsal sensory roots at the spinal cord. Used mainly for intractable focal neuropathic pain or spasticity in lower extremities. Highly invasive, high risk of ataxia/weakness; not first-line for visceral pancreatic pain.</p></br><p>C. Intercostal nerve blockade<br /></br>• Targets somatic nerves of thoracic wall; used for rib fracture, post-thoracotomy, or herpes zoster pain along a specific dermatome. Will not address deep visceral afferents from the pancreas.</p></br><p>D. Intrathecal neurolysis<br /></br>• Phenol/alcohol destruction of spinal nerve roots within the thecal sac, producing bilateral sensory loss below the level. Reserved for end-stage pelvic or perineal cancer pain when more localized techniques have failed. Associated with paralysis, bladder/bowel dysfunction; over-treatment here.</p></br><p>E. Sympathectomy<br /></br>• Surgical or chemical interruption of sympathetic chain—classically for ischemic limb pain, Raynaud phenomenon, hyperhidrosis. Does not specifically target celiac plexus and would not reliably relieve pancreatic visceral pain.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Confusing celiac plexus block (organ-specific, minimal motor loss) with more destructive central procedures (intrathecal neurolysis, rhizotomy) that carry greater morbidity and are used only after simpler regional blocks fail.</p></br><p>Take-Home Point: In patients with opioid-refractory visceral pain from pancreatic or other upper-abdominal cancers, a celiac plexus block/neurolysis is the preferred next therapeutic step, providing substantial pain relief and reducing opioid requirements with relatively low risk.</p>
110. A 67-year-old man comes to the physician for a routine examination. He has chronic lymphocytic leukemia and currently takes no medications. He fees well. His temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 84/min, respirations</br>are 16/min, and blood pressure is 136/72 mm Hg. Examination shows several small, firm, nontender lymph nodes in the neck and axilla. Abdominal examination shows mild splenomegaly. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hemoglobin 13 g/dL</br>Leukocyte count 84,000/mm 3</br>Segmented neutrophils 30 percent</br>Eosinophils 3 percent</br>Basophils 2 percent</br>Lymphocytes 60 percent</br>Monocytes 5 percent</br>Platelet count 149,000/mm 3</br>This patient is at greatest risk for which of the following?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Blast crisis</br>b. Bleeding</br>c. Gout</br>d. Hyperviscosity syndrome</br>e. Infection</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Infection  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Chronic lymphocytic leukemia (CLL) is explicitly stated.<br /></br>• Age &gt;60 yr, painless peripheral lymphadenopathy, splenomegaly, and a very high leukocyte count composed predominantly of mature lymphocytes (≈60%) are classic for CLL.<br /></br>• No constitutional (“B”) symptoms or treatment-related adverse effects are present, so the question is asking about natural-history complications of CLL itself.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Recall the two most common, test-favorite complications of CLL<br /></br>  – Severe infections (due to hypogammaglobulinemia)<br /></br>  – Autoimmune hemolytic anemia and ITP<br /></br>Step 2: Match the answer choices to these complications. Only “infection” appears.<br /></br>Step 3: Exclude distractors by linking them to other leukemias/disorders (see Incorrect Answer Analysis below).  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: CLL → malignant but mature B-cells → faulty immunoglobulin production (hypogammaglobulinemia) → recurrent/severe bacterial and viral infections are the single most frequent and clinically important complication.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Etiology: Clonal proliferation of mature CD5+, CD19+, CD20+ B-cells accumulating in blood, marrow, and lymphoid tissues.<br /></br>Mechanism of infection risk:<br /></br>• Malignant clone crowds out normal B-cell precursors AND cannot mount normal class-switching → low IgG/IgM.<br /></br>• Poor opsonization → encapsulated organisms (Strep pneumo, H. influenzae) and viral infections (e.g., VZV) are common.<br /></br>Clinical manifestations: Painless LAD, splenomegaly, marked lymphocytosis, smudge cells, ± autoimmune cytopenias.  </p></br><p>Treatments / Diagnostics (high-yield)<br /></br>• Asymptomatic early disease: watchful waiting.<br /></br>• Recurrent infections: periodic IVIG replacement.<br /></br>• Symptomatic/high-stage: chemo-immunotherapy (fludarabine, bendamustine, rituximab) or BTK inhibitor (ibrutinib).<br /></br>• Transformation: Richter (→ diffuse large B-cell lymphoma).  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Blast crisis – Refers to chronic myelogenous leukemia (CML) converting to acute leukemia (myeloid &gt; lymphoid blasts). CLL involves mature lymphocytes and does not transform into an acute blast phase; instead, it can undergo Richter transformation.<br /></br>b. Bleeding – Would require marked thrombocytopenia (&lt;50 k/µL) or platelet dysfunction. This patient’s platelets are 149 k/µL (low-normal); bleeding is far less common than infection in CLL.<br /></br>c. Gout – Hyperuricemia from rapid cell turnover is typical in myeloproliferative neoplasms (PV, ET, CML) or after cytotoxic therapy (tumor lysis). CLL generally has indolent turnover; gout is not a hallmark complication.<br /></br>d. Hyperviscosity syndrome – Seen in Waldenström macroglobulinemia (IgM), multiple myeloma with very high paraprotein, or extreme leukocytosis with blasts. Mature lymphocytes in CLL rarely reach the leukostasis threshold, and serum IgM is not elevated.<br /></br>e. Infection – Fits the classic and most common complication driven by hypogammaglobulinemia; therefore correct.  </p></br><p>Key Exam Pearls<br /></br>1. CLL = elderly + mature B-cell lymphocytosis + smudge cells.<br /></br>2. Greatest morbidity/mortality: infections due to hypogammaglobulinemia.<br /></br>3. IVIG prophylaxis is appropriate in patients with recurrent infections.<br /></br>4. Richter transformation (→ DLBCL) presents with rapidly enlarging lymph node, B-symptoms, ↑LDH.  </p></br><p>Remember: “CLL patients succumb to infection, not blast crisis.”</p>
111. A 52-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 2-week history of mild abdominal cramps and diarrhea containing blood and mucus. During this time, he also has had fecal urgency and mild pain with bowel movements. He is otherwise asymptomatic. He has hypothyroidism treated with levothyroxine. Wital signs are within normal limits. The abdomen is nondistended and nontender; there are no masses, and bowel sounds are normal. Test of the stool for occult blood is positive. Results of stool culture and testing of the stool for C/ostridium difficile, ova, and parasites are negative. Colonoscopy shows 30 cm of continuously friable, erythematous rectal and sigmoid mucosa; no polyps or masses are noted. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Erythromycin therapy</br>b. Left hemicolectomy</br>c. Loperamide therapy</br>d. Mesalamine therapy</br>e. Observation only</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Mesalamine therapy  </p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Bloody, mucous-containing diarrhea + fecal urgency/tenesmus → inflammatory (not secretory/osmotic) diarrhea<br /></br>• Colonoscopy: “30 cm of continuously friable, erythematous rectal and sigmoid mucosa; no polyps or masses”<br /></br>  – Continuous (no skip areas)<br /></br>  – Always involves rectum → classic for ulcerative colitis (UC)<br /></br>• Limited extent (proctosigmoiditis only) and only mild systemic symptoms → mild-to-moderate distal UC  </p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Stepwise therapy for an ACTIVE flare of mild-to-moderate distal UC:<br /></br>1. 5-aminosalicylate (5-ASA) preparation, preferably topical (mesalamine suppository or enema) ± oral mesalamine.<br /></br>2. If inadequate → topical steroids (hydrocortisone enema) or oral budesonide → systemic steroids → immunomodulators/biologics.  </p></br><p>Because this is his initial presentation and disease is mild and distal, first-line treatment is mesalamine.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Distal, mild UC is treated initially with topical (± oral) 5-ASA; escalation or surgery is reserved for refractory or extensive disease.  </p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Ulcerative colitis<br /></br>• Autoimmune, limited to colonic mucosa/submucosa<br /></br>• Always starts in rectum, spreads proximally in a continuous fashion<br /></br>• Presents with bloody diarrhea, urgency, tenesmus<br /></br>• Extraintestinal: PSC, scleritis, arthritis, erythema nodosum, etc.<br /></br>• Complications: toxic megacolon, colorectal carcinoma (risk correlates with duration/extent)  </p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Treatments &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>First-line (mild–moderate):<br /></br>• Mesalamine (5-ASA) suppositories/enemas, or oral formulations if disease extends beyond splenic flexure.<br /></br>Escalation:<br /></br>• Topical glucocorticoids → oral budesonide → systemic prednisone<br /></br>• Immunomodulators: azathioprine, 6-MP, methotrexate<br /></br>• Biologics: infliximab, adalimumab, vedolizumab, ustekinumab<br /></br>• Colectomy: refractory disease, dysplasia/cancer, fulminant colitis, uncontrolled bleeding, toxic megacolon.  </p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Erythromycin therapy<br /></br>   • Macrolide used for Campylobacter, pertussis, gastroparesis (prokinetic). Stool cultures were negative; endoscopy shows chronic inflammatory changes, not infection.  </p></br><p>b. Left hemicolectomy<br /></br>   • Surgical resection indicated only for refractory disease, dysplasia/malignancy, or fulminant complications (e.g., toxic megacolon). Initial mild flare does not warrant colectomy.  </p></br><p>c. Loperamide therapy<br /></br>   • Antimotility agents are relatively contraindicated in active inflammatory bowel disease due to risk of precipitating toxic megacolon. Treat inflammation, not just symptoms.  </p></br><p>e. Observation only<br /></br>   • Active UC requires anti-inflammatory therapy to induce remission and prevent complications; watchful waiting would allow ongoing mucosal damage.  </p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Continuous rectal-sigmoid inflammation with bloody diarrhea = mild distal ulcerative colitis. Initial management is topical (± oral) 5-aminosalicylate—mesalamine.</p>
112. A 32-year-old woman comes to the i:2 hysician for a routine health maintenance examination. She had used a diaphragm for contraception for 10 years but switched to a combination oral contraceptive 6 months ago. She is 168 cm 5 ft 6 in tall and weighs 70 kg 154 lb; 8MI is 25 kg/m 2. Her blood pressure has averaged 144/92 mm Hg on three visits during the past 3 months. Her pulse is 84/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure now is 145/90 mm Hg. Examination shows no other abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. 24-Hour urine collection for measurement of catecholamine concentration</br>b. Renal Doppler ultrasonography</br>c. Low-dose hydrochlorothiazide therapy and reexamination in 1 month</br>d. Switching to a progesterone-only oral contraceptive and reexamination in 3 months</br>e. Intravenous pyelography</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>D. Switch to a progesterone-only oral contraceptive and re-examine blood pressure in 3 months  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• New risk factor: The patient was normotensive for years while using a diaphragm but developed persistent blood-pressure readings ~145/90 mm Hg after she began a combined estrogen-progestin oral contraceptive (COC) 6 months ago.<br /></br>• Mild, persistent hypertension: Three separate elevated readings over ≥3 months satisfy the diagnosis of chronic (primary) hypertension, but the timing strongly points to a secondary, reversible cause.<br /></br>• No end-organ signs: Normal exam makes secondary etiologies such as renal artery stenosis or pheochromocytoma less likely.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Identify temporal association → new medication (estrogen-containing pill) started just before BP rose.<br /></br>Step 2: Recall mechanism → estrogen increases hepatic production of angiotensinogen → ↑ renin-angiotensin activity → hypertension in ~5% of users; risk rises with age, smoking, obesity.<br /></br>Step 3: Management priority → remove the offending agent, then re-assess. If BP normalizes, no further work-up or lifelong therapy needed.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Always look for reversible/iatrogenic causes of newly discovered hypertension—especially estrogen-containing contraceptives in young women—before initiating lifelong pharmacotherapy or extensive investigations.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Estrogen → ↑ hepatic angiotensinogen synthesis → ↑ angiotensin I/II → vasoconstriction, aldosterone-mediated sodium/water retention → elevated BP.<br /></br>Progestin alone does not up-regulate angiotensinogen and is usually normotensive; some formulations (e.g., norethindrone, levonorgestrel IUD, depot medroxyprogesterone) are good alternatives.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>1. Remove estrogen component (switch to progesterone-only pill, IUD, implant, or barrier).<br /></br>2. Re-check BP in 2-3 months. If remains ≥130/80 (per guidelines), begin standard antihypertensive evaluation/therapy.<br /></br>No labs or imaging are necessary unless hypertension persists after estrogen withdrawal.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>A. 24-hour urine catecholamines (pheochromocytoma)<br /></br>• Would present with episodic headaches, palpitations, diaphoresis, ± paroxysmal BP spikes—not isolated mild persistent hypertension temporally linked to COC use.<br /></br>• Prevalence far lower than COC-induced HTN; costly and unnecessary first test.  </p></br><p>B. Renal Doppler ultrasonography (renal artery stenosis)<br /></br>• Consider in resistant HTN, recurrent flash pulmonary edema, abdominal bruit, or onset &lt;30 y without risk factors. Temporal relationship to estrogen makes RAS unlikely.  </p></br><p>C. Start low-dose hydrochlorothiazide now<br /></br>• Treats essential HTN, but current best practice is to stop the offending drug first. Starting a long-term medication without addressing a reversible cause exposes her to needless side effects and costs.  </p></br><p>E. Intravenous pyelography<br /></br>• Obsolete for renovascular or parenchymal renal disease; no indication here.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Jumping straight to antihypertensive therapy or expensive testing without reviewing medication history and discontinuing potential culprits.  </p></br><p>Bottom Line: In a young woman who develops hypertension soon after starting a combined estrogen-progestin contraceptive, first stop estrogen (switch to progesterone-only contraception) and reassess blood pressure before pursuing further work-up or pharmacologic treatment.</p>
113. A 16-year-old boy is brought to the physician by his parents because of a 6-month history of excessive daytime sleepiness. He is tired after sleeping more than 10 hours at night. He frequently falls asleep during class, and, last week, he was involved in a motor vehicle collision after falling asleep while driving home from a party. He snores at night. His academic performance was good but has deteriorated to almost failing. Examination shows no abnormalities except for mildly enlarged tonsils. Polysomnography shows normal airflow and gas exchange, normal sleep stages with short REM latency, and poor sleep continuity with multiple brief arousals and four sustained awakenings. On the following day, multiple sleep latency testing shows REM episodes at the onset of sleep during three of four naps with a mean sleep latency of 1 minute. Which of the following is the most likely explanation for these findings?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Central sleep apnea</br>b. Kleine-Levin syndrome</br>c. Narcolepsy</br>d. Obstructive sleep apnea</br>e. Primary hypersomnia</br>F. Normal adolescent sleeping behavior</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Narcolepsy  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Excessive daytime sleepiness … tired after sleeping more than 10 h” – pathologic hypersomnolence.<br /></br>• “Falls asleep during class” and “motor-vehicle collision after falling asleep” – classic functional impairment.<br /></br>• Polysomnography:<br /></br>  – “Normal airflow and gas exchange” rules out obstructive or central sleep-apnea events.<br /></br>  – “Short REM latency” (entering REM &lt;70 min after sleep onset) raises suspicion for narcolepsy.<br /></br>• Multiple-sleep-latency test (MSLT):<br /></br>  – “REM episodes at the onset of sleep during three of four naps” (≥2 SOREMPs) plus<br /></br>  – “Mean sleep latency 1 min” (≤8 min is abnormal) – this MSLT pattern is essentially diagnostic for narcolepsy.<br /></br>Key pattern: pathologic sleepiness + sleep-onset REM episodes (SOREMPs) on MSLT → narcolepsy.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Rule out primary sleep-disordered breathing (no apneas/hypopneas recorded, normal O2 &amp; airflow).<br /></br>Step 2: Evaluate hypersomnolence disorders with MSLT. Only narcolepsy consistently shows ≥2 SOREMPs and very short sleep latency.<br /></br>Take-home: SOREMPs on MSLT = narcolepsy unless the patient is sleep deprived or on REM-suppressing meds (neither present here).</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Narcolepsy type 1 = loss of hypothalamic orexin (hypocretin)-producing neurons (autoimmune mechanism; HLA-DQB1*06:02 association). Loss of orexin destabilizes the sleep-wake switch, allowing REM physiology to intrude into wakefulness (sleep attacks, cataplexy, hypnagogic/hypnopompic hallucinations, sleep paralysis) and causing reduced REM latency. Type 2 is similar but without cataplexy and with normal CSF orexin.</p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>Diagnosis: Overnight polysomnography followed by next-day MSLT (≥2 SOREMPs + mean sleep latency ≤8 min) OR low CSF orexin (&lt;110 pg/mL).<br /></br>First-line daytime therapy: stimulants (modafinil first, methylphenidate, amphetamines).<br /></br>Cataplexy: REM-suppressing agents (venlafaxine, fluoxetine) or sodium oxybate (gamma-hydroxybutyrate). Good sleep hygiene and scheduled naps are essential.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Central sleep apnea – Characterized by cessation of respiratory effort with periodic O₂ desaturations/CHF or opiate history; polysomnography would show central apneas and abnormal airflow, which are absent here.<br /></br>b. Kleine-Levin syndrome – Recurrent hypersomnolence episodes (days-weeks) with hyperphagia, hypersexuality, cognitive changes between episodes; MSLT does NOT show SOREMPs. Our patient’s symptoms are chronic, not episodic, and REM findings fit narcolepsy.<br /></br>d. Obstructive sleep apnea – Loud snoring, enlarged tonsils support possibility, BUT polysomnography would reveal apneas/hypopneas, airflow limitation, desaturations; here airflow and gas exchange are normal.<br /></br>e. Primary hypersomnia (idiopathic) – Causes chronic daytime sleepiness but MSLT shows short latency WITHOUT SOREMPs (≤1 SOREMP). Presence of ≥2 SOREMPs rules it out.<br /></br>f. Normal adolescent sleep behavior – Teens often need more sleep, but falling asleep in hazardous situations and abnormal MSLT values are pathologic, not normal variation.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: On NBME/Step 2, ≥2 sleep-onset REM periods plus mean sleep latency ≤8 minutes on MSLT clinches narcolepsy.</p>
114. A 23-year-old primigravid woman at 18 weeks' gestation comes to the physician because of a 3-week history of heat intolerance and an inability to sit quietly for more than a few minutes. She also has had five to six bowel</br>movements daily and a 3.6-kg 8-lb weight loss despite an increased appetite during this period. She is 168 cm 5 ft 6 in tall and now weighs 55 kg 122 lb. Her temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 133/min,</br>respirations are 19/min, and blood pressure is 160/50 mm Hg. Examination shows diffuse thyroid gland enlargement and a fine tremor of the outstretched hands. The uterus is 18 cm from the pubic symphysis. Fetal heart</br>tones are audible at 160/min. Her free thyroxine index is 14 N=5-13. Which of the following is the most appropriate treatment for this patient?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Increased caloric intake</br>b. 131 I therapy</br>c. Nifedipine therapy</br>d. Propylthiouracil therapy</br>e. Pregnancy termination</br>Correct Answer: D</br></br></br><p>Correct answer: Propylthiouracil (PTU) therapy  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 18-week pregnant (2nd-trimester) primigravida with classic thyrotoxicosis picture: heat intolerance, tremor, weight loss despite ↑ appetite, tachycardia 133/min, widened pulse pressure 160/50, frequent stools, diffuse goiter.<br /></br>• Laboratory: elevated free thyroxine index (FTI 14; N 5-13) → biochemical hyperthyroidism.<br /></br>• No eye or skin findings given, but Graves disease is the overwhelmingly most common cause of hyperthyroidism in pregnancy.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1 – Confirm condition: S/Sx + ↑FTI ⇒ overt hyperthyroidism.<br /></br>Step 2 – Recognize pregnancy alters drug choices.<br /></br>• Radioiodine absolutely contraindicated.<br /></br>• β-blockers can be used for symptom control but do not treat the disease.<br /></br>• Thioamides (PTU, methimazole) are the definitive medical therapy; pregnancy dictates which.<br /></br>Step 3 – Pick safest thioamide in early pregnancy:<br /></br>• PTU preferred during 1st trimester (organogenesis) and often continued until ≈16-20 wk before optional switch to methimazole. NBME/Step questions usually accept PTU any time before delivery if methimazole isn’t offered as a choice.  </p></br><p>Core “take-home”: Hyperthyroidism in pregnancy is treated with propylthiouracil early (≤20 wk) because methimazole is teratogenic, and radioactive iodine is forbidden.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Graves disease: IgG thyroid-stimulating antibodies → ↑T3/T4, suppressed TSH.<br /></br>• Maternal risks if untreated: miscarriage, pre-eclampsia, HF, thyroid storm.<br /></br>• Fetal risks: growth restriction, prematurity, fetal/neonatal thyrotoxicosis (TSI crosses placenta).<br /></br>• PTU: inhibits thyroid peroxidase (organification &amp; coupling) AND peripheral 5’-deiodinase (T4→T3). Crosses placenta but less than methimazole and lacks major teratogenic effects; main concern = rare severe hepatotoxicity.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>1. First-line (≤20 wk): Propylthiouracil 50–150 mg PO TID; titrate to keep FT4 at high-normal.<br /></br>2. After 20 wk: consider switch to methimazole 5–20 mg daily to lower maternal hepatotoxicity risk.<br /></br>3. Symptom control: short-term β-blocker (propranolol) if needed; use lowest dose, monitor fetal growth.<br /></br>4. Radioiodine (¹³¹I) never in pregnancy or lactation (permanent fetal thyroid ablation → cretinism).<br /></br>5. Surgery (2nd-trimester subtotal thyroidectomy) only if refractory to meds or intolerant.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Incorrect<br /></br>A. Increased caloric intake – Addresses weight loss symptom rather than the underlying thyrotoxicosis; would not resolve tachycardia, tremor, or pregnancy complications.<br /></br>B. ¹³¹I therapy – Contraindicated in any trimester; crosses placenta, destroys fetal thyroid, causes congenital hypothyroidism.<br /></br>C. Nifedipine therapy – Calcium-channel blocker used for hypertension and tocolysis; does not treat hyperthyroidism, tachycardia, or metabolic symptoms.<br /></br>E. Pregnancy termination – Hyperthyroidism is readily treatable; termination is unnecessary and inappropriate.  </p></br><p>Common pitfall: choosing methimazole when given as an option before 20 wk—remember teratogenic associations (aplasia cutis, choanal/esophageal atresia).  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Pregnant women with overt hyperthyroidism should receive propylthiouracil during the first half of pregnancy to control maternal disease and protect the fetus.</p>
115. A 40-year-old woman comes to the office to establish care. She has felt well and reports no symptoms. Medical history is unremarkable and she takes no medications. Family history is remarkable for kidney stones in he</br>mother. The patient's vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows no abnormalities. Initial serum laboratory studies show a calcium concentration of 11 mgl dl. Further testing shows a serum intact parathyroid</br>hormone concentration of 75 pg/ml N=10-60. Results of 24-hour urine collection show a calcium concentration of 50 mg. Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient's condition?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Familial hypocalciuric hypercalcemia</br>b. Primary hyperparathyroidism</br>c. Sarcoidosis</br>d. Secondary hyperparathyroidism</br>e. Vitamin D toxicity</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>A. Familial hypocalciuric hypercalcemia (FHH)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Calcium 11 mg/dL” – mild, persistent hypercalcemia.<br /></br>• “Serum intact PTH 75 pg/mL (N = 10-60)” – inappropriately high-normal / mildly elevated PTH in the face of hypercalcemia → PTH-dependent hypercalcemia.<br /></br>• “24-hour urine calcium 50 mg” – LOW (normal ≈ 100-300 mg/day). This single datum is the key discriminator.<br /></br>The triad of mild hypercalcemia + non-suppressed PTH + hypocalciuria is classic for FHH.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-by-step approach to PTH-dependent hypercalcemia:<br /></br>1. Confirm hypercalcemia.<br /></br>2. Check PTH – if not suppressed, think primary hyperparathyroidism vs FHH.<br /></br>3. Measure 24-hr urine Ca/Cr clearance ratio.<br /></br>   • &lt;0.01 → FHH (low urinary calcium).<br /></br>   • &gt;0.02 → primary hyperparathyroidism (high urinary calcium).<br /></br>This patient’s total 24-hr Ca is only 50 mg (very low) → ratio will be &lt;0.01 → FHH.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In patients with mild, lifelong hypercalcemia and non-suppressed PTH, always look at urinary calcium excretion; hypocalciuria = FHH.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>FHH is an autosomal dominant loss-of-function mutation in the calcium-sensing receptor (CaSR) on:<br /></br>• Parathyroid chief cells → falsely “perceive” serum calcium as low → do not suppress PTH.<br /></br>• Renal thick ascending limb &amp; distal tubule → decreased Ca excretion.<br /></br>Net: Mild, asymptomatic hypercalcemia, normal‒high PTH, and hypocalciuria from birth; benign course, no kidney stones.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>• Diagnostic: 24-hr urine Ca or Ca/Cr clearance ratio. Genetic testing if uncertain.<br /></br>• Treatment: None needed; parathyroidectomy is NOT indicated (won’t correct calcium).<br /></br>Patient education and reassurance.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Primary hyperparathyroidism<br /></br>   – Usually shows elevated PTH with HYPERcalciuria (stones, osteoporosis). 24-hr Ca &gt;250 mg or Ca/Cr ratio &gt;0.02.  </p></br><p>C. Sarcoidosis<br /></br>   – Non-caseating granulomas ↑1-α-hydroxylase → ↑1,25-(OH)₂-Vit D → hypercalcemia with SUPPRESSED PTH and increased urinary Ca. Respiratory or constitutional symptoms common.  </p></br><p>D. Secondary hyperparathyroidism<br /></br>   – Results from chronic hypocalcemia (e.g., CKD, vitamin D deficiency). Labs: low/normal Ca, high PTH, often low 1,25-(OH)₂-Vit D. Not hypercalcemic.  </p></br><p>E. Vitamin D toxicity<br /></br>   – Excessive 1,25-(OH)₂-Vit D → ↑GI Ca absorption → hypercalcemia with SUPPRESSED PTH and increased urinary Ca. History of supplement use; often symptomatic (polyuria, nausea).  </p></br><p>Key difference for all wrong choices: they would not produce low urinary calcium with an inappropriately normal/high PTH.</p>
116. A 27-year-old man with asthma comes to the physician as a new patient. He has a 10-year history of chronic cough productive of sputum and a 3-year history of intermittent diarrhea. He has nasal allergies. During the past</br>10 years, he has had numerous respiratory tract infections treated with antibiotics. He had three episodes of pneumonia during childhood. His medications are cetirizine, montelukast, and nasal fluticasone; he uses an</br>albuterol inhaler four times daily. He does not smoke cigarettes. He is 170 cm 5 ft 7 in tall and weighs 52 kg 115 lb; 8MI is 18 kg/m 2. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows erythematous, boggy nasal</br>mucosa. A few scattered rhonchi are heard in the lower lung fields bilaterally. Abdominal examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Measurement of serum immunoglobulin concentrations~-</br>b. Pulmonary function testing</br>c. CT scan of the paranasal sinuses</br>d. Inhaled corticosteroid therapy</br>e. Allergen injection immunotherapy</br>Correct Answer: A</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>A. Measurement of serum immunoglobulin concentrations  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “10-year history of chronic cough productive of sputum” + “numerous respiratory tract infections” + “three episodes of pneumonia during childhood” → suggests recurrent pyogenic sinopulmonary infections.<br /></br>• “Intermittent diarrhea” → think of Giardia or fat-malabsorption, both classic in antibody deficiencies.<br /></br>• “Erythematous, boggy nasal mucosa” + “nasal allergies” + asthma → strong atopic background, which is commonly seen in IgA deficiency/CVID.<br /></br>• Low BMI (18 kg/m², weight 52 kg) → chronic illness/malabsorption.<br /></br>These findings cluster around a humoral (B-cell/antibody) immunodeficiency such as selective IgA deficiency or common variable immunodeficiency (CVID). First diagnostic step = quantitative serum immunoglobulin levels (IgG, IgA, IgM ± subclasses).</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Recurrent sinopulmonary bacterial infections in an adult → consider bronchiectasis, cystic fibrosis, primary ciliary dyskinesia, or antibody deficiency.<br /></br>Step 2: Add chronic non-bloody diarrhea (often giardiasis) → classic for antibody deficiency (IgA protects mucosal surfaces; Giardia infections common).<br /></br>Step 3: Adult onset, normal fertility implied, no neonatal meconium ileus → cystic fibrosis less likely; absence of chronic otitis/media, situs inversus → primary ciliary dyskinesia less likely.<br /></br>Therefore: evaluate humoral immunity → quantitative immunoglobulins.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Any patient (child or adult) with recurrent sinopulmonary infections ± chronic Giardia diarrhea warrants serum Ig measurement to screen for antibody deficiency.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• IgA deficiency (most common primary immunodeficiency): low/absent serum and secretory IgA → poor mucosal defense, recurrent sinus/lung infections, chronic diarrhea (Giardia), allergies &amp; autoimmune disease common.<br /></br>• CVID: usually presents later (teens–adults) with low IgG plus low IgA and/or IgM; similar clinical picture but with broader immune dysregulation (autoimmunity, lymphoma risk).<br /></br>• Both conditions are diagnosed initially with quantitative serum immunoglobulins; further work-up (B-cell phenotyping, vaccine response testing) follows if abnormal.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Diagnostic:<br /></br>1. Quantitative IgG, IgA, IgM ± IgG subclasses.<br /></br>2. If low → check specific antibody titers (e.g., pre/post-pneumococcal vaccination), flow cytometry for B-cell numbers.<br /></br>Treatment (once confirmed):<br /></br>• Aggressive infection control + prophylactic antibiotics PRN.<br /></br>• Monthly IVIG or SCIG for CVID (not helpful in isolated IgA deficiency).<br /></br>• Vaccinate with non-live vaccines, manage associated atopy and GI infections (tinidazole/metronidazole for Giardia).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Pulmonary function testing<br /></br>– Would help stage asthma but does not explain recurrent bacterial infections and chronic diarrhea. Even if asthma is suboptimally controlled, it does not cause repeated pneumonias/diarrhea.</p></br><p>C. CT scan of the paranasal sinuses<br /></br>– Could evaluate chronic sinus disease but is secondary; imaging will not uncover the systemic immune problem causing both lung and GI symptoms.</p></br><p>D. Inhaled corticosteroid therapy<br /></br>– Already uses nasal fluticasone; starting an inhaled corticosteroid for asthma might slightly improve symptoms but again fails to address the root cause of infections/diarrhea.</p></br><p>E. Allergen injection immunotherapy<br /></br>– Allergen immunotherapy is for refractory allergic rhinitis after maximal medical therapy and in well-controlled asthmatics. It offers no benefit for chronic bacterial pneumonias or diarrheal illness due to immune deficiency.</p></br><p>Key differentiator: Only option A investigates a unifying diagnosis (humoral immunodeficiency) that explains every element in the vignette.</p>
117. A study is conducted to assess the accuracy of a new rapid test to detect streptococcal pharyngitis. A total of 200 patients with sore throat undergo rapid testing; these results are compared with the results of subsequent throat cultures for group A beta-hemolytic streptococcus. The results show:</br>                culture      </br>                45   10   55</br>rapid test      5   140   145</br>total           50  150 200</br>Which of the following best represents the prevalence of streptococcal pharyngitis within this population?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. 10/150 = 6.7 percent</br>b. 45/150 = 30 percent</br>c. 45/200 = 22.5 percent</br>d. 50/200 = 25 percent</br>e. 55/200 = 27.5 percent</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. 50/200 = 25 %</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Culture” is the gold-standard test (disease present = culture positive).<br /></br>• The 2×2 table reconstructed from the numbers provided:<br /></br>        Culture + Culture – Total<br /></br>Rapid +   45    10   55<br /></br>Rapid –    5    140  145<br /></br>Total     50    150  200  </p></br><p>• Prevalence = proportion of the total sample that truly has the disease, i.e., all culture-positive patients (45 + 5 = 50) out of the total population tested (200).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Stepwise thought process:<br /></br>1. Identify which cell(s) represent people who truly have the condition → all culture-positive cells, regardless of rapid-test result (true positives + false negatives).<br /></br>2. Divide by the grand total studied to obtain prevalence.<br /></br>   50 ÷ 200 = 0.25 → 25 %.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Prevalence depends only on the reference-standard diagnosis in the entire population, not on the index test’s performance.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (concise)<br /></br>Group A β-hemolytic Streptococcus (Strep pyogenes) causes suppurative pharyngitis. Gold-standard diagnosis remains throat culture, although rapid antigen detection tests are commonly used for quicker results.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatments / Diagnostics Review (brief)<br /></br>• Gold-standard: throat culture (24–48 h).<br /></br>• Rapid antigen detection test (RADT): point-of-care; sensitivity ~85–95 %, specificity ~90–95 %.<br /></br>• Treatment when confirmed or highly suspected: penicillin V or amoxicillin; alternatives for allergy include cephalexin (if non-IgE) or azithromycin/clindamycin.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. 10/150 = 6.7 %<br /></br> Represents the FALSE-POSITIVE rate (FP / all disease-negative). Does not measure prevalence.</p></br><p>b. 45/150 = 30 %<br /></br> Compares true positives to disease-negative patients—an uninterpretable fraction for standard test characteristics.</p></br><p>c. 45/200 = 22.5 %<br /></br> Uses only true positives (rapid + &amp; culture +) instead of all diseased patients (omits false negatives).</p></br><p>e. 55/200 = 27.5 %<br /></br> Uses all rapid-test positives (true + false) as the numerator—this is the proportion of the population that tested positive, not the proportion that truly has disease.</p></br><p>Common pitfall: confusing prevalence with predictive values or test-positive fractions. Always anchor prevalence to the gold-standard diagnosis across the whole sample.</p>
118. A 77-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 4-year history of a lesion over her forearm that has increased gradually in size. She works outdoors in her garden 3 days weekly and does not wear sunscreen,</br>and she is concerned the lesion could be cancer. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. A photograph of the lesion is shown. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Which of</br>the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Observation only</br>b. Topical 5 percent fluorouracil therapy</br>c. Topical triamcinolone therapy</br>d. Punch biopsy of the lesion</br>e. Wide elliptical excision of the lesion</br></br></br><p>Correct answer</br>A. Observation only</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• 77-year-old woman → cumulative sun exposure–related skin changes are common<br /></br>• 4-year history, very slow growth → argues against aggressive malignancy<br /></br>• Forearm, avid gardener without sunscreen → chronically sun-exposed site<br /></br>• No symptoms, no ulceration, bleeding, or rapid change<br /></br>• Photograph (USMLE-style) typically shows a flat or slightly macular, oval, uniformly tan-to-brown lesion with sharp, scalloped edges—classic for a solar (actinic) lentigo (“liver/age spot”)</p></br><p>These features point toward a benign solar lentigo, which requires no therapy.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step 1: Is this premalignant or malignant?  Malignancy would show ABCDE warning signs (Asymmetry, irregular Border, Color variegation, Diameter &gt;6 mm, Evolution/rapid change). Nothing in the stem or photo suggests this.</br>Step 2: Is it an actinic keratosis (AK)? AKs are rough, gritty, erythematous papules or plaques with adherent scale—not a smooth, uniformly pigmented macule.</br>Conclusion: Benign sun-induced lentigo → reassurance and observation.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>Solar lentigo<br /></br>• Etiology: Chronic UV-B exposure → localized melanocyte hyperplasia and ↑ melanin within keratinocytes.<br /></br>• Epidemiology: Common after age 40 on face, dorsal hands, forearms, shoulders.<br /></br>• Clinical: Uniform tan-brown macules/patches, sharp borders, flat, may very slowly enlarge or darken.<br /></br>• Malignant potential: NONE.</p></br><p>Treatments/Diagnostics</br>• No treatment necessary (cosmetic removal possible with laser/cryotherapy if desired).<br /></br>• Educate on sun protection to prevent new lesions.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>B. Topical 5% fluorouracil – First-line field therapy for actinic keratoses. Those appear as erythematous, rough, sandpaper-like papules/plaques that can feel better than they look; this lesion is smooth, evenly pigmented.</p></br><p>C. Topical triamcinolone – Used for inflammatory dermatoses (eczema, lichen planus, psoriasis). Pigmented macules do not improve with steroids.</p></br><p>D. Punch biopsy – Indicated for lesions with suspicious ABCDE features or if diagnosis is uncertain. Uniform color and border, slow growth, and classic sun-lentigo photo make biopsy unnecessary.</p></br><p>E. Wide elliptical excision – Management for melanoma or high-risk non-melanoma skin cancer. No clinical signs of invasive tumor here.</p></br><p>Take-Home Message</br>A solitary, uniformly pigmented, flat macule on a chronically sun-exposed area of an elderly patient = solar lentigo → benign, observe and reassure; reserve treatment/biopsy for lesions with ABCDE changes suggestive of malignancy.</p>
119. A 25-year-old man comes to the physician 2 hours after the sudden onset of sharp right buttock pain that radiates to the back of his knee. The pain worsens when he sits for a prolonged period or drives. He is able to walk.</br>Use of over-the-counter ibuprofen has provided mild relief. He has no history of serious illness and takes no other medications. On examination, ipsilateral and contralateral straight-leg raise testing is positive. Muscle</br>strength is normal throughout. Sensation is intact throughout. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Application of cold compresses</br>b. Recommendation of bed rest for 1 week</br>c. Recommendation of normal activities as tolerated</br>d. Traction</br>e. Use of a lumbar brace</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Recommendation of normal activities as tolerated  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Sudden unilateral buttock pain radiating down the posterior thigh to the knee → classic radicular pattern (sciatica) most commonly from an L4-L5 or L5-S1 disk herniation.<br /></br>• Pain worsens with sitting and straight-leg raise (SLR) test positive bilaterally → supports nerve-root irritation.<br /></br>• Normal strength and intact sensation → NO focal neurologic deficit (no motor weakness, no sensory loss, no bowel/bladder dysfunction).<br /></br>• Duration &lt; 6 weeks (only 2 hours) and no “red-flag” features (fever, trauma, weight loss, cancer history, IV-drug use).  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>In an otherwise healthy adult with acute uncomplicated lumbosacral radiculopathy:<br /></br>1. First-line management is conservative.<br /></br>2. Evidence shows early return to usual activities shortens recovery time, reduces chronic disability, and lowers healthcare utilization.<br /></br>3. Extended bed rest, traction, braces, or urgent imaging/ surgery are reserved for neurologic deficits or red-flag signs.  </p></br><p>Take-home message: Acute mechanical low-back pain (with or without mild radiculopathy) → NSAIDs/acetaminophen + stay active as tolerated.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Herniated nucleus pulposus protrudes posterolaterally, compressing the exiting nerve root (most often L5 or S1).<br /></br>• Sitting increases intradiscal pressure; SLR stretches the sciatic nerve, reproducing pain.<br /></br>• Most cases (&gt; 90 %) improve spontaneously within 4–6 weeks due to resorption of herniated material.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Initial outpatient management:<br /></br>1. Activity modification: return to work/normal life; avoid heavy lifting but stay mobile.<br /></br>2. NSAIDs (e.g., ibuprofen, naproxen) ± short course of muscle relaxant.<br /></br>3. Heat/ice may be used for comfort but are adjuncts, not primary therapy.<br /></br>4. If symptoms persist &gt; 6 weeks or neurologic deficits develop → MRI lumbar spine ± epidural steroid injection or surgery (microdiscectomy).</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Application of cold compresses<br /></br>• Ice can reduce muscle spasm/pain but alone does not address guideline-recommended early mobilization; it is supportive, not the key next step.</p></br><p>b. Recommendation of bed rest for 1 week<br /></br>• Prolonged bed rest is associated with slower recovery, more chronic pain, and deconditioning. Modern guidelines advise against routine bed rest.</p></br><p>d. Traction<br /></br>• Lacks proven benefit for acute disk herniation in randomized trials; not recommended in contemporary care pathways.</p></br><p>e. Use of a lumbar brace<br /></br>• Bracing has no clear benefit for acute lumbosacral radiculopathy without instability; can lead to muscle atrophy and dependency.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Thinking that pain severity automatically mandates rest. On exams and in practice, absence of neurologic deficits = conservative therapy with continued activity is best.</p>
120. A 2-year-old boy is brought to the emergency department 30 minutes after he was involved in a motor vehicle collision. He was sitting unrestrained on his aunt's lap in the front passenger seat during the collision, and the automobile's air bags deployed on impact. Paramedics at the scene immobilized his spine with a cervical collar, and his head was taped to a backboard during transport to the hospital. He has no history of serious illness and receives no medications. On arrival, he is crying and moving all extremities. Temperature is 37.2 C 99.0 F, pulse is 120/min, respirations are 25/min, and blood pressure is 110/60 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 99 percent. Examination shows abrasions over the face and neck. No other abnormalities are noted. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. CT scan of the chest and abdomen</br>b. Intravenous administration of antibiotics</br>c. Intravenous administration of sedatives</br>d. MRI of the brain</br>e. X-rays of the cervical spine and chest</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Obtain plain radiographs of the cervical spine and chest  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Unrestrained 2-year-old in front passenger seat” + “air bags deployed” = high-energy deceleration mechanism with substantial risk for cervical spine and intrathoracic injury.<br /></br>• Child is alert, moving all extremities, hemodynamically stable → primary survey (ABCs) is complete and no immediate life-threats are evident.<br /></br>• Abrasions to face/neck suggest the head/neck took the brunt of the air-bag impact → focus on cervical spine.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>ATLS sequence:<br /></br>1. Primary survey &amp; resuscitation (done).<br /></br>2. Adjuncts to primary survey (pulse ox, CXR if needed, etc.).<br /></br>3. Secondary survey, which begins with clearing the cervical spine.<br /></br>   • Standard initial “trauma series” in a stable patient: lateral cervical spine film + anteroposterior (AP) chest (± pelvis) radiograph.<br /></br>   • Plain films are the first-line screen in a pediatric patient to minimize radiation; CT reserved if X-rays are abnormal or nondiagnostic.  </p></br><p>Take-home: After stabilization, the very next step in any blunt-trauma patient with an immobilized neck is to image the cervical spine (and chest) to look for occult fractures, dislocations, or pulmonary injuries.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Children &lt;5 yr have proportionally larger heads and more ligamentous laxity; flexion/extension forces from air-bag deployment place them at high risk for atlanto-occipital or upper cervical spine injuries even without neurological deficit.<br /></br>• Associated intrathoracic injuries (pulmonary contusion, pneumothorax) also warrant a screening chest film.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>First-line imaging (stable patient):<br /></br>1. Lateral, AP, and odontoid cervical spine X-rays<br /></br>2. AP chest X-ray<br /></br>If any of the following occur, proceed to CT cervical spine ± CT chest/abdomen:<br /></br>• Neurologic deficit, focal tenderness, abnormal plain films, or inadequate visualization.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. CT scan of the chest and abdomen<br /></br>   • High radiation; indicated only when clinical exam, FAST, or chest X-ray suggests internal injury. This child has no abdominal or thoracic signs, so CT would be premature.  </p></br><p>b. Intravenous antibiotics<br /></br>   • Used for open fractures, penetrating trauma, or suspected sepsis—not blunt MVC without signs of infection.  </p></br><p>c. Intravenous sedatives<br /></br>   • Sedation may be needed for agitated patients during imaging, but only after the imaging has been ordered and the airway is secured; not the “next step” in work-up.  </p></br><p>d. MRI of the brain<br /></br>   • Reserved for neurological symptoms or abnormal CT; not used acutely for primary screening in a stable trauma patient.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Core Concept<br /></br>In a hemodynamically stable pediatric trauma patient who was immobilized in the field, the immediate priority after ABCs is to obtain screening cervical-spine and chest radiographs to rule out occult fractures or thoracic injuries before removing immobilization or proceeding to more advanced imaging.</p>
121. A 52-year-old man comes to the physician 2 days after noticing that his eyes and skin appear yellow. Four days ago, he received the diagnosis of sinusitis and began treatment with trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole. During the past 6 months, he has traveled to Nicaragua and Belize. He has been sexually active with 12 partners during the past 2 years and uses condoms inconsistently. He drinks three to four beers weekly. He does not smoke cigarettes or use illicit drugs. His temperature is 36.6 C 97.8 F , pulse is 72/min, respirations are 14/min, and blood pressure is 136/84 mm Hg. Examination shows generalized jaundice and a diffuse, macula, nonblanching rash over the chest and back. There is scleral icterus. Laboratory studies show: </br>Hemoglobin</br>Serum</br>Bilirubin, total</br>Direct</br>Alkaline phosphatase</br>AST</br>ALT</br>HBsAg</br>Hepatitis B surface antibody</br>13.2 g/dL</br>5.5 mg/dL</br>2.2 mg/dL</br>58 U/L</br>372 U/L</br>250 U/L</br>negative</br>positive</br>Which of the following is the most appropriate initial step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Discontinuation of trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole therapy</br>b. Subcutaneous peginterferon alfa-2a therapy</br>c. Intravenous ceftriaxone therapy</br>d. Endoscopic retrograde cholangiopancreatography</br>e. Liver biopsy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>A. Discontinuation of trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole therapy  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Eyes and skin appear yellow … began treatment with trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole 4 days ago” – temporal relationship between a new medication and onset of jaundice.<br /></br>• “Diffuse, macular, non-blanching rash” – typical of a hypersensitivity reaction (often accompanies drug-induced liver injury).<br /></br>• Labs: Total bilirubin 5.5 mg/dL (mixed, mostly direct), marked AST (372 U/L) and ALT (250 U/L) elevations, normal/low alkaline phosphatase (58 U/L) → hepatocellular pattern (not cholestatic or obstructive).<br /></br>• HBsAg negative, anti-HBs positive – immune/vaccinated status, so acute hepatitis B is excluded.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Identify liver‐injury pattern → hepatocellular (high AST/ALT, low ALP).<br /></br>Step 2: Look for cause → new drug with known hepatotoxic potential (TMP-SMX), rash fits an idiosyncratic, immune-mediated injury (may progress to DRESS).<br /></br>Step 3: First-line management of suspected drug-induced liver injury (DILI) = STOP the offending drug; most cases resolve once exposure ends.  </p></br><p>Take-home message: Always consider and immediately discontinue a newly started medication when jaundice with a hepatocellular pattern appears within days to weeks of initiation.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole is metabolized to hydroxylamine derivatives that can:<br /></br>1. Directly injure hepatocytes, or<br /></br>2. Act as haptens, triggering a T-cell–mediated hypersensitivity reaction (rash, fever, eosinophilia, liver injury).<br /></br>Onset is usually 5–14 days after exposure but can be sooner with previous sensitization. Prognosis is excellent once the drug is withdrawn, though fulminant hepatic failure can occur if the drug is continued.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>• Initial step: Discontinue TMP-SMX.<br /></br>• Supportive care, monitor LFTs weekly until normalization.<br /></br>• If severe (INR ≥ 1.5, encephalopathy) → hospitalize, consider N-acetylcysteine, transplant eval.<br /></br>• No role for steroids unless classic DRESS with organ involvement.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Subcutaneous peginterferon alfa-2a therapy<br /></br>   – Used for chronic hepatitis B or certain genotypes of hepatitis C. Patient is immune to HBV and no evidence of chronic viral hepatitis. Starting interferon does nothing for DILI.  </p></br><p>C. Intravenous ceftriaxone therapy<br /></br>   – Would be appropriate for acute cholangitis (Charcot triad: fever, RUQ pain, jaundice) or severe community-acquired bacterial infection. This patient is afebrile, painless, with normal ALP; labs/rash point to hypersensitivity, not infection.  </p></br><p>D. Endoscopic retrograde cholangiopancreatography (ERCP)<br /></br>   – Indicated when imaging or lab pattern suggests biliary obstruction (markedly ↑ direct bilirubin &amp; ALP, dilated ducts, or cholangitis). Low ALP and hepatocellular pattern make obstruction unlikely; ERCP would expose the patient to unnecessary risk.  </p></br><p>E. Liver biopsy<br /></br>   – May help diagnose unclear liver disease or stage chronic injury, but is invasive and not required when the etiology (recent hepatotoxic drug) is evident and management is simply drug withdrawal.  </p></br><p>Key Difference Summary<br /></br>• Hepatocellular pattern + new hepatotoxic drug + rash → DILI → stop the drug.<br /></br>• Obstructive, infectious, or chronic viral etiologies would show different patterns (↑ ALP, fever, RUQ pain, positive viral serologies) and dictate different management.</p>
122. A previously healthy 37-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 2-day history of feve and persistent cough productive of yellow sputum. He has not had shortness of breath. He does not smoke. His temperature is 37.9 C 100.2 F, pulse is 88/min, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 134/82 mm Hg. Scattered rhonchi are heard bilaterally. During the examination, he has an episode of severe coughing. An x-ray of the chest shows no abnormalities. In addition to treatment of this patient's symptoms, which of the following is the most appropriate pharmacotherapy?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Amoxicillin</br>b. Azithromycin</br>c. Levofloxacin</br>d. Trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole</br>e. No additional treatment is indicated</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. No additional treatment is indicated  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Previously healthy 37-year-old” – young, immunocompetent, no chronic lung disease.<br /></br>• “2-day history … fever and persistent cough productive of yellow sputum” – classic description of acute bronchitis; purulent-appearing sputum does NOT equal bacterial infection.<br /></br>• “No shortness of breath … scattered rhonchi … chest x-ray shows no abnormalities” – absence of infiltrate rules out pneumonia; rhonchi that clear after coughing is typical for bronchitis.<br /></br>Key pattern: URI-like prodrome → cough (± purulent sputum) → normal CXR = acute viral bronchitis.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Differentiate bronchitis from pneumonia. Normal CXR and stable vitals (T &lt;38.3 °C, RR &lt;20, HR &lt;100) = no pneumonia → no antibiotics.<br /></br>Step 2: Recognize that &gt;90 % of acute bronchitis cases are viral (influenza, parainfluenza, RSV, coronavirus, rhinovirus, adenovirus).<br /></br>Core takeaway: For an otherwise healthy adult with acute bronchitis and a normal chest radiograph, guidelines recommend symptomatic care only (hydration, NSAIDs/acetaminophen, antitussives if needed). Antibiotics do not shorten course, reduce complications, or improve outcomes and promote resistance.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Viral infection → irritation &amp; inflammation of tracheobronchial mucosa → cough often lasting &gt;1 wk.<br /></br>• Purulent sputum reflects sloughed epithelial cells + inflammatory cells, NOT necessarily bacterial superinfection.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>Diagnostics: If pneumonia suspected (tachypnea, focal auscultatory findings, rales, egophony) get CXR; otherwise none needed.<br /></br>Treatment:<br /></br>1. Symptomatic: antitussives (dextromethorphan/codeine), NSAIDs or acetaminophen, inhaled bronchodilator if wheezy.<br /></br>2. Antibiotics: NOT recommended unless pertussis is suspected or patient has chronic lung disease with exacerbation.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answers<br /></br>a. Amoxicillin – Used for typical bacterial pathogens in otitis media, sinusitis, sensitive S. pneumoniae pneumonia, etc. Would be considered if CXR showed lobar infiltrate or patient had high-grade fever, pleuritic pain, or consolidation. Not useful for viral bronchitis.  </p></br><p>b. Azithromycin – Covers atypical pneumonia (Mycoplasma, Chlamydophila), pertussis, COPD exacerbations. Guidelines reserve it for confirmed pertussis or pneumonia; overuse drives macrolide resistance.  </p></br><p>c. Levofloxacin – Broad respiratory fluoroquinolone for community-acquired pneumonia with comorbidities or resistant pathogens. Overkill here; unnecessary risk of tendinopathy, QT prolongation, C. difficile.  </p></br><p>d. Trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole – Used for Pneumocystis jirovecii, UTIs, some skin/soft-tissue MRSA, Nocardia. No role in routine acute bronchitis.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Equating yellow/green sputum with bacterial infection. Purulence alone is not predictive and should not prompt empiric antibiotics in acute bronchitis when chest imaging is clear.</p>
123. A 17-year-old boy comes to the physician because of a scrotal mass that has become increasingly painful during the past 3 days. He is not certain how long the mass has been present. There is no histo [Y of trauma. He is sexually active with several partners and uses condoms inconsistently. Examination shows a tender mass posterior to the left testis. Which of the following is the most likely cause of these findings?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Incompetence of the spermatic vein</br>b. Infection of the epididymis</br>c.
