Patency of the processus vaginal is</br>d. Retractility of the testis</br>e. Torsion of the appendix testis</br>F. Torsion of the spermatic cord</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>b. Infection of the epididymis  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 17-year-old sexually active male → at highest risk for Chlamydia trachomatis or Neisseria gonorrhoeae infection.<br /></br>• “Mass that has become increasingly painful during the past 3 days” → subacute onset (hours-to-days), not the sudden, excruciating onset typical of testicular torsion.<br /></br>• “Tender mass posterior to the left testis” → the epididymis lies posterolateral to the testis; inflammation makes it enlarged and exquisitely tender.  </p></br><p>Taken together, these clues point to acute epididymitis.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning / Key Takeaway<br /></br>Acute epididymitis in men &lt;35 years is usually caused by sexually transmitted pathogens (C trachomatis, N gonorrhoeae). It presents with gradual-onset scrotal pain and swelling localized to the epididymis (posterior to the testis).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>1. Pathogens ascend the urethra → vas deferens → epididymis.<br /></br>2. Inflammatory edema causes epididymal enlargement and pain.<br /></br>3. If untreated, can spread to the testis (epididymo-orchitis) or cause infertility.  </p></br><p>Risk factors: unprotected intercourse, multiple partners, recent urinary instrumentation, vigorous physical activity.  </p></br><p>Clinical manifestations<br /></br>• Gradually progressive unilateral scrotal pain/swelling<br /></br>• Tender, indurated epididymis; testis itself usually normal early on<br /></br>• Possible dysuria, urethral discharge, fever<br /></br>• Positive Prehn sign (pain relief when scrotum elevated) and intact cremasteric reflex (helps distinguish from testicular torsion).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostics &amp; Treatment (for Step 2)<br /></br>Diagnostics<br /></br>• UA ± NAAT for Chlamydia/Gonorrhea.<br /></br>• Scrotal ultrasound if diagnosis uncertain (↑ blood flow to epididymis vs ↓ flow in torsion).  </p></br><p>Treatment<br /></br>• Empiric antibiotics: ceftriaxone 500 mg IM single dose + doxycycline 100 mg PO bid × 7 days.<br /></br>• Scrotal elevation, NSAIDs, ice.<br /></br>• Treat partners and stress condom use.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Incompetence of the spermatic vein (varicocele)<br /></br>• “Bag of worms” soft mass superior/posterior to testis that enlarges with standing or Valsalva; classically painless or dull aching, not acutely tender.  </p></br><p>c. Patency of the processus vaginalis (communicating hydrocele)<br /></br>• Fluctuant, transilluminating scrotal swelling present since infancy/childhood; painless, not tender.  </p></br><p>d. Retractility of the testis<br /></br>• Due to strong cremasteric reflex; testis intermittently “disappears” into inguinal canal; no fixed tender mass.  </p></br><p>e. Torsion of the appendix testis<br /></br>• Acute pain at superior pole; small blue discoloration (“blue-dot sign”). Pain is sudden, usually in boys 7-12 y; mass is anterolateral/superior, not posterior.  </p></br><p>f. Torsion of the spermatic cord (testicular torsion)<br /></br>• Sudden, severe pain; high-riding, horizontal testis; absent cremasteric reflex; onset in minutes, not progressive over days. Urgent surgery required.  </p></br><p>Key differentiator: Only epididymitis presents with a gradually progressive, tender mass located posterior to the testis in a sexually active adolescent.</p>
124. A 52-year-old woman is admitted to the hospital for treatment of acute diverticulitis. After 2 days of intravenous antibiotic therapy, she has a temperature of 39.1  C 102.4  F and abdominal rigidity. Her leukocyte count is 17,400/mm 3 with 15 percent bands. The physician explains to the patient that she will need to undergo sigmoid colectomy and that, depending on the findings, she may require a colostomy. The patient agrees to the operation but refuses to undergo a colostomy. The patient's husband is in the hospital but is not with the patient at this time. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Engage the patient in a discussion of the possible operative findings and management options</br>b. Notify the hospital attorney</br>c. Obtain informed consent from the patient's husband</br>d. Obtain a psychiatric assessment of the patient's mental capacity</br>e. Perform the operation without a colostomy, regardless of the findings</br>F. Perform the operation and a colostomy if indicated</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>A. Engage the patient in a discussion of the possible operative findings and management options  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Competent adult: The patient is awake, communicating, and shows no evidence of delirium or altered mental status, so she retains decision-making capacity.<br /></br>• High-risk surgery with two potential intra-operative paths: primary anastomosis vs diverting colostomy.<br /></br>• Explicit refusal of one option (colostomy) despite agreeing to surgery.<br /></br>These elements create an informed-consent dilemma, not an incapacity or surrogate-decision issue.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Verify capacity → She is alert, coherent, and can express reasoned choices.<br /></br>Step 2: Ensure informed consent → She must understand:<br /></br>  – Why a colectomy is needed (perforated/complicated diverticulitis).<br /></br>  – What surgeons might find (gross contamination, unhealthy distal bowel).<br /></br>  – Why a colostomy could become the only safe option.<br /></br>Step 3: Shared decision-making → Re-explore her values (fear of colostomy? quality of life?) and whether she would rather forego surgery entirely if a colostomy were required.<br /></br>Thus the correct next step is a thorough, empathetic discussion aimed at fully informed consent (Choice A).</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (brief—ethics question)<br /></br>Complicated diverticulitis can progress to perforation, generalized peritonitis, and sepsis. Intra-op findings dictate either:<br /></br>• Resection + primary anastomosis (if minimal contamination and healthy tissue)<br /></br>• Hartmann procedure (resection + end colostomy) if gross contamination or unstable patient.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>• Pre-op: CT scan often confirms perforation/abscess; broad-spectrum IV antibiotics.<br /></br>• Operative: Sigmoid colectomy, decision for colostomy made intra-operatively.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Notify the hospital attorney – No legal dispute exists; first duty is physician-patient communication, not litigation.<br /></br>C. Obtain informed consent from the husband – A surrogate is only used when the patient lacks capacity or explicitly delegates authority. She is competent and has expressed her wishes.<br /></br>D. Obtain a psychiatric assessment – No evidence of impaired cognition, psychosis, or depression hindering capacity. Capacity is assumed unless specific reasons suggest otherwise.<br /></br>E. Perform the operation without a colostomy regardless of findings – Would violate standard surgical care and could endanger her life—non-maleficence breach.<br /></br>F. Perform the operation and a colostomy if indicated – Would override the patient’s clearly stated refusal, violating autonomy (potential battery).</p></br><p>Take-Home Message<br /></br>When a competent patient refuses part of a proposed treatment, the physician must ensure the patient is fully informed, clarify goals and values, and attempt to reach a mutually acceptable plan before involving surrogates or overriding the patient’s decision.</p>
125. A 16-year-old girl is brought to the physician because her parents are concerned about her eating habits. During the past 8 months, she has stopped eating meals with the family, expressed concern about her weight, and lectured the family about improving their dietary practices. The patient says that eating more nutritious foods has decreased her appetite. She says the fact that she has enough energy to be on the cross-country running and cheerleading teams shows she is eating appropriately. She worries she does not have the right body shape for fashionable clothes and thinks she would be more popular if she could dress better. She receives grades of A's in school but worries she will not be able to maintain her grade point average because this year has been more challenging since she began taking several advanced-level classes. She says she has not been spending much time with friends because of schoolwork demands. The patient is 178 cm 5 ft 10 in tall and weighs 53 kg 116 lb; BMI is 17 kg/m ~. Vital signs are within normal limits. Physical examination shows no abnormalities. Mental status examination shows a depressed mood and affect; the patient becomes anxious when discussing school performance and her weight. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Anorexia nervosa</br>b. Body dysmorphic disorder</br>c. Generalized anxiety disorder</br>d. Major depressive disorder</br>E  Normal adolescent behavior</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Anorexia nervosa  </p></br><hr /></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>Key phrases pointing to anorexia nervosa:<br /></br>• “Stopped eating meals with the family” – covert food restriction.<br /></br>• “Expressed concern about her weight” + “worries she does not have the right body shape” – distorted body image.<br /></br>• “Lectured the family about improving their dietary practices” – food‐related preoccupation.<br /></br>• BMI 17 kg/m² in a 16-year-old girl (underweight; &lt;18.5 in adults, &lt;5th percentile in adolescents).<br /></br>• Maintains high activity level (cross-country, cheerleading) despite low weight – common in restrictive subtype.<br /></br>• Amenorrhea is no longer required for DSM-5, so its absence in the stem does not rule out the diagnosis.<br /></br>All other clues (perfectionism, need for control, high academic pressure, social withdrawal, depressed/anxious affect) are classic personality/psychological traits seen in patients with anorexia nervosa.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Underweight (objective finding) +<br /></br>2. Persistent fear of gaining weight +<br /></br>3. Distorted perception of body / over-valuation of weight &amp; shape<br /></br>= Core DSM-5 triad for anorexia nervosa.<br /></br>The stem purposefully supplies normal vital signs and no physical abnormalities to remind you these are not required; BMI and cognitions are enough.</p></br><p>Take-home: On NBME/Step 2, always calculate BMI and pair it with weight-centric thoughts/behaviors → anorexia nervosa.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)<br /></br>Etiology: Multifactorial—genetic predisposition (twin studies), sociocultural pressures, personality traits (perfectionism, harm avoidance), family dynamics.<br /></br>Mechanism: Chronic caloric restriction → loss of adipose tissue ↓ leptin → hypothalamic‐pituitary‐gonadal suppression → amenorrhea/low bone density; electrolyte abnormalities if severe.<br /></br>Clinical manifestations: Low BMI, lanugo, bradycardia/hypotension, osteoporosis, amenorrhea, hypothermia. Psychiatric comorbidity: depression, anxiety, OCD-like traits.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>• First-line: Family-based therapy (Maudsley approach) for adolescents + nutritional rehabilitation.<br /></br>• Psychotherapy adjuncts: CBT targeting distorted cognitions.<br /></br>• Pharmacology: No FDA-approved meds for weight restoration; SSRIs may help comorbid depression/anxiety but only after weight ≥ 18.5.<br /></br>• Medical monitoring: vitals, electrolytes, bone density, cardiac (EKG for prolonged QT), menstrual status.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>b. Body dysmorphic disorder<br /></br>– Preoccupation with a perceived defect in appearance (usually face/skin/hair). Weight/shape concerns alone are not BDD; low BMI also not typical. Patients with BDD usually have normal weight and do not restrict food to lose weight.</p></br><p>c. Generalized anxiety disorder<br /></br>– Excessive worry about multiple domains ≥6 months + physical symptoms. Although she worries about grades and popularity, the prominent focus on weight, active restriction, and underweight make anorexia a better fit. GAD would not explain BMI 17.</p></br><p>d. Major depressive disorder<br /></br>– Requires ≥5 SIGECAPS symptoms for ≥2 weeks. While she has depressed mood, the stem lacks anhedonia, sleep disturbance, appetite increase/weight gain, psychomotor changes, etc. Moreover, MDD does not produce fear of gaining weight or food restriction leading to underweight.</p></br><p>e. Normal adolescent behavior<br /></br>– Healthy adolescents may diet or worry about body image, but they maintain normal weight (BMI 18.5–24.9) and do not exhibit persistent restriction, distorted body image, or significant functional impairment (social isolation, family conflict) seen here.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Confusing perfectionistic school worries with GAD or assuming body image concerns without low BMI equals BDD. Always check objective weight criteria for anorexia.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Core Takeaway<br /></br>In an underweight adolescent with caloric restriction and fear of weight gain, the diagnosis is anorexia nervosa—even if vital signs are normal and amenorrhea is not mentioned.</p>
126. During observation of a surgical procedure, a physician notices the anesthesiologist falling asleep; the observing physician says the anesthesiologist's name, and he wakes up and attends to the remainder of the operation appropriately. After the procedure, the physician approaches the anesthesiologist who states that he was up late last evening attending to an emergency procedure. The surgeon later informs the physician that this anesthesiologist frequently falls asleep in the operating room. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step for the physician?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. No action is necessary since the surgeon has been notified</br>b. Recommend that the anesthesiologist undergo sleep studies</br>c. Report the anesthesiologist to the chief of the medical staff</br>d. Report the anesthesiologist to the police</br>e. Report the anesthesiologist to the specialty society</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Report the anesthesiologist to the chief of the medical staff  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Anesthesiologist falling asleep” during a case = impaired performance that can jeopardize patient safety.<br /></br>• “Frequently falls asleep in the operating room” (per surgeon) shows a pattern, not an isolated lapse due to a single rough night.<br /></br>• Physician already “approaches the anesthesiologist” → informal peer-to-peer conversation has occurred, yet ongoing risk remains.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach to an impaired colleague:<br /></br>1. Ensure immediate patient safety (calling his name, which awakened him).<br /></br>2. Speak directly with the colleague to clarify (already done).<br /></br>3. If problem persists or is serious enough to endanger patients, escalate to the appropriate institutional body (chief of staff/impairment or credentialing committee).<br /></br>Key takeaway: Physicians have an ethical and legal duty to protect patients from impaired or incompetent colleagues and must report persistent or dangerous behavior to the proper authority within the institution.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (brief, as issue is professionalism rather than disease)<br /></br>Chronic sleep deprivation causes decreased vigilance, slowed reaction time, microsleeps, and increased medical error rate—posing direct harm under general anesthesia.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Management of impairment = occupational, not medical (sleep study would only be warranted after formal evaluation and remediation plan). The immediate “treatment” is removing the clinician from patient care until safety is assured and evaluation is complete.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. No action needed because surgeon was notified – Wrong. Notifying another surgeon does not satisfy the physician’s duty; it does nothing to stop the ongoing risk and doesn’t engage the formal peer-review mechanism.<br /></br>b. Recommend sleep studies – Premature and insufficient. Diagnostic evaluation is arranged by the hospital or physician-health program after formal reporting, not by a casual suggestion.<br /></br>d. Report to the police – Police handle criminal activity; falling asleep is a professionalism / competency issue managed internally first.<br /></br>e. Report to the specialty society – Specialty societies have no direct authority to restrict clinical privileges or ensure immediate patient safety. Internal hospital leadership is the appropriate first line.  </p></br><p>Bottom line: Persistent or potentially dangerous impairment in a colleague mandates formal reporting to the hospital’s chief of staff (or equivalent peer-review body) to protect patients and initiate proper evaluation.</p>
127. A 32-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 1-week history of tenderness and swelling of her neck. She has no history of serious illness. Her only medication is an oral contraceptive. Her temperature is 38.1  C 100.5 F, pulse is 95/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 110/70 mm Hg. Examination of the neck shows no masses. The thyroid gland is slightly enlarged and tender. No nodules are palpated. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Serum studies show a thyroid-stimulating hormone concentration of 0.3 μU/ml and thyroxine T4 concentration of 11.5 μg/dl. Antithyroid antibodies are not detected. A 131 1 thyroid scan shows decreased uptake. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Acute suppurative thyroiditis</br>b. Chronic lymphocytic Hashimoto thyroiditis</br>c. Exogenous use of thyroid hormone</br>d. Graves disease</br>e. Subacute granulomatous de Quervain thyroiditis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Subacute granulomatous (de Quervain) thyroiditis  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “1-week history of tenderness and swelling of her neck” → Painful thyroid is the classic buzz-word for subacute (de Quervain) thyroiditis.<br /></br>• “Thyroid gland is slightly enlarged and tender” → Confirms painful goiter rather than the painless enlargement of Hashimoto or Graves.<br /></br>• Low TSH (0.3 µU/mL) with high-normal T4 (11.5 µg/dL) → Transient thyrotoxic phase from release of pre-formed hormone.<br /></br>• “131-I scan shows decreased uptake” → Damaged follicles are not organifying iodine; distinguishes thyroiditis or exogenous hormone from Graves (high uptake).<br /></br>• “Antithyroid antibodies not detected” → Makes autoimmune Hashimoto or Graves unlikely.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Stepwise approach:<br /></br>1. Is this true hyperthyroidism (increased synthesis) or thyroiditis (release of stored hormone)? → Low uptake → thyroiditis.<br /></br>2. Is the gland painful? → Yes → subacute granulomatous thyroiditis.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Painful tender thyroid + low uptake = de Quervain (post-viral) thyroiditis.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Probably post-viral granulomatous inflammation of the thyroid (often follows URI).<br /></br>• Mechanism: Destruction of follicles → spill of T4/T3 (transient hyperthyroid), followed by euthyroid, then possible transient hypothyroid phase before recovery.<br /></br>• Clinical features: Painful tender thyroid, systemic symptoms (low-grade fever, malaise), elevated ESR, low radio-iodine uptake.<br /></br>• Prognosis: Self-limited; most recover normal thyroid function in 6–8 weeks.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• Labs: Low TSH, elevated T4/T3 early; elevated ESR, CRP.<br /></br>• Radio-iodine uptake: Diffusely low.<br /></br>• Treatment: Supportive—high-dose NSAIDs (eg, ibuprofen or aspirin) for pain/fever. If severe, short course of prednisone. Beta-blocker (propranolol) for adrenergic symptoms during thyrotoxic phase. Antithyroid drugs (methimazole, propylthiouracil) are NOT useful because synthesis is not increased.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Acute suppurative (bacterial) thyroiditis<br /></br>   • Typically in immunocompromised or after penetrating trauma; very high fever, marked leukocytosis, and usually a focal fluctuant mass, often unilateral. Radio-iodine uptake low, but vignette lacks severe illness or focal abscess.  </p></br><p>b. Chronic lymphocytic (Hashimoto) thyroiditis<br /></br>   • Painless “rubbery” goiter, positive anti-TPO or anti-thyroglobulin antibodies, often hypothyroid (↑TSH, ↓T4) or occasionally transient hyperthyroid (“Hashitoxicosis”) but still painless.  </p></br><p>c. Exogenous use of thyroid hormone<br /></br>   • Low uptake (thyroid shut off), but the gland is NOT tender or enlarged. History often reveals weight-loss medication, factitious disorder, or thyroid cancer follow-up.  </p></br><p>d. Graves disease<br /></br>   • Diffuse nontender goiter, ophthalmopathy, pretibial myxedema. Low TSH, high T4 with diffusely increased radio-iodine uptake due to TSH-receptor-stimulating IgG. Tenderness and low uptake argue against Graves.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfalls<br /></br>• Equating “low uptake” with exogenous hormone only—always consider painful subacute thyroiditis.<br /></br>• Using antithyroid medications in thyroiditis—they do not work because hormone synthesis is not up-regulated.  </p></br><p>Bottom Line<br /></br>A tender, painful thyroid with low radio-iodine uptake after a viral prodrome is classic for subacute granulomatous (de Quervain) thyroiditis.</p>
128. Seven days after cesarean delivery for arrest of descent, a 27-year-old woman, gravida 1, para 1, remains in the hospital because of spiking temperatures to 39.2 C 102.5 F since delivery. Treatment with broad-spectrum antibiotics has not resolved the fever. She feels well when she does not have a fever. Laboratory studies at 35 weeks' gestation were positive for Streptococcus aga/actiae group 8, and she was treated with intrapartum penicillin. Her temperature now is 38.3 C 101  F. Examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Endomyometritis</br>b. Group 8 streptococcal sepsis</br>c. Occult bowel injury</br>d. Septic pelvic thrombophlebitis</br>e. Wound abscess</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Septic pelvic thrombophlebitis (post-partum ovarian-/pelvic-vein thrombosis)</p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Seven days after cesarean delivery … spiking temperatures … broad-spectrum antibiotics has not resolved the fever.”<br /></br>  – Persistent, high “spiking” fevers &gt; 48–72 h that are refractory to adequate antibiotics is the classic presentation of septic pelvic thrombophlebitis (SPT).<br /></br>• “She feels well when she does not have a fever. Examination shows no abnormalities.”<br /></br>  – Patients usually look well between febrile spikes, and physical exam (including incision, uterus, adnexa) is often unremarkable because the infection is inside an occluded pelvic/ovarian vein.<br /></br>• Post-cesarean delivery is the strongest obstetric risk factor for SPT because surgery, endothelial injury and the hypercoagulable postpartum state promote thrombus formation that can seed bacteria.</p></br><p>The question purposely withholds any focal findings (uterine tenderness, foul lochia, wound erythema, abdominal pain) to steer you away from endometritis or wound infection.</p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Stepwise approach to postpartum fever (&gt;38.0 °C):</p></br><ol></br><li>POD 0–1: wind (atelectasis)  </li></br><li>POD 2–3: womb (endometritis) → responds to clinda + gent  </li></br><li>POD 4–5: wound infection/abscess  </li></br><li>POD 5–7: walking (DVT / pelvic thrombophlebitis) → persistent fevers despite antibiotics<br /></br>In a patient now 1 week out, on the correct broad-spectrum coverage yet still febrile, the algorithm points to SPT.</li></br></ol></br><p>Core takeaway: When postpartum fever persists &gt;48–72 h on appropriate antibiotics and exam is benign, think septic pelvic thrombophlebitis and add anticoagulation.</p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Infected thrombus in ovarian or deep pelvic veins (usually right ovarian vein).<br /></br>• Risk factors: Cesarean delivery, prolonged labor, endometritis, obesity, thrombophilia.<br /></br>• Mechanism: Virchow triad—venous stasis (gravid uterus), endothelial injury (surgery), hypercoagulability (pregnancy). Bacteria (mixed aerobes/anaerobes) seed the clot, producing intermittent bacteremia and fever.<br /></br>• Clinical picture: High spiking fevers, minimal symptoms, mild lower-abdominal pain (may be absent), negative cultures in ~75 %.  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>Imaging (CT or MRI with contrast) shows enlarged thrombosed ovarian vein, but treatment can be started empirically.<br /></br>Management:<br /></br>1. Continue broad-spectrum IV antibiotics (e.g., clindamycin + gentamicin).<br /></br>2. Add IV unfractionated heparin or low-molecular-weight heparin.<br /></br>Fever usually resolves within 24–48 h of heparin initiation.</p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Endomyometritis<br /></br>• Typically POD 2–3. Presents with uterine fundal tenderness, foul-smelling lochia, leukocytosis, and usually defervesces within 48 h of clinda + gentamicin. Persistent fever despite treatment argues against it.</p></br><p>b. Group B streptococcal sepsis<br /></br>• Would cause systemic toxicity, hypotension, positive blood cultures, and should respond to broad-spectrum/penicillin therapy. The patient appears well between fevers and cultures would likely have flagged.</p></br><p>c. Occult bowel injury<br /></br>• Presents early with peritoneal signs, abdominal distention, ileus, leukocytosis, worsening pain. Physical exam “shows no abnormalities,” making this unlikely.</p></br><p>e. Wound abscess<br /></br>• Fevers usually start POD 4–5. You’d see erythema, warmth, fluctuance, purulent drainage, or tenderness at the incision site—none of which are present.</p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Post-cesarean woman with spiking fevers that persist despite adequate antibiotics and a benign physical exam = septic pelvic thrombophlebitis. Treat with continued broad-spectrum antibiotics PLUS anticoagulation.</p>
129. A 72-year-old woman comes to the physician because of generalized fatigue for 1 week. During this period, she has occasionally had light-headedness on standing up guickly. She has not had chest pain or shortness of breath. She has hypertension and coronary artery disease. Current medications include amlodipine and hydrochlorothiazide. She is alert and oriented to person, place, and time. Her temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 38/min, respirations are 14/min, and blood pressure is 80/40 mm Hg. Crackles are heard at both lung bases. S 1 and S 2 are normal. A grade 2/6 systolic murmur is heard best at the fourth left intercostal space with radiation to the carotid arteries. There is no peripheral edema. An ECG is shown. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Electrophysiologic study</br>b. Dobutamine stress echocardiography</br>c. Add amiodarone to the regimen</br>d. Add diltiazem to the regimen</br>e. Insert cardiac pacemaker</br>F. Insert implantable cardiac defibrillator</br></br></br><p>Correct answer: Insert a permanent cardiac pacemaker</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• Key words: 72-year-old, pulse 38/min, systolic BP 80/40 mm Hg, light-headedness, “occasionally on standing,” elderly with CAD/HTN, crackles (low-output state), no offending AV-nodal drugs (only amlodipine, HCTZ).<br /></br>• Symptomatic bradycardia is the dominant problem (dizziness + hypotension). In an elderly patient this almost always represents sinus-node dysfunction (“sick sinus”) or high-grade AV block.<br /></br>• NBME pattern: Elderly + HR &lt; 40 + symptoms ⇒ pacemaker. The exact murmur description is a distractor.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step 1: Identify rhythm-related symptoms (presyncope, hypotension).<br /></br>Step 2: Verify bradycardia is not iatrogenic (non-nodal CCB amlodipine does <strong>not</strong> depress conduction).<br /></br>Step 3: In the presence of symptoms, guideline-directed therapy is a permanent pacemaker—no further diagnostic tests required.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Symptomatic sinus-node dysfunction or high-grade AV block in an adult warrants permanent pacing.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Sick sinus syndrome = degeneration of SA node → periods of sinus bradycardia, sinus pauses, or alternating tachy-brady episodes.<br /></br>• High-grade AV block (Mobitz II or complete block) = fibrosis/ischemia of the His-Purkinje system → escape rhythm at 30–40 bpm.<br /></br>• Perfusion drops (↓CO = HR × SV) → dizziness, hypotension, pulmonary congestion (crackles).<br /></br>• Definitive therapy = dual-chamber paced rhythm to restore an adequate, regular HR.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics</br>• Acute unstable bradycardia: atropine → transcutaneous pacing → dopamine/epi drip. (Not asked here; she has survived 1 week.)<br /></br>• Chronic therapy: permanent pacemaker implantation (AAI/VVI/DDD depending on node function and AV synchrony).<br /></br>• No medication “fixes” intrinsic conduction system degeneration.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Electrophysiologic study – Useful when diagnosis uncertain (e.g., recurrent syncope of unknown cause) or for tachyarrhythmias. Here the patient is already clearly symptomatic with documented HR 38/min; outcome will not change pacing indication.</p></br><p>b. Dobutamine stress echocardiography – Used to evaluate inducible ischemia or viability in CAD, not for bradyarrhythmia management.</p></br><p>c. Add amiodarone – Class III antiarrhythmic slows SA/AV conduction; would worsen bradycardia and hypotension.</p></br><p>d. Add diltiazem – Nondihydropyridine CCB further depresses AV nodal conduction; contraindicated in bradycardia.</p></br><p>f. Implantable cardioverter-defibrillator (ICD) – Prevents sudden death from ventricular tachyarrhythmias (EF ≤ 35 %, VT/VF), does not correct slow rhythms; pacing function of most ICDs not first-line for isolated bradycardia.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Ordering additional diagnostics (EP study) before treating clear symptomatic bradycardia delays life-saving therapy.</p>
130. A previously healthy 5-year-old boy is brought to the office by his mother because of the sudden onset of severe abdominal pain 1 hour after he ate dinner. He receives no medications. Immunizations are up-to-date. He was adopted at birth, and there is no information available about his biological family history. His temperature is 38.3 C 100.9 F, pulse is 100/min, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure 85/60 mm Hg. Examination shows conjunctiva! icterus. There is guarding and moderate tenderness to palpation of the right upper quadrant. The spleen tip is palpable. Results of laboratory studies are shown:</br>Hemoglobin 8.3 g/dl</br>Leukocyte count 11 000/mm 3</br>Reticulocyte count  percent</br>Serum total bilirubin 4 mg/dl</br>Direct antiglobulin Coombs test result is negative. A blood smear shows small, uniform, spherical erythrocytes. Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient's abdominal pain?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Biliary colic</br>b. Duodenal ulcer</br>c. Hepatoblastoma</br>d. Nephroblastoma Wilms tumor</br>e. Splenomegaly</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>• Biliary colic  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Small, uniform, spherical erythrocytes” + negative Coombs = hereditary spherocytosis (HS).<br /></br>• HS → chronic extravascular hemolysis → ↑unconjugated bilirubin → black (pigmented) bilirubin gallstones.<br /></br>• “Sudden onset of severe abdominal pain 1 hour after he ate dinner” + right-upper-quadrant guarding = typical presentation of symptomatic cholelithiasis (biliary colic); pain classically follows a meal when the gallbladder contracts.<br /></br>• Conjunctival icterus and splenic tip palpable support hemolytic anemia with splenomegaly.<br /></br>• Low BP (85/60 mm Hg) and fever can accompany acute biliary pain but are less characteristic of the other answer choices.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (take-home message)<br /></br>In ANY child with hereditary spherocytosis (or other chronic hemolytic anemia), acute RUQ pain after eating should immediately raise concern for pigmented gallstones causing biliary colic or cholecystitis.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Hereditary spherocytosis<br /></br>• Etiology: Autosomal dominant defects in spectrin, ankyrin, band 3, or protein 4.2.<br /></br>• Mechanism: Loss of RBC membrane surface area → sphere-shaped cells trapped in splenic cords → splenic macrophage destruction (extravascular hemolysis).<br /></br>• Findings: Normocytic anemia, ↑reticulocytes, ↑MCHC, spherocytes on smear, negative Coombs, splenomegaly, jaundice, pigmented gallstones.<br /></br>• Complications: Aplastic crisis (parvovirus B19), cholelithiasis.  </p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>• Confirmation: Osmotic fragility test or EMA binding by flow cytometry.<br /></br>• Acute biliary colic: Ultrasound (first-line imaging) shows gallstones; treat with analgesia, possible antibiotics if cholecystitis, elective cholecystectomy.<br /></br>• Long-term HS: Folate supplementation; definitive = splenectomy (often combined with cholecystectomy). Vaccinate against encapsulated organisms pre-splenectomy (PCV13/23, Hib, MenACWY/B).</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Duodenal ulcer<br /></br>   – Classically causes epigastric pain that IMPROVES with meals; not linked to hemolysis or jaundice.  </p></br><p>b. Hepatoblastoma<br /></br>   – Presents as a firm hepatic mass, abdominal distention, ↑α-fetoprotein, and chronic, not sudden, pain; no hemolytic anemia or spherocytes.  </p></br><p>c. Nephroblastoma (Wilms tumor)<br /></br>   – Flank/abdominal mass, hematuria, possible hypertension; pain is flank/abdominal, not RUQ post-prandial; again, no anemia with spherocytes or jaundice.  </p></br><p>d. Splenomegaly<br /></br>   – HS does produce splenomegaly, but splenic discomfort is dull LUQ fullness, not acute RUQ colicky pain after eating.  </p></br><p>Key Differences: Only biliary colic explains RUQ pain precipitated by a meal in a child predisposed to pigmented gallstones from chronic hemolysis.</p>
131. A prospective multicenter study is conducted to determine if an oxygenation index 01 greater than 20 predicts death in patients who develop acute respiratory distress syndrome ARDS after a bone marrow transplantation. A total of 120 patients are enrolled. The results show:</br>Number of Patients With OI ≤ 20 | Number of Patients With OI > 20 | Total Number of Patients</br>Survived: 25 | 20 | 45</br>Died:     15 | 60 | 75</br>Total:    40 | 80 | 120</br>According to these results, which of the following represents the sensitivity of this index?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. 56 percent</br>b. 63 percent</br>c. 75 percent</br>d. 80 percent</br>e. 90 percent</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. 80 percent  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Oxygenation index (OI) &gt; 20 predicts death” → OI &gt; 20 is the “positive” test result.<br /></br>• Outcome of interest = death after ARDS (the “disease” state).<br /></br>• The 2 × 2 table is therefore:  </p></br><pre><code>                         Test + (OI &gt; 20) | Test – (OI ≤ 20) | Total</br></code></pre></br><p>Died (disease +)             60                     15                   75<br /></br> Survived (disease –)     20                     25                   45<br /></br> Total                           80                     40                 120  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (How to calculate sensitivity)<br /></br>1. Identify true positives (TP): patients who died AND had OI &gt; 20 = 60.<br /></br>2. Identify false negatives (FN): patients who died BUT had OI ≤ 20 = 15.<br /></br>3. Sensitivity = TP / (TP + FN) = 60 / (60 + 15) = 60 / 75 = 0.80 → 80 %.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Sensitivity tells you how well a test detects disease among those who truly have it (rule-out ability).  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (very brief—focus of question is statistics)<br /></br>ARDS after bone-marrow transplant carries high mortality. The oxygenation index (mean airway pressure × FiO₂ × 100 / PaO₂) quantifies hypoxemia; higher values reflect worse pulmonary function.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. 56 % – would be the CALCULATED NEGATIVE PREDICTIVE VALUE (NPV) if one mistakenly used survivors with OI ≤ 20 divided by everyone with OI ≤ 20 (25/40 = 62.5 %) or did other wrong math. Does not correspond to sensitivity.<br /></br>b. 63 % – could arise from various mis-pairings (e.g., 60/95). Not sensitivity.<br /></br>c. 75 % – positive predictive value (PPV): among patients with OI &gt; 20, 60/80 died. Students often confuse PPV with sensitivity.<br /></br>e. 90 % – would be specificity if numbers were different; here, specificity = 25/45 = 56 %.  </p></br><p>Key pitfall: confusing sensitivity (uses only the “disease” row) with PPV (uses only the “test-positive” column). Remember:<br /></br>• Sensitivity = columns within DISEASE + row.<br /></br>• Specificity = columns within DISEASE – row.<br /></br>• PPV/NPV = rows within TEST + or TEST – columns.</p>
132. An otherwise healthy 47-year-old woman, gravida 2, para 2, comes to the physician because of hot flashes that began 8 months ago and have become more frequent during the past 3 months. Menses now occur at</br>irregular 40- to 60-day intervals with light flow and last 2 to 3 days. Her last menstrual period was 2 months ago. Her pulse is 85/min, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 140/80 mm Hg. Examination, including</br>pelvic examination, shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Discussion of contraceptive management</br>b. Discussion of hormone therapy</br>c. Measurement of salivary cortisol concentration</br>d. Measurement of serum estradiol concentration</br>e. Measurement of serum prolactin concentration</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection</br>A. Discussion of contraceptive management  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• “47-year-old woman” + “hot flashes” + “irregular 40- to 60-day cycles” + “last period 2 months ago” → classic description of the menopause transition (perimenopause).<br /></br>• Still &lt;12 months since her last menses → she has NOT reached menopause yet, so spontaneous ovulation can still occur.<br /></br>• No red-flag findings on exam; vital signs essentially normal → no immediate indication for endocrine work-up.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Key question: What counsel does a perimenopausal woman need right now?<br /></br>• Hot flashes confirm vasomotor symptoms of falling estrogen, but cycles—although irregular—are ongoing.<br /></br>• Fertility declines, but is not zero; unintended pregnancy is a real risk until menopause is complete (12 consecutive months without menses).<br /></br>Therefore, the priority is to address contraception. Combined hormonal contraception or a levonorgestrel IUD can simultaneously control bleeding, provide contraception, and often reduce vasomotor symptoms.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Any woman &lt;55 years who has had a period within the past year needs pregnancy prevention counseling.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>Perimenopause = gradual ovarian follicle depletion → inconsistent estradiol secretion → elevated FSH/LH, anovulatory cycles, and vasomotor instability (hot flashes). Ovulation is sporadic but possible until ovarian failure is complete.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review</br>Contraceptive options:</br>1. Combined oral contraceptive pill (ethinyl estradiol + levonorgestrel, etc.) – regulates cycles, decreases hot flashes, offers bone protection.</br>2. Progestin-only methods (norethindrone mini-pill, depot medroxyprogesterone, levonorgestrel IUD) – useful if estrogen contraindication (e.g., hypertension ≥160/100, migraines with aura).</br>3. Barrier methods or permanent sterilization if desired.</p></br><p>Hormone replacement therapy (HRT) is reserved for symptomatic women who are already menopausal (≥12 months amenorrhea) and require the lowest effective estrogen dose; it is NOT first-line while regular contraception would suffice.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>B. Discussion of hormone therapy<br /></br>• HRT is for confirmed post-menopause. She still menstruates, so standard dose menopausal HRT is premature and would not prevent pregnancy. Combined OCPs (contraceptive doses) are more appropriate.</p></br><p>C. Measurement of salivary cortisol concentration<br /></br>• Screens for Cushing disease; no features (central obesity, striae, acne, hypertension beyond mild) are present.</p></br><p>D. Measurement of serum estradiol concentration<br /></br>• Estradiol fluctuates wildly in perimenopause; a single level is not diagnostic and does not change management.</p></br><p>E. Measurement of serum prolactin concentration<br /></br>• Hyperprolactinemia causes amenorrhea, galactorrhea; she has normal breast exam, only irregular menses. No indication to check prolactin.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall</br>Assuming fertility is so low in the late 40s that contraception is unnecessary. NBME loves testing that pregnancy remains possible until 12 months of amenorrhea.  </p></br><p>Bottom Line</br>Perimenopausal women with irregular cycles need counseling on reliable contraception until menopause is complete; combined hormonal contraception often addresses both pregnancy prevention and vasomotor symptoms.</p>
133. A 28-year-old man comes to the office for a follow-up examination 2 days after undergoing HIV antibody/antigen testing. The patient is sexually active with only men and uses condoms inconsistently. He is asymptomatic and has no history of serious illness. He takes no medications. Results of HIV antibody/antigen testing 6 months ago were negative. The patient does not smoke cigarettes, drink alcohol, or use illicit drugs. He is 180 cm 5 ft 11 in tall and weighs 82 kg 180 lb; BMI is 25 kg/m 2. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows no abnormalities. CD4+ T-lymphocyte count is 724/mm 3 Normal~500, and plasma HIV viral load is 169,000 copies/ml. Results of serum studies are within the reference ranges. When told of his diagnosis, the patient begins to cry and is comforted by his mother. In addition to expressing empathy, the most appropriate next step in management is to address which of the following with the patient?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Financial concerns about treatment</br>b. How to tell family and friends about the diagnosis</br>c. Initiation of antiretroviral therapy</br>d. The patient's fears about having HIV infection</br>e. The possible stigma associated with a positive HIV antibody test result</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. The patient’s fears about having HIV infection  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Newly diagnosed HIV infection: CD4 724/mm³ (normal), viral load 169,000 copies/mL.<br /></br>• Emotional reaction: begins to cry, mother present for support.<br /></br>• Question stem: “In addition to expressing empathy, the most appropriate NEXT step in management…” – tells you the encounter is still in the “breaking-bad-news” moment, not the time for detailed medical planning.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>SPIKES is the standard protocol for delivering bad news (Setting, Perception, Invitation, Knowledge, Empathy, Summarize/Strategize).<br /></br>1. You have given the Knowledge (“positive HIV test”).<br /></br>2. You offered Empathy.<br /></br>3. Next step before strategy or logistics is to explore the patient’s emotional response—eg, “Tell me what worries you most right now?”  </p></br><p>Failing to address emotion first is a common pitfall that leads to the patient’s not absorbing additional information. Therefore the immediate focus should be on his fears and concerns about the diagnosis.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>N/A for this communication-skills question, but recall: acute/early HIV can present with very high viral load despite preserved CD4 count; guideline-directed therapy is immediate ART. These details will be discussed in future visits after emotions are addressed.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>First-line ART today is typically a two-nucleoside backbone (eg, tenofovir-emtricitabine) plus an integrase inhibitor (eg, dolutegravir or bictegravir). Baseline labs (HLA-B*57:01, viral genotype, hepatitis B serology, CMP, CBC, fasting lipids, pregnancy test in females) are obtained before or at initiation. However, this conversation should take place only after the patient is ready to process information.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Financial concerns about treatment – Important, but logistics ≈ “strategy”; comes after emotions are addressed.<br /></br>b. How to tell family and friends – Also premature; patient must first process the news.<br /></br>c. Initiation of antiretroviral therapy – Clinically urgent but not the NEXT step in this encounter. Starting ART without first gauging understanding and emotions violates good communication practice.<br /></br>e. Possible stigma – A specific concern that might arise, but you must first ask what HIS concerns actually are; otherwise you risk imposing issues that may not be foremost in his mind.  </p></br><p>Take-home Message<br /></br>When delivering bad news, follow the SPIKES framework: after conveying the information and expressing empathy, explore the patient’s feelings and fears before moving on to treatment details or practical matters.</p>
134. A 37-year-old man comes to the office for a routine follow-up examination. He has a 7-year history of bipolar disorder, most recent episode depressed, that has been well controlled for 18 months with lithium carbonate, valproic acid, fluoxetine, propranolol, and levothyroxine. He had two manic episodes, 7 and 2 years ago, that resulted in psychiatric hospitalizations. The episodes were preceded and followed by 6 months of depression. Physical examination today shows no abnormalities. On mental status examination his mood is normal. He says that he feels fine and would like to discontinue all or some of his medications. Discontinuation of which of the</br>following medications is most likely to increase this patient's risk for suicide?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Fluoxetine</br>b. Lithium carbonate</br>c. Propranolol</br>d. Thyroxine</br>e. Valproic acid</br></br></br><p>Correct answer selection<br /></br>b. Lithium carbonate  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette analysis<br /></br>• Chronic bipolar I disorder (two prior manic episodes, inter-episode depression).<br /></br>• Stable for the past 18 months on a multidrug maintenance regimen.<br /></br>• Patient now feels well and wants to “stop some pills.”<br /></br>Key phrase for the test-taker: “Which discontinuation is most likely to increase this patient’s risk for suicide?” → Think of the one mood-stabilizing agent with a well-documented, unique anti-suicidal effect.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical critical reasoning<br /></br>1. Bipolar patients are at markedly elevated lifetime risk of suicide, especially during depressive episodes or shortly after abrupt medication changes.<br /></br>2. Of all psychotropic agents, lithium is the only one repeatedly shown (meta-analyses, naturalistic studies, RCTs) to cut suicide deaths and attempts by ≥60 %.<br /></br>3. Stopping lithium—particularly abruptly—carries a “rebound” effect: rapid relapse into depression or mixed states with a sharp spike in suicidal behavior within the first few months.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Lithium has specific anti-suicide properties independent of its mood-stabilizing effect; removing it carries the highest suicide risk.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology review (concise)<br /></br>• Mechanism (uncertain): lithium modulates second-messenger systems (↓ inositol monophosphatase, ↓ protein kinase C activity) leading to stabilization of neuronal firing, but its anti-suicidal effect is thought to involve serotonergic up-regulation and reduction in impulsivity/aggression.<br /></br>• Protective effect is dose-dependent; therapeutic serum level 0.6–1.2 mEq/L.<br /></br>• Sudden withdrawal → neurochemical “rebound” changes, rapid mood destabilization, and increased impulsivity → higher suicide risk.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; monitoring pearls<br /></br>First-line long-term management of bipolar disorder with demonstrated suicide-prevention benefit = lithium.<br /></br>Monitoring: baseline/periodic creatinine (nephrotoxicity), TSH (hypothyroidism—hence this patient’s levothyroxine), calcium, pregnancy test if applicable.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the other options are incorrect<br /></br>a. Fluoxetine (SSRI)<br /></br>• Helps bipolar depression but does NOT have the robust anti-suicide data lithium has.<br /></br>• Abrupt discontinuation can cause discontinuation syndrome (flu-like, insomnia, imbalance) and potential relapse of depression, but the suicide-protection evidence is far weaker than for lithium.<br /></br>• Moreover, chronic antidepressant monotherapy in bipolar patients may precipitate mania rather than reliably prevent suicide.</p></br><p>c. Propranolol<br /></br>• Used here for lithium-induced tremor or migraine prophylaxis.<br /></br>• No proven impact on suicide risk; discontinuation may cause β-blocker rebound hypertension/tachycardia, not mood destabilization.</p></br><p>d. Levothyroxine<br /></br>• Treats lithium-induced hypothyroidism.<br /></br>• Stopping it could worsen fatigue and depressive symptoms over time but does not carry the acute and well-documented spike in suicide seen with lithium cessation.</p></br><p>e. Valproic acid<br /></br>• Effective mood stabilizer, especially for mania and mixed states, but studies show it does NOT lower suicide risk to the same extent as lithium.<br /></br>• Discontinuation increases relapse risk, yet the magnitude of suicide-protection loss is smaller and slower than with lithium withdrawal.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Exam tip<br /></br>When you see a bipolar patient and the question involves “suicide prevention,” default to lithium unless a contraindication (CKD, pregnancy, etc.) is explicitly stated.</p>
135. A 14-year-old boy is brought to the emergency department 3 weeks after stepping on a nail. He was wearing tennis shoes at the time of the injury. His mother treated the puncture wound by cleansing it with soap and water. Initially the pain subsided, but it has returned and worsened over the past 3 days; he now refuses to bear weight on the foot. He is afebrile. Examination shows redness around the puncture site; it is tender to palpation. An x-ray of the foot shows an area of radiolucency with subperiosteal new bone formation of the metatarsal bone. Which of the following is the most likely causal organism?	Correct answer: F</br></br>a. Burkholderia cepacia</br>b. Eikenella corrodens</br>c. Group A J3-hemolytic streptococcus</br>d. Haemophilus influenzae</br>e. Pasteurella multocida</br>F. Pseudomonas aeruginosa</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>F. Pseudomonas aeruginosa  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Stepped on a nail … wearing tennis shoes”: Penetrating trauma through a rubber-soled athletic shoe is the classic setup for Pseudomonas infection.<br /></br>• “Pain returned and worsened … refuses to bear weight” + “x-ray … radiolucency with subperiosteal new bone formation”: Radiographic description of subacute osteomyelitis.<br /></br>Key pattern: Puncture wound through sneaker → Pseudomonal osteomyelitis of the foot.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Penetrating injury introduces bacteria deep to periosteum.<br /></br>2. Warm, moist environment inside sneakers favors P. aeruginosa growth.<br /></br>3. Osteomyelitis typically becomes symptomatic 2–3 weeks after inoculation (fits the timeline).<br /></br>Take-home: On exams, nail-through-tennis-shoe + osteomyelitis = Pseudomonas aeruginosa.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• P. aeruginosa is an oxidase-positive, non–lactose-fermenting, Gram-negative rod; produces pigments (pyocyanin, pyoverdin) and thrives in moist environments.<br /></br>• Virulence factors: Exotoxin A (inhibits EF-2), endotoxin (fever, shock), biofilm formation.<br /></br>• Causes hot-tub folliculitis, malignant otitis externa, ventilator pneumonia, burn-wound sepsis, and osteomyelitis after puncture wounds.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>• Diagnosis: MRI is most sensitive for early osteomyelitis; bone biopsy for culture and sensitivities.<br /></br>• Empiric therapy: IV antipseudomonal coverage—e.g., ciprofloxacin or levofloxacin, ceftazidime, cefepime, or piperacillin-tazobactam ± aminoglycoside. Duration ≈ 4–6 weeks; surgical debridement if necrotic bone.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>A. Burkholderia cepacia<br /></br>– Opportunistic lung infections in cystic fibrosis or chronic granulomatous disease; not linked to footwear or puncture wounds.</p></br><p>B. Eikenella corrodens<br /></br>– Part of human oral flora; causes cellulitis or osteomyelitis after human bite wounds (“clenched-fist injury”). No relation to nail through shoe.</p></br><p>C. Group A β-hemolytic Streptococcus (Strep pyogenes)<br /></br>– Causes cellulitis, erysipelas, necrotizing fasciitis, and hematogenous osteomyelitis, but classic trauma-through-sneaker association is absent.</p></br><p>D. Haemophilus influenzae<br /></br>– Encapsulated strains cause epiglottitis, meningitis, septic arthritis in young children; osteomyelitis rare today with vaccination.</p></br><p>E. Pasteurella multocida<br /></br>– Rapid-onset soft-tissue infection after cat/dog bites or scratches. No association with puncture wounds through shoes.</p></br><p>Core Concept<br /></br>Puncture wound through a rubber-soled shoe that progresses to osteomyelitis almost always points to Pseudomonas aeruginosa; recognize and treat with an appropriate IV antipseudomonal agent.</p>
136. A 42-year-old primigravid woman at 34 weeks' gestation is brought to the emergency department because of a 4-hour history of decreased fetal movement. Pregnancy has been complicated by gestational diabetes</br>treated with insulin. She has no history of serious illness or other pregnancy complications. She is 160 cm 5 ft 3 in tall. She weighed 82 kg 180 lb prior to her pregnancy; BMI was 32 kg/m 2. She has had an 18-kg 40-</br>lb weight gain during her pregnancy. Her temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 70/min, and blood pressure is 130/70 mm Hg. The cervix is not dilated. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Her</br>fingerstick blood glucose concentration is 110 mg/dl. A fetal heart tracing is shown. This pattern is unchanged during the next hour. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Bed rest in the left lateral decubitus position</br>b. Induction of labor</br>c. Intravenous insulin drip</br>d. Amnioinfusion</br>e. Cesarean delivery</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Bed rest in the left-lateral decubitus position  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “4-hour history of decreased fetal movement” → prompts an immediate assessment of fetal well-being (→ electronic fetal monitoring).<br /></br>• “Fetal heart tracing is shown. This pattern is unchanged during the next hour.” → we are dealing with a non-reassuring but not frankly ominous tracing. The stem does not supply the image, but the only first-line intervention among the options that can plausibly correct most indeterminate (category II) tracings is maternal repositioning.<br /></br>• No fever, bleeding, ruptured membranes, or uterine tachysystole → rules out other causes (e.g., chorioamnionitis, placental abruption, hyperstimulation).<br /></br>• Gestational diabetes is well controlled (finger-stick 110 mg/dL) → hyper-/hypoglycemia is not the culprit; insulin drip is unnecessary.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise management of a category II fetal heart rate (FHR) pattern:<br /></br>1. Correct reversible, maternal causes first:<br /></br>   • Left-lateral positioning (relieves aortocaval compression and improves uteroplacental perfusion)<br /></br>   • Maternal oxygen, IV fluids, d/c oxytocin if running, treat hypotension, etc.<br /></br>2. If the pattern resolves → expectant management.<br /></br>3. If it persists or deteriorates to a category III tracing → move to invasive measures (amnioinfusion for persistent variable decelerations, operative delivery, or emergency cesarean depending on the etiology and gestational age).  </p></br><p>Take-home message: For indeterminate FHR patterns, ALWAYS try maternal repositioning (left lateral) first; escalate only if the tracing fails to improve.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• A gravid uterus can compress the inferior vena cava and aorta when the patient lies supine (“supine hypotension syndrome”). Decreased maternal venous return → ↓ uteroplacental blood flow → fetal hypoxemia → variable or late decelerations and decreased fetal movement.<br /></br>• Simply turning the mother onto her side removes the uterine pressure on great vessels, improving placental perfusion within minutes.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>First-line (non-invasive) for non-reassuring FHR patterns<br /></br>• Maternal repositioning (left-lateral, right-lateral, knee-chest)<br /></br>• IV bolus of lactated Ringer’s<br /></br>• Maternal oxygen (10 L/min via non-rebreather)<br /></br>• Discontinue uterotonics, treat hypotension, correct tachysystole with terbutaline if needed.  </p></br><p>Escalation (if unresolved)<br /></br>• Amnioinfusion (saline via intrauterine pressure catheter) for persistent variable decelerations due to cord compression, usually in the setting of oligohydramnios.<br /></br>• Operative vaginal delivery or emergent cesarean if category III tracing persists despite corrective measures.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Induction of labor – At 34 weeks with a closed cervix and a potentially correctable tracing, induction is premature and would expose the preterm fetus to unnecessary risks.<br /></br>c. Intravenous insulin drip – Blood glucose is 110 mg/dL (normal target 60–120 mg/dL in GDMA1/2). The tracing abnormality is unrelated to maternal glycemia.<br /></br>d. Amnioinfusion – Appropriate only for persistent, recurrent variable decelerations that fail to resolve with simple measures. It is second-line after maternal repositioning.<br /></br>e. Cesarean delivery – Reserved for a persistent category III pattern (absent variability with recurrent late decels, bradycardia, or sinusoidal rhythm) that does not respond to intrauterine resuscitation. The stem describes a less severe, potentially reversible pattern.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Jumping straight to invasive interventions (amnioinfusion, induction, cesarean) without first attempting simple, low-risk corrective maneuvers (maternal repositioning, IV fluids, oxygen).</p>
137. A previously healthy 62-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 2-day history of severe right orbital headache. She takes no medications. She does not smoke cigarettes or drink alcohol. Her blood pressure</br>is 170/90 mm Hg. Examination shows a supple neck. Visual acuity is 20/40 in the left eye and 20/60 in the right eye. The cup:disc ratio is 7:10 in the left eye and 9:10 in the right eye. Neurologic examination shows no</br>other focal findings. Her erythrocyte sedimentation rate is 15 mm/h. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Acute glaucoma</br>b. Cluster headache</br>c. Migraine</br>d. Pseudotumor cerebri</br>e. Temporal arteritis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>A. Acute (angle-closure) glaucoma  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “62-year-old” – incidence of glaucoma rises with age.<br /></br>• “Severe right orbital headache for 2 days” – acute unilateral ocular pain is classic for angle-closure glaucoma.<br /></br>• “Visual acuity 20/60 (↓) in the right eye” – acute rise in intraocular pressure (IOP) impairs vision.<br /></br>• “Cup : disc ratio 9/10 (right), 7/10 (left)” – physiologic ratio should be ≤0.5; marked cupping indicates glaucomatous optic-nerve damage.<br /></br>• “Supple neck” rules out meningitis/subarachnoid irritation; “ESR 15 mm/h (normal)” argues strongly against temporal arteritis.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Key pattern: Older patient + abrupt unilateral ocular pain/headache + decreased vision + markedly enlarged cup:disc ratio → think angle-closure glaucoma. The question focuses on recognizing funduscopic evidence (optic-nerve cupping) rather than corneal haze or fixed pupil, which may not always be provided. The normal ESR eliminates giant-cell arteritis; absence of papilledema eliminates pseudotumor cerebri.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Angle-closure glaucoma = sudden obstruction of aqueous humor outflow (closure of trabecular meshwork by the peripheral iris). Rapid IOP rise → ischemia of optic nerve head → ganglion-cell axon loss → enlarged optic cup. Untreated, vision loss becomes permanent within hours–days.</p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Emergent IOP-lowering:<br /></br>1. Topical timolol + apraclonidine + pilocarpine (miotic)<br /></br>2. IV/PO acetazolamide or IV mannitol<br /></br>Definitive: Laser peripheral iridotomy in affected and fellow eye.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Cluster headache – Unilateral periorbital pain with lacrimation/rhinorrhea; normal vision and normal fundus (no cupping). More common in middle-aged men.<br /></br>C. Migraine – Pulsatile headache ± aura, photophobia, nausea; fundus typically normal. Head pain not specifically orbital, no optic-nerve cupping.<br /></br>D. Pseudotumor cerebri (idiopathic intracranial hypertension) – Young obese women; bilateral papilledema (swelling, not cupping), normal cup:disc ratio, elevated opening pressure on LP.<br /></br>E. Temporal arteritis – Elderly with temporal region tenderness, jaw claudication, markedly elevated ESR (&gt;50 mm/h). Vision loss results from ischemic optic neuropathy, not disc cupping.</p></br><p>Take-home Message<br /></br>A markedly increased cup:disc ratio signifies glaucomatous optic-nerve damage. Sudden unilateral ocular pain and visual loss in an older patient = acute (angle-closure) glaucoma, a vision-threatening emergency requiring immediate IOP reduction.</p>
138. A 27-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a progressive rash and easy bruising during the past 2 weeks. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. She enjoys horseback riding and first</br>noticed the rash on her legs when she took off her riding boots. She initially believed it was caused by the long time spent horseback riding that day. Her pulse is 62/min, respirations are 12/min, and blood pressure is</br>113/77 mm Hg. Examination shows a blood-filled blister on the right buccal mucosa. There is a petechial rash over both shins and dorsum of the feet. Laboratory studies show a platelet count of 9000/mm 3. Prothrombin time</br>is 12 sec INR=1.1 and partial thromboplastin time is 29 sec. Results of thyroid, liver, and renal function tests are within the reference ranges. A blood smear shows decreased platelets. Which of the following is the most</br>appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Observation only</br>b. Cyclosporine therapy</br>c. Prednisone therapy</br>d. Platelet transfusion</br>e. Splenectomy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>Observation only  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Isolated thrombocytopenia: platelet count 9000/mm³, normal PT/INR and PTT → points to a peripheral platelet-destructive process rather than a bone-marrow production problem or a coagulation-factor disorder.<br /></br>• No medications, no systemic illness, normal thyroid/liver/kidney tests → eliminates drug-induced thrombocytopenia, DIC, TTP, liver disease, uremia, etc.<br /></br>• Petechiae, mucosal blood blister, otherwise well → classic bleeding pattern of quantitative platelet disorders (ITP).<br /></br>• Young adult with an acute 2-week history → suggests acute, possibly self-limited immune thrombocytopenia rather than chronic ITP.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>ITP in adults is stratified by two things:<br /></br>1. Severity of thrombocytopenia.<br /></br>2. Severity of bleeding.  </p></br><p>General treatment thresholds (tested on NBME exams):<br /></br>• Platelet count &lt; 20–30 k OR clinically significant bleeding → treat (steroids, IVIG, anti-D).<br /></br>• Platelet count &gt; 20–30 k AND only minor cutaneous bleeding → observation.  </p></br><p>In this vignette:<br /></br>– Count is 9000 (severe) but bleeding is limited to petechiae and a single oral blood-filled blister. NBME questions often label this degree of mucocutaneous bleeding as “minor” (ie, not visceral, GI, GU, or intracranial).<br /></br>– Therefore the board-style teaching point being tested is: “Do not overtreat isolated mild mucocutaneous bleeding—observe first.”  </p></br><p>Take-home core concept: The presence of severe thrombocytopenia alone isn’t enough; treatment is reserved for life-threatening or organ-threatening bleeding, or for platelet counts &lt; 20 k with more than minor cutaneous manifestations.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Immune thrombocytopenia (ITP)<br /></br>• Etiology: IgG auto-antibodies (usually against GpIIb/IIIa) → opsonization → splenic macrophage clearance.<br /></br>• Can be post-viral, post-vaccination, or idiopathic.<br /></br>• Acute form more common in children and young adults; often resolves spontaneously within weeks to months.<br /></br>Clinical manifestations<br /></br>• Platelet-type bleeding: petechiae, purpura, ecchymoses, mucosal bleeding, epistaxis.<br /></br>Laboratory findings<br /></br>• Isolated low platelets; normal WBC/RBC, normal PT/PTT, normal smear except ↓ platelets.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>First-line if treatment is indicated<br /></br>• Corticosteroids (prednisone 1 mg/kg/day).<br /></br>• IVIG (especially if need rapid rise).<br /></br>• Anti-D immune globulin (Rh-positive, non-splenectomized pts).<br /></br>Second-line / refractory<br /></br>• Rituximab, thrombopoietin receptor agonists (eltrombopag, romiplostim), splenectomy.<br /></br>Not routinely helpful<br /></br>• Platelet transfusion (only for life-threatening hemorrhage because transfused platelets are rapidly destroyed).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Cyclosporine therapy – Calcineurin inhibitor used in severe refractory ITP, aplastic anemia, and transplant rejection prophylaxis. Not first-line for new ITP.<br /></br>c. Prednisone therapy – Standard initial therapy when treatment is required, but NBME is asking you to recognize that, with only minor mucocutaneous bleeding, observation is preferred first.<br /></br>d. Platelet transfusion – Transfused platelets are destroyed rapidly in ITP; reserved for active serious bleeding (eg, intracranial, GI) together with IVIG/steroids.<br /></br>e. Splenectomy – Definitive second-line therapy reserved for chronic or refractory ITP (&gt; 12 mo) or when medical therapy fails.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Students see “platelets 9000” and reflexively pick steroids. NBME loves to test nuance: treat the patient, not just the number. Absent serious bleeding, acute ITP—particularly in younger patients—can be safely observed because many cases resolve spontaneously.</p>
139. A 69-year-old man comes to the clinic for a routine examination. He says he feels well. He has hypertension, hypercholesterolemia, and osteoarthritis. His medications are lisinopril, hydrochlorothiazide, and lovastatin. He smoked two packs of cigarettes daily for 30 years but quit 8 years ago. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate screening test to decrease</br>this patient's risk for a cardiovascular event?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Abdominal ultrasonograph</br>b. Determination of ankle brachia! indices</br>c. ECG</br>d. Echocardiography</br>e. Exercise stress test</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>• Abdominal ultrasonography (one-time screening for abdominal aortic aneurysm)</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 69-year-old man (age 65–75)<br /></br>• Male who “ever smoked” (≥100 cigarettes in his lifetime; here 60-pack-year history)<br /></br>• Currently asymptomatic, routine visit</p></br><p>These two bolded points (male age 65–75 + history of smoking) exactly match the USPSTF recommendation for a ONE-TIME abdominal duplex ultrasound to screen for abdominal aortic aneurysm (AAA).</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Stepwise thought process:<br /></br>1. Ask: “What screening tests have high-quality evidence to improve outcomes in an asymptomatic smoker age ≥65?”<br /></br>2. AAA rupture accounts for substantial sudden cardiovascular mortality in older men; ultrasound screening followed by elective repair lowers mortality.<br /></br>3. No other listed tests (ABI, resting ECG, echo, stress test) have evidence that population screening (without symptoms) improves cardiovascular outcomes.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: The only USPSTF grade-B cardiovascular screening test uniquely indicated for an asymptomatic male smoker in this age range is a one-time abdominal ultrasound for AAA.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Abdominal aortic aneurysm:<br /></br>• Etiology/Risk factors – Age ≥65, male sex, smoking (strongest modifiable), hypertension, atherosclerosis.<br /></br>• Mechanism – Chronic transmural inflammation and proteolytic degradation of the aortic wall → dilation.<br /></br>• Clinical manifestations – Usually silent until expansion or catastrophic rupture (high mortality).<br /></br>• Screening – Duplex abdominal ultrasound: highly sensitive &amp; specific, non-invasive, no radiation.<br /></br>• Management – Elective repair if diameter ≥5.5 cm or rapid growth; risk reduction with smoking cessation, statins, BP control.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>If the aneurysm is detected:<br /></br>• Surveillance imaging interval based on size (e.g., yearly if 3.0–3.9 cm, q6 mos if 4.0–5.4 cm).<br /></br>• Elective repair (open or EVAR) once criteria met.<br /></br>• Medical optimization (aggressive HTN &amp; lipid control, smoking cessation).</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><ol></br><li></br><p>Determination of ankle-brachial indices (ABI)<br /></br>   • Detects peripheral arterial disease.<br /></br>   • USPSTF: Insufficient evidence (grade I) for screening asymptomatic adults; does NOT demonstrate reduction in cardiovascular events when used for population screening.</p></br></li></br><li></br><p>Resting ECG<br /></br>   • USPSTF: Grade D for low-risk asymptomatic adults; grade I for higher risk. No proven mortality benefit; leads to false positives and unnecessary procedures.</p></br></li></br><li></br><p>Echocardiography<br /></br>   • Used to evaluate symptomatic murmurs, heart failure, structural disease, thoracic aortic aneurysm—not recommended as a screening tool for asymptomatic individuals.</p></br></li></br><li></br><p>Exercise stress test<br /></br>   • Reserved for symptomatic patients or those with high cardiac risk who are about to start vigorous exercise programs or as pre-operative risk stratification. Routine screening increases false positives without reducing MI or mortality.</p></br></li></br></ol></br><p>Key distinguishing point: Only AAA ultrasound has a clear, guideline-endorsed mortality benefit in this specific demographic.</p>
140. A previously healthy 37-year-old man comes to the office because of a 4-week history of intermittent difficulty swallowing solids and liquids. The patient describes a sensation of something lodged in the center of his chest and says that the sensation is often painful. He has not had any other symptoms. He takes no medications. Examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Esophageal carcinoma</br>b. Esophageal spasm</br>c. Gastroesophageal reflux disease</br>d. Hiatal hernia</br>e. Pharyngoesophageal Zenker diverticulum</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Esophageal spasm  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Intermittent difficulty swallowing solids and liquids” – Simultaneous involvement of both food consistencies points away from a fixed mechanical obstruction (e.g., carcinoma, stricture, Schatzki ring), which would begin with solids only and progress to liquids.<br /></br>• “4-week history … sensation often painful” – Esophageal chest pain that comes and goes fits a motility disorder; pain is classically described in diffuse esophageal spasm (DES).<br /></br>• “Previously healthy 37-year-old” – Cancer risk is low at this age; DES can occur in younger adults.<br /></br>• “No other symptoms … examination normal” – No weight loss, no reflux complaints, no halitosis or regurgitation of undigested food—further narrowing toward a motility problem.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Dysphagia pattern → solids + liquids from the outset = motility disorder.<br /></br>Step 2: Intermittent course with chest pain → diffuse esophageal spasm (corkscrew contractions).<br /></br>Core takeaway: Initial dysphagia to both solids and liquids = think motility (achalasia, DES, scleroderma). Intermittent, painful episodes without chronic progressive course favors DES.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Diffuse esophageal spasm is due to uncoordinated, high-amplitude, simultaneous contractions of the esophageal body while LES function remains intermittently normal. The exact cause is unknown but may involve neural dysfunction of inhibitory (nitric-oxide–mediated) pathways. Clinically it produces:<br /></br>• Intermittent dysphagia to both solids and liquids<br /></br>• Substernal, occasionally crushing chest pain that can mimic angina<br /></br>• Symptoms often precipitated by hot/cold liquids or stress  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Diagnostic work-up:<br /></br>1. Barium swallow → corkscrew or rosary-bead esophagus.<br /></br>2. Esophageal manometry (gold standard) → intermittent, simultaneous, repetitive high-pressure contractions.<br /></br>3. Endoscopy usually normal (done to rule out structural disease).  </p></br><p>Treatment:<br /></br>• Smooth-muscle relaxants before meals (e.g., nifedipine, diltiazem)<br /></br>• Sublingual nitroglycerin for acute pain<br /></br>• PDE-5 inhibitors (sildenafil) or botulinum toxin injections in refractory cases<br /></br>• Rarely, surgical long esophagomyotomy  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Esophageal carcinoma<br /></br>– Typical presentation: progressive dysphagia (solids → liquids), weight loss, age &gt;50, long-standing GERD or smoking/alcohol risk factors. The vignette’s intermittent course and equal difficulty with solids and liquids argue strongly against carcinoma.  </p></br><p>c. Gastroesophageal reflux disease<br /></br>– Produces heartburn, regurgitation, nocturnal cough. Dysphagia is usually late and mild when present; pain is burning/acidic rather than spasmodic chest pain.  </p></br><p>d. Hiatal hernia<br /></br>– Sliding hernia commonly causes GERD symptoms; paraesophageal hernia causes post-prandial pain and possible obstruction but typically presents with chronic reflux or anemia, not isolated intermittent dysphagia to both solids and liquids.  </p></br><p>e. Pharyngoesophageal (Zenker) diverticulum<br /></br>– Characterized by regurgitation of undigested food, halitosis, chronic cough, aspiration, and muffled gurgling in the neck. None are described here; moreover, Zenker causes oropharyngeal (transfer) dysphagia, not substernal chest pain.  </p></br><p>Bottom Line: Initial, intermittent dysphagia to both solids and liquids coupled with chest pain in a young adult is classic for diffuse esophageal spasm—a motility disorder, not a structural blockage.</p>
141. An 8-year-old girl is brought to the physician because of a 2-week history of vaginal discharge and itching. She has no history of serious illness and receives no medications. Vital signs are within normal limits. Pelvic examination shows erythema of the perineum and thin, white vaginal discharge. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Wet mount preparation of the discharge shows epithelial cells and a small number of leukocytes. Addition of KOH preparation to the discharge produces no odor. Which of the following is the most likely explanation for these findings?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Candidiasis</br>b. Foreign body in the vagina</br>c. Herpes simplex</br>d. Nonspecific vulvovaginitis</br>e. Trichomoniasis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>d.
