Nonspecific vulvovaginitis (also called nonspecific/irritant vulvitis or physiologic leukorrhea)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis (Key Words/Phrases)</br>• Age: 8-year-old prepubertal girl (low estrogen environment)<br /></br>• Symptoms: 2-week history of thin white vaginal discharge + itching, mild erythema<br /></br>• Wet mount: only epithelial cells and a “small number of leukocytes” (no organisms)<br /></br>• KOH whiff test: no fishy odor<br /></br>These findings point toward a benign, noninfectious irritation that is extremely common in prepubertal girls.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning &amp; Take-Home</br>1. Prepubertal girls have thin, poorly estrogenized vaginal epithelium, minimal labial fat, and a relatively alkaline vaginal pH → very susceptible to mild chemical or mechanical irritation (bubble baths, tight clothing, poor hygiene, wiping back-to-front).<br /></br>2. Microscopy shows scant or no inflammatory cells and no pathogens.<br /></br>3. The absence of odor (rules against BV), absence of budding yeast (rules against Candida), and absence of motile organisms (rules against Trichomonas) clinch the diagnosis.<br /></br>Core concept: A non-sexually active prepubertal girl with mild discharge + few/no leukocytes + no organisms = nonspecific vulvovaginitis.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Etiology: Local irritants (soaps, tight synthetic underwear), poor perineal hygiene, transient changes in vaginal flora.<br /></br>• Low estrogen → thin mucosa &amp; decreased lactobacilli → less acidic environment → overgrowth of normal skin flora or mild inflammation without a dominant pathogen.<br /></br>• Clinical manifestations: Mild pruritus/erythema, thin mucoid or watery white/clear discharge, usually no systemic symptoms.</p></br><p>Management</br>1. Reassurance that this is common and benign.<br /></br>2. Eliminate irritants: cotton underwear, no bubble baths/scented soaps, wipe front-to-back.<br /></br>3. Sitz baths with lukewarm water; topical petrolatum if needed for irritation.<br /></br>4. No antibiotics or antifungals unless a specific pathogen is later identified.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Candidiasis<br /></br>• Requires estrogenized vagina; uncommon before menarche.<br /></br>• Thick “cottage cheese” discharge, intense pruritus, many budding yeast/pseudohyphae on KOH, abundant PMNs. None seen here.</p></br><p>b. Foreign body in the vagina<br /></br>• Causes foul-smelling, often bloody or purulent discharge and marked leukocytosis on wet mount. May see toilet paper fragments on exam. This patient’s discharge is thin/white, non-malodorous.</p></br><p>c. Herpes simplex<br /></br>• Painful vesicles/ulcers, dysuria, systemic prodrome possible. Wet mount/Tzanck shows multinucleated giant cells. Not present here.</p></br><p>e. Trichomoniasis<br /></br>• Sexually transmitted (virtually never in an 8-year-old without abuse). Green/yellow frothy discharge, “strawberry cervix,” many motile trichomonads and PMNs on saline wet mount, sometimes positive whiff test. None of these findings are present.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall</br>Students often reflexively pick Candida for “vaginal itching,” forgetting that candidal vulvovaginitis is rare in estrogen-deficient prepubertal girls and produces a very different microscopic picture.</p>
142. Four days after undergoing open reduction and internal fixation of an intertrochanteric fracture of the right hip sustained in a fall, a 72-year-old woman has the sudden onset of fever and rigors. A central venous catheter had been placed preoperatively in the ri ht internal ju ular vein for vascular access. She received perioperative cefazolin for 24 hours. Her temperature is 39.2 C 102.6 F, pulse is 104/min, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 80/50 mm Hg. Examination shows a well-healing incision. There are no localizing signs of infection. Which of the following is most likely to have prevented these findings?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Preoperative chest physiotherapy</br>b. Appropriate barrier precautions during catheter insertion</br>c. Continued cefazolin prophylaxis after 24 hours</br>d. Perioperative J3-adrenergic blocking agent therapy</br>e. Epidural anesthesia</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Appropriate barrier precautions during catheter insertion  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Central venous catheter … placed pre-operatively” – indwelling CVC is the major modifiable source of nosocomial bloodstream infection in the first postoperative week.<br /></br>• “Sudden onset of fever and rigors … BP 80/50 mm Hg” – abrupt high-grade bacteremia with septic shock picture, classic for catheter-related bloodstream infection (CRBSI).<br /></br>• “No localizing signs of infection” – argues against wound infection, pneumonia, UTI, etc., further implicating the line.<br /></br>Key pattern: Post-op day 3-5 patient with central line + high fever/rigors + hypotension → think CLABSI.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Stepwise thought process:<br /></br>1. Identify potential infectious sources in early postop period (wind, water, wound, walk, wonder drugs, and “wires” = lines).<br /></br>2. Evaluate timing: Day 4 makes wound infection possible but incision looks fine; pneumonia/UTI usually present with focal findings; antibiotics already cover skin flora.<br /></br>3. Abrupt sepsis without focus strongly implicates the “wire.”<br /></br>Take-home: Strict insertion technique (full sterile barrier, chlorhexidine prep, sterile drape, mask, cap, sterile gown, sterile gloves) is the single most effective preventive measure for CLABSI.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Skin flora (S. aureus, coagulase-negative staph), Gram-negatives, Candida can migrate extraluminally or intraluminally along the catheter → bacteremia/sepsis.<br /></br>• Risk factors: Prolonged catheter dwell time, femoral site, multilumen catheters, TPN, breaks in sterile technique at insertion or during maintenance.<br /></br>• Mechanism: Biofilm formation on catheter surface → protected bacterial colony → intermittent shedding → fever/rigors; severe cases → systemic inflammatory response → vasodilation → hypotension.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>• Diagnosis: Blood cultures (peripheral and catheter), catheter removal with culture of catheter tip (&gt;15 CFU by roll-plate).<br /></br>• Management: Prompt empiric IV vancomycin + Gram-negative coverage (e.g., cefepime) ± antifungal; remove line if severely septic or tunnel infection.<br /></br>• Prevention: Full barrier precautions at insertion, chlorhexidine skin prep, ultrasound-guided insertion to minimize attempts, daily assessment for need, hand hygiene before accessing line.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Preoperative chest physiotherapy<br /></br>   – Prevents postoperative pulmonary complications (atelectasis, pneumonia) but would not avert hematogenous sepsis from a catheter. No hypotension/rigors pattern.  </p></br><p>c. Continued cefazolin prophylaxis after 24 h<br /></br>   – Prolonged antibiotic prophylaxis does NOT reduce line infections; instead ↑ resistance &amp; C. difficile. Guidelines limit surgical prophylaxis to ≤24 h.  </p></br><p>d. Perioperative β-adrenergic–blocking therapy<br /></br>   – Used to blunt sympathetic response and lower cardiac complications, has no impact on infectious risk.  </p></br><p>e. Epidural anesthesia<br /></br>   – Decreases postoperative pain, ileus, and possibly DVT but not CRBSI risk.  </p></br><p>Core Takeaway<br /></br>In postoperative patients, sudden high fever and septic shock without a focal source, especially in the presence of a central venous catheter, should raise suspicion for CLABSI. Adhering to strict sterile barrier precautions at the time of catheter insertion is the most effective strategy to prevent these infections and their potentially fatal sequelae.</p>
143. A 17-year-old girl is brought to the physician by her mother because she is concerned that her daughter has never had a menstrual period. The mother reports that her own menarche occurred at the age of 13 years. The patient has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. She is in the 11th grade, receives grades of A's and B's, and sings in the school choir. She has never been sexua ly active. She is 173 cm 5 ft 8 in tall a nd wei hs 66 kg 145 lb; BMI is 22 k /m ri.. Her blood pressure is 100/60 mm Hg. Breast development is sexual maturity ratin SMR stage 5, and pubic hair development is SMR stage 2. Examination of the external genitalia shows no abnormalities. Insertion of a speculum into the vagina is difficult. The cervix cannot be visualized or palpated. The uterus is not palpable. Pelvic ultrasonography and MRI of the pelvis confirm the absence of a uterus and ovaries. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Karyotype analysis</br>b. MRI of the brain</br>c. Intravenous pyelography</br>d. Laparoscopy</br>e. Gonadectomy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Karyotype analysis  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Primary amenorrhea at age 17 (never menstruated).<br /></br>• Breast development Tanner (SMR) 5 → adequate estrogen exposure.<br /></br>• Sparse pubic/axillary hair (SMR 2) → decreased/ineffective androgen action.<br /></br>• Pelvic US + MRI: no uterus, no cervix, no ovaries → Müllerian structures absent; gonads not visualized.<br /></br>This classic triad (normal breasts, scant pubic hair, absent uterus/ovaries) points to complete androgen-insensitivity syndrome (AIS, “testicular feminization”).  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. In AIS the patient is genotypically 46,XY with intra-abdominal or inguinal testes.<br /></br>2. Sertoli cells secrete anti-Müllerian hormone → regression of uterus/cervix/upper vagina.<br /></br>3. Testosterone → aromatized to estradiol → normal breast development.<br /></br>4. Androgen-receptor defect → minimal pubic/axillary hair.<br /></br>Before making management decisions (e.g., timing of gonadectomy), one must document the genotype; thus, karyotype analysis is the most appropriate next step.<br /></br>Take-home: Primary amenorrhea + breasts present + no uterus = order a karyotype to distinguish AIS (46,XY) from Müllerian agenesis (46,XX).  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: X-linked mutation in androgen-receptor gene.<br /></br>• Phenotype: Female external genitalia, blind-ending vagina, absent/undescended testes, scant body hair.<br /></br>• Hormones: ↑ Testosterone (male range), ↑ LH, normal/↑ estradiol.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• Initial: Karyotype (and serum testosterone) to confirm AIS.<br /></br>• Definitive: Gonadectomy AFTER completion of puberty (to allow full breast growth) to prevent testicular germ-cell malignancy (seminoma, gonadoblastoma).<br /></br>• Vaginal lengthening procedures if desired for sexual activity.<br /></br>• Estrogen replacement if needed post-gonadectomy.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. MRI of the brain – Indicated when hypogonadotropic hypogonadism is suspected (low FSH/LH, absent secondary sex traits). This patient has normal secondary sex traits, pointing away from central lesions.<br /></br>c. Intravenous pyelography – Used to screen for renal anomalies in Müllerian agenesis (uterus absent, BUT pubic hair and ovaries present). Here ovaries are absent and pubic hair is sparse, making Müllerian agenesis unlikely.<br /></br>d. Laparoscopy – Could directly visualize intra-abdominal testes, but non-invasive karyotype (and serum hormones) is the appropriate first diagnostic study.<br /></br>e. Gonadectomy – Necessary in AIS but only after diagnosis is confirmed and after pubertal completion; not the immediate next step before confirming genotype.</p>
144. A 2-week-old male newborn is brought to the emergency department by his mother because of a 2-day history of sweating while feeding and decreased formula intake. He has not had fever, vomiting, diarrhea, or cough. He was born at term following an uncomplicated pregnancy and delivery. On arrival, he appears cyanotic and is in moderate respiratory distress. He is at the 30th percentile for length and 20th percentile for weight. Temperature is 37.2 C 99.0 F, pulse is 160/min, respirations are 80/min, and blood pressure is 80/50 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 80 percent. A rade 4/6, systolic ejection murmur is heard best at the middle and upper left sternal borders. Chest x-ray is shown. In addition to a displaced aorta, this patient most likely has which of the following sets of cardiac anomalies?	Correct answer: F</br></br>a. Atrial septal defect ASD and left ventricular outflow tract obstruction</br>b. ASD and right ventricular outflow tract obstruction</br>c. Patent ductus arteriosus PDA and left ventricular outflow tract obstruction</br>d. PDA and right ventricular outflow tract obstruction</br>e. Ventricular septal defect VSD and left ventricular outflow tract obstruction</br>F. VSD and right ventricular outflow tract obstruction</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>F. Ventricular septal defect (VSD) and right-ventricular outflow-tract obstruction (subpulmonic/pulmonary stenosis)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• 2-week-old with sweating while feeding &amp; progressive cyanosis → typical timing for a ductus-dependent or cyanotic congenital heart disease once the PDA begins to close.<br /></br>• Cyanosis (SaO2 80%), tachypnea (RR 80/min) &amp; grade 4/6 systolic EJECTION murmur at the mid- to upper LSB → classic for pulmonic/subpulmonic stenosis rather than a holosystolic VSD murmur.<br /></br>• “Displaced (overriding) aorta” on CXR + boot-shaped heart (RV hypertrophy) = buzz phrase for Tetralogy of Fallot (TOF).</p></br><p>Key pattern: newborn cyanosis + harsh systolic ejection murmur LSB + overriding aorta = think TOF.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Tetralogy of Fallot consists of four lesions:</br>1. Right-ventricular outflow-tract obstruction (pulmonary stenosis) → major determinant of cyanosis; causes the harsh systolic ejection murmur.</br>2. Malalignment VSD.</br>3. Overriding (displaced) aorta (already mentioned in the stem).</br>4. Right-ventricular hypertrophy (boot-shaped heart on CXR).</p></br><p>Since the stem explicitly tells you there is a displaced/overriding aorta, the remaining two hallmark anomalies you must pick are:</br>• VSD<br /></br>• Right-ventricular outflow-tract obstruction</p></br><p>That corresponds to answer choice F.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: The pathognomonic triad you must recall for TOF on exams is VSD + RV outflow obstruction (pulmonary stenosis) + overriding aorta (with RVH seen radiographically).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Etiology: Anterosuperior (anterocephalad) displacement of the infundibular (conal) septum during cardiac looping → malalignment VSD and narrowed RV outflow tract.<br /></br>• RVOT obstruction forces deoxygenated blood across the VSD into the overriding aorta → cyanosis. Severity of pulmonic stenosis dictates degree of hypoxemia.<br /></br>• RV pressure overload → RV hypertrophy → boot-shaped heart silhouette.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Management Snapshot (high-yield board points)</br>1. Acute “tet spell”: knee-chest positioning, 100% O₂, intranasal/intravenous β-blocker (propranolol), and/or morphine; increases SVR &amp; reduces R→L shunt.<br /></br>2. Keep ductus open (if severe obstruction) with IV prostaglandin E₁.<br /></br>3. Definitive: surgical repair (VSD closure + RVOT widening) typically in infancy.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</br>A. ASD + LV outflow obstruction<br /></br>– ASD does not produce a loud ejection murmur at LSB; LV outflow obstruction gives a systolic murmur at RUSB/apex, not mid-LSB. Not part of TOF.</p></br><p>B. ASD + RV outflow obstruction<br /></br>– Still lacks the obligatory VSD of TOF; ASD usually gives wide, fixed S₂ splitting rather than a harsh ejection murmur.</p></br><p>C. PDA + LV outflow obstruction<br /></br>– PDA produces a continuous “machine-like” murmur at the infraclavicular area, not a harsh ejection murmur LSB. LV outflow obstruction (e.g., aortic stenosis, coarctation) localizes to right upper sternal border or back.</p></br><p>D. PDA + RV outflow obstruction<br /></br>– Again PDA murmur is continuous; also does not explain overriding aorta or boot-shaped heart.</p></br><p>E. VSD + LV outflow obstruction<br /></br>– Simple VSD → holosystolic murmur, not ejection; LVOT obstruction murmur is heard at RUSB. Overriding aorta is not a feature. Combination does not match TOF pattern.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Confusing the ejection murmur of RVOT obstruction (pulmonary stenosis) with the holosystolic murmur of an isolated VSD. Remember: murmur quality + location are pivotal clues.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Take-Home Message</br>When you see a cyanotic neonate with an overriding aorta on imaging and a loud systolic ejection murmur at the left sternal border, immediately think Tetralogy of Fallot: VSD + right-ventricular outflow-tract obstruction are the other key lesions that must be present.</p>
145. A 14-year-old girl is brought to the physician by her parents because of an 8-month history of shaking of her hands that occurs when she tries to do things that require steadiness, such as writing, threading a needle, or doing art work. Her teachers have reported that her handwriting is sometimes difficult to read. The shaking is worse when the patient is stressed or tired, but she says she is not bothered by it. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. Her father also has mild shaking of the hands. The patient is 15 cm 5 ft 2 in tall and wei hs 43 kg 95 lb; BM I is 17 kg/m 2. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination of the eyes shows full ocular movements and mild end-gaze nystagmus. There is a fast tremor of the hands when the patient holds her arms outstretched. When the patient draws spirals and straight lines, the tremor causes waviness in the figures drawn. There is no dysmetria on finger-nose and heel-shin testing. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. DNA screening for spinocerebellar atrophy</br>b. Electromyography and nerve conduction studies</br>c. Measurement of serum ceruloplasmin concentration</br>d. MRI of the brain</br>e. No further diagnostic studies are indicated~-</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. No further diagnostic studies are indicated  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “8-month history of shaking … when she tries to do things that require steadiness” = action/postural tremor, not present at rest.<br /></br>• “Worse when stressed or tired” = classic feature of essential (familial) tremor.<br /></br>• “Father also has mild shaking” = autosomal-dominant inheritance common in essential tremor.<br /></br>• Normal neurologic exam except for fine, high-frequency tremor and mild end-gaze nystagmus; no dysmetria or other cerebellar signs → argues against spinocerebellar ataxias, Wilson disease, or cerebellar lesions.<br /></br>• Teenager who is “not bothered by it” and is otherwise healthy → benign process.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Pattern recognition: high-frequency, low-amplitude, symmetric postural/action tremor that improves with rest and is exacerbated by physiologic stress, with positive family history → essential (familial) tremor.<br /></br>Essential tremor is diagnosed clinically. No imaging or laboratory workup is recommended in a patient with a classic presentation and normal neurologic examination. The “next step” is reassurance ± discussion of symptomatic treatment (propranolol or primidone) only if the patient is bothered.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: When an otherwise healthy patient presents with a classic postural/action tremor and a family history, essential tremor is a clinical diagnosis—additional tests are unnecessary.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Likely abnormal oscillatory activity in cerebellothalamocortical circuit; 50–60 % familial (AD).<br /></br>• Manifestations: High-frequency (6–10 Hz) tremor of hands, head, or voice that is absent at rest, worse with movement, stress, caffeine; may temporarily improve with small amounts of alcohol.<br /></br>• Course: Slowly progressive but benign; disability is variable.  </p></br><p>Treatments (for symptomatic patients)<br /></br>1. Non-pharmacologic: Avoid caffeine, adequate sleep, ↓ stress.<br /></br>2. First-line drug: Propranolol (non-selective β-blocker).<br /></br>3. Alternate/second-line: Primidone, topiramate, gabapentin.<br /></br>4. Refractory: Botulinum toxin injections, deep-brain stimulation (VIM thalamus).  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. DNA screening for spinocerebellar atrophy<br /></br>   • Spinocerebellar ataxias present with progressive gait ataxia, dysarthria, oculomotor abnormalities, and appendicular dysmetria, not an isolated action tremor. No family history of ataxia and the exam lacks ataxic signs → not indicated.  </p></br><p>b. Electromyography and nerve conduction studies<br /></br>   • Useful for neuropathic or myopathic causes of weakness or peripheral tremor (e.g., orthostatic tremor). Essential tremor originates centrally; EMG/NCV would be normal and provide no diagnostic value.  </p></br><p>c. Measurement of serum ceruloplasmin concentration (Wilson disease)<br /></br>   • Wilson disease can cause tremor, but typically presents with dystonia, parkinsonism, dysarthria, psychiatric changes, liver disease, and Kayser-Fleischer rings. None are present here; family history of isolated tremor favors essential tremor over Wilson disease.  </p></br><p>d. MRI of the brain<br /></br>   • Indicated when focal neurologic deficits, cerebellar signs, or rapid progression suggest a structural lesion. Normal neuro exam except tremor makes imaging low yield.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Ordering a battery of tests for every tremor. Exam questions often reward recognizing essential tremor’s classic benign presentation and avoiding unnecessary work-ups.  </p></br><p>Bottom line: A typical, isolated, familial action tremor in a healthy adolescent requires no further diagnostic testing.</p>
146. An 11-year-old girl is brought to the office by her mother because of a 1-year history of behavioral issues at school. The mother reports her daughter has become more isolated and her grades have fallen from A's and B's to</br>C's. The mother is also concerned about her daughter's appearance. She has dyed her hair green and has an asymmetric haircut. The patient has no history of serious illness and receives no medications. When her mothe</br>leaves the room, the patient says she is tired of everyone at school. She feels that she is in the wrong body. She also hates her name and would prefer using a gender-neutral one. She states she has felt this way her</br>entire life but now feels that she has to say something. She has tried to talk with her parents about her feelings, but they dismiss her when she brings up her concerns. The patient says the few friends she has have become</br>different and interested in things she does not really like. She is also upset about her body changes and wants them to stop. Physical examination discloses no abnormalities. On mental status examination, she is fully</br>oriented and exhibits no deficits. This patient is at greatest risk for which of the following disorders?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Antisocial personality disorder</br>b. Body dysmorphic disorder</br>c. Dissociative identity disorder</br>d. Major depressive disorder</br>e. Schizoaffective disorder</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Major depressive disorder  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Feels that she is in the wrong body,” “hates her name,” “wants her body changes to stop” → classic language of gender dysphoria.<br /></br>• “More isolated, grades falling, few friends” → loss of social support/peer problems, a well-known precipitant of mood disorders in gender-dysphoric youth.<br /></br>• Parents “dismiss her concerns” → lack of family acceptance, another strong risk factor for depression and suicidality in transgender adolescents.<br /></br>• No psychotic symptoms, no identity fragmentation, no preoccupation with a slight physical flaw, no conduct-like behavior → rules out the other answer choices.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Core takeaway: Children and adolescents with gender dysphoria have markedly elevated rates of mood disorders—particularly major depressive disorder and suicidality—related to social rejection, bullying, and internal distress. Therefore the single greatest psychiatric risk in this scenario is major depressive disorder.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Gender dysphoria<br /></br>• Etiology: multifactorial (biologic influences, prenatal hormone milieu, genetic factors) + environmental stressors.<br /></br>• Distress stems from incongruence between experienced gender and assigned sex, amplified by pubertal body changes.<br /></br>• Consequences: chronic stress → HPA-axis activation, impaired social functioning → ↑ mood and anxiety disorders, self-harm, substance use. Depression prevalence in transgender/gender-dysphoric youth approaches 40–60%; suicide attempt rates 25–30%.  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Management of gender dysphoria (pediatric):<br /></br>1. Comprehensive mental-health evaluation.<br /></br>2. Affirming psychotherapy/family counseling.<br /></br>3. Puberty blockers (GnRH analogs such as leuprolide) at Tanner stage II–III to pause unwanted secondary sex characteristics.<br /></br>4. Later, gender-affirming hormone therapy (e.g., estradiol or testosterone) if persistent dysphoria.<br /></br>5. Screen at every visit for depression, anxiety, self-injury, suicidality; refer to psychiatry if positive.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Antisocial personality disorder<br /></br>• Requires age ≥18 and history of conduct disorder (aggression, property destruction, theft, rule violations). None are present; GD does not predict ASPD.  </p></br><p>b. Body dysmorphic disorder<br /></br>• Preoccupation with an imagined or slight defect (e.g., “my nose is ugly”) leading to repetitive behaviors (mirror-checking). This patient’s distress is global (sex characteristics) and identity-related, not focal appearance-related.  </p></br><p>c. Dissociative identity disorder<br /></br>• Characterized by ≥2 distinct personality states, amnesia, and dissociative symptoms, typically after severe trauma. Not indicated here; patient is fully oriented, no memory gaps.  </p></br><p>e. Schizoaffective disorder<br /></br>• Requires psychosis (delusions/hallucinations) plus a mood episode. Patient shows no psychotic symptoms, only identity dysphoria.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Mistaking gender dysphoria for body dysmorphic disorder or psychosis. Gender dysphoria revolves around gender identity, not a minor physical flaw or delusional belief. Always remember the high comorbidity with depression and suicide risk.</p>
147. A 13-year-old boy is brought to the office by his father because the father is concerned that his son seems to have a small penis. The patient has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. Vaccinations are up-to-date. Vital signs are within normal limits. Physical examination shows a small amount of long, downy pubic hair at the base of the penis. The scrotum is pink and enlarged; the penis is 3 cm in length. Which of the</br>following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Measurement of serum luteinizing hormone concentration</br>b. Measurement of serum testosterone concentration</br>c. Testosterone therapy</br>d. Ultrasonography of the scrotum</br>e. Reassurance that the patient's sexual development is normal~-</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection</br>e. Reassurance that the patient’s sexual development is normal</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• Age: 13-year-old male – normal onset of puberty in boys is between 9 and 14 years.<br /></br>• Genital findings: “Small amount of long, downy pubic hair at the base of the penis” + “pink and enlarged scrotum” + no significant penile lengthening.<br /></br>  – These findings describe Tanner stage 2 for male puberty.<br /></br>• No systemic illness, normal vitals, no red-flag symptoms.</p></br><p>Together, the vignette shows a boy in early, NORMAL puberty; the only “problem” is parental anxiety.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>1. Determine if puberty is delayed.<br /></br>   – Delayed puberty in males = no testicular enlargement (Tanner 2) by 14 yr or no progression for &gt;5 yr.<br /></br>   – This patient already shows Tanner 2 changes at 13 yr → within normal range.<br /></br>2. Therefore, no work-up or treatment is needed. The correct management is reassurance.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Do not pursue endocrine testing or imaging in a boy who is within the normal age range and already demonstrating physiologic pubertal progression (Tanner 2).</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (concise)</br>• Puberty in boys begins with gonadarche driven by pulsatile GnRH → ↑LH/FSH → testicular enlargement, scrotal thinning, sparse pubic hair (Tanner 2).<br /></br>• Penile growth lags behind testicular enlargement and typically becomes obvious in Tanner 3.<br /></br>• Micropenis is defined at birth (&lt;2.5 cm stretched) and is not diagnosed during normal puberty unless persistent into later stages.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review</br>None required in a normally developing 13-year-old. Monitoring during routine health maintenance is sufficient.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Measurement of serum LH<br /></br>   – Indicated if delayed puberty (no Tanner 2 by 14 yr) or signs of hypogonadism. Here puberty has begun; testing would add no value.</p></br><p>b. Measurement of serum testosterone<br /></br>   – Same rationale as option a; testosterone naturally rises later in Tanner staging, so a “low” value now could be misleading.</p></br><p>c. Testosterone therapy<br /></br>   – Reserved for confirmed hypogonadotropic or hypergonadotropic hypogonadism after full evaluation. Exogenous testosterone would prematurely close growth plates and is unnecessary.</p></br><p>d. Ultrasonography of the scrotum<br /></br>   – Used for suspected testicular pathology (torsion, mass, undescended testis). Normal testicular enlargement is present, so imaging is not indicated.</p></br><p>Key differences: Each incorrect option assumes pathologic delay or abnormality. The vignette instead describes normal early puberty, making reassurance the only appropriate choice.</p></br><p>Common pitfall: Confusing early Tanner 2 findings with “micropenis” and initiating unnecessary endocrine work-up. Always consider Tanner stage and age range before pursuing evaluation.</p>
148. A 57-year-old woman comes to the emergency department 1 hour after the onset of left-sided chest pain and shortness of breath. The pain is exacerbated by inspiration. She has no history of exertional chest pain or shortness of breath. She has received estrogen and progestin replacement therapy since menopause 7 years ago. Her temperature is 38 C 100.4  F, pulse is 105/min and regular, respirations are 24/min, and blood pressure is 120/80 mm Hg. A rub and decreased breath sounds are heard over the left posterior hemithorax. Heart sounds are normal. Examination of the lower extremities shows no abnormalities. Arterial blood gas analysis on room air shows:</br>pH .48</br>Pco~ ~8 mm Hg</br>Po 2 65 mm Hg</br>An x-ray of the chest shows blunting of the left costophrenic angle and a wedge-shaped opacity in the left lower lobe. An ECG shows right heart strain. Venous duplex ultrasonography of the lower extremities shows no abnormalities. Ventilation-perfusion lung scans show a perfusion defect in the left lower lobe and are consistent with a high probability of pulmonary embolus. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Venography of the lower extremities</br>b. Spiral CT scan of the chest</br>c. Lung biopsy</br>d. Pulmonary angiography</br>e. Thoracentesis</br>F. No additional studies are indicated</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>F. No additional studies are indicated</p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Sudden‐onset pleuritic chest pain + dyspnea → acute pulmonary process, classically pulmonary embolism (PE).<br /></br>• Estrogen-progestin replacement therapy = strong hypercoagulable risk factor.<br /></br>• Physical: pleural friction rub, ↓ breath sounds over posterior lung field, fever → small pulmonary infarction with overlying pleuritis.<br /></br>• Chest x-ray: wedge-shaped, pleural-based opacity (Hampton hump) + blunted costophrenic angle (small effusion) = classic but nonspecific PE findings.<br /></br>• ECG: right-heart strain (S₁Q₃T₃, RBBB, or T-wave inversions) supports PE.<br /></br>• ABG: pH 7.48, PCO₂ 28 mm Hg → acute respiratory alkalosis from hyperventilation typical of PE.<br /></br>• V/Q scan: “perfusion defect … high probability of pulmonary embolus.” In a patient with compatible clinical picture, a HIGH-probability V/Q scan virtually clinches the diagnosis (&gt;85–90% PPV).</p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Diagnostic algorithms (ACCP, ACC/AHA) for suspected PE:<br /></br>1. Determine pre-test clinical probability (Wells or Geneva).<br /></br>2. If noninvasive testing (V/Q scan or CTPA) is HIGH probability/positive and clinical suspicion is moderate–high, further imaging is unnecessary; proceed to treatment.<br /></br>Therefore, the next “step” is not another test but anticoagulation (or thrombolysis if unstable). From a diagnostic standpoint: No further studies are required.</p></br><p>Take-home message: A HIGH-probability V/Q scan in the correct clinical context is considered diagnostic of PE; pulmonary angiography is reserved for indeterminate or contradictory studies.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>• PE = thrombus (usually DVT) that embolizes to pulmonary arteries.<br /></br>• Hypercoagulable states: estrogen therapy, OCPs, cancer, surgery, immobility, inherited thrombophilias.<br /></br>• Obstruction → ventilation without perfusion → V/Q mismatch → hypoxemia → hyperventilation → ↓PCO₂ &amp; respiratory alkalosis.<br /></br>• Pulmonary infarction causes pleuritic pain, fever, rub, Hampton hump.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>• Immediate: Heparin (unfractionated or LMWH) → transition to warfarin or DOAC.<br /></br>• Thrombolysis (tPA) or embolectomy for massive PE with hemodynamic compromise.<br /></br>• Imaging hierarchy:<br /></br>  – CTPA = first-line in most centers.<br /></br>  – V/Q scan used when CTPA contraindicated (renal failure, contrast allergy, pregnancy).<br /></br>  – Pulmonary angiography = invasive gold standard but rarely needed.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Venography of the lower extremities<br /></br>   • Detects DVT source but adds nothing diagnostically once PE is essentially proven; invasive and not routinely performed.  </p></br><p>b. Spiral (CT) pulmonary angiography of the chest<br /></br>   • Excellent test, but redundant here. Used when V/Q is nondiagnostic or when clinical suspicion conflicts with V/Q result.  </p></br><p>c. Lung biopsy<br /></br>   • Not used to diagnose PE; excessively invasive and unnecessary.  </p></br><p>d. Pulmonary angiography (catheter-based)<br /></br>   • Historically gold standard, but reserved for indeterminate cases or when intervention planned; carries contrast &amp; procedural risks.  </p></br><p>e. Thoracentesis<br /></br>   • Small reactive pleural effusions are common in PE; tapping them does not aid the diagnosis of PE itself and delays therapy.  </p></br><p>Key distinction: With a high-probability V/Q scan that matches clinical presentation, further testing is a waste of time and resources—initiate therapy.</p>
149. An 80-year-old woman, who resides in a nursing care facility, is brought to the office by a nurse because of a 1-month history of moderate lower abdominal pain and constant low-volume urinary incontinence that now requires use of an absorbent pad. She also has had frequent urinary tract infections during the past year. She has hypertension, type 2 diabetes mellitus, and dementia. Her medications are insulin, labetalol, and donepezil. She appears frail and sits in a wheelchair. She is 160 cm 5 ft 3 in tall and weighs 51 kg 113 lb ; BMI is 20 kg/m 2. Vital signs are within normal limits. Abdominal examination shows a soft, nontender midline mass below the umbilicus. Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient's urinary incontinence?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Medication adverse effect</br>b. Neurogenic bladder</br>c. Stress incontinence</br>d. Urethral hypermobility</br>e. Uterine prolapse</br></br></br><p>Correct answer<br /></br>b. Neurogenic bladder (overflow incontinence from diabetic autonomic neuropathy)</p></br><p>Vignette analysis<br /></br>• Key phrases: “80-year-old woman,” “long-term nursing facility,” “type 2 diabetes,” “frequent UTIs,” “moderate lower abdominal pain,” “constant low-volume urinary incontinence,” “midline mass below the umbilicus.”<br /></br>• A palpable, nontender midline mass just below the umbilicus in an incontinent patient is classic for a markedly distended bladder.<br /></br>• Constant dribbling of small amounts of urine + recurrent UTIs = overflow incontinence.<br /></br>• Diabetes mellitus in the elderly → autonomic (parasympathetic) neuropathy of the detrusor → impaired sensation of bladder filling &amp; impaired detrusor contraction → chronic urinary retention with overflow.</p></br><p>Clinical critical reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Identify the type of incontinence (stress, urge, overflow, functional).<br /></br>Step 2: Match physical findings. Distention → overflow.<br /></br>Step 3: Find the etiology. In an elderly diabetic, the most common cause is neurogenic bladder from diabetic autonomic neuropathy.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology review<br /></br>• Long-standing diabetes damages autonomic nerves (pelvic splanchnics, pudendal afferents).<br /></br>• Denervated detrusor loses contractility, so the bladder fills to very high volumes.<br /></br>• High residual volume → continual dribbling (overflow) and acts as a culture medium → recurrent UTIs.<br /></br>• Palpable suprapubic/fullness is typical.<br /></br>• Post-void residual (PVR) &gt; 300 mL confirms the diagnosis.</p></br><p>Treatment / diagnostics<br /></br>1. Measure PVR with bladder scan or catheterization (first diagnostic step).<br /></br>2. Timed voiding, double voiding.<br /></br>3. Intermittent clean catheterization is definitive.<br /></br>4. Bethanechol (cholinergic agonist) can help stimulate detrusor contraction, but is usually inadequate alone.</p></br><p>Incorrect answer analysis<br /></br>a. Medication adverse effect – Labetalol, insulin, and donepezil do not cause urinary retention. Donepezil (AChE inhibitor) would actually increase detrusor activity, so the opposite effect.<br /></br>c. Stress incontinence – Presents with loss of urine during increased intra-abdominal pressure (cough, laugh). Physical exam often shows urethral hypermobility but NOT a distended bladder; no constant dribbling.<br /></br>d. Urethral hypermobility – Same clinical picture as stress incontinence; again, no palpable suprapubic mass and no high PVR.<br /></br>e. Uterine prolapse – Would manifest with a vaginal/pelvic bulge, pelvic pressure, and possibly stress incontinence; it does not produce a suprapubic abdominal mass.</p></br><p>Core takeaway<br /></br>Constant low-volume dribbling + palpable suprapubic fullness = overflow incontinence. In an elderly diabetic, diabetic autonomic neuropathy (neurogenic bladder) is the most likely underlying cause.</p>
150. An 18-year-old man is brought to the physician by his parents because of a 1-week history of increasingly severe pain, swelling, and locking sensation in his right knee. Last week, his knee was twisted and bent as he was pinned during a wrestling match. He was unable to finish the match. He has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. Vital signs are within normal limits. The skin is intact. Examination of the right knee shows 2+ effusion. Ran e of motion of the left knee is full. Range of motion of the right knee in extension is limited by pain. McMurray testing of the right knee produces pain over the medial joint line. Lachman and abduction and adduction stress tests are negative bilaterally. Dorsalis pedis pulses are 2+ bilaterally. Sensation to light touch is intact over the lower extremities. X-rays of the right knee show no abnormalities. An MRI of the medial aspect of the right knee is shown. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Arthroscopy and operative repair of the knee</br>b. Aspiration and manipulation of the knee</br>c. Physical therapy and knee immobilization</br>d. Physical therapy and use of crutches</br>e. Rest and ibuprofen therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>A. Arthroscopy and operative repair of the knee  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Twisted and bent … wrestling” → classic non-contact, torsional injury that shears the meniscus.<br /></br>• “Locking sensation,” “effusion,” “pain over the medial joint line,” “positive McMurray” → classic triad for a meniscal tear, especially a bucket-handle tear when locking/extension block is present.<br /></br>• “Extension is limited by pain” + “MRI (medial)” picture (not shown here) → imaging is confirming a displaced tear.<br /></br>• Ligament tests (Lachman = ACL, valgus/varus stress = MCL/LCL) are normal, ruling out collateral or cruciate injury.<br /></br>• X-ray normal → rules out fracture or osteochondral defect.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Identify the injury pattern: twisting injury → meniscus.<br /></br>2. Look for mechanical symptoms: locking, catching, extension block → suggests displaced/unstable tear.<br /></br>3. Young athlete with functional limitation → conservative care is unlikely to heal; requires anatomic repair.<br /></br>Core takeaway: A symptomatic, mechanically blocking meniscal tear in a young patient is treated surgically (arthroscopic meniscal repair or partial meniscectomy, depending on tear location/vascularity).  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Meniscus = fibrocartilage shock absorber.<br /></br>• Vascular supply: outer 1/3 (red-red zone) heals; inner 2/3 (white-white zone) is avascular.<br /></br>• Twisting on a flexed knee produces a longitudinal (“bucket-handle”) tear.<br /></br>• Displaced fragment flips into the intercondylar notch → physical block to extension → “locking.”<br /></br>• Untreated, fragments abrade articular cartilage → early osteoarthritis.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>First-line for small, stable, peripheral tears or degenerative tears in older pts: rest, NSAIDs, PT.<br /></br>Indications for surgery:<br /></br>• Young/athletic patient<br /></br>• Large, displaced, or bucket-handle tear<br /></br>• Mechanical symptoms (locking, catching)<br /></br>• Failure of conservative therapy<br /></br>Arthroscopy allows:<br /></br>• Direct visualization<br /></br>• Repair (suture) if in vascular zone or partial meniscectomy if non-repairable.<br /></br>Post-op: early ROM, quadriceps strengthening; return to sports ~3-6 months.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Aspiration and manipulation of the knee<br /></br>   – Joint aspiration for hemarthrosis or infection; manipulation is for adhesions. Does not address the torn meniscal fragment causing mechanical block.  </p></br><p>C. Physical therapy and knee immobilization<br /></br>   – Immobilization treats collateral ligament sprains or patellar dislocation. Meniscus tears improve with motion, not prolonged immobilization; will not relieve locking.  </p></br><p>D. Physical therapy and use of crutches<br /></br>   – Reasonable for mild, stable tears or ligament sprains; would not resolve a bucket-handle fragment and locking.  </p></br><p>E. Rest and ibuprofen therapy<br /></br>   – NSAIDs help pain/inflammation but won’t heal an avascular displaced tear or correct mechanical obstruction.  </p></br><p>Key differentiator: presence of locking/extension block and MRI-confirmed displaced medial meniscal tear mandates arthroscopic operative repair.</p>
151. A 22-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 3-day history of a sore throat and difficulty opening his jaw. He has no history of serious illness, and he takes no medications. He is allergic to penicillin. His temperature is 38.4  C 101  F, pulse is 120/min, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 100/70 mm Hg. There is cervical lymphadenopathy bilaterally. Examination of the throat shows deviation of the uvula to the right, edema of the soft palate on the left and bilateral pharyngeal erythema with exudates. After drainage of the abscess, which of the following is the most appropriate pharmacotherapy?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Amoxicillin</br>b. Cephalexin</br>c. Clindamycin</br>d. Erythromycin</br>e. Metronidazole</br>F. Tetracycline</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Clindamycin  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 3-day sore throat + “difficulty opening his jaw” (trismus) → red flag for a deep‐space infection, most commonly a peritonsillar abscess (PTA).<br /></br>• Physical exam: uvular deviation to the contralateral side (right), unilateral soft-palate edema (left) = classic for PTA.<br /></br>• Fever (38.4 °C) and bilateral tender cervical nodes support bacterial etiology.<br /></br>• Key modifier: “He is allergic to penicillin.” This immediately influences antibiotic selection once the abscess is drained.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Goal after incision-and-drainage = eradicate the typical polymicrobial flora (Group A Strep ± Staph aureus + oral anaerobes such as Bacteroides, Fusobacterium). Standard IV/PO choices are ampicillin-sulbactam or amoxicillin-clavulanate. Penicillin allergy ⇒ need a non–β-lactam with both aerobic streptococcal and anaerobic coverage. Clindamycin fulfills both requirements and is guideline-endorsed for PTA in penicillin-allergic patients.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: For peritonsillar abscess, use clindamycin when the patient cannot receive a penicillin/β-lactam.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Peritonsillar abscess = suppurative complication of acute tonsillitis. Infection tracks between the tonsillar capsule and the pharyngeal muscles, creating a walled-off pus pocket. The edema pushes the tonsil medially → uvular deviation; inflammation of the pterygoid muscles causes trismus. Untreated, the infection can spread to the parapharyngeal or retropharyngeal space → airway compromise or mediastinitis.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>1. Secure airway if needed.<br /></br>2. Needle aspiration or I&amp;D of abscess (performed in vignette).<br /></br>3. Antibiotics:<br /></br>   • First-line (no allergy): Ampicillin-sulbactam IV or Amoxicillin-clavulanate PO.<br /></br>   • Penicillin allergy: Clindamycin IV/PO.<br /></br>4. Tonsillectomy considered for recurrent PTA.<br /></br>Imaging (CT neck with contrast) is used if diagnosis uncertain or deep-space extension suspected.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Amoxicillin – Excellent Group A Strep coverage but no β-lactamase inhibitor, so poor anaerobic coverage; additionally patient has a penicillin allergy.<br /></br>b. Cephalexin – 1st-gen cephalosporin; limited anaerobic activity and ≈1% cross-reactivity with penicillin allergy.<br /></br>d. Erythromycin – Covers GAS but minimal anaerobic activity and increasing macrolide resistance in Streptococcus.<br /></br>e. Metronidazole – Outstanding anaerobic coverage but no activity against aerobic streptococci; would need to be combined with a second agent.<br /></br>f. Tetracycline – Broad spectrum but unreliable against Group A Strep and not first-line for deep oropharyngeal infections.  </p></br><p>Key Differences: Only clindamycin simultaneously (1) avoids β-lactams, (2) reliably covers streptococci and staphylococci, and (3) has strong anaerobic coverage—making it the single-agent therapy of choice after drainage of a peritonsillar abscess in a penicillin-allergic patient.</p>
152. A 16-year-old boy who was diagnosed with HIV infection 1 year ago comes for a follow-up examination. He has had no opportunistic infections. Immunizations are up-to-date. His CD4+ T-lymphocyte count is 550/mm 3 Normal~S00, and plasma HIV viral load is less than 1000 copies/ml, a hundredfold decrease since he began triple antiretroviral therapy 1 year ago. He and his girlfriend are sexually active and use condoms consistently. His plans for the summer include traveling to Africa for 2 weeks on a medical missionary trip, buying a cat, joining a swim team, and obtaining a part-time job in a day-care center which involves caring for toilet-trained toddlers. Avoidance of which of the following activities is most likely to minimize his chances of opportunistic infection?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Cleaning the cat litter</br>b. Joining the swim team</br>c. Sexual activity</br>d. Traveling to Africa</br>e. Working in a day-care center</br></br></br><p>Correct answer<br /></br>A. Cleaning the cat litter</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key facts: HIV-positive teenager, on effective HAART, CD4⁺ count 550/mm³ (well above 200), planning several routine activities.<br /></br>• Task: Identify which proposed activity uniquely increases risk for an OPPORTUNISTIC (CD4-dependent) pathogen.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Why A is right)<br /></br>1. Cat feces contain oocysts of Toxoplasma gondii.<br /></br>2. T. gondii reactivates in immunocompromised hosts and causes life-threatening cerebral toxoplasmosis.<br /></br>3. Although this patient’s CD4 count is currently high, lifelong latent infection acquired now could reactivate later if his immunity wanes.<br /></br>4. CDC and HIV guidelines therefore recommend that HIV patients “avoid changing cat litter boxes; if unavoidable, wear gloves and wash hands.”  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: The single most high-yield environmental source for a classic AIDS-defining opportunistic infection is exposure to cat excreta (toxoplasmosis ± Bartonella).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology High-Yield Review<br /></br>• Toxoplasma life cycle: cat = definitive host; humans acquire oocysts from soil, cat litter, raw meat.<br /></br>• In HIV: CD4 &lt;100 → reactivation of latent brain cysts → multiple ring-enhancing lesions, seizures, encephalitis.<br /></br>• Prevention:<br /></br>  – Primary: avoid cat litter/raw meat.<br /></br>  – Secondary (when CD4 &lt;100 and IgG+): trimethoprim–sulfamethoxazole prophylaxis.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Joining the swim team<br /></br>• Water exposure mainly raises risk of Pseudomonas folliculitis or otitis externa, which are NOT AIDS-defining opportunistic infections and occur in healthy hosts.</p></br><p>C. Sexual activity (using condoms consistently)<br /></br>• Risk is mainly for conventional STIs (e.g., gonorrhea, chlamydia, HPV) rather than CD4-dependent opportunistic infections. Condom use already mitigates these risks.</p></br><p>D. Traveling to Africa<br /></br>• May expose him to malaria, typhoid, or travelers’ diarrhea—important but not classified as opportunistic infections. With proper vaccines/chemoprophylaxis, risk is manageable.</p></br><p>E. Working in a day-care center (toilet-trained toddlers)<br /></br>• Highest infection concern is CMV spread via urine/saliva. CMV disease becomes a problem mainly when CD4 &lt;50 and with close contact with diapers. Toilet-trained toddlers markedly lower exposure, and universal precautions suffice.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Exam-Level Pearls<br /></br>1. Think “cat litter = toxoplasmosis” whenever you see an HIV patient and a cat.<br /></br>2. Opportunistic infection risk is dictated by both exposure AND immune status; avoidance of high-risk exposures is recommended even when CD4 is currently adequate.<br /></br>3. Know CD4 cutoffs and prophylaxis:<br /></br>   • &lt;200: Pneumocystis → TMP-SMX.<br /></br>   • &lt;100: Toxoplasma (if IgG+) → TMP-SMX.<br /></br>   • &lt;50: MAC → azithromycin/clarithromycin; CMV → valganciclovir (if prior CMV disease).</p></br><p>By recognizing these associations, you can quickly eliminate distractors and select the activity uniquely linked to an AIDS-defining pathogen—cleaning the cat litter.</p>
153. A 42-year-old woman, gravida 2, para 1, at 12 weeks' gestation comes to the physician for her first prenatal visit. She conceived a dichorionic-diamniotic twin gestation after in vitro fertilization. She has no history of serious illness, and her only medication is a prenatal vitamin. Two years ago, her first pregnancy ended in cesarean delivery at 39 weeks' gestation because of breech presentation. She is 165 cm 5 ft 5 in tall. She weighed 68 kg 150 lb prior to her pregnancy; BMI was 25 kg/m 2. She has had a 2-kg 4-lb weight gain during her pregnancy. Her blood pressure is 110/70 mm Hg. Examination shows a uterus consistent in size with a 12-week gestation. This patient is at greatest risk for which of the following pregnancy complications?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Fetal aneuploidy</br>b. Preterm labor</br>c. Twin-twin transfusion syndrome</br>d. Umbilical cord entanglement</br>e. Uterine rupture</br>F. Vanishing twin syndrome</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Preterm labor  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 42-year-old gravida 2 para 1 at 12 wk<br /></br>• Conceived dichorionic-diamniotic twins after IVF (assisted reproduction ⇒ high multiple-gestation rate)<br /></br>• Prior low-transverse cesarean but intends routine care (no trial of labor mentioned)<br /></br>Key takeaway: Multiple gestation (regardless of chorionicity) is the single strongest risk factor for spontaneous and indicated preterm birth.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach to the stem:<br /></br>1. Identify the major “abnormal” variable → twin pregnancy.<br /></br>2. Ask: “Which listed complication has the highest absolute incidence in twin pregnancies?”<br /></br>   • Preterm labor/birth occurs in ≈55–60 % of twin gestations (vs ≈10 % in singletons).<br /></br>3. Age &gt;35 does raise aneuploidy risk, but its absolute frequency (≈1–2 % of pregnancies at age 42) is still far lower than the preterm-birth rate in twins.<br /></br>Core concept: Multiple gestation → uterine over-distention, cervical insufficiency, increased medically indicated deliveries (preeclampsia, fetal growth abnormality) → overwhelmingly high preterm-birth rate.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Uterine over-distention stimulates myometrial oxytocin receptors and prostaglandin release → earlier contractions.<br /></br>• Cervical shortening is accelerated.<br /></br>• Complications (twin growth discordance, preeclampsia, placental abruption) often force iatrogenic early delivery.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>• Serial cervical-length measurements beginning 16 wk.<br /></br>• Consider vaginal progesterone or cervical cerclage if shortening develops.<br /></br>• Tocolytics and antenatal corticosteroids when preterm labor ensues.<br /></br>• Delivery timing: uncomplicated dichorionic twins are electively delivered 37 wk because going longer confers little benefit and increases stillbirth risk.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Fetal aneuploidy<br /></br>   • Maternal age &gt;35 does increase risk (Down syndrome ≈1/60 at 42 yr), but absolute risk (~1–2 %) is far lower than 55–60 % risk of preterm birth.<br /></br>   • Screening (first-trimester screening, cell-free DNA) is recommended but does not eclipse preterm-birth frequency.  </p></br><p>c. Twin-twin transfusion syndrome (TTTS)<br /></br>   • Requires shared chorion &amp; shared placental vascular anastomoses (monochorionic twins).<br /></br>   • The pregnancy is dichorionic-diamniotic, so TTTS risk ≈0.  </p></br><p>d. Umbilical cord entanglement<br /></br>   • Seen in monoamniotic twins (one amniotic sac).<br /></br>   • Risk is minimal in separate sacs (diamniotic).  </p></br><p>e. Uterine rupture<br /></br>   • Risk with trial of labor after cesarean (TOLAC) ≈0.5–1 %; even lower with elective repeat cesarean.<br /></br>   • Far less common than preterm birth.  </p></br><p>f. Vanishing twin syndrome<br /></br>   • Spontaneous loss of one twin before 14 wk; most losses occur before first prenatal visit in IVF pregnancies.<br /></br>   • At 12 wk twins are alive with appropriate uterine size; incidence now substantially reduced (&lt;5 %).  </p></br><p>Take-home Message<br /></br>Twin gestations—regardless of maternal age or method of conception—carry an extraordinarily high risk of preterm labor and delivery, making this the most common and therefore “greatest” complication to anticipate and counsel about.</p>
154. On arrival to the postanesthesia care unit 15 minutes after undergoing fourth and fifth metacarpophalangeal arthroplasty, a 32-year-old woman with rheumatoid arthritis has a fingerstick blood glucose concentration of 350 mg/dl. During induction of general anesthesia, endotracheal intubation was complicated by arthritic changes to the cervical spine, and dexamethasone was administered to decrease airway edema. She received lactated Ringer solution during the 60-minute procedure; intraoperative blood loss was minimal. Medications are prednisone and methotrexate. She is awake. Vital signs are within normal limits. Cardiopulmonary examination shows no abnormalities. Neurologic examination shows no focal findings. Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient's hyperglycemia?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Dexamethasone administration during induction of anesthesia</br>b. Methotrexate-induced pancreatic insufficiency</br>c. New-onset diabetes mellitus</br>d. Prednisone-induced adrenal insufficiency</br>e. Normal perioperative stress response</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>A. Dexamethasone administration during induction of anesthesia</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• Timing – Finger-stick done only 15 minutes after surgery; very acute rise.<br /></br>• Magnitude – 350 mg/dL is a markedly elevated value.<br /></br>• Meds given intra-op – “Dexamethasone was administered to decrease airway edema.” A single 8–10 mg IV dose is roughly equipotent to 200–250 mg of hydrocortisone.<br /></br>• No other physiologic stressors – Minimal blood loss, short (60-min) case, hemodynamically stable.</p></br><p>These clues point toward an exogenous, high-dose glucocorticoid effect.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>1. Exogenous glucocorticoids (especially potent ones such as dexamethasone) rapidly:<br /></br>   • Increase hepatic gluconeogenesis and glycogenolysis.<br /></br>   • Cause peripheral insulin resistance.<br /></br>   • Inhibit pancreatic β-cell insulin secretion.<br /></br>   The spike in serum glucose begins within 30–60 minutes and can reach &gt;300 mg/dL after a single IV dose.</p></br><ol></br><li>Usual peri-operative “stress hyperglycemia” (endogenous catecholamines/cortisol) rarely exceeds 200–220 mg/dL in a healthy young adult after a minor procedure.</li></br></ol></br><p>Take-home: Any IV glucocorticoid bolus (dexamethasone, methylprednisolone) can produce transient, sometimes extreme, hyperglycemia—even in non-diabetic patients.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Dexamethasone: 25–30× more potent than cortisol, negligible mineralocorticoid effect, long biologic half-life (36–54 h).<br /></br>• Mechanism of hyperglycemia – Up-regulation of PEPCK &amp; other gluconeogenic enzymes, down-regulation of GLUT-4 translocation ➜ ↑ hepatic glucose output, ↓ peripheral uptake.<br /></br>• Peaks within 1–2 h; usually resolves over 12–24 h as insulin secretion catches up or dexamethasone levels fall.</p></br><p>Treatments / Monitoring</br>• Check serum glucose every 2–4 h in PACU and initiate sliding-scale insulin if &gt;180 mg/dL.<br /></br>• No long-term therapy required; level returns to baseline once steroid effect wanes.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>B. Methotrexate-induced pancreatic insufficiency<br /></br>   – MTX toxicity: hepatotoxicity, mucositis, cytopenias, pneumonitis. Does not damage endocrine pancreas nor cause acute hyperglycemia.<br /></br>C. New-onset diabetes mellitus<br /></br>   – DM develops gradually; unlikely to present as a single 350 mg/dL value 15 min post-op in an otherwise asymptomatic 32-year-old. Need fasting or HbA1c confirmation.<br /></br>D. Prednisone-induced adrenal insufficiency<br /></br>   – Chronic prednisone suppresses ACTH, but adrenal insufficiency causes cortisol deficiency → hypotension, hyponatremia, hypoglycemia (not hyperglycemia).<br /></br>E. Normal perioperative stress response<br /></br>   – Endogenous stress response raises glucose modestly (≈140–200 mg/dL). Levels &gt;300 mg/dL immediately after a short, uncomplicated procedure strongly suggest an exogenous steroid bolus.</p></br><p>Key Concept</br>A single IV dose of a potent glucocorticoid (e.g., dexamethasone for airway edema or PONV prophylaxis) can precipitate marked, transient hyperglycemia—monitor and treat as needed.</p>
155. Three days after an operation for aortic stenosis, a 67-year-old white man is being evaluated by the surgical team. A medical student takes one of the two patient information charts hanging outside the room. While reading the chart, the student realizes that the ethnicity listed on the chart does not match the ethnicity of the patient. Verification of the name on the chart shows that the student has taken the chart for the other patient in the double room. Both charts are labeled room 456-1, instead of the chart for the window bed being labeled 456-2. Individual members of the team laugh at the mix-up, and a resident states that the charts are often misnumbered due to patients shifting room position. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step by the senior member of the team?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Emphasize during new intern orientation that chart information is often incorrect</br>b. Report the incident to the quality assurance committee for further analysis</br>c. Verbally reprimand the medical student for reading the incorrect chart</br>d. Verbally reprimand the members of the team who laughed</br>e. Verbally reprimand the nurse who mislabeled the chart</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Report the incident to the quality-assurance (QA) / patient-safety committee for further analysis (root-cause analysis).</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key phrases: “wrong chart,” “ethnicity does not match,” “both charts labeled 456-1,” “patients shifting room position,” “often misnumbered.”<br /></br>• This is a near-miss event involving patient misidentification. No individual was harmed yet, but the system failure could easily lead to a serious error (e.g., wrong medication, wrong procedure).<br /></br>• Because the error reflects a SYSTEM problem (two charts with the same bed number, no double identifier), the appropriate response is to escalate to the hospital’s formal patient-safety / QA process.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Identify the event type: near miss involving patient identification.<br /></br>2. Decide immediate action: ensure correct chart goes to the correct patient (done).<br /></br>3. Prevent recurrence: engage the institutional QA mechanism so the process causing the error (bed-numbering system) can be analyzed and fixed.<br /></br>Take-home message: Errors that arise from faulty systems—not individual negligence—require reporting and root-cause analysis, not personal reprimands or informal reminders.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>(Not applicable—systems-based practice question.)<br /></br>However, misidentification errors are one of the Joint Commission’s “National Patient Safety Goals.” Hospitals must have two patient identifiers and robust chart-labeling processes to prevent harm.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>• “Treatment” here = system correction.<br /></br>• QA committee will:<br /></br>  – Collect data (how often mislabeling occurs, when patients are moved).<br /></br>  – Perform root-cause analysis.<br /></br>  – Implement fixes (e.g., bar-coded wristbands, electronic medical record alerts, strict relabeling protocol when beds change).<br /></br>  – Educate staff after system changes are in place.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Emphasize during new-intern orientation that chart info is often incorrect<br /></br>   – Education alone is weak and shifts acceptance toward working around a faulty system. Does not correct the root cause.<br /></br>c. Verbally reprimand the medical student<br /></br>   – The student did nothing negligent; he identified the error. Blame culture discourages future reporting.<br /></br>d. Verbally reprimand the team members who laughed<br /></br>   – Addresses unprofessional behavior, but does not address the safety hazard itself.<br /></br>e. Verbally reprimand the nurse who mislabeled the chart<br /></br>   – Individual blame ignores the underlying systemic problem (patients moved without a standardized relabeling process). Errors are rarely due to a single person.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>On the exam, anything that smells like a “system error” (mislabeling, equipment malfunction, unclear protocols) → think “report to QA / root-cause analysis” rather than “punish an individual” or “tell people to be more careful.”</p>
156. A 42-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 1-month history of intermittent mild pain in her right lower abdomen. She has not had fever, chills, changes in bowel habits, or urinary symptoms. Three years ago, she underwent bilateral prophylactic mastectomy because her maternal grandmother, mother, and two older sisters have a history of breast cancer. The patient has no history of serious illness, and her only medication is a calcium supplement containing vitamin D. Her temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 80/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 116/68 mm Hg. Cardiopulmonary examination shows no abnormalities. The abdomen is soft and nondistended, and the liver is not enlarged. Examination of the right lower quadrant shows tenderness to deep palpation; there are no signs of peritonitis. On pelvic examination, a mass is palpated in the right adnexal region; no other abnormalities are noted. Pelvic ultrasonography shows an 8 x 6-cm complex, multiseptate, right ovarian mass; there is no ascites. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Fine-needle biopsy of the mass</br>b. Operative exploration</br>c. PET scan</br>d. Serum carcinoembryonic antigen CEA assay</br>e. Testing for BRCA mutations</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Operative exploration (exploratory laparotomy with intent to remove &amp; stage the mass)</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues<br /></br>  – 42-year-old woman with strong family history of breast cancer who already underwent prophylactic mastectomy → Flag for possible BRCA1/2 carrier, which markedly raises epithelial ovarian cancer risk.<br /></br>  – 1-month intermittent pelvic pain + adnexal mass palpated on bimanual exam.<br /></br>  – Ultrasound: 8 × 6-cm “complex, multiseptate” ovarian mass (solid + cystic components, septations) → highly suspicious pattern for malignancy.<br /></br>  – No ascites yet, afebrile, no signs of PID or torsion.<br /></br>Together, these findings meet criteria for a presumed malignant ovarian tumor that requires surgical staging.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Stepwise approach to an adnexal mass in a premenopausal woman:<br /></br>1. Characterize on ultrasound. Simple, thin-walled &lt;5 cm → observe; anything complex, solid, papillary, or &gt;5–10 cm → malignant until proven otherwise.<br /></br>2. DO NOT stick a needle in it (risk of spilling malignant cells into peritoneum).<br /></br>3. Next step is operative exploration (laparotomy or laparoscopy by gynecologic oncologist) with frozen-section-directed staging and cytoreduction if cancer is confirmed.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Suspected malignant ovarian masses are managed surgically, not by percutaneous biopsy.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>• Most epithelial ovarian cancers arise from the surface epithelium; risk is highest in BRCA1 &gt; BRCA2 mutation carriers and patients with strong family histories of breast &amp; ovarian cancer.<br /></br>• Tumor sheds cells into peritoneal cavity early; prognosis hinges on complete surgical staging/debulking. Peritoneal rupture worsens stage → why biopsy is contraindicated.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Diagnostic/therapeutic surgery:<br /></br>• Midline laparotomy (or laparoscopy) by gynecologic oncologist.<br /></br>• Procedures: TAH-BSO, omentectomy, peritoneal washings, lymph node sampling; remove mass intact to avoid rupture.<br /></br>Adjunct pre-op labs: CA-125 may be drawn for future surveillance, but its result does not change the need for surgery here.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Fine-needle biopsy of the mass<br /></br>   – Contraindicated. Violates oncologic principle: risk of capsular disruption &amp; peritoneal seeding, upstaging the tumor. Appropriate only for clearly metastatic disease where surgery is not planned.</p></br><p>c. PET scan<br /></br>   – Gives metabolic information but cannot replace histologic diagnosis. Imaging still leaves uncertainty &amp; delays definitive treatment.</p></br><p>d. Serum carcinoembryonic antigen (CEA) assay<br /></br>   – CEA is a GI adenocarcinoma marker, occasionally elevated in mucinous ovarian tumors but far less useful than CA-125. Even CA-125 level would not alter that surgery is mandatory.</p></br><p>e. Testing for BRCA mutations<br /></br>   – She already behaved as if BRCA-positive (prophylactic mastectomy). Regardless of genetic status, the current complex mass warrants immediate surgical evaluation; testing does not guide the next step.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line: A complex, septated ovarian mass in a BRCA-risk woman is presumed malignant. Percutaneous biopsy is dangerous; prompt operative exploration with intra-operative frozen section and full surgical staging is the correct next step.</p>
157. A 28-year-old man comes to the emergency department because of a 1-week history of increasingly severe right lower abdominal pain. During this time, he also has had fever, nausea, and vomiting. He has not had chills. He has no history of serious illness or operative procedures and takes no medications. Temperature is 38.3 C 101.0 F, pulse is 100/min, and blood pressure is 120/60 mm Hg. Cardiopulmonary examination shows no abnormalities. On abdominal examination, a 6 x 8-cm, tender mass is palpated in the right lower quadrant. There is no overlying erythema or induration. CT scan of the abdomen and pelvis shows a 6 x 8-cm fluid collection with an enhancing rim in the right lower quadrant. In addition to intravenous broad-spectrum antibiotic therapy, which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Aspiration of the fluid</br>b. Colonoscopy</br>c. Exploratory laparotomy</br>d. Percutaneous drainage</br>e. Observation only</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Percutaneous drainage (CT- or US-guided catheter placement)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 1-week crescendo of RLQ pain + fever, nausea/vomiting → classic timeline for perforated appendicitis evolving into a contained abscess.<br /></br>• CT: “6 × 8-cm fluid collection with an enhancing rim” = walled-off abscess rather than free perforation.<br /></br>• No diffuse peritonitis → source is localized; patient is hemodynamically stable.<br /></br>These features point to an appendiceal abscess ≥3 cm, the key size at which source control is required in addition to IV antibiotics.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise management of a late-presenting appendiceal abscess:<br /></br>1. Broad-spectrum IV antibiotics to cover gut flora.<br /></br>2. Image-guided percutaneous catheter drainage for abscesses ≥3 cm (or symptomatic).<br /></br>3. Interval (elective) appendectomy 6–8 wk later, once inflammation subsides.<br /></br>Take-home: Large, walled-off intra-abdominal abscesses without generalized peritonitis are best treated with image-guided catheter drainage, not immediate surgery.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Acute appendicitis → luminal obstruction → ischemia → bacterial overgrowth → perforation.<br /></br>• When perforation is slow/contained by omentum, an abscess forms rather than diffuse peritonitis.<br /></br>• Pus + necrotic tissue cannot be sterilized with antibiotics alone if collection ≥3 cm; drainage lowers bacterial load and allows healing.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Initial:<br /></br>• IV piperacillin-tazobactam (or cefepime + metronidazole) to cover gram-negative rods &amp; anaerobes.<br /></br>• CT-guided percutaneous catheter left in place until output minimal &amp; follow-up scan shows resolution.<br /></br>Follow-up:<br /></br>• Colonoscopy is done electively in &gt;40 yr to rule out cecal cancer (not urgent here).<br /></br>• Interval appendectomy prevents recurrence.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Aspiration of the fluid<br /></br>• A single-needle aspiration often re-accumulates; guidelines recommend catheter drainage for abscesses &gt;3 cm or thick pus.</p></br><p>b. Colonoscopy<br /></br>• Not therapeutic; risk of perforation in an acutely inflamed area. Reserved for later malignancy work-up.</p></br><p>c. Exploratory laparotomy<br /></br>• Indicated for diffuse peritonitis, hemodynamic instability, or failure of percutaneous drainage. Unnecessary morbidity in a well-localized abscess.</p></br><p>e. Observation only<br /></br>• Antibiotics alone have high failure/recurrence rates with collections &gt;3 cm; inadequate source control.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Core Concept<br /></br>Contained appendiceal (or any intra-abdominal) abscess larger than ~3 cm in a stable patient → IV broad-spectrum antibiotics plus image-guided percutaneous catheter drainage, followed by interval surgery once inflammation resolves.</p>
158. A 77-year-old woman comes to the emergency department 1 hour after the sudden onset of pain, numbness, and stiffness of her right forearm and hand. She has hypertension and atrial fibrillation. She had a myocardial infarction 2 years ago. Her medications are metoprolol, lisinopril, and warfarin. Her temperature is 36.8 C 98.2 F, pulse is 60/min and irregularly irregular, respirations are 14/min, and blood pressure is 140/90 mm Hg. Cardiopulmonary examination shows no abnormalities. The right forearm and hand are pale and cool with a capillary refill time of 8 seconds. Pulses are absent in the distal aspect of the right upper extremity. The right axillary pulse and pulses in the left upper extremity are within normal limits. An ECG shows atrial fibrillation with a ventricular rate of 72/min. There are no acute changes. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hemoglobin 12.3 g/dL</br>Hematocrit G7 percent</br>Leukocyte count 10,500/mmB</br>Platelet count 354,000/mm 3</br>Prothrombin time 14 sec INR=1.5</br>Partial thromboplastin time 27 sec</br>She is scheduled for embolectomy in 90 minutes. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Addition of adenosine to the medication regimen</br>b. Addition of heparin to the medication regimen ---~</br>c. Addition of sodium nitroprusside to the medication regimen</br>d. Application of a heating pad to the right upper extremity</br>e. Discontinuation of metoprolol therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>Addition of heparin to the medication regimen  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Sudden onset of pain, numbness, and stiffness” + “pale and cool” extremity, capillary refill 8 s, absent distal pulses → classic 6 P’s of acute limb ischemia (pain, pallor, pulselessness, paresthesia, poikilothermia, paralysis).<br /></br>• Atrial fibrillation + subtherapeutic INR 1.5 despite warfarin → high risk for embolic arterial occlusion.<br /></br>• Axillary pulse present, distal pulses absent → embolus likely lodged at or distal to brachial bifurcation.<br /></br>• Embolectomy scheduled in 90 min → limb-threatening ischemia, but still a window before revascularization.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-1: Recognize acute arterial occlusion (need revascularization within 6 h).<br /></br>Step-2: Immediate pharmacologic priority = prevent clot propagation and secondary thrombosis of the distal microcirculation.
