Standard of care: IV unfractionated heparin bolus followed by continuous infusion as soon as the diagnosis is suspected—do not wait for the OR.<br /></br>Take-home message: In acute limb ischemia, systemic anticoagulation with IV heparin is initiated immediately, even if surgery or catheter-directed therapy is planned.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Chronic AF → stasis in left atrial appendage → thrombus formation.<br /></br>• Embolus travels to peripheral artery (here, arm) → abrupt arterial occlusion.<br /></br>• Ongoing platelet/fibrin deposition proximally and distally enlarges the obstructing clot; heparin inhibits thrombin &amp; factor Xa, halting this propagation.</p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Immediate management (after confirming vascular exam):<br /></br>1. IV unfractionated heparin (80 U/kg bolus → 18 U/kg/h infusion; adjust by aPTT).<br /></br>2. Analgesia, keep limb dependent, avoid external heat/cold.<br /></br>3. Emergent imaging (arterial duplex or CTA) only if diagnosis uncertain; otherwise go straight to the OR for embolectomy or catheter-directed thrombolysis.<br /></br>4. Post-op: resume therapeutic anticoagulation (warfarin with heparin bridge) and optimize INR 2-3.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>A. Adenosine—rapid AV-node blocker for paroxysmal supraventricular tachycardia; has no role in AF-related limb ischemia or embolus prevention.<br /></br>C. Sodium nitroprusside—potent arterial/venous vasodilator used for hypertensive emergencies or acute decompensated heart failure; will not restore flow across an embolic occlusion.<br /></br>D. Heating pad to the limb—risk of burns in an insensate, ischemic extremity; does not improve macrovascular blood flow and is specifically discouraged.<br /></br>E. Discontinuation of metoprolol—rate control in AF remains beneficial; stopping it neither treats the current embolus nor prevents further events.</p></br><p>Key Differential Clues that Rule Out Alternatives<br /></br>• Problem is mechanical obstruction, not vasospasm (rules out nitroprusside).<br /></br>• Rhythm already AF with adequate ventricular rate; adenosine not indicated.<br /></br>• Problem is not bradycardia or β-blocker toxicity; discontinuing metoprolol addresses none of the 6 P’s.<br /></br>• External warming does nothing for occlusive arterial disease and can worsen tissue injury.</p></br><p>Core concept to remember: “Heparin first, revascularize second” for any suspected acute limb ischemia.</p>
159. A 45-year-old woman comes to the physician requesting a second opinion of a recent diagnosis of multiple sclerosis MS. She has smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 25 years and asks if cigarette smoking contributed</br>to her current condition. The physician finds a recently published study evaluating the relationship between cigarette smoking and MS. A total of 100,000 women between the ages of 18 and 40 years were polled about their</br>medical and social histories over a 10-year period. Data show that 205 participants reported developing MS. The rates of cigarette smoking in participants with MS were compared with those of 615 age- and</br>location-matched participants without MS. Which of the following best describes this study design?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Case series</br>b. Case-control</br>c. Cross-sectional</br>d. Historical cohort</br>e. Prospective cohort</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Case-control study  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “205 participants reported developing MS” = the study first identifies persons with the outcome (cases).<br /></br>• “Rates of cigarette smoking … were compared with those of 615 age- and location-matched participants without MS” = selects a control group without the outcome and compares prior exposure.<br /></br>• Data on past cigarette use were obtained from the participants’ histories (looking backward in time for exposure).</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>A case-control design always:<br /></br>1. Starts with outcome status (case vs control).<br /></br>2. Looks backward to measure prior exposure.<br /></br>3. Compares exposure odds between the two groups.<br /></br>This is ideal for rare diseases such as multiple sclerosis (≈0.2% incidence here).</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Identifying disease first and then assessing prior exposure = case-control.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (brief, high-yield)<br /></br>• Multiple sclerosis is an autoimmune demyelinating disease of the CNS.<br /></br>• Environmental risk factors include low vitamin D, EBV infection, and cigarette smoking; smoking is thought to increase inflammatory oxidative stress, hastening demyelination.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>(Not directly tested here, but recall:)<br /></br>• Acute flare: high-dose IV methylprednisolone.<br /></br>• Disease-modifying: interferon-β, glatiramer acetate, ocrelizumab, natalizumab, teriflunomide, etc.<br /></br>• MRI with T2 lesions disseminated in time and space is diagnostic gold standard.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Case series – Describes characteristics of a group with a disease; there is no control group or exposure comparison.  </p></br><p>c. Cross-sectional study – Measures exposure and outcome at a single point in time (“snapshot”); cannot establish temporal direction and doesn’t look backward for exposure after selecting cases.  </p></br><p>d. Historical cohort – Identifies exposure first (e.g., smokers vs nonsmokers in the past) and then looks forward through records to see who developed the outcome. Here the sequence is reversed.  </p></br><p>e. Prospective cohort – Enrolls participants based on exposure status in the present and follows them forward in real time for outcome development; again, opposite order from the vignette.</p></br><p>Key exam pitfall: Do not equate “10-year period” with a cohort; focus on whether the study starts with exposure (cohort) or outcome (case-control).</p>
160. A previously healthy 57-year-old man is admitted to the hospital for evaluation of a 7-hour history of palpitations. He takes no medications. He drinks six beers daily. He does not smoke. He is 168 cm 5 ft 6 in tall and weighs 70 kg 155 lb; BMI is 25 kg/m 2. His temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 110/min and irregularly irregular, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 126/70 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 97 percent. Examination shows no jugular venous distention. Cardiac examination shows no murmurs, rubs, or gallops. There is no pedal edema. Test of the stool for occult blood is negative. An ECG shows atrial fibrillation at a ventricular rate of 114/min with no ischemic changes. The physician decides to administer oral digoxin. Which of the following is the most appropriate way to record an order on this patient's chart?	Correct answer: F</br></br>a. Digoxin 125 μg PO qd</br>b. Digoxin 125 μg PO daily</br>c. Digoxin .125 mg PO daily</br>d. Digoxin 125 mcg PO qd</br>e. Digoxin 125.0 mcg PO daily</br>F. Digoxin 0.125 mg PO daily</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>F. Digoxin 0.125 mg PO daily  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Previously healthy … no medications” → first inpatient dose of a narrow-therapeutic-index drug.<br /></br>• Digoxin toxicity is common when doses are written or interpreted incorrectly, therefore the question is testing medication-safety (not cardiology management).  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Core Take-away)<br /></br>The safest prescription format must:<br /></br>1. Use a leading zero in front of a decimal point (0.125 mg, not .125 mg) to prevent a ten-fold overdose.<br /></br>2. Avoid trailing zeros (125.0 mcg can be misread as 1250 mcg).<br /></br>3. Express the dose in milligrams when possible; writing “μg” or “mcg” has been associated with misinterpretation as “mg.”<br /></br>4. Spell out “daily”; the abbreviation “qd” can be misread as “qid.”<br /></br>Choice F satisfies every Institute for Safe Medication Practices (ISMP) “Do Not Use” recommendation.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology / Clinical Relevance<br /></br>• Digoxin has a very narrow therapeutic window (target serum 0.5-0.9 ng/mL).<br /></br>• Accidental 10-fold dosing errors (e.g., 1.25 mg instead of 0.125 mg) can precipitate life-threatening arrhythmias, GI symptoms, visual changes, and hyper-K⁺.<br /></br>• Therefore exact, unambiguous written orders are mandatory.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>A. “Digoxin 125 μg PO qd”<br /></br>   – Problem 1: μg can be mistaken for mg.<br /></br>   – Problem 2: “qd” is on the Joint Commission “Do Not Use” list (can be misread as qid).  </p></br><p>B. “Digoxin 125 μg PO daily”<br /></br>   – μg still at risk of mg/μg mix-up.  </p></br><p>C. “Digoxin .125 mg PO daily”<br /></br>   – Missing leading zero (.125 mg) can be misread as 125 mg (1000-fold overdose).  </p></br><p>D. “Digoxin 125 mcg PO qd”<br /></br>   – “mcg” less risky than “μg” but “qd” still prohibited.  </p></br><p>E. “Digoxin 125.0 mcg PO daily”<br /></br>   – Trailing zero after decimal (125.0) can cause 10-fold error if decimal is not seen.<br /></br>   – mcg instead of mg.  </p></br><p>Correct answer F avoids all hazardous abbreviations and formatting errors:<br /></br>Digoxin 0.125 mg PO daily  </p></br><p>(Treatment pearl: Typical oral loading for atrial fibrillation is 0.5-1 mg divided; maintenance 0.125-0.25 mg daily adjusted for renal function.)</p>
161. A 37-year-old woman comes to the emergency department 12 hours after the onset of severe epigastric pain that radiates to her back. Two weeks ago, abdominal ultrasonography showed gallstones, and she began a low-fat diet. She has no other history of serious illness. On arrival, her temperature is 37.9 C 100.2  F, pulse is 130/min, respirations are 28/min and shallow, and blood pressure is 95/60 mm Hg. Abdominal examination shows distention and ecchymoses over the periumbilical area. There is diffuse severe tenderness of all quadrants. Bowel sounds are absent. Serum studies show a total bilirubin concentration of 1 mg/dl, amylase activity of 1400 U/L, and lipase activity of 950 U/L N=14-280. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. 24-Hour urine collection for measurement of amylase activity</br>b. CT scan of the abdomen with contrast</br>c. MRI of the abdomen</br>d. Endoscopic retrograde cholangiopancreatography</br>e. Paracentesis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. CT scan of the abdomen with IV contrast  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Severe epigastric pain radiating to the back,” amylase 1400 U/L, lipase 950 U/L → classic for acute pancreatitis.<br /></br>• Temperature 100.2 °F, pulse 130, BP 95/60 mm Hg, RR 28, absent bowel sounds, abdominal distention, Cullen sign (periumbilical ecchymosis) → worrisome markers of severe/necrotizing or hemorrhagic pancreatitis.<br /></br>• Gallstones documented 2 wks earlier → biliary pancreatitis is the probable etiology, but there is no evidence of obstructing CBD stone (bilirubin 1 mg/dL, no jaundice).</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Diagnosis of pancreatitis is already established (typical pain + &gt;3×-nl lipase/amylase).<br /></br>2. The immediate question is disease severity and complications (necrosis, hemorrhage, fluid collections) because vitals and physical exam suggest a fulminant course.<br /></br>3. Contrast-enhanced CT of the abdomen is the imaging modality of choice to:<br /></br>   • Confirm/quantify pancreatic necrosis.<br /></br>   • Detect hemorrhage, abscess, or early pseudocyst formation.<br /></br>   • Guide subsequent interventions (e.g., drainage, necrosectomy).</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Once pancreatitis is clinically diagnosed, CT with IV contrast is obtained only when the episode appears severe or the diagnosis/complications are unclear—exactly this patient’s scenario.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>• Gallstone temporarily lodges at the ampulla → pancreatic duct obstruction → premature activation of trypsinogen → autodigestion → inflammation, vascular damage (hemorrhage) → third-spacing → hypotension/tachycardia.<br /></br>• Cullen sign/Grey-Turner sign = retroperitoneal blood tracking to the skin, indicating hemorrhagic pancreatitis and high mortality.<br /></br>• Contrast CT delineates non-enhancing (necrotic) pancreatic tissue and peripancreatic fluid/blood.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests (Step 2 level)<br /></br>Initial management (before imaging results):<br /></br>• Aggressive lactated Ringer bolus, pain control (IV hydromorphone/fentanyl), NPO, NG tube if ileus, early enteral feeds once tolerated.<br /></br>• Antibiotics only if infected necrosis is proven.<br /></br>• ERCP within 24 h if cholangitis or persistent biliary obstruction (bilirubin ↑, jaundice, cholestatic LFTs).<br /></br>• Imaging timeline: CT at presentation if severe, or after 48–72 h if clinical status worsens or fails to improve.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. 24-hour urine amylase<br /></br>   • Rarely done; does not influence acute management. Serum lipase is already diagnostic.<br /></br>c. MRI of the abdomen<br /></br>   • Can visualize pancreatic ducts but is not first-line in the emergent setting; CT is faster, cheaper, and more widely available for assessing necrosis.<br /></br>d. Endoscopic retrograde cholangiopancreatography (ERCP)<br /></br>   • Invasive and can worsen pancreatitis. Indicated urgently only for gallstone pancreatitis WITH evidence of ongoing CBD obstruction/cholangitis (jaundice, cholangitis triad), which this patient lacks.<br /></br>e. Paracentesis<br /></br>   • Evaluates ascites, spontaneous bacterial peritonitis, or hemorrhage into the peritoneal cavity—not required here; bleeding is retroperitoneal (Cullen sign), and diagnosis/severity assessment is better with CT.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Students sometimes think “gallstones → do ERCP.” Remember: do ERCP acutely only if there is cholangitis or persistent obstruction. For severe pancreatitis without obstruction, image first with contrast CT.</p>
162. A 72-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 3-month history of progressive shortness of breath with exertion and nonproductive cough. She has systemic sclerosis scleroderma treated with lisinopril and prednisone. Her temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 90/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 130/80 mm Hg. Examination shows tight facial skin and telangiectasias over the cheeks. There is thick, taut skin over the fingers. Bilateral basilar crackles are heard. On cardiac examination, a right ventricular heave is palpated at the left sternal border where a grade 2/6, systolic ejection murmur is also heard best. A fixed, split S 2 and an S 3 gallop are heard. An ECG is shown. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Aortic stenosis</br>b. Cor pulmonale</br>c. Left ventricular hypertrophy</br>d. Pulmonary edema</br>e. Ventricular septal defect</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>b. Cor pulmonale (right-sided heart failure/RV hypertrophy secondary to pulmonary hypertension)</p></br><p>──────────────────────── Vignette Analysis</br>1. Underlying disease: “Systemic sclerosis (scleroderma)” → strongly associated with  • Pulmonary arterial hypertension (limited cutaneous type)  • Interstitial lung disease → chronic hypoxic vasoconstriction → pulmonary HTN.</br>2. Key cardiorespiratory findings: <br /></br>   • Progressive exertional dyspnea + non-productive cough (pulmonary cause)<br /></br>   • Bilateral basilar crackles (interstitial lung disease)<br /></br>   • Right-ventricular (RV) heave at LSB + grade 2/6 systolic ejection murmur at LSB (flow murmur across a dilated pulmonic valve)<br /></br>   • Fixed, split S2 (delayed closure of pulmonic valve due to ↑ PVR)<br /></br>   • S3 gallop (RV volume overload/failure)</br>3. ECG (not shown but classic for cor pulmonale): right-axis deviation, P pulmonale, RV strain pattern (tall R in V1 + T-wave inversion V1–V3).  </p></br><p>These constellation of pulmonary findings + signs of RV pressure/volume overload → cor pulmonale.</p></br><p>──────────────────────── Clinical Critical Reasoning (why the answer is B)</br>Stepwise thought process:</br>• Dyspnea + basilar crackles in a patient with scleroderma → suspect interstitial lung disease → chronic hypoxia → pulmonary hypertension.<br /></br>• Pulmonary hypertension → RV pressure overload → RV heave, pulmonic flow murmur, fixed split S2, right-sided S3.<br /></br>• Resultant isolated right-sided heart failure is the definition of cor pulmonale.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In systemic sclerosis, chronic pulmonary vascular disease produces pulmonary hypertension and isolated right-heart failure (cor pulmonale) with classic RV physical signs.</p></br><p>──────────────────────── Pathophysiology Review</br>Systemic sclerosis ➜ endothelial injury &amp; fibrosis of pulmonary arterioles or fibrotic ILD ➜ ↑ pulmonary vascular resistance ➜ pulmonary arterial hypertension ➜ RV pressure overload ➜ concentric RV hypertrophy ➜ eventual RV dilatation &amp; failure (cor pulmonale).  </p></br><p>Clinical manifestations:</br>• Exertional dyspnea, fatigue, syncope<br /></br>• RV heave, loud/“fixed” P2 or split S2, systolic ejection murmur over pulmonic area (↑ flow)<br /></br>• Right-sided S3, peripheral edema, hepatomegaly in later stages.</p></br><p>──────────────────────── Diagnosis &amp; Key Tests</br>First: Transthoracic echocardiogram → RV hypertrophy/dilatation, elevated estimated PASP.<br /></br>Gold standard: Right-heart catheterization (mean pulmonary artery pressure ≥25 mm Hg).<br /></br>CXR/HRCT: interstitial fibrosis; prominent pulmonary arteries.<br /></br>ECG: right-axis deviation, R/S &gt;1 in V1, RBBB pattern.</p></br><p>──────────────────────── Treatment (stepwise)</br>1. Treat underlying cause (interstitial lung disease, hypoxia).<br /></br>2. Pulmonary vasodilators for group 1 PAH: endothelin-receptor antagonists (bosentan), PDE-5 inhibitors (sildenafil), prostacyclin analogs (epoprostenol).<br /></br>3. Supplemental O2, diuretics for volume overload, careful use of vasodilators.<br /></br>4. Lung or heart-lung transplantation in end-stage disease.</p></br><p>──────────────────────── Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Aortic stenosis<br /></br>   • Murmur: harsh crescendo–decrescendo systolic murmur at RIGHT upper sternal border, radiates to carotids; delayed, diminished carotid upstroke; single S2.<br /></br>   • LV (not RV) heave.  None of these findings are described.</p></br><p>c. Left ventricular hypertrophy<br /></br>   • Would present with displaced, sustained PMI and LV S4; ECG shows high-voltage QRS in left-sided leads.<br /></br>   • No RV heave, no fixed split S2.</p></br><p>d. Pulmonary edema<br /></br>   • Acute crackles/wheezes, S3 from LV failure; bilateral interstitial/alveolar infiltrates on CXR.<br /></br>   • Would not give RV heave or fixed split S2; chronic progressive course favors pulmonary fibrosis with cor pulmonale, not acute edema.</p></br><p>e. Ventricular septal defect<br /></br>   • Pansystolic (holosystolic) murmur at LSB, often with thrill; wide variable splitting but not fixed.<br /></br>   • Unlikely to present for first time at age 72; no mention of cyanosis or volume overload signs on left side.</p></br><p>──────────────────────── Summary “One-Liner”</br>An elderly woman with systemic sclerosis, basilar crackles, pulmonic flow murmur, fixed split S2, and RV heave has pulmonary hypertension–induced right-sided heart failure—classic cor pulmonale.</p>
163. A 16-year-old girl is brought to the physician by her mother because she has not yet had a menstrual period. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. Her immunizations are up-to-date. She does not appear to be in acute distress. She is at the 75th percentile for height, 80th percentile for weight, and 55th percentile for BMI. Breast and pubic hair development are sexual maturity rating stage 1. Pelvic examination shows no abnormalities. Serum studies show a thyroid-stimulating hormone concentration of 2.4 μU/ml, follicle-stimulating hormone concentration of 76 mlU/mL, and prolactin concentration of 13 ng/ml. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Karyotype analysis</br>b. Measurement of serum estradiol concentration</br>c. MRI of the brain</br>d. 12-Day course of medroxyprogesterone therapy</br>e. EEG</br>F. No further evaluation is indicated at this time</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>A. Karyotype analysis  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 16-year-old with primary amenorrhea (no menses by 15–16 y).<br /></br>• Sexual maturity rating (Tanner) stage 1 breasts and pubic hair → absent estrogen effect and no adrenarche driven by ovaries.<br /></br>• FSH markedly elevated (76 mIU/mL; normal mid-teen ≈ 2–10) → hypergonadotropic hypogonadism = primary ovarian failure/gonadal dysgenesis.<br /></br>• Normal TSH and prolactin rule out thyroid disease &amp; hyperprolactinemia.<br /></br>These data place the defect at the level of the gonads; the next step in the standard algorithm is to determine the chromosomal cause → karyotype.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach to primary amenorrhea:<br /></br>1. Confirm pregnancy (always first, assumed done here).<br /></br>2. Examine secondary sexual characteristics.<br /></br>3. Measure FSH/LH.<br /></br>   • Low/normal FSH → central (hypogonadotropic) → pituitary/ hypothalamus imaging.<br /></br>   • High FSH → peripheral (hypergonadotropic) → karyotype ± pelvic US.<br /></br>Take-home: High FSH + no secondary sex characteristics = gonadal dysgenesis → karyotype analysis is diagnostic and guides counseling (Turner 45,X; 46,XY Swyer; 46,XX pure gonadal dysgenesis, etc.).  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Primary ovarian insufficiency before puberty means streak or non-functioning ovaries. Lack of estradiol leads to:<br /></br>• No breast development.<br /></br>• Elevated GnRH → ↑↑ FSH/LH via loss of negative feedback.<br /></br>Etiologies:<br /></br>• Turner syndrome (45,X or mosaics)—most common.<br /></br>• 46,XY complete gonadal dysgenesis (Swyer) → risk of gonadoblastoma.<br /></br>• 46,XX pure gonadal dysgenesis (FSH-R/LH-R defects, autoimmune, chemo/radiation).<br /></br>Karyotype distinguishes these entities and directs further management (cardiac &amp; renal US in Turner; bilateral gonadectomy if Y chromosome material).  </p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostics Review<br /></br>• Immediate: Karyotype.<br /></br>• Follow-up: pelvic ultrasound, renal/cardiac evaluation if Turner, consider estrogen replacement for pubertal induction.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Serum estradiol – Would be low but adds no new information; elevated FSH already tells you ovaries are not producing estrogen.<br /></br>C. MRI of the brain – Appropriate for hypogonadotropic amenorrhea (low/normal FSH), not when FSH is markedly elevated.<br /></br>D. 12-day medroxyprogesterone challenge – Used when estrogen is present but anovulation suspected (e.g., PCOS). She lacks estrogen (no breasts), so test would be negative and uninformative.<br /></br>E. EEG – Evaluates seizures, not amenorrhea.<br /></br>F. No further evaluation – Incorrect; age &gt;15 without menarche and absent puberty demands work-up.  </p></br><p>Core Concept<br /></br>In primary amenorrhea, a markedly elevated FSH/LH level pinpoints primary ovarian failure; the first diagnostic step is a karyotype to identify chromosomal causes such as Turner syndrome or Swyer syndrome.</p>
164. In a recent study of Americans, the following data were presented:</br>Group: Mortality Rate From Firearms per 100,000</br>Native – 2.2</br>African – 1.2</br>Caucasian – 0.8</br>Asian – 0.1</br>The authors concluded that, in the USA, a greater number of Native Americans died of injuries sustained from firearms than did Caucasian Americans. Which of the following is the most accurate interpretation of the</br>authors' conclusion?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Correct</br>b. Incorrect, because of the cohort effect</br>c. Incorrect, because the conclusion is based on rates</br>d. Incorrect, because the conclusion is not adjusted for socioeconomic factors</br>e. Incorrect, because the conclusion is not adjusted for urban/rural differences</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Incorrect, because the conclusion is based on rates (not absolute numbers)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Mortality rate per 100 000” = the study is reporting incidence density (deaths ÷ population) rather than raw counts.<br /></br>• Authors’ conclusion: “a greater number of Native Americans died … than did Caucasian Americans.”<br /></br> – Key phrase: “greater number” (absolute deaths).<br /></br> – The data provided concern only rates, not population sizes.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-by-step logic:<br /></br>1. Identify what the table shows: firearm-related mortality rates for each ethnic group.<br /></br>2. Recognize that rate × population size = absolute number of events.<br /></br>3. U.S. population size of Caucasians &gt;&gt; Native Americans.<br /></br>4. Therefore, even with a higher rate, Native Americans could still have far fewer total deaths.<br /></br>Take-home message: NEVER draw conclusions about absolute numbers when only relative rates are provided.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (concise, because pathophys not the testable focus)<br /></br>Firearm mortality is multifactorial (socioeconomics, geography, substance use, etc.), but the key epidemiologic concept tested here is the distinction between rate and count, not the underlying pathology.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Not applicable—question is epidemiologic, not diagnostic or therapeutic.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. “Correct” – Wrong. The statement is not correct for the reasons outlined above.<br /></br>b. “Incorrect, because of a cohort effect” – A cohort effect refers to differences attributable to being born/entering the study at different times. Nothing in the vignette invokes time-period or birth-year effects.<br /></br>d. “Incorrect, because the conclusion is not adjusted for socioeconomic factors” – Socioeconomic status may confound firearm mortality, but adjustment would affect rate comparison, not the specific misinterpretation of counts versus rates.<br /></br>e. “Incorrect, because the conclusion is not adjusted for urban/rural differences” – Same reasoning as (d): lack of adjustment could bias rate comparisons, but it does not explain why one cannot infer absolute numbers from rates alone.</p></br><p>Key misconception to avoid: Higher RATE ≠ higher NUMBER unless population sizes are equal.</p>
165. An 8-year-old girl with attention-deficit/hyperactivity disorder is brought to the office by her mother for a well-child examination. The mother says that her daughter has been generally healthy and has been doing well in school since beginning long-acting methylphenidate therapy 2 years ago. The mother is concerned about her daughter having to take the medication for an extended period of time. Medical history is otherwise unremarkable and she takes no other medications. The patient is at the 25th percentile for height and weight. Vital signs are normal. Physical examination discloses no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate response to the mother regarding this patient's need for medication?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Her current medication regimen will likely continue to benefit her into adulthood</br>b. She will likely need increased doses of the medication during adulthood</br>c. She will likely need to switch to a different medication as she approaches adulthood</br>d. She will outgrow the need for medication during late adolescence</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>Her current medication regimen will likely continue to benefit her into adulthood.</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Diagnosis: Attention-deficit/hyperactivity disorder (ADHD) already established and treated effectively with long-acting methylphenidate.<br /></br>• Key clues: 2 years of good symptom control, normal vitals/exam, mother worried about “extended period” of treatment.<br /></br>• Testing point: Long-term natural history of ADHD and the durability of stimulant benefit.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Does ADHD simply disappear in late adolescence? No. Long-term cohort studies show ~60% of affected children continue to meet partial or full criteria in adulthood and many still require medication.<br /></br>2. If the current stimulant works and is well-tolerated, best practice is to continue the same agent; no evidence that routine switching or escalating dosing is necessary.<br /></br>3. Therefore, the appropriate counseling is reassurance that the regimen can keep helping her into adult years.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: ADHD is a chronic neurodevelopmental disorder; stimulant efficacy and safety extend into adulthood, so ongoing treatment is often recommended.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• ADHD stems from dysregulation of frontostriatal circuits and catecholamine (dopamine &gt; norepinephrine) signaling.<br /></br>• Methylphenidate blocks DAT/NET, boosting synaptic dopamine/NE and improving executive function, impulse control, and attention.<br /></br>• Brain maturation does reduce symptom severity in some, but most patients maintain clinically significant symptoms into adult life.</p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>First-line pharmacotherapy:<br /></br>• Stimulants (methylphenidate, dextroamphetamine, mixed amphetamine salts).<br /></br>Second-line/adjuncts:<br /></br>• Atomoxetine, guanfacine XR, clonidine XR, bupropion.<br /></br>Monitoring: annual BP/HR, height/weight, sleep/appetite, diversion/misuse screening.<br /></br>If control satisfactory and side-effects minimal, remain on same drug/​dose; periodic drug “holidays” are not routinely advised but can be tried.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. “She will likely need increased doses of the medication during adulthood.”<br /></br>– Dose requirements are patient-specific; many adults maintain childhood doses or even need less as weight-based dosing becomes irrelevant and self-management skills improve. Routine up-titration is not expected.</p></br><p>c. “She will likely need to switch to a different medication as she approaches adulthood.”<br /></br>– No evidence that stimulants lose efficacy with age. Switching only if efficacy wanes or adverse effects occur.</p></br><p>d. “She will outgrow the need for medication during late adolescence.”<br /></br>– Common misconception. Only ~30-40% achieve full remission; majority continue to have symptoms impairing academic, occupational, or social functioning. Abrupt discontinuation risks relapse.</p></br><p>Summary<br /></br>ADHD commonly persists into adulthood. If a long-acting stimulant is effective and well-tolerated, continuing the same regimen provides sustained benefit, so reassure the parent that her daughter will likely keep gaining from the current medication as she matures.</p>
166. A 68-year-old man comes to the emergency department because of a 1-week history of severe low back pain that worsens with movement. He also has a 2-month history of fatigue and decreased appetite resulting in a 7- kg 15-lb weight loss. He has not had fever, night sweats, bowel incontinence, urinary retention, or weakness. He has hypertension treated with hydrochlorothiazide. He smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 30 years but quit 20 years ago. He does not drink alcohol or use illicit drugs. He is 178 cm 5 ft 10 in tall and weighs 77 kg 170 lb; BMI is 24 kg/m 2. Vital signs are within normal limits. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 99 percent. There is tenderness to palpation at L2-4. Muscle strength is 5/5 in the lower extremities. Deep tendon reflexes are normal at the knees and ankles; there is no clonus. Rectal tone is norma l. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Results of laboratory studies are shown: Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in evaluation?</br>Hemoglobin: 11.2 g/dL</br>Serum</br>Urea nitrogen: 54 mg/dL</br>Creatinine: 2.1 mg/dL</br>Protein, total: 7.8 g/dL</br>Albumin: 3.2 g/dL</br>Alkaline phosphatase: 67 U/L	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. CT scan of the chest</br>b. MRI of the spine</br>c. Renal ultrasonography</br>d. Serum and urine protein electrophoresis</br>e. Serum iron and ferritin concentrations</br>F. Serum parathyroid hormone concentration</br>g. Serum prostate-specific antigen concentration</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Serum and urine protein electrophoresis (SPEP + UPEP)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis – Key Clues</br>• 68-year-old man with new, severe low-back pain → think vertebral pathology (compression fracture, metastatic disease, multiple myeloma).<br /></br>• Constitutional symptoms: fatigue, ↓ appetite, 7-kg weight loss → malignancy or chronic inflammatory process.<br /></br>• Laboratory triad that screams “myeloma”:<br /></br>  – Mild normocytic anemia (Hgb 11.2 g/dL)<br /></br>  – Renal insufficiency (Cr 2.1 mg/dL, BUN 54 mg/dL)<br /></br>  – High globulin gap: total protein 7.8 g/dL – albumin 3.2 g/dL = 4.6 g/dL (↑)<br /></br>• Normal alkaline phosphatase (67 U/L) → argues against widespread blastic bone metastases (e.g., prostate) and Paget disease.<br /></br>• Neuro exam and sphincter tone are normal → no evidence of acute spinal cord compression; imaging can wait.</p></br><p>Combining bone pain + anemia + renal failure + elevated globulins → classic for multiple myeloma. The single best next step to confirm the suspected monoclonal gammopathy is SPEP and UPEP.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning / Take-Home Point<br /></br>USMLE loves the “CRAB” mnemonic for multiple myeloma work-up:<br /></br>• C – hyperCalcemia<br /></br>• R – Renal failure<br /></br>• A – Anemia<br /></br>• B – Bone pain/lytic lesions<br /></br>When ≥1 of these findings is present, the first diagnostic test is serum AND urine protein electrophoresis looking for an M-spike or Bence-Jones proteins.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>• Plasma-cell clone over-produces a single immunoglobulin or free light chains → monoclonal (M-protein).<br /></br>• Light chains precipitate in renal tubules → cast nephropathy (renal failure).<br /></br>• Neoplastic plasma cells secrete osteoclast-activating factors → lytic vertebral lesions → back pain, pathologic fractures.<br /></br>• Bone-marrow crowding → normocytic anemia, infections (↓ normal Ig).<br /></br>• Total protein increases, albumin falls → ↑ globulin gap.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment / Diagnostic Algorithm (overview)<br /></br>1. Screening: SPEP &amp; UPEP ± serum free light-chain assay.<br /></br>2. If positive → bone-marrow biopsy (≥10 % clonal plasma cells) and skeletal survey/MRI/PET.<br /></br>3. Therapy (beyond scope of question): proteasome inhibitors (bortezomib), IMiDs (lenalidomide), corticosteroids ± autologous stem-cell transplant.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. CT scan of the chest<br /></br>   • Would evaluate a primary lung mass, but lung cancer does not explain the elevated globulin gap or renal failure. Myeloma work-up should come first.</p></br><p>b. MRI of the spine<br /></br>   • Indicated for neurologic deficits or suspected cord compression. Normal strength, reflexes, and rectal tone make urgent MRI unnecessary now.</p></br><p>c. Renal ultrasonography<br /></br>   • Looks for obstructive uropathy; obstructive pattern would not raise total protein or globulin gap and wouldn’t cause bone pain.</p></br><p>e. Serum iron and ferritin<br /></br>   • Would help classify anemia but does not address renal failure or bone pain. You must rule out myeloma first.</p></br><p>f. Serum parathyroid hormone (PTH)<br /></br>   • Hyperparathyroidism causes bone pain and renal stones, but would show hypercalcemia; nothing in the vignette suggests this.</p></br><p>g. Serum prostate-specific antigen (PSA)<br /></br>   • Prostate cancer metastasis causes low-back pain and ↑ alkaline phosphatase (blastic lesions). Normal ALP and elevated globulin gap make this less likely.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Core Takeaway<br /></br>Back pain + anemia + renal insufficiency + elevated total protein in an older adult → think multiple myeloma. The initial diagnostic test is serum and urine protein electrophoresis to detect a monoclonal protein.</p>
167. Four days after undergoing uncomplicated left total hip replacement, a hospitalized 75-year-old man has a 1-day history of progressive confusion. He began ambulating on postoperative day 1 and previously had been alert, fully oriented, and cognitively intact. He has been receiving hydrocodone for pain as needed; he has requested one dose during the past 24 hours. He has hypertension well controlled with atenolol. He has no other history of serious illness and takes no other medications. On admission, the patient said he did not drink alcohol; today, his brother says that the patient drinks six to eight 12-oz beers daily. The patient's temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 90/min, respirations are 14/min, and blood pressure is 130/80 mm Hg. Physical examination shows lateral gaze palsy. The surgical incision is clean, dry, and nonerythematous. Neurologic examination shows ataxia. On mental status examination, he is alert but not oriented to person, place, or time. He identifies only one of three objects after six attempts. He recalls zero of three objects after 5 minutes. Results of a complete blood count, measurement of serum electrolyte and glucose concentrations, and liver and renal function tests are within the reference ranges. Urine toxicology screening is negative. An ECG shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Administration of lorazepam</br>b. Administration of vitamin B 1 thiamine</br>c. Blood and urine cultures</br>d. CT scan of the head</br>e. Discontinuation of atenolol</br>F. Discontinuation of hydrocodone</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>b. Administration of vitamin B₁ (thiamine)</p></br><p>───────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Progressive confusion” 4 days post-op in a long-time ethanol user.<br /></br>• Triad present:<br /></br>  – Ocular findings → “lateral gaze palsy” (ophthalmoplegia)<br /></br>  – Cerebellar findings → “ataxia”<br /></br>  – Global confusion → disorientation, very poor short-term memory<br /></br>• Normal vitals, normal basic labs → metabolic, infectious or hemodynamic causes less likely.<br /></br>These three key findings in a drinker = classic pattern recognition for Wernicke encephalopathy.</p></br><p>───────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning / Core Take-away<br /></br>Wernicke encephalopathy is an acute neuropsychiatric emergency caused by thiamine deficiency in patients with malnutrition or chronic alcohol use. Immediate IV thiamine is required to halt progression (and must always precede any glucose administration). Delay in therapy risks irreversible Korsakoff syndrome.</p></br><p>───────────</br>Essential Pathophysiology<br /></br>• Alcohol → ↓ intestinal absorption &amp; ↑ urinary loss of thiamine.<br /></br>• Thiamine is a cofactor for pyruvate dehydrogenase, α-ketoglutarate dehydrogenase, branched-chain ketoacid dehydrogenase, and transketolase. Deficiency → impaired cerebral energy metabolism, especially in mammillary bodies, medial thalamus, periaqueductal gray → confusion, ataxia, ophthalmoplegia.</p></br><p>───────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>First-line:<br /></br>1. Immediate IV thiamine 100 mg (often daily for 3–5 days)<br /></br>2. THEN give IV dextrose if needed.<br /></br>3. Nutritional optimization, Mg²⁺ repletion.</p></br><p>No imaging or cultures are necessary before empiric thiamine when the triad is present; treatment is safe, cheap, and time-sensitive.</p></br><p>───────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Lorazepam<br /></br>   • Used for alcohol withdrawal (autonomic hyperactivity, tremor, hallucinations, seizures).<br /></br>   • This patient lacks tremor, tachycardia, hypertension, diaphoresis—signs of withdrawal. Giving benzodiazepines masks mental status and does not treat thiamine deficiency.</p></br><p>c. Blood &amp; urine cultures<br /></br>   • Fever, leukocytosis, tachycardia, or surgical wound infection would prompt cultures. None are present.</p></br><p>d. CT head<br /></br>   • Useful to rule out hemorrhage, stroke, or subdural hematoma, but the classic Wernicke triad in a heavy drinker makes thiamine repletion a “treat-now” diagnosis. Imaging should not delay therapy.</p></br><p>e. Discontinue atenolol<br /></br>   • β-blockers do not cause confusion, ataxia, or ophthalmoplegia; BP and HR are normal.</p></br><p>f. Discontinue hydrocodone<br /></br>   • Opioid intoxication would present with pinpoint pupils, respiratory depression; patient took only one dose and is not hypoventilating.</p></br><p>───────────</br>High-Yield Memory Aid<br /></br>“CAN” of beer → Confusion, Ataxia, Nystagmus/ophthalmoplegia: think Wernicke; give thiamine NOW.</p>
168. A 42-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 1-day history of increasingly severe pain of his right knee associated with redness and swelling for the past 6 hours. He has had two episodes of pain in his right great toe over the past 8 months and missed 2 weeks of work in construction during each episode. He has been otherwise healthy. Examination of the right knee shows marked tenderness, swelling, and erythema. Laboratory findings are within the reference range except for a serum uric acid concentration of 7.6 mg/dl. Examination of synovial fluid aspirate shows uric acid crystals. Which of the following is the most appropriate initial pharmacotherapy?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Allopurinol</br>b. Celecoxib</br>c. lndomethacin</br>d. Probenecid</br>e. Sulfinpyrazone</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Indomethacin  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Increasingly severe pain … redness and swelling … 1-day history” = acute monoarticular arthritis.<br /></br>• Prior identical attacks in the 1st MTP (“podagra”) → classic gout history.<br /></br>• Synovial fluid “uric acid crystals” seals the diagnosis.<br /></br>Key question: What drug do you start during an acute flare?</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>First-line therapy for an acute gout attack is a potent NSAID (e.g., indomethacin, naproxen).<br /></br>• Rapidly lowers inflammation by inhibiting COX-1/COX-2 → ↓ prostaglandins &amp; neutrophil chemotaxis in the joint.<br /></br>• Must be given immediately; urate-lowering drugs are postponed until the flare resolves.<br /></br>Take-home: Treat the flare first (NSAID, colchicine, steroids). Start prophylaxis only after the patient is pain-free.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>Gout = precipitation of monosodium urate crystals in joints due to hyperuricemia. Crystal phagocytosis → neutrophil activation → intense inflammatory response (IL-1, TNF-α, prostaglandins) → severe pain, erythema, warmth, swelling. NSAIDs dampen this cytokine cascade within hours.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Acute flare options<br /></br>1. NSAIDs (indomethacin, naproxen) – first-line in most patients.<br /></br>2. Colchicine – if NSAID contraindicated (e.g., PUD, CKD).<br /></br>3. Intra-articular or systemic glucocorticoids – if unable to take NSAID/colchicine.  </p></br><p>Chronic (inter-critical) management – start only after flare subsides<br /></br>• Xanthine-oxidase inhibitors: allopurinol, febuxostat.<br /></br>• Uricosurics: probenecid, sulfinpyrazone.<br /></br>• Pegloticase (IV recombinant uricase) for refractory disease.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Allopurinol – Lowers uric acid chronically by inhibiting xanthine oxidase. Initiating it during an acute attack can PROLONG/WORSEN the flare because rapid serum urate shifts mobilize tissue urate stores. Start ≥1–2 weeks after complete symptom resolution, with NSAID/colchicine prophylaxis.  </p></br><p>b. Celecoxib – A COX-2–selective NSAID. It can treat an acute flare, but indomethacin (non-selective NSAID) has the most evidence and is the “classic” NBME answer. Celecoxib is reserved for patients at high GI-bleed risk who are not at increased CV risk.  </p></br><p>d. Probenecid – Uricosuric; increases renal excretion of uric acid. Ineffective and potentially harmful in acute gout (can mobilize urate and precipitate stones). Use only for long-term prophylaxis in under-excretors with good renal function.  </p></br><p>e. Sulfinpyrazone – Another uricosuric agent; same issues as probenecid. Not used to terminate an acute attack.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Starting a urate-lowering drug (allopurinol, probenecid, sulfinpyrazone) during the flare rather than waiting until the inflammation has resolved.  </p></br><p>Core Concept<br /></br>Acute gout = inflammatory pain → treat inflammation first (NSAID/colchicine/steroid). Long-term urate manipulation comes later.</p>
169. A 14-month-old girl is brought to the physician by her father because of a 3-week history of cough. One month ago, she had the onset of nasal congestion and runny nose. Two days later, she was noted to have a cough. The nasal congestion and runny nose resolved spontaneously after 7 days; the cough has not improved. Her father describes the cough as a dry, smoker's cough. The patient has had similar symptoms twice durin the past 6 months. Each episode resolved spontaneously after 2 weeks. At the age of 3 months, she had bronchiolitis. She has eczema treated with topical hydrocortisone. She was born at term following an uncomplicated pregnancy and spontaneous vaginal delivery. She receives no other medications. Her mother has a childhood history of asthma, and her father has seasonal allergies. The patient attends day care 5 days weekly. She coughs intermittently in the examination room. She is at the 20th percentile for length and 10th percentile for weight. Her temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 72/min, respirations are 22/min, and blood pressure is 103/72 mm Hg. The lungs are clear to auscultation. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Asthma</br>b. Chronic bronchitis</br>c. Cystic fibrosis</br>d. Hypersensitivity pneumonitis</br>e. lgA immunodeficiency</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Asthma  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Dry, smoker-sounding cough” that began a couple of days after an apparent viral URI and has persisted &gt;3 weeks.<br /></br>• Two similar self-limited cough episodes in the past 6 months.<br /></br>• Personal atopy (eczema) and strong atopic family history (mother with childhood asthma, father with allergic rhinitis).<br /></br>• Normal vitals, clear lungs, normal growth curve (slightly low but tracking), completely normal physical examination between cough paroxysms.  </p></br><p>These clues point to cough-variant asthma—an asthma phenotype in young children in which chronic or recurrent cough is the sole or predominant manifestation.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Typical viral URIs resolve within 7–10 days; cough persisting &gt;3 weeks raises concern for an underlying airway-hyperreactive process triggered by the infection. Recurrent, self-limited episodes, personal/family atopy, and a normal inter-episode examination are classic for asthma. The absence of wheeze does NOT exclude asthma in toddlers; small-airway obstruction may manifest only as cough.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: In infants/toddlers, recurrent or persistent cough after viral illnesses plus an atopic background = asthma until proven otherwise.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Asthma is a chronic airway inflammation with intermittent bronchial hyperresponsiveness. Triggers (viral infection, allergens, cold air) → mast-cell mediator release, eosinophilic inflammation → airway edema, mucus, and smooth-muscle constriction. In cough-variant asthma, mechanoreceptor stimulation produces cough without obvious wheeze.</p></br><p>Treatment / Diagnostics (high-yield)<br /></br>1. Diagnostic confirmation: trial of inhaled bronchodilator ± controller therapy or spirometry with bronchodilator response once the child is old enough (≥5 y).<br /></br>2. Acute management: short-acting β2-agonist (albuterol).<br /></br>3. Controller if frequent: low-dose inhaled corticosteroid (e.g., budesonide, fluticasone).<br /></br>4. Allergen avoidance, influenza vaccine.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Chronic bronchitis – Requires chronic productive cough for ≥3 mo/year over ≥2 years, almost unheard of in 14-month-olds, classically seen in adult smokers or CF patients. No sputum, clubbing, or hypoxia here.  </p></br><p>c. Cystic fibrosis – Would show failure to thrive, steatorrhea, salty skin, recurrent bacterial pneumonias/bronchiolitis beginning in infancy, persistent crackles/wheezes, digital clubbing. This patient has only intermittent dry cough and otherwise normal exam.  </p></br><p>d. Hypersensitivity pneumonitis – Presents with acute febrile, dyspneic episodes hours after exposure to environmental antigen (moldy hay, bird droppings). Bibasilar crackles, diffuse interstitial infiltrates on CXR. None of those findings or exposure history is present.  </p></br><p>e. IgA immunodeficiency – Causes recurrent otitis media, sinusitis, and bacterial pneumonia due to lack of mucosal IgA; often associated with chronic diarrhea and autoimmune disease. Episodes would be frequent bacterial infections, not isolated post-viral cough with normal exam. Serum IgA would be low; patient otherwise well.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Assuming wheeze must be present to diagnose asthma. Up to 30% of toddlers with asthma present primarily with chronic cough (cough-variant asthma).</p>
170. A previously healthy 24-year-old nurse is brought to the emergency department because of weakness of the lower extremities for 24 hours. She takes no medications. Her temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 100/min, and blood pressure is 112/78 mm Hg. Muscle strength is 4/5 in the upper and lower extremities. Laboratory studies show:</br>Serum</br>Na⁺: 134 mEq/L</br>Cl⁻: 94 mEq/L</br>K⁺: 2.4 mEq/L</br>HCO₃⁻: 34 mEq/L</br>Mg²⁺: 2.4 mEq/L</br>Urea nitrogen: 14 mg/dL</br>Glucose: 118 mg/dL</br>Urine</br>Na⁺: 46 mEq/L</br>K⁺: 64 mEq/L</br>Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient's hypokalemia?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Aldosterone-secreting tumor</br>b. Hypomagnesemia</br>c. Juxtaglomerular cell hyperplasia</br>d. Surreptitious diuretic use</br>e. Surreptitious laxative use</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection</br>a. Aldosterone-secreting tumor (primary hyperaldosteronism / Conn syndrome)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• Progressive “weakness of the lower extremities” → classic symptom of severe hypokalemia.<br /></br>• Serum K⁺ 2.4 mEq/L + HCO₃⁻ 34 mEq/L → hypokalemic metabolic alkalosis.<br /></br>• Urine K⁺ 64 mEq/L (high) → kidneys are actively losing K⁺ (not GI loss).<br /></br>• Urine Na⁺ 46 mEq/L (≥ 20 mEq/L) → natriuresis rather than avid Na⁺ retention.<br /></br>• Normal Mg²⁺ (2.4 mEq/L) rules out hypomagnesemia-mediated kaliuresis.<br /></br>These findings point to a renal mineralocorticoid effect with the “aldosterone escape” phenomenon explaining the high urine Na⁺ despite total-body Na⁺ excess.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step 1: Hypokalemic metabolic alkalosis → think of (i) vomiting, (ii) diuretics, (iii) hyperaldosteronism.<br /></br>Step 2: Urine chloride or sodium ≥ 20 rules out vomiting (would be low).<br /></br>Step 3: Normal Mg²⁺ and the absence of volume depletion (BP 112/78, no orthostasis) argue against loop/thiazide diuretics.<br /></br>Step 4: A kidney source of mineralocorticoid excess with “escape” is left → primary hyperaldosteronism from an adrenal adenoma.</p></br><p>Core Take-away</br>Primary hyperaldosteronism presents with:</br>• HTN or inappropriately normal BP for a high ECF state<br /></br>• Metabolic alkalosis, hypokalemia<br /></br>• High urinary Na⁺ and K⁺ (aldosterone escape)<br /></br>• Normal serum magnesium</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>Aldosterone (zona glomerulosa) ↑ ENaC activity in the collecting duct:<br /></br>1. Na⁺ reabsorption → ↑ ECF volume and initial hypertension.<br /></br>2. K⁺ &amp; H⁺ secretion → hypokalemia + metabolic alkalosis.<br /></br>ECF expansion triggers pressure-natriuresis (“escape”), so urinary Na⁺ is no longer low even though aldosterone remains high.</p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests</br>Confirm with:</br>• Plasma aldosterone-to-renin ratio (high).<br /></br>• Adrenal CT or adrenal venous sampling.<br /></br>Treatment:</br>• Unilateral adenoma → laparoscopic adrenalectomy.<br /></br>• Bilateral hyperplasia or poor surgical candidate → mineralocorticoid receptor antagonists (spironolactone, eplerenone).</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>b. Hypomagnesemia<br /></br>   – Low Mg²⁺ impairs ROMK closure → renal K⁺ wasting. Serum Mg²⁺ would be &lt; 1.7 mEq/L (here it’s 2.4), and metabolic alkalosis is not obligatory.</p></br><p>c. Juxtaglomerular cell hyperplasia (renin-secreting tumor / secondary hyperaldosteronism)<br /></br>   – Would also cause hypokalemic alkalosis, but marked hypertension (angiotensin II vasoconstriction) and often high plasma renin activity. Not distinguishable in labs provided, but Conn syndrome is vastly more common, and BP here is only 112/78.</p></br><p>d. Surreptitious diuretic use<br /></br>   – Loop/thiazide diuretics: hypokalemic metabolic alkalosis and high urine Na⁺/K⁺. Clue that argues against it: normal Mg²⁺ (loop/thiazide often lower Mg²⁺) and absence of volume depletion signs (tachycardia with hypotension, orthostasis).</p></br><p>e. Surreptitious laxative use<br /></br>   – Produces hypokalemic metabolic acidosis (loss of bicarbonate in stool), low urine Na⁺ (&lt; 20 mEq/L) because kidneys conserve Na⁺. Neither finding is present.</p></br><p>Bottom line: Renal K⁺ loss with metabolic alkalosis, normal Mg²⁺, and urine studies consistent with mineralocorticoid excess make an aldosterone-secreting adrenal tumor the most likely etiology.</p>
171. A 47-year-old man comes to the office because of a 6-month history of a nonpainful, nonitchy lesion over his left forearm. Three years ago, he underwent a renal transplant for chronic renal failure secondary to lgA</br>nephropathy. His only medication is tacrolimus. Vital signs are within normal limits. A photograph of the lesion is shown. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most likely</br>diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Actinic keratosis</br>b. Contact dermatitis</br>c. Lichen plan us</br>d. Molluscum contagiosum</br>e. Squamous cell carcinoma</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Squamous cell carcinoma (SCC)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Chronicity: “6-month history” → rules out most acute inflammatory rashes.<br /></br>• Quality: “nonpainful, non-itchy” → argues against eczematous or allergic processes.<br /></br>• Location: “left forearm” = chronically sun-exposed site.<br /></br>• Major clue: solid-organ transplant patient on tacrolimus → long-term immunosuppression increases the incidence and aggressiveness of cutaneous SCC by &gt;100-fold.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Transplant recipient = think skin cancer (especially SCC).<br /></br>Step 2: Sun-exposed area with a slowly enlarging keratotic lesion = classic pattern for SCC.<br /></br>Take-home message: In immunosuppressed patients, any persistent keratotic or ulcerative lesion on sun-exposed skin should be presumed SCC until proven otherwise.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Malignant proliferation of keratinocytes in the epidermis.<br /></br>• Risk factors: UV radiation, fair skin, chronic immunosuppression (cyclosporine, tacrolimus), chronic wounds, arsenic.<br /></br>• Mechanism: UV-induced DNA damage and impaired immune surveillance allow oncogenic mutations (e.g., p53) to accumulate.<br /></br>• Clinical manifestations: Firm, hyperkeratotic papule/nodule; may ulcerate or bleed; rarely painful or pruritic.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Diagnostic: Full-thickness shave or punch biopsy (look for invasive cords of atypical squamous cells with keratin pearls).<br /></br>Treatment:<br /></br>1. Low-risk lesions → excisional surgery with 4–6-mm margins or electrodesiccation &amp; curettage.<br /></br>2. High-risk/large/face lesions → Mohs micrographic surgery.<br /></br>3. Adjuvant options: topical 5-fluorouracil, imiquimod, radiation for non-surgical candidates.<br /></br>4. Close skin surveillance is mandatory in transplant recipients.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Actinic keratosis<br /></br>• Usually &lt;6 mm, “sand-paper” feel; precursor to SCC but not typically a solitary larger lesion after 6 months.<br /></br>• Histology shows atypical keratinocytes confined to basal layer.<br /></br>• Would be described as a rough, scaly patch rather than a distinct lesion of concern.</p></br><p>b. Contact dermatitis<br /></br>• Intensely pruritic, erythematous, often vesicular; develops hours–days after exposure; resolves with removal of allergen.<br /></br>• Pruritus and acute course absent here.</p></br><p>c. Lichen planus<br /></br>• Pruritic, purple, polygonal, planar papules &amp; plaques with Wickham striae; often wrists/ankles, mucosal involvement.<br /></br>• This lesion is non-itchy and solitary.</p></br><p>d. Molluscum contagiosum<br /></br>• Dome-shaped, flesh-colored, umbilicated papules (2–5 mm); multiple in immunosuppressed.<br /></br>• Lesion in vignette likely bigger, keratotic, not umbilicated.</p></br><p>Key difference: Only SCC matches a solitary, non-healing, hyperkeratotic lesion on sun-exposed skin in an immunosuppressed patient.</p>
172. rA. 62-year-old man with chronic obstructive pulmonary disease comes to the physician because of a 1-month history of leg swelling and progressive shortness of breath. He has not had fever or cough. He smoked one and one-half packs of cigarettes daily for 45 years but quit 1 year ago. His medications are theophylline and albuterol and ipratropium inhalers. His temperature is 36.7 C 98 F, pulse is 96/min, respirations are 22/min, and blood pressure is 150/76 mm Hg. Examination shows jugular venous distention 10 cm above the sternal angle. Breath sounds are markedly decreased throughout all lung fields. Cardiac examination shows decreased heart sounds with no murmurs, gallops, or rubs. The liver edge is palpated 3 cm below the right costal margin. There is 2+ pitting edema to the knees. His FEV 1 is 1.05 L 40 percent of predicted. Arterial blood gas analysis on room air shows:</br>pH: 7.42</br>PCO₂: 36 mm Hg</br>PO₂: 45 mm Hg</br>HCO₃⁻: 30 mEq/L</br>An x-ray of the chest shows hyperinflated lungs with apical bullae. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Polysomnography</br>b. Home oxygen therapy</br>c. Mechanical ventilation</br>d. Bullectomy</br>e. Lung volume reduction operation</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Home oxygen therapy</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Chronic lung disease: Long smoking history, severe COPD (FEV1 = 40 % predicted; hyperinflated lungs with apical bullae).<br /></br>• Evidence of right-sided heart failure (cor pulmonale): JVD 10 cm, hepatomegaly, peripheral edema.<br /></br>• Severe resting hypoxemia: PaO₂ 45 mm Hg on room air.<br /></br>• ABG shows chronic respiratory acidosis with metabolic compensation (HCO₃⁻ 30 mEq/L, normal pH) → indicates longstanding CO₂ retention typical of advanced COPD.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Long-term (≥15 h/day) supplemental oxygen is the only intervention proven to improve survival in COPD. Indications:<br /></br>1. PaO₂ ≤55 mm Hg OR SaO₂ ≤88 % at rest.<br /></br>2. PaO₂ 55-59 mm Hg (SaO₂ ≤89 %) with cor pulmonale, right-heart failure, or polycythemia.<br /></br>This patient meets criterion 1 (PaO₂ 45 mm Hg) and criterion 2 (signs of cor pulmonale), so the next step is to start home oxygen therapy.</p></br><p>Take-home message: In COPD, severe resting hypoxemia and/or cor pulmonale mandate long-term oxygen because it decreases pulmonary hypertension, lowers mortality, and relieves symptoms.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Chronic hypoxic vasoconstriction → pulmonary hypertension → right ventricular hypertrophy and failure (cor pulmonale). Supplemental O₂ reverses hypoxic vasoconstriction, reducing right-heart strain and improving survival.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics Review<br /></br>First-line life-prolonging therapies in severe COPD:</br>• Smoking cessation<br /></br>• Vaccinations (influenza, pneumococcal)<br /></br>• Long-term O₂ when criteria met<br /></br>Lung volume reduction surgery, bullectomy, transplant are considered only when maximal medical therapy (bronchodilators, pulmonary rehab, O₂) fails.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Polysomnography – Appropriate to evaluate suspected obstructive sleep apnea; does not address daytime PaO₂ 45 mm Hg or cor pulmonale due to COPD. No snoring, daytime somnolence, or obesity clues given.<br /></br>c. Mechanical ventilation – Indicated for acute exacerbation with respiratory distress, CO₂ retention, or acidemia (pH &lt;7.25). Patient is stable, pH normal; not needed.<br /></br>d. Bullectomy – Reserved for symptomatic giant bullae compressing adjacent lung, causing recurrent pneumothorax, or refractory dyspnea despite O₂. The primary problem here is diffuse hypoxemia, not mass effect from bullae.<br /></br>e. Lung volume reduction operation – Considered in selected emphysema patients after maximal medical therapy including O₂; improves quality of life but not first-line, and mortality benefit is limited to upper-lobe–predominant emphysema with low exercise capacity. Must optimize medical therapy (including home O₂) first.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Students sometimes focus on FEV1 % predicted to decide on surgery, forgetting that hypoxemia criteria trump spirometry when choosing long-term O₂. Always check PaO₂/SaO₂ values and signs of cor pulmonale.</p>
173. A 21-year-old man is brought to the emergency department because of a 2-hour history of muscle spasms of the face and neck; his eyes appear to be rolling up inside his head. He began treatment with haloperidol 24 hours ago for paranoid ideation. His temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 96/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 130/82 mm Hg. He is fully oriented and cognitively intact, but he is adamant that he has been poisoned. Which of the following is most appropriate to add to his medication regimen?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Amitriptyline</br>b. Benztropine</br>c. Chlorpromazine</br>d. Flumazenil</br>e. Naloxone</br>F. Nifedipine</br>g. Physostigmine</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Benztropine  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Muscle spasms of the face and neck … eyes appear to be rolling up” = acute dystonic reaction (includes torticollis, oculogyric crisis, trismus).<br /></br>• “Began haloperidol 24 h ago” – acute dystonia classically appears within hours-1 day after a potent D₂-blocking antipsychotic is started or the dose is increased.<br /></br>• Normal vital signs and mental status argue against neuroleptic malignant syndrome (fever, rigidity, autonomic instability) or delirium.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Identify extrapyramidal symptom (EPS) timeline.<br /></br> • 4 h–4 d → Acute dystonia<br /></br> • 4 d–4 wk → Akathisia<br /></br> • Weeks–months → Parkinsonism<br /></br> • Months–years → Tardive dyskinesia  </p></br><p>Step 2: Treat acute dystonia. The pathophysiology is dopamine blockade → relative excess of muscarinic cholinergic tone in the nigrostriatal pathway. Anticholinergic medication (benztropine or IV/IM diphenhydramine) quickly restores balance.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Acute dystonia from D₂ antagonists is reversed with centrally acting antimuscarinics.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• High-potency typical antipsychotics (haloperidol, fluphenazine) strongly block D₂ receptors in the basal ganglia.<br /></br>• Dopamine normally inhibits acetylcholine interneurons; blockade removes this brake → increased ACh activity → sustained involuntary muscle contraction.<br /></br>• Anticholinergics (benztropine, trihexyphenidyl) or H₁ antihistamines with strong antimuscarinic action (diphenhydramine) blunt the excess cholinergic tone.  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>1. Stop or lower the offending antipsychotic dose if possible.<br /></br>2. Give benztropine 1–2 mg IM/IV or diphenhydramine 25–50 mg IM/IV; relief usually within minutes.<br /></br>3. Prophylaxis: maintain low doses or co-prescribe oral benztropine in high-risk patients.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Amitriptyline – Tricyclic antidepressant; anticholinergic but used chronically for depression/neuropathic pain, not for acute dystonia. Risk of arrhythmia; onset too slow.<br /></br>c. Chlorpromazine – Another typical antipsychotic; would worsen dopamine blockade and EPS.<br /></br>d. Flumazenil – Benzodiazepine antagonist used for reversing benzo overdose (respiratory depression, somnolence). Not relevant; patient is awake and spastic, not sedated.<br /></br>e. Naloxone – μ-opioid antagonist for opioid toxicity (pinpoint pupils, respiratory depression). No opioid signs.<br /></br>f. Nifedipine – Dihydropyridine calcium-channel blocker for hypertension or vasospasm; has no effect on dystonia.<br /></br>g. Physostigmine – Acetylcholinesterase inhibitor used to reverse severe anticholinergic toxicity (e.g., atropine overdose). Would intensify cholinergic tone and worsen dystonia.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Confusing acute dystonia with akathisia (subjective restlessness treated with β-blocker or benzodiazepine) or neuroleptic malignant syndrome (requires dantrolene/bromocriptine). Recognize the timing and focal muscle spasms.</p>
174. A 60-year-old man with stage 4 chronic kidney disease comes to the office for a follow-up examination. He also has type 2 diabetes mellitus and hypertension. Medications are insulin, lisinopril, furosemide, amlodipine, and aspirin. Vital signs are temperature 37.0 C 98.6 F, pulse 80/min, respirations 15/min, and blood pressure 145/85 mm Hg. Cardiopulmona [Y examination discloses no abnormalities. There is 1+ lower extremity edema. Laboratory studies show a serum intact parathyroid hormone concentration of 258 pg/ml N=10-60. Measurement of additional serum laboratory studies is most likely to show which of the following?</br>Phosphorus Calcium Calcitriol	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Decreased decreased decreased</br>b. Decreased increased decreased</br>c. Decreased increased increased</br>d. Increased decreased decreased</br>e. Increased decreased increased</br>F. Increased increased increased</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Increased phosphorus – decreased calcium – decreased calcitriol  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Stage 4 chronic kidney disease” → GFR 15-29 mL/min.<br /></br>• “Serum intact PTH 258 pg/mL (↑)” → secondary hyperparathyroidism.<br /></br>Key physiologic consequences of CKD to keep in mind:<br /></br>1. ↓ GFR → ↓ phosphate filtration → phosphate retention (hyperphosphatemia).<br /></br>2. Diseased renal tubules ↓ 1-α-hydroxylase activity → ↓ conversion of 25-OH-vitamin D to 1,25-OH2-vitamin D (calcitriol).<br /></br>3. ↓ Calcitriol → ↓ intestinal Ca²⁺ absorption → hypocalcemia.<br /></br>4. High phosphate itself also complexes with Ca²⁺, further lowering ionized Ca²⁺.<br /></br>5. Low Ca²⁺ + high phosphate stimulate PTH release → secondary hyperparathyroidism (already shown).  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Pattern: CKD ➔ phosphate retention + ↓ calcitriol ➔ hypocalcemia ➔ ↑ PTH.<br /></br>The question asks what additional labs will show. Follow the sequence above:<br /></br>• Phosphorus: increased.<br /></br>• Calcium: decreased.<br /></br>• Calcitriol: decreased.<br /></br>Only option d matches all three.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Secondary hyperparathyroidism of renal failure = ↑PTH, ↑phosphate, ↓Ca²⁺, ↓calcitriol.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Etiology<br /></br>• Failing kidneys cannot excrete phosphate or activate vitamin D.<br /></br>Mechanisms<br /></br>1. Hyperphosphatemia—due to reduced filtration.<br /></br>2. Decreased calcitriol—loss of 1-α-hydroxylase in proximal tubule.<br /></br>3. Hypocalcemia—less gut absorption, Ca²⁺–phosphate precipitation.<br /></br>4. Compensatory ↑PTH (“secondary hyperparathyroidism”), which tries to normalize Ca²⁺ by mobilizing bone Ca²⁺.<br /></br>Clinical manifestations<br /></br>• Bone pain, fractures (renal osteodystrophy), pruritus, vascular/soft-tissue calcifications.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>First-line management of secondary hyperparathyroidism in CKD:<br /></br>1. Dietary phosphate restriction.<br /></br>2. Non–calcium-based phosphate binders (sevelamer, lanthanum).<br /></br>3. Active vitamin D analogs (calcitriol, alfacalcidol) or calcimimetics (cinacalcet) if PTH remains high.<br /></br>Monitoring: serial Ca²⁺, phosphate, PTH, alkaline phosphatase.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Decreased phosphorus – decreased calcium – decreased calcitriol<br /></br>• Would suggest renal phosphate wasting (e.g., Fanconi) not CKD; phosphate is high, not low, in CKD.  </p></br><p>b. Decreased phosphorus – increased calcium – decreased calcitriol<br /></br>• Hypercalcemia with low phosphate occurs in primary hyperparathyroidism, not secondary hyperparathyroidism of CKD.  </p></br><p>c. Decreased phosphorus – increased calcium – increased calcitriol<br /></br>• Matches granulomatous disease (sarcoid) or exogenous vitamin D toxicity, not CKD.  </p></br><p>e. Increased phosphorus – decreased calcium – increased calcitriol<br /></br>• Calcitriol cannot be high in advanced CKD because the kidney cannot activate vitamin D.  </p></br><p>f. Increased phosphorus – increased calcium – increased calcitriol<br /></br>• Seen in vitamin D intoxication; Ca²⁺ would be high, not low, in CKD secondary hyperparathyroidism.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Students remember “high PTH → high Ca²⁺” (true for primary hyperparathyroidism) but forget that in renal failure the kidney cannot raise Ca²⁺ because vitamin D activation is impaired and phosphate retention drives Ca²⁺ down.  </p></br><p>Take-Home Message: Secondary hyperparathyroidism of CKD = ↑PTH, ↑phosphate, ↓Ca²⁺, ↓calcitriol.</p>
175. A 7-year-old boy is brough to the office by his mother for an initial well-child visit to establish care. The patient's family recently emigrated from Eastern Europe. The mother is concerned about her son's behavior at home and at school. He has been easily distracted, has difficulty completing his chores at home, has been fidgety during classes, and often disrupts others by talking out of turn. His mother says that as an infant he underwent surgical correction of a cleft palate and a heart defect, but she does not have his medical records with her today. The patient is at the 5th percentile for height and weight and the 10th percentile for BMI. Examination shows an elongated face with narrow palpebral fissures, low-set ears, a broad nasal bridge, micrognathia, downward turned mouth, and small teeth. Results of which of the following laboratory studies are most likely to be abnormal in this patient?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Erythrocyte sedimentation rate</br>b. Hematocrit</br>c. Leukocyte count</br>d. Serum calcium concentration</br>e. Serum creatinine concentration</br>F. Serum thyroid-stimulating hormone concentration</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Serum calcium concentration (↓)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis – spotting 22q11.2-deletion (DiGeorge / velocardiofacial)<br /></br>• Surgical repair of CLEFT PALATE → abnormal development of the 3rd/4th pharyngeal arches.<br /></br>• Repair of a HEART DEFECT in infancy → typical conotruncal lesions (tetralogy of Fallot, truncus arteriosus, interrupted aortic arch).<br /></br>• Characteristic FACIES: elongated face, narrow palpebral fissures, broad nasal bridge, micrognathia, low-set ears, down-turned mouth, small teeth.<br /></br>• Behavioral / learning problems (ADHD-like, speech delay) common in velocardiofacial syndrome.<br /></br>These features together are classic for a 22q11.2 microdeletion.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>22q11.2 deletion → failed development of the 3rd &amp; 4th pharyngeal pouches<br /></br>• Parathyroid hypoplasia → ↓ PTH → chronic hypocalcemia<br /></br>• Thymic hypoplasia → T-cell immunodeficiency (lymphopenia may be present early)<br /></br>• Conotruncal cardiac anomalies<br /></br>The most consistent laboratory abnormality across the lifespan is hypocalcemia. Therefore a serum calcium level is most likely to be abnormal in this patient.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: When you see cleft palate + conotruncal heart disease + characteristic facies, think 22q11.2 deletion and look for hypocalcemia from parathyroid aplasia.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Gene defect: Microdeletion on chromosome 22q11.2 (TBX1 gene region).<br /></br>• Embryology: Failure of 3rd/4th pharyngeal pouch derivatives (thymus, inferior &amp; superior parathyroids).<br /></br>• Resulting problems<br /></br>  – Immunodeficiency (↓ mature T-cells, recurrent viral/fungal infections)<br /></br>  – Hypocalcemia (tetany, seizures, prolonged QT, chronic subtle symptoms)<br /></br>  – Conotruncal cardiac malformations<br /></br>  – Craniofacial anomalies<br /></br>  – Neuropsychiatric disorders (ADHD, autism spectrum, schizophrenia risk)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostic / Treatment Pearls<br /></br>Diagnostics<br /></br>• FISH or microarray confirms 22q11.2 deletion.<br /></br>• Labs: ↓ Ca²⁺, ↓ PTH, variable ↓ T-cell counts, normal total WBC.<br /></br>Treatment<br /></br>• Calcium &amp; calcitriol supplementation for hypocalcemia.<br /></br>• Thymic transplant or prophylactic antibiotics / immunizations if severe T-cell deficiency.<br /></br>• Early intervention for speech, behavioral, and learning issues.<br /></br>• Surgical repair of cardiac / palatal defects as needed.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Answers Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Erythrocyte sedimentation rate – Elevated in inflammatory or autoimmune disease (e.g., JIA, temporal arteritis). No chronic inflammation is described here.<br /></br>b. Hematocrit – Anemia could occur with cyanotic heart disease, but this child is postoperative, not cyanotic, and no pallor/fatigue is noted.<br /></br>c. Leukocyte count – Total WBC is often NORMAL; only the T-cell subset is decreased. Routine CBC may miss the immunodeficiency.<br /></br>e. Serum creatinine concentration – Renal dysfunction is not part of the classic 22q11.2 phenotype described.<br /></br>f. Serum thyroid-stimulating hormone – Thyroid disease is not specifically increased in 22q11.2 deletion; no goiter, growth pattern, or symptoms suggest hypothyroidism.</p></br><p>Key difference: only low serum calcium directly results from the hallmark parathyroid hypoplasia of 22q11.2 deletion.</p>
176. A 4-year-old boy is brought to the emergency department 30 minutes after the sudden onset of shortness of breath and wheezing at a birthday party. Witnesses report that he was acting normally until he was noted to be in distress; they did not see him choke on anything. He has no history of serious illness and receives no medications. His temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 100/min, respirations are 24/min, and blood pressure is 90/60 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 95 percent. Examination shows decreased breath sounds over the left hemithorax. lnspiratory and expiratory chest x-rays are shown. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Asthma</br>b. Foreign body aspiration</br>c. Laryngotracheobronch itis croup</br>d. Pneumothorax</br>e. Rib fracture</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b.
