Foreign body aspiration  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Sudden onset” of respiratory distress in a previously healthy child at a party → think aspiration event.<br /></br>• Unilateral (left-sided) “decreased breath sounds” → localizes the problem to one lung.<br /></br>• Normal pulse-ox (95 %) and only mild tachypnea suggest ventilation of the other lung is intact.<br /></br>• Inspiratory + expiratory films are provided → classic way to look for air-trapping from an obstructing foreign body.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Key diagnostic step: In foreign-body obstruction of a mainstem/segmental bronchus, the involved lung cannot exhale normally (ball-valve effect).<br /></br>• Inspiratory film: both lungs look relatively symmetric.<br /></br>• Expiratory film: normal lung deflates and becomes denser, while the obstructed lung stays hyperlucent and hyperinflated, +/- mediastinal shift to the contralateral side.<br /></br>This pattern is virtually pathognomonic for foreign body aspiration and is not seen in asthma, croup, rib fracture, or pneumothorax.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Sudden unilateral wheeze/decreased breath sounds in a toddler + expiratory hyperinflation on x-ray = foreign body aspiration.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Commonly a peanut or small toy lodges in a bronchus (R &gt; L overall, but either side possible).<br /></br>• Creates a one-way valve: air enters during inspiration but cannot escape on expiration → focal air-trapping, over-distention, and wheeze.<br /></br>• Untreated, can progress to obstructive emphysema, atelectasis, or infection.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>1. Immediate rigid bronchoscopy (both diagnostic and therapeutic).<br /></br>2. If complete airway obstruction: back blows/chest thrusts (infants) or Heimlich (children) en route to definitive bronchoscopy.<br /></br>3. Post-removal CXR to rule out residual fragments or complications.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Asthma<br /></br>• Typically bilateral, diffuse expiratory wheezes; onset not as abrupt from asymptomatic to distress within seconds.<br /></br>• CXR usually normal or shows bilateral hyperinflation, not unilateral findings.</p></br><p>c. Laryngotracheobronchitis (croup)<br /></br>• Viral prodrome, barking cough, inspiratory stridor; upper-airway issue, not unilateral decreased breath sounds.<br /></br>• Classic AP neck x-ray shows “steeple sign,” not hyperinflated lung.</p></br><p>d. Pneumothorax<br /></br>• Would also give unilateral decreased breath sounds, BUT expiratory film is not needed for diagnosis.<br /></br>• CXR shows visible pleural line with absent lung markings beyond, not persistent hyperinflation.<br /></br>• Sudden pleuritic pain often reported; mediastinum may shift toward opposite side with tension physiology.</p></br><p>e. Rib fracture<br /></br>• Presents with focal chest wall pain and tenderness; breath sounds generally normal unless secondary pneumothorax.<br /></br>• CXR would show cortical break, not unilateral over-inflation.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Confusing unilateral hyperlucency from air-trapping (foreign body) with pneumothorax. Always look for vascular markings (present in air-trapping, absent in pneumothorax) and the pleural line of a collapsed lung in pneumothorax.</p>
177. A hospitalized 59-year-old man has undergone three unsuccessful attempts at endoscopic sclerotherapy of esophageal varices during the past 4 days. On admission, he had a 1-week history of recurrent upper gastrointestinal bleeding. He has necrotizing pancreatitis, splenic vein thrombosis, type 2 diabetes mellitus, and hypertension. His medications are insulin, hydrochlorothiazide, lisinopril, and pancrelipase. He has a history of alcohol use disorder but has not consumed alcohol during the past 8 years. He has no other history of psychiatric illness. He is an inmate in a federal prison and has 18 years remaining on his sentence. He asks that no further efforts to control the bleeding be undertaken. His brother, who has not visited the patient in 6 years, says this is the patient's way of committing suicide; the brother insists that efforts to extend the patient's life be continued. The patient appears cachectic but alert. His pulse is 104/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 88/68 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on 4 Umin of oxygen by nasal cannula shows an oxygen saturation of 94 percent. Physical examination shows scattered ecchymoses and petechiae over the upper extremities. On mental status examination, he provides an accurate account of his current condition and the probable outcome, including death, if he continues to bleed. He says he is down but then says he means tired. He reports no suicidal ideation. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Abide by the patient's request</br>b. Attempt to obtain emergency legal guardianship of the patient for medical decision-making</br>c. Consult the prison authorities</br>d. Continue treatment in accordance with the brother's wishes</br>e. Perform complete neurocognitive testing</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>• Abide by the patient’s request  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>Key phrases pointing to intact decision-making capacity<br /></br>• “Alert,” “provides an accurate account of his current condition and the probable outcome, including death” → understands information and appreciates consequences.<br /></br>• “He says he is down but then says he means tired. He reports no suicidal ideation” → no evidence of depression-driven impaired judgment or desire for self-harm.<br /></br>• Consistent, repeated refusal of further therapy (“asks that no further efforts … be undertaken”).  </p></br><p>No red flags for delirium (or hepatic encephalopathy), intoxication, severe depression with suicidal intent, or other cognitive deficits are described; vitals and examination do not suggest acute encephalopathy. Therefore he has capacity.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Decision-making capacity = ability to (1) understand information, (2) appreciate consequences, (3) reason about options, (4) communicate a choice. When present, the competent patient’s autonomy is paramount. Surrogates, family, physicians, or prison authorities cannot override.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: A capacitated adult has the absolute right to refuse any medical intervention, even life-saving care.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (brief)<br /></br>Esophageal variceal bleeding → life-threatening hemorrhage. Endoscopic sclerotherapy unsuccessful; mortality without further intervention is high. Understanding this prognosis yet still refusing therapy underscores intact capacity.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics (context)<br /></br>Standard next steps after failed endoscopic therapy might include TIPS or balloon tamponade, but these require consent. Here, the patient refuses; ethical focus moves from treatment options to respecting autonomy.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Emergency legal guardianship – Only indicated if patient lacks capacity. He demonstrates capacity, so guardianship would be unethical and illegal.<br /></br>c. Consult prison authorities – Prison officials have no medical decision-making authority over a competent inmate; their role is custodial, not surrogate.<br /></br>d. Continue treatment per brother – A surrogate’s input is irrelevant while the patient himself is competent and expresses a clear choice.<br /></br>e. Complete neurocognitive testing – Capacity is a clinical, task-specific assessment done at bedside. No evidence presented suggests need for formal cognitive testing; delaying to test would disrespect his expressed wishes.</p></br><p>Common pitfall: Equating a refusal of care with suicidal intent. Capacity assessment hinges on cognition and understanding, not on the perceived desirability of the choice.</p>
178. A 12-year-old girl is brought to the emergency department by her parents because of a 2-hour history of severe weakness involving the right side of her face. The weakness has been present since the patient awoke from sleep. She is now drooling from the right side of her mouth. She has had an upper respiratory illness for the past 3 days, with symptoms of nasal congestion, cough, and muffling of sounds and pain in her right ear. Medical history is otherwise unremarkable. She has been taking acetaminophen as needed for the ear pain. Vital signs are normal. The patient is awake and alert. Her speech is dysarthric. The right side of her face is severely weakened, with inability to sustain eye closure against resistance. The remainder of the physical examination discloses no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most likely natural evolution of her condition if left untreated?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Gradual improvement over several months</br>b. Improvement with normalization in 2 to 3 days</br>c. Progression to involve the left limbs</br>d. Progression to involve the left side of the face</br>e. Progression to involve the right limbs</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>a. Gradual improvement over several months</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• “Awoke from sleep” with complete unilateral facial weakness → acute, painless, lower-motor-neuron facial nerve palsy (Bell palsy).<br /></br>• Weakness involves the forehead (“inability to sustain eye closure”) → rules out an upper-motor-neuron stroke, which spares the forehead.<br /></br>• Recent URI/ear pain → classic viral or post-infectious trigger that causes edema of CN VII in the facial canal.<br /></br>• No extremity weakness, sensory loss, or cerebellar signs → local, not central, lesion.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step 1: Localize – isolated, complete ipsilateral CN VII deficit = peripheral facial nerve palsy.<br /></br>Step 2: Etiology – most common in children is idiopathic/viral (Bell palsy) ± otitis media–related inflammation.<br /></br>Step 3: Natural history – 70–90 % recover spontaneously; improvement begins in weeks, maximal recovery in 3–6 mo.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Bell palsy is usually self-limited; most patients recover gradually over months even without therapy.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Likely viral (HSV-1 reactivation, VZV, or nonspecific URI) → inflammation &amp; edema in the narrow facial canal → ischemia/demyelination of CN VII.<br /></br>• Lower-motor-neuron signs: loss of forehead wrinkling, inability to close eye, drooping mouth, loss of corneal reflex (efferent limb).<br /></br>• Children have an excellent prognosis because axonal degeneration is uncommon.</p></br><p>Treatment / Diagnostic Pearl</br>• First-line (if &lt;72 h) = oral prednisone ± acyclovir; artificial tears, eye patch at night.<br /></br>• Lyme titers/EMG only if atypical exam or endemic exposure.<br /></br>• Imaging unnecessary unless progressive or recurrent.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>b. “Improvement with normalization in 2–3 days”<br /></br> Bell palsy rarely resolves that quickly; edema takes weeks to regress. A transient facial droop that vanishes in ≤72 h would suggest a TIA or hemiplegic migraine, both inconsistent with this exam (LMN involvement).</p></br><p>c. “Progression to involve the left limbs”<br /></br> Would indicate a brain-stem stroke or demyelinating lesion causing crossed findings (ipsilateral face, contralateral body). Normal vitals and isolated facial palsy argue strongly against.</p></br><p>d. “Progression to involve the left side of the face”<br /></br> Bilateral sequential facial palsy is uncommon and usually reflects Guillain–Barré, sarcoidosis, or Lyme. No systemic or neuropathic signs here.</p></br><p>e. “Progression to involve the right limbs”<br /></br> Ipsi-facial + ipsi-limb weakness implies a pontine lesion extending into corticospinal fibers, which would already produce extremity findings at presentation, not appear later in an otherwise healthy child.</p></br><p>Therefore, gradual spontaneous recovery over several months (choice A) is the expected natural course if untreated.</p>
179. A 56-year-old woman comes to the clinic for examination prior to undergoing a dental procedure. She will be visiting her dentist frequently for multiple procedures to have braces placed on her teeth because of severe temporomandibular joint pain; she is concerned about her risk for infection. She has mitral valve prolapse and intermittent palpitations. She also has hypertension and osteopenia. Her only medication is atenolol. Her blood pressure is 110/60 mm Hg. Examination shows no abnormalities. Antibiotic prophylaxis is indicated for this patient for which of the following procedures?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Orthodontic appliance adjustment</br>b. Placement of orthodontic brackets</br>c. Teeth cleaning</br>d. Tooth extraction</br>e. No prophylaxis is indicated</br></br></br><p>Correct answer<br /></br>E. No prophylaxis is indicated</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette analysis – what matters &amp; why<br /></br>• “Mitral valve prolapse” – common but LOW-risk valve lesion.<br /></br>• No history of infective endocarditis, no prosthetic valve, no cyanotic congenital heart disease, no cardiac transplant with valvulopathy → therefore NOT a high-risk cardiac condition per current AHA/ACC guidelines.<br /></br>• Planned procedures (brackets, extraction, cleaning, appliance adjustment) are all dental, but antibiotic prophylaxis is dictated first by the cardiac condition, not the procedure type.  </p></br><p>Key pattern: Only four cardiac groups get prophylaxis for dental work.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical critical reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Ask “Does the patient have a high-risk cardiac lesion?”<br /></br> • Prosthetic heart valve or material? No<br /></br> • Previous infective endocarditis?   No<br /></br> • Certain congenital heart dz?    No<br /></br> • Cardiac transplant + valvulopathy? No<br /></br>→ She is NOT in a prophylaxis group, so the answer is “no prophylaxis,” independent of the dental work planned.</p></br><p>Take-home concept: For the USMLE, memorize that mitral valve prolapse (with or without mild regurgitation) is NOT an indication for infective endocarditis prophylaxis.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Brief pathophysiology review<br /></br>Dental manipulation can seed the bloodstream with viridans streptococci, which can colonize abnormal endocardium. However, the absolute risk is only clinically significant in the four “high-risk” cardiac settings listed above. In low-risk lesions (e.g., MVP, bicuspid aortic valve, ASD, acquired valvular disease such as rheumatic or mitral regurgitation), the risk/benefit ratio of routine prophylaxis is unfavorable (antibiotic adverse events &gt; prevented infections).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Management pearls<br /></br>Prophylaxis regimen when truly indicated:<br /></br>• First-line: Amoxicillin 2 g PO 30–60 min before procedure.<br /></br>• Alternatives for penicillin-allergic: Cephalexin, clindamycin, azithromycin, or clarithromycin (dose and route per guideline).<br /></br>No prophylaxis is needed for:<br /></br>• Local oral anesthetic injections, radiographs, placement/adjustment of braces, shedding of deciduous teeth, bleeding from oral trauma, etc.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the other options are wrong<br /></br>A. Orthodontic appliance adjustment – Even if manipulation of hardware occurs, guidelines are based on cardiac risk. MVP alone does not qualify.<br /></br>B. Placement of orthodontic brackets – Same reasoning; also considered a low-risk dental maneuver.<br /></br>C. Teeth cleaning – Does involve gingival manipulation (would warrant prophylaxis in a high-risk heart patient), but our patient’s cardiac status doesn’t meet criteria.<br /></br>D. Tooth extraction – Highest bacterial-load dental procedure, but prophylaxis still not given because the deciding factor is the ABSENCE of a high-risk cardiac lesion.<br /></br>E. No prophylaxis – Correct because patient lacks an indication.</p></br><p>Common pitfall: Students often remember “dental work → antibiotics for valve disease” and forget that the 2007 guideline revision eliminated routine prophylaxis for MVP, rheumatic valvular disease, and most acquired lesions.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Core memory aid<br /></br>“PROS”:<br /></br>P – Prosthetic valve/material<br /></br>R – pRevious infective endocarditis<br /></br>O – congenital ‘O2-poor’ (cyanotic) or repaired with prosthetic material, or Residual defect<br /></br>S – cardiac transplant with valvular dysSfunction<br /></br>If none of the above, no dental prophylaxiS.</p>
180. A 62-year-old man comes to the physician for a preoperative evaluation before undergoing elective cataract removal. He has a 3-month history of progressive mild shortness of breath with exertion. He takes no medications. He drinks three beers daily. His blood pressure is 150/92 mm Hg. Examination shows no other abnormalities. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hematocrit: 26 percent</br>Mean corpuscular volume: 75 µm3</br>Leukocyte count: 6,000 /mm3</br>Platelet count: 700,000 /mm3</br>Prothrombin time: 11 sec INR = 1</br>Serum</br>Glucose: 89 mg/dL</br>Creatinine: 1.6 mg/dL</br>Alkaline phosphatase: 142 U/L</br>AST: 55 U/L</br>Urine</br>Glucose: 2+</br>Protein: 1+</br>Which of the following is most likely to have prevented this patient's hematologic condition?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Annual blood pressure measurement</br>b. Avoidance of alcohol</br>c. Avoidance of radiation exposure</br>d. Colorectal cancer screening</br>e. Measurement of serum vitamin 812 cobalamin concentration every 2 years</br>F. Thyroid function tests every 2 years</br>g. Daily multivitamin supplementation</br>h. Subcutaneous erythropoietin therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Colorectal cancer screening  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 62-year-old man with new exertional dyspnea → think anemia.<br /></br>• Hematocrit 26% (low) with MCV 75 µm³ → microcytic anemia.<br /></br>• Platelet count 700 000/mm³ → marked reactive thrombocytosis, classically accompanies iron-deficiency anemia.<br /></br>• No mention of overt bleeding; creatinine mildly high but MCV is low (anemia of CKD is normocytic).<br /></br>Key pattern: “Older adult + microcytic anemia + thrombocytosis” → chronic occult gastrointestinal blood loss, most worrisome source = colorectal carcinoma or large adenomatous polyp.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Iron-deficiency anemia in a man &gt;50 y/o is gastrointestinal blood loss until proven otherwise. Detecting and removing premalignant polyps/carcinoma through recommended screening colonoscopy (starting at age 45–50 for average-risk patients) would have eliminated the occult bleeding and therefore prevented the iron-deficiency state.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Adequate colorectal cancer screening prevents the most common cause of iron-deficiency anemia in older adults.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Colorectal tumors ulcerate → chronic microscopic blood loss → depletion of iron stores → microcytosis, hypochromia.<br /></br>• Low iron levels stimulate thrombopoietin and IL-6 → reactive thrombocytosis.<br /></br>• Resulting ↓O₂-carrying capacity produces exertional dyspnea.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Work-up of suspected iron-deficiency anemia: CBC with indices, ferritin level (↓), transferrin saturation (↓), stool guaiac, and colonoscopy/upper endoscopy.<br /></br>First-line treatment once source is identified: endoscopic removal or surgical resection of lesion + oral ferrous sulfate supplementation.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Annual blood pressure measurement – detects hypertension, unrelated to iron loss microcytosis.<br /></br>b. Avoidance of alcohol – Chronic alcohol use causes folate deficiency (macrocytic) or liver-related anemia, not microcytic iron-deficiency with thrombocytosis.<br /></br>c. Avoidance of radiation exposure – Prevents aplastic anemia/pancytopenia, not isolated microcytic anemia with high platelets.<br /></br>e. Q2-year serum vitamin B12 – Would prevent macrocytic megaloblastic anemia.<br /></br>f. Thyroid function tests every 2 years – Hypothyroidism causes normo-/macrocytic anemia.<br /></br>g. Daily multivitamin – Typical multivitamin contains only 18 mg elemental iron; would not keep up with chronic blood loss.<br /></br>h. Subcutaneous erythropoietin – Treats anemia of CKD; does not address iron loss and is not preventive.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Assuming any microcytic anemia is dietary in origin; always rule out occult GI bleeding in adults, particularly via appropriate colorectal cancer screening.</p>
181. A 27-year-old man with asthma comes to the emergency department because of progressive shortness of breath and wheezing for 24 hours. His symptoms began 1 week ago with increasing wheezing. Treatment with inhaled J3-adrenergic agonists and corticosteroids has been ineffective. He appears mildly dehydrated and in moderate to severe respiratory distress with labored breathing and use of accessory muscles. His pulse is 90/min, respirations are 30/min, and blood pressure is 130/80 mm Hg. Arterial blood gas analysis on room air shows: </br>pH: 7.25</br>PCO2: 51 mm Hg</br>PO2: 65 mm Hg</br>HCO3⁻: 20 mEq/L</br>Which of the following is the most likely cause of these findings?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Metabolic acidosis with a compensatory respiratory alkalosis</br>b. Metabolic alkalosis with a compensatory respiratory acidosis</br>c. Mixed respiratory and metabolic acidosis</br>d. Pure respiratory acidosis</br>e. Respiratory acidosis with a compensatory metabolic alkalosis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection</br>c. Mixed respiratory and metabolic acidosis</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• “Progressive shortness of breath … wheezing … inhaled β-agonists and corticosteroids ineffective” → severe asthma exacerbation (status asthmaticus).<br /></br>• “Moderate to severe respiratory distress, RR 30/min, use of accessory muscles” → impending ventilatory failure.<br /></br>• ABG: pH 7.25 (acidic), PCO₂ 51 mm Hg (↑), HCO₃⁻ 20 mEq/L (↓) → both an elevated CO₂ and a decreased bicarbonate.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step 1: Identify the primary disorder(s).<br /></br>• If respiratory acidosis were the only problem, HCO₃⁻ should be normal or slightly ↑ (renal retention).<br /></br>• If metabolic acidosis were the only problem, PCO₂ should be ↓ (respiratory compensation).<br /></br>Here, PCO₂ is ↑ (respiratory acidosis) AND HCO₃⁻ is ↓ (metabolic acidosis) → two primary acid–base disturbances.</p></br><p>Step 2: Check appropriate compensation to confirm mixed vs compensated disorder.<br /></br>Acute respiratory acidosis compensation rule: ΔHCO₃⁻ ≈ +1 mEq/L for every +10 mm Hg rise in PCO₂.<br /></br>PCO₂ rise: 51 − 40 = 11 mm Hg → expected HCO₃⁻ ≈ 24 + 1 ≈ 25 mEq/L.<br /></br>Actual HCO₃⁻ = 20 mEq/L (5 mEq/L lower than expected) → a simultaneous metabolic acidosis, not compensation.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: When the direction of change in HCO₃⁻ is opposite to what renal compensation predicts, a second primary disorder is present.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Respiratory acidosis: Severe asthma causes airway obstruction → hypoventilation → CO₂ retention.<br /></br>• Metabolic acidosis: Hypoxemia and increased work of breathing → anaerobic metabolism → lactic acidosis, lowering serum HCO₃⁻.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review</br>Management priorities in status asthmaticus with impending respiratory failure:<br /></br>1. High-dose inhaled/albuterol nebulizers + ipratropium.<br /></br>2. IV corticosteroids (methylprednisolone).<br /></br>3. Magnesium sulfate IV for bronchodilation.<br /></br>4. Non-invasive ventilation; early intubation if mental status, rising CO₂, or exhaustion worsen.<br /></br>5. Treat metabolic acidosis indirectly by correcting hypoxia (oxygen, bronchodilation, ventilation). Sodium bicarbonate rarely indicated unless severe pH &lt; 7.1 with hemodynamic instability.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Metabolic acidosis with a compensatory respiratory alkalosis<br /></br>• Would show low pH or normal pH with low HCO₃⁻ and appropriately low PCO₂ (hyperventilation). This patient has high PCO₂, not low.</p></br><p>b. Metabolic alkalosis with a compensatory respiratory acidosis<br /></br>• Expect elevated pH, elevated HCO₃⁻, and mild PCO₂ elevation. Vignette shows acidic pH and low HCO₃⁻.</p></br><p>d. Pure respiratory acidosis<br /></br>• Predicted HCO₃⁻ would be normal/slightly elevated (acute) or markedly elevated (chronic). The markedly low HCO₃⁻ here rules this out.</p></br><p>e. Respiratory acidosis with a compensatory metabolic alkalosis<br /></br>• HCO₃⁻ would be elevated, not decreased. Compensation never moves variables past normal in the opposite direction.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Assuming any deviation in HCO₃⁻ is “compensation.” Always apply compensation rules; when the actual value deviates in the wrong direction, suspect a mixed disorder.</p>
182. A 16-year-old girl comes to the office because of a 1-month history of green, sticky discharge from the right nipple. She initially noticed the discharge on her bra and was also able to express it manually. She has not had breast pain or skin changes. During the past 3 months, she has had a 4.5-kg 10-lb weight gain despite no change in appetite. She also has had frequent frontal headaches since beginning oral contraceptive therapy 3 months ago. Six months ago, she was diagnosed with major depressive disorder and was started on fluoxetine therapy. Menses occur at regular 28-day intervals. Her last menstrual period was 2 weeks ago. She is sexually active with one partner and uses condoms inconsistently. On examination, scant greenish discharge can be expressed from the right breast. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Ductal ectasia</br>b. Hypothyroid ism</br>c. Medication adverse effect</br>d. Pregnancy</br>e. Prolactinoma</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Ductal ectasia  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Green, sticky discharge,” “scant,” “expressed,” and “unilateral (right breast)” are the key descriptors.<br /></br>• No breast pain, skin changes, or palpable mass.<br /></br>• Regular menses and normal sexual development argue against systemic endocrine causes.<br /></br>• The color (green-brown), viscosity, and unilateral nature strongly point to benign ductal ectasia.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Pattern-recognition rule:<br /></br>• Benign duct pathology (ductal ectasia or intraductal papilloma) → unilateral, spontaneous discharge that is colored (green-brown, serosanguinous).<br /></br>• Endocrine or medication-induced galactorrhea → usually bilateral, milky/clear, often accompanied by menstrual irregularity.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Colored, sticky, unilateral nipple discharge in a young woman with otherwise normal breast/ endocrine findings is classic for ductal ectasia.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Ductal ectasia = dilation and inflammation of subareolar ducts → breakdown of duct wall → lipid-laden debris → green-brown, sticky discharge.<br /></br>Risk factors: multiparity, smoking, age &gt;40, but it can present in adolescents and young adults.<br /></br>Course: benign; may resolve or require excision if persistent.  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>1. Breast ultrasound ± mammography (age dependent) to exclude mass.<br /></br>2. If imaging benign → reassurance; consider duct excision if bothersome or recurrent.<br /></br>3. Smoking cessation if applicable.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Hypothyroidism – Can increase TRH → ↑PRL → bilateral milky galactorrhea + menstrual irregularities, fatigue, cold intolerance, constipation; discharge not green.<br /></br>c. Medication adverse effect – SSRIs, OCPs, antipsychotics raise prolactin, but produce bilateral milky/clear discharge; color and unilateral nature don’t fit.<br /></br>d. Pregnancy – Usually bilateral colostrum (thin/yellow-white), and patient typically has amenorrhea or delayed menses. Here menses are regular.<br /></br>e. Prolactinoma – Causes bilateral milky galactorrhea, headaches, and classically oligomenorrhea/amenorrhea; rarely produces colored discharge. This patient’s menses are normal, and discharge color is atypical.  </p></br><p>Bottom line: Unilateral green-brown sticky nipple discharge in an otherwise healthy adolescent = benign ductal ectasia.</p>
183. A 14-year-old girl is brought to the physician by her mother because she has never had a menstrual period. The patient has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. Thelarche began 18 months ago. Her mother's menarche was at the age of 13 years. The patient plays soccer and runs 3 miles weekly. She is at the 50th percentile for height, 10th percentile for weight, and 25th percentile for BMI. Breast and pubic hair development are sexual maturity rating stage 4. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Karyotype analysis</br>b. Measurement of serum gonadotropin concentrations</br>c. Measurement of serum prolactin concentration</br>d. Thyroid function testing</br>e. Reassurance only</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Reassurance only  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 14-year-old girl with normal pubertal development (breast &amp; pubic hair Tanner stage 4) but no menses yet.<br /></br>• Thelarche started 18 months ago. In most girls, menarche follows thelarche by up to 2–3 years.<br /></br>• Height, weight and BMI are within normal percentiles; only mild athletic activity (soccer, 3 mi/wk) – not the level usually associated with hypothalamic amenorrhea.<br /></br>• No symptoms suggesting thyroid disease, hyperprolactinemia, androgen excess, or chronic illness.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Primary amenorrhea is investigated when EITHER:<br /></br>1. No menses by age 15 y with otherwise normal secondary sexual characteristics, OR<br /></br>2. No menses ≥3 y after thelarche, OR<br /></br>3. No menses by age 13 y in the absence of secondary sexual characteristics.  </p></br><p>This patient is 14 y and only 1.5 y post-thelarche, so she has not yet reached an evaluation threshold. Therefore the appropriate management is simple reassurance, with routine follow-up if menses have not started by age 15 or 36 months after breast budding.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (brief)<br /></br>The hypothalamic-pituitary-ovarian axis matures over ~2–3 years after thelarche. Irregular or absent bleeding during this window is physiologic (“pubertal anovulation”).  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>No laboratory or imaging studies are indicated at this time. Education about expected timing of menarche and instructions to return if periods do not begin within the next year are sufficient.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Karyotype analysis – Appropriate when primary amenorrhea is accompanied by high FSH (gonadal dysgenesis) or absent uterus (Müllerian agenesis/androgen-insensitivity). Neither criterion is met here.<br /></br>b. Measurement of serum gonadotropin concentrations – First step once evaluation is warranted (age ≥15 y or ≥3 y post-thelarche). Premature now.<br /></br>c. Measurement of serum prolactin concentration – Indicated if galactorrhea or headaches/visual changes suggest hyperprolactinemia. Not present.<br /></br>d. Thyroid function testing – Ordered when there are symptoms or signs of thyroid disease (fatigue, weight change, goiter, constipation, etc.). None cited.  </p></br><p>Take-Home Message<br /></br>A 14-year-old who is fewer than 3 years beyond breast development and otherwise healthy still falls within the normal window for menarche. Reassurance and watchful waiting are the correct approach; a full amenorrhea work-up begins only once established age/time criteria are met.</p>
184. A hospitalized 57-year-old man is confused, agitated, and combative 2 days after undergoing laparoscopic resection for hepatocellular carcinoma. His medications are omeprazole, propranolol, and hydrocodone. He has drunk one gallon of Scotch whiskey weekly for 3 years. He has used heroin once daily for 3 years. His temperature is 38.5 C 101.3 F, pulse is 112/min, respirations are 24/min, and blood pressure is 170/100 mm Hg. Physical examination shows a tremor of the hands. On mental status examination, he is not oriented to person, place, or time and reports seeing cockroaches on the hospital bed. Laboratory studies show:</br>Platelet count: 46,000 /mm3</br>Prothrombin time: 15.8 sec INR = 1.4</br>Serum</br>Albumin: 2.2 g/dL</br>Total bilirubin: 3.1 mg/dL</br>AST: 66 U/L</br>ALT: 98 U/L</br>Ammonia: 11 µg/dL</br>Urine toxicology screening is negative. Administration of which of the following is the most appropriate initial step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Folic acid</br>b. Lactulose</br>c. Lorazepam</br>d. Methadone</br>e. Vitamin thiamine</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Lorazepam  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “2 days after undergoing surgery” = 48 h since last drink; classic window for the onset of delirium tremens (DTs).<br /></br>• Vital-signs triad of autonomic hyperactivity: T 101.3 °F, HR 112/min, BP 170/100 mm Hg, RR 24/min.<br /></br>• Neuro findings: agitation, confusion, disorientation, visual hallucinations (“seeing cockroaches”), coarse hand tremor.<br /></br>• Long, heavy alcohol use (≈1 gallon Scotch/week for 3 years) with abrupt cessation while hospitalized.<br /></br>• Normal ammonia (11 µg/dL) argues strongly against hepatic encephalopathy despite underlying liver disease.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Pattern recognition: an alcoholic who stops drinking in the hospital and develops fever, tachycardia, hypertension, tremor, agitation, and visual hallucinations 48-96 h later = delirium tremens.<br /></br>First-line/initial therapy for DTs is IV benzodiazepines. In patients with liver disease, use short-acting agents metabolized by phase II glucuronidation (lorazepam, oxazepam, temazepam). Therefore lorazepam is the most appropriate initial step.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Alcohol is a CNS depressant (↑GABA, ↓NMDA glutamate). Chronic use causes receptor down-/up-regulation leading to dependence. Sudden cessation produces unopposed NMDA and decreased GABA activity → autonomic hyperactivity, tremor, hallucinations, seizures, and DTs. Benzodiazepines act as GABA-A positive allosteric modulators, substituting for the suddenly absent alcohol effect.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Management ladder for alcohol withdrawal<br /></br>1. Benzodiazepines (diazepam, chlordiazepoxide; lorazepam/oxazepam in liver disease).<br /></br>2. Supportive care (IV fluids, thiamine, glucose, multivitamins, electrolytes, folate) once patient stabilized.<br /></br>3. Escalate with phenobarbital, propofol if refractory.<br /></br>Lab/monitoring: serial CIWA-Ar scores, vital signs, electrolytes, glucose, Mg²⁺, and consider ICU if severe.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Folic acid – Alcoholics are often folate-deficient; supplementation prevents megaloblastic anemia but does not treat acute DTs or resolve life-threatening autonomic instability.<br /></br>b. Lactulose – Used for hepatic encephalopathy to lower ammonia by gut trapping. Ammonia level is low/normal; patient lacks asterixis or somnolence. Hallucinations and autonomic storm fit DTs, not encephalopathy.<br /></br>d. Methadone – Treats opioid withdrawal (lacrimation, piloerection, yawning, diarrhea, myalgias) which typically begins 6-24 h after last heroin use and does NOT cause severe hypertension, fever, or visual hallucinations. Urine tox is negative; symptoms match alcohol, not opioid withdrawal.<br /></br>e. Vitamin B1 (thiamine) – Should be given to alcoholics before glucose to prevent Wernicke’s encephalopathy, but it does not rapidly control tremors, agitation, or autonomic hyperactivity of DTs. Thiamine is important but not the “most appropriate initial step” for this acute presentation.  </p></br><p>Take-Home Message<br /></br>Delirium tremens arises 48-96 h after abrupt alcohol cessation and presents with agitation, hallucinations, tremor, and marked autonomic instability. Immediate IV benzodiazepine therapy—preferably lorazepam in liver-diseased patients—is lifesaving and is the first step in management.</p>
185. A 4-month-old female infant is brought to the office by her mother for a well-child examination. She was born at term following an uncomplicated pregnancy and vaginal delivery. She is breast-feeding well and gaining weight. She has been smiling and cooing and has begun rolling onto her side. The mother says she noticed an asymmetric appearance to her daughter's face and head 4 weeks ago that has slightly worsened since that time. The patient otherwise has been well. Family history is noncontributory. The infant is 63 cm 25 in; 50th percentile long and weighs 6200 g 13 lb 11oz; 50th percentile; head circumference is 40 cm 16 in; 25th percentile. Vital signs are within normal limits. Physical examination shows an open and flat anterior fontanel and a palpable ridge between the right parietal and temporal bones. The right eye and eyebrow appear superiorly displaced compared with the left. The remainder of the physical examination discloses no abnormalities. CT scan of the head is shown. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Coronal craniosynostosis</br>b. Fracture of the skull base</br>c. Osteogenesis imperfecta</br>d. Positional plagiocephaly</br>e. Torticollis</br></br></br><p>Correct answer: a. Coronal craniosynostosis  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Asymmetric appearance to her daughter’s face and head … slightly worsened” – progressive, fixed deformity (suggests bone, not molding/position).<br /></br>• “Palpable ridge between the right parietal and temporal bones” – you can feel a raised, immobile bony ridge directly over a cranial suture → hallmark of premature suture fusion.<br /></br>• “Right eye and eyebrow appear superiorly displaced” – ipsilateral orbital elevation is classic for unilateral coronal suture fusion (“anterior plagiocephaly,” ‟harlequin eye” appearance on CT).<br /></br>• Normal growth parameters and otherwise healthy infant – points away from systemic bone disease or acute trauma.<br /></br>• CT (not shown here) in craniosynostosis typically demonstrates loss of the coronal suture line on the affected side ± harlequin orbit.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Is the skull asymmetry positional (soft, occipital flattening) or structural (suture ridge, orbital changes, frontal asymmetry)?<br /></br>Step 2: Presence of a palpable ridge + progressive facial/orbital distortion → structural → craniosynostosis.<br /></br>Step 3: Identify which suture: unilateral coronal = elevation of ipsilateral orbit &amp; forehead flattening, palpable ridge across frontoparietal region.</p></br><p>Take-home concept: A firm, immobile ridge along a cranial suture with progressive, fixed skull asymmetry equals craniosynostosis; orbital elevation localizes to coronal suture.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Craniosynostosis = premature fusion of ≥1 cranial sutures → restricted growth perpendicular to the fused suture and compensatory growth at remaining sutures.<br /></br>• Coronal suture (runs ear-to-ear) when fused unilaterally causes:<br /></br>  – Ipsilateral forehead flattening.<br /></br>  – Elevation of ipsilateral superior orbital rim (“harlequin orbit”).<br /></br>  – Skull grows toward the open sutures on the opposite side, causing anterior plagiocephaly.<br /></br>• Etiology: usually sporadic, occasionally syndromic (FGFR2 mutations, e.g., Crouzon, Apert).<br /></br>• Untreated → cosmetic deformity, strabismus, and, if multiple sutures involved, ↑ICP.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>Diagnostics: Skull X-ray or, preferably, 3-D CT showing fused suture and compensatory bossing elsewhere.<br /></br>Treatment: Referral to craniofacial surgery. Open or endoscopic suturectomy + helmet therapy ideally between 3–9 months to allow normal brain/skull growth.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Fracture of the skull base – Requires significant trauma, would present with acute symptoms (bruising around ears/eyes, CSF leak), not a slowly progressive deformity or palpable, smooth ridge. CT would show fracture lines, not suture obliteration.  </p></br><p>c. Osteogenesis imperfecta – Generalized bone fragility, multiple fractures, blue sclerae, hearing loss. Skull deformities are not localized to one suture, and a palpable ridge is absent.  </p></br><p>d. Positional plagiocephaly – Very common; occipital flattening from lying supine, ear displaced anteriorly, no bony ridge, skull is malleable. Forehead/orbital asymmetry is secondary and mild; improves with repositioning/helmet therapy.  </p></br><p>e. Torticollis – Sternocleidomastoid tightness tilts head, can secondarily cause positional plagiocephaly (again, posterior flattening). No suture ridging; physical exam would show limited neck range of motion and SCM mass/fibrosis.  </p></br><p>Key distinction: Only craniosynostosis presents with a firm, immobile suture ridge and primary orbital/forehead distortion.</p>
186. A 57-year-old woman comes to the physician because she has felt sad and tearful and has thought about killing herself for the past 2 weeks. Her symptoms began 1 week after losing 25,000 dollars during a weekend of gambling. She reports that she had boundless energy all week and gambled continuously throughout the weekend without needing to sleep. She says, I felt great even when I was losing and did not realize what I had done until a week later. Twice yearly for the past 10 years, she has had similar gambling episodes but has never lost this much money before. Physical examination shows no abnormalities. On mental status examination, she is distraught and tearful. She says that she has not developed a plan to take her own life but admits to wishing for a convenient car accident. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Bipolar disorder</br>b. Cyclothymic disorder</br>c. Major depressive disorder</br>d. Malingering</br>e. Pathological gambling</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection</br>A. Bipolar disorder (currently in a major depressive episode with a history of true manic episodes)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• “Boundless energy … gambled continuously … without needing to sleep” → classic manic symptoms (elevated/expansive mood, ↑ goal-directed activity, ↓ need for sleep, risky behaviors).<br /></br>• “Twice yearly for the past 10 years, she has had similar gambling episodes” → recurrent manic episodes.<br /></br>• “Felt sad and tearful … thought about killing herself … 2 weeks” → current major depressive episode.<br /></br>• Key phrase: gambling binges happen only when she is manic; outside those episodes there is no mention of persistent gambling urge.</p></br><p>Putting these pieces together gives a bipolar spectrum illness, not an isolated gambling addiction.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>1. Any history of a full manic episode automatically rules in bipolar I disorder, even if the patient is currently depressed.<br /></br>2. Manic episodes often feature poor judgment and high-risk activities (e.g., spending sprees, gambling, sexual indiscretions).<br /></br>Core takeaway: Always screen for past mania in a patient presenting with depression; the presence of mania shifts the diagnosis (and the treatment) to bipolar disorder.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Bipolar I: lifetime history of ≥1 manic episode (≥1 week of elevated/irritable mood plus ≥3 DIG FAST symptoms).<br /></br>• Etiology: complex genetic predisposition (strong family association), dysregulated monoamine neurotransmission, disrupted circadian rhythms.<br /></br>• Course: recurrent mood episodes with high suicide risk (≈15–20 × general population). </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review</br>Acute mania or mixed: lithium, valproate, carbamazepine, or atypical antipsychotics (e.g., quetiapine).<br /></br>Acute bipolar depression: quetiapine, lurasidone, lithium, lamotrigine; avoid antidepressant monotherapy (can precipitate mania).<br /></br>Maintenance: lithium (↓ suicide risk), valproate, lamotrigine, or atypical antipsychotics; psychotherapy for lifestyle/stress management.<br /></br>Assess suicide risk; hospitalize if needed.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>B. Cyclothymic disorder<br /></br>• Characterized by ≥2 years of numerous periods of mild hypomanic symptoms and mild depressive symptoms that never meet full criteria for mania or major depression.<br /></br>• This patient meets full manic criteria and full major-depressive criteria → exceeds cyclothymic threshold.</p></br><p>C. Major depressive disorder<br /></br>• Excluded by any history of manic or hypomanic episodes; presence of true mania → bipolar, not unipolar, mood disorder.</p></br><p>D. Malingering<br /></br>• Conscious fabrication for external gain (money, drugs, avoiding work/jail). No evidence she is faking symptoms or seeking tangible benefits.</p></br><p>E. Pathological gambling (Gambling disorder)<br /></br>• Defined as persistent, maladaptive gambling not better explained by a manic episode. DSM-5 specifically instructs: “Do NOT diagnose gambling disorder if gambling occurs exclusively during a manic episode.” Her gambling coincides with mania and remits otherwise → not primary gambling disorder.</p></br><p>Key Exam Pearl</br>In patients with risky behaviors such as gambling or spending sprees, always ask about mood elevation, sleeplessness, and other manic symptoms; one documented manic episode overrides all and establishes the diagnosis of bipolar I disorder.</p>
187. A previously healthy 57-year-old woman comes to the emergency department because of a 2-week history of fever, nonproductive cough, and increasing shortness of breath. She is no longer able to walk up one flight of stairs without resting. She takes no medications. She does not smoke cigarettes, drink alcohol, or use illicit drugs. She has been sexually active with multiple partners, and they use condoms inconsistently. She appears cachectic and in moderate respiratory distress. Her temperature is 39.1 C 102.4 F, pulse is 120/min, respirations are 32/min, and blood pressure is 104/68 mm Hg. Examination shows submental and cervical lymphadenopathy. There is diffuse white exudate over the buccal mucosa. Pulmonary examination shows good air movement; no crackles or rhonchi are heard. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Her hemoglobin concentration is 7.8 g/dl, and leukocyte count is 3800/mm 3. Arterial blood gas analysis on room air shows:</br>pH 7.48</br>Pco2 30 mm Hg</br>Po2 68 mm Hg</br>An x-ray of the chest shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most likely causal organism?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Epstein-Barr virus</br>b. Legionella pneumophila</br>c. Mycop/asma pneumoniae</br>d. Pneumocystis jirovecii formerly P. carinil</br>e. Streptococcus pneumoniae</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Pneumocystis jirovecii (formerly P. carinii)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 2-week subacute course of fever, dry cough, progressive dyspnea → classic tempo for Pneumocystis pneumonia (PCP).<br /></br>• Cachexia, generalized lymphadenopathy, oral thrush → red-flag clues for advanced, untreated HIV (CD4 &lt; 200 cells/µL).<br /></br>• Hypoxemia (PaO₂ 68 mm Hg) with respiratory alkalosis (tachypnea → PaCO₂ 30, pH 7.48).<br /></br>• Normal chest x-ray early in disease (up to 10–20 % of cases) despite significant hypoxemia; when abnormal, shows bilateral interstitial “ground-glass” infiltrates, not lobar consolidation.<br /></br>• Anemia and leukopenia further support immunosuppression.</p></br><p>Key pattern: “AIDS patient + fever / non-productive cough / dyspnea out of proportion to clear lung exam or minimal imaging findings” = Pneumocystis jirovecii.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Recognize the patient is likely HIV-positive (thrush, cachexia, lymphopenia, risky sexual history).<br /></br>2. In an immunocompromised host, the most common cause of atypical pneumonia with these features and a near-normal CXR is PCP.<br /></br>3. Alternative pathogens (EBV, Mycoplasma, Legionella, S. pneumoniae) either do not fit the immune status or radiographic/laboratory picture.</p></br><p>Take-home concept: Dyspnea with hypoxemia out of proportion to lung exam/imaging in an AIDS patient should immediately trigger suspicion for Pneumocystis jirovecii.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Opportunistic yeast-like fungus Pneumocystis jirovecii, inhaled → interstitial pneumonitis.<br /></br>• Risk factors: CD4 &lt; 200, chronic steroids, chemotherapy, transplant.<br /></br>• Mechanism: Diffuse alveolar-interstitial inflammation → impaired oxygen diffusion → marked A-a gradient &amp; hypoxemia.<br /></br>• Clinical manifestations: Fever, nonproductive cough, exertional dyspnea; may have oral candidiasis concomitantly.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostic &amp; Treatment Pearls<br /></br>Diagnostics<br /></br>• CXR: Bilateral diffuse, reticular/ground-glass infiltrates; can be normal early.<br /></br>• CT chest: Diffuse ground-glass opacities (more sensitive).<br /></br>• Definitive: DFA staining, methenamine-silver stain, or PCR of induced sputum/BAL.<br /></br>• Labs: ↑LDH (non-specific but supportive).</p></br><p>Treatment<br /></br>1. First-line: Trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole (TMP-SMX) × 21 days.<br /></br>2. Adjunctive corticosteroids (e.g., prednisone 40 mg BID) if PaO₂ &lt; 70 mm Hg or A-a gradient ≥ 35 mm Hg (reduces mortality by dampening inflammatory response).<br /></br>3. Alternatives (sulfa allergy): IV pentamidine, atovaquone, clindamycin + primaquine, dapsone + trimethoprim.<br /></br>4. Secondary prophylaxis: TMP-SMX daily until CD4 &gt; 200 for ≥ 3 months on ART.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Epstein-Barr virus<br /></br>• Causes infectious mononucleosis: exudative pharyngitis, extreme fatigue, posterior cervical LAD, +/- splenomegaly.<br /></br>• Does not produce hypoxemia or pneumonia in immunocompetent or AIDS patients; CXR typically normal.  </p></br><p>b. Legionella pneumophila<br /></br>• Causes severe lobar/segmental pneumonia with high fever, GI symptoms, hyponatremia.<br /></br>• CXR: patchy unilobar infiltrate; cannot be “normal” in symptomatic disease.<br /></br>• Generally affects smokers, elderly, or those with chronic lung disease, not classically AIDS with thrush.</p></br><p>c. Mycoplasma pneumoniae<br /></br>• “Walking pneumonia” in young adults; diffuse interstitial infiltrates on CXR, but patients usually appear less toxic, oxygenation relatively preserved, and no immunosuppression stigmata.<br /></br>• Oral thrush and severe hypoxemia are not typical.</p></br><p>e. Streptococcus pneumoniae<br /></br>• Most common cause of community-acquired lobar pneumonia → acute presentation, productive cough, focal crackles, lobar consolidation on CXR.<br /></br>• Would produce abnormal breath sounds and an abnormal radiograph.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Believing a normal chest x-ray rules out pneumonia—PCP is the classic exception, especially in AIDS.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Core Concept<br /></br>In an immunocompromised (AIDS) patient, subacute fever, nonproductive cough, dyspnea, hypoxemia with a normal or subtly abnormal CXR = Pneumocystis jirovecii pneumonia; treat with TMP-SMX ± steroids.</p>
188. A 24-year-old nulligravid woman comes to the physician because of a 3-day history of pain with urination and urinary frequency. She has a 1-year history of frequent bladder infections characterized by similar symptoms. The infections occur after sexual intercourse and resolve with antibiotic therapy. She has no other history of serious illness and takes no medications. She is currently sexually active with one male partner, and they use condoms consistently. Examination shows bladder tenderness and urethral elevation; no other abnormalities are noted. Urine culture grows Escherichia coli. Which of the following is most likely to prevent recurrence of these symptoms?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Obtain a swab of the urethra of the patient's partner</br>b. Prescribe an antibiotic to be taken following sexual intercourse</br>c. Perform cystoscopy to rule out detrusor instability</br>d. Perform cystoscopy to rule out anatomic cause</br>e. Recommend weekly self-catheterization to determine postvoid residual volume</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>Prescribe an antibiotic to be taken following sexual intercourse.</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “1-year history of frequent bladder infections … occur after sexual intercourse” → classic post-coital (honeymoon) cystitis pattern.<br /></br>• Age &lt;30, nulligravid, otherwise healthy → no anatomic or neurogenic risk factors.<br /></br>• Escherichia coli on culture, no fever/flank pain → uncomplicated recurrent cystitis.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Define “recurrent UTI” (≥2 infections in 6 mo or ≥3 in 12 mo).<br /></br>Step 2: Identify a clear precipitating factor (sexual intercourse).<br /></br>Step 3: Apply guideline‐directed prevention: single-dose post-coital antibiotic prophylaxis (e.g., nitrofurantoin 100 mg, trimethoprim–sulfamethoxazole 80/400 mg, or cephalexin 250–500 mg).<br /></br>Core takeaway: In healthy young women with intercourse-triggered recurrent cystitis, targeted post-coital chemoprophylaxis is first-line.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• During intercourse, periurethral bacteria are massaged into the bladder.<br /></br>• Host defenses clear most organisms, but in some women inoculum exceeds the threshold, producing symptomatic cystitis.<br /></br>• Low-dose antibiotic within 2 h after intercourse suppresses bacterial proliferation before infection takes hold.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>First-line prophylaxis<br /></br>1. Behavioral: void pre-/post-intercourse, increase fluids → already implied but insufficient alone.<br /></br>2. Post-coital single-dose antibiotic (as above).<br /></br>Second-line<br /></br>• Nightly low-dose antibiotic for 6 mo if infections not strictly post-coital.<br /></br>• Vaginal estrogen in post-menopausal women.<br /></br>No imaging or invasive testing is required unless red-flag signs (gross hematuria, nephrolithiasis risk, neurogenic bladder, anatomic anomaly) are present.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>A. Obtain a swab of the urethra of the patient’s partner<br /></br>• UTIs in women are almost always due to their own colonic/ perineal flora; partner screening is not recommended and would not prevent her infections.  </p></br><p>C. Perform cystoscopy to rule out detrusor instability<br /></br>• Detrusor overactivity causes urgency incontinence, not culture-proven E. coli cystitis. No neurologic or irritative bladder symptoms here.  </p></br><p>D. Perform cystoscopy to rule out anatomic cause<br /></br>• Anatomic abnormalities (e.g., ureterocele, diverticulum) present in childhood, men, or complicated cases. Young woman with otherwise normal history/exam doesn’t warrant invasive cystoscopy.  </p></br><p>E. Recommend weekly self-catheterization to determine post-void residual volume<br /></br>• Neurogenic bladder with high residual volumes predisposes to UTIs, but patient has normal voiding and no neurologic disease. Self-catheterization is unnecessary and exposes her to infection risk.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Confusing post-coital prophylaxis with continuous daily prophylaxis—daily dosing is reserved for recurrences unrelated to sexual activity or failures of post-coital strategy.</p>
189. An asymptomatic 32-year-old man comes to the physician 2 days after his male sexual partner was diagnosed with gonorrhea. He has not had penile discharge or dysuria. Over the past 4 months, he and his partner have had unprotected sexual intercourse several times weekly. Four months ago, HIV testing of the patient and his partner was negative. Examination shows no abnormalities. A Gram stain of mucus from a urethral swab is negative for diplococci. A urethral culture is ordered. He gives written consent for HIV antibody testing and serologic testing for syphilis. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in therapy?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Withhold any treatment until results of the culture are known</br>b. Ceftriaxone only</br>c. Ceftriaxone and doxycycline</br>d. Ceftriaxone and metronidazole</br>e. Ciprofloxacin only</br>F. Ciprofloxacin and doxycycline</br>g. Doxycycline only</br>h. Doxycycline and metronidazole</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Ceftriaxone and doxycycline  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Male sexual partner was diagnosed with gonorrhea” = proven exposure to N. gonorrhoeae.<br /></br>• “Asymptomatic … Gram-stain negative … culture pending” = infection cannot be ruled out; Gram stain lacks sensitivity in asymptomatic men.<br /></br>• Time frame: exposure occurred within the last 60 days → CDC recommends empiric treatment of all sexual partners regardless of symptoms or preliminary test results.<br /></br>• “Unprotected intercourse” increases likelihood of concurrent Chlamydia trachomatis infection (up to 30–50 % co-infection).  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Partner has confirmed gonorrhea → patient is a close contact.<br /></br>Step 2: Treat empirically to (a) eradicate possible infection, (b) prevent complications, and (c) curb transmission—do NOT wait for culture.<br /></br>Step 3: Give dual therapy covering both organisms most commonly transmitted together:<br /></br> • N. gonorrhoeae – single-dose ceftriaxone (3rd-generation cephalosporin).<br /></br> • C. trachomatis – doxycycline 100 mg PO bid × 7 days (or single-dose azithromycin 1 g PO).  </p></br><p>Core Take-Away: Sexual contacts of a patient with proven gonorrhea receive immediate dual therapy for gonorrhea AND chlamydia, independent of symptoms or preliminary laboratory results.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Neisseria gonorrhoeae: gram-negative diplococcus, infects columnar epithelium of urethra, cervix, pharynx, rectum → risk of PID, epididymitis, disseminated infection.<br /></br>• Chlamydia trachomatis: obligate intracellular, serovars D–K cause urethritis/cervicitis; frequent asymptomatic carriage.<br /></br>• Co-infection common due to identical transmission route and inadequate condom use.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>First-line (CDC 2015 guideline, still tested on exams):<br /></br> • Ceftriaxone 250 mg IM once PLUS<br /></br> • Doxycycline 100 mg PO bid for 7 days (or azithromycin 1 g PO once).<br /></br>2020 CDC update: high-dose ceftriaxone (500 mg) alone if chlamydia ruled out, but dual coverage remains default on NBME/Step examinations.<br /></br>Additional steps in this patient: HIV Ab/Ag testing, RPR/VDRL for syphilis, counseling on barrier protection, and partner notification.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Withhold treatment until culture results: Violates CDC mandate to treat contacts immediately; risks ongoing transmission and complications.<br /></br>b. Ceftriaxone only: Covers gonorrhea but misses possible chlamydial co-infection → incomplete therapy.<br /></br>d. Ceftriaxone + metronidazole: Adds anaerobic/Trichomonas coverage that is unnecessary; still omits chlamydia.<br /></br>e. Ciprofloxacin only / f. Ciprofloxacin + doxycycline: Fluoroquinolones are no longer recommended because of widespread N. gonorrhoeae resistance.<br /></br>g. Doxycycline only: Misses gonorrhea; β-lactam therapy required.<br /></br>h. Doxycycline + metronidazole: Misses gonorrhea and adds unneeded anaerobic coverage.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Trusting a negative Gram stain in an asymptomatic screening context—culture (and newer NAAT) is more sensitive, but empiric therapy is still mandatory.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Key Message<br /></br>Always give immediate dual therapy (ceftriaxone + doxycycline or azithromycin) to any sexual partner of a patient with confirmed gonorrhea, regardless of symptoms or preliminary lab findings.</p>
190. An unconscious 32-year-old woman is found by a nurse outside the emergency department. There is a gunshot wound to her right temple. She does not have an advance directive or an organ donor card among her possessions. On arrival, she is unresponsive and has a Glasgow Coma Scale score of 3. Her pulse is 48/min, respirations are 8/min, and blood pressure is 180/100 mm Hg. Pupils are dilated and nonreactive to light. She is intubated, and mechanical ventilation is started. After fluid resuscitation, her hemodynamic status is stabilized. A CT scan of the head shows a large intracranial hematoma with a massive midline shift. After consulting with a neurosurgeon, the most appropriate next step is to contact which of the following?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Hospital ethics committee</br>b. Local police department</br>c. Medical examiner</br>d. Municipal court</br>e. Organ procurement specialist</br></br></br><p>Correct answer</br>a. Hospital ethics committee</p></br><hr /></br><p>Vignette analysis</br>• Unconscious, gravely injured patient (gunshot to head) ⇒ lacks decision-making capacity.<br /></br>• No advance directive, no donor card, no family or friends present ⇒ no readily identifiable surrogate.<br /></br>• CT: “large intracranial hematoma with massive midline shift,” GCS 3, fixed pupils ⇒ virtually no chance of meaningful neurologic recovery.<br /></br>• Question asks, “After consulting with a neurosurgeon, the most appropriate next step is to contact which of the following?” – i.e., who should help guide further decisions about goals of care (possible withdrawal of life-sustaining treatment, code status, etc.).</p></br><hr /></br><p>Clinical critical reasoning</br>1. Patient is incapacitated → requires a surrogate or other mechanism for substituted judgment / best-interest decisions.<br /></br>2. When no legally authorized surrogate is immediately available, clinicians should turn to the hospital’s ethics committee (or similar multidisciplinary ethics consultation service) to:</br>   • Review prognosis and treatment options.<br /></br>   • Help determine whether continued aggressive treatment is ethically appropriate or futile.<br /></br>   • Assist in identifying potential surrogates and documenting efforts.<br /></br>   • Avoid unilateral physician decisions and reduce risk of conflict or liability.<br /></br>Core takeaway: In the absence of an advance directive AND an identifiable surrogate, an institutional ethics committee is the proper resource before seeking court involvement.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Pathophysiology review (concise)</br>Severe penetrating traumatic brain injury → intracranial hemorrhage, herniation, autonomic dysregulation (Cushing triad: bradycardia, hypertension, irregular respirations) → essentially unsalvageable neurologic status.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Treatment / diagnostic considerations</br>Immediate life-saving maneuvers (intubation, fluids, neurosurgery consult, ICP control) are already performed under implied consent. Now the question shifts to ongoing care versus withdrawal; that requires ethical and legal guidance, not more diagnostic tests.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Incorrect answer analysis</br>b. Local police department<br /></br>   • Police will eventually investigate the shooting, but their involvement does not guide medical decision-making for an incapacitated patient. Clinical management should not be delayed for law-enforcement notification.</p></br><p>c. Medical examiner<br /></br>   • Must be notified after death from violent trauma, BUT the patient is still alive. ME involvement occurs post-mortem, so calling now does nothing to help with treatment decisions.</p></br><p>d. Municipal court<br /></br>   • Courts can appoint a legal guardian when no surrogate exists, but this is a time-consuming last resort. Standard practice is to consult the hospital ethics committee first; only if disputes persist or no consensus is reached is judicial intervention sought.</p></br><p>e. Organ procurement specialist<br /></br>   • Federal regulations require notifying the organ procurement organization (OPO) when death is imminent or declared, but ONLY after either (1) documented consent (donor card/registry) or (2) next-of-kin authorization. Neither exists yet, and the patient is not legally brain-dead. Contacting OPO now is premature and ethically inappropriate.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Key exam point</br>When a patient lacks decision-making capacity, has no advance directive, and no surrogate is immediately available, the hospital ethics committee is the next step to guide appropriate, ethically sound care before involving courts or other external agencies.</p>
191. A 45-year-old woman comes to the office because of a wound on her right foot that she first noticed 8 weeks ago. During the past 3 days, it has increased in size and depth and has become moderately painful. She does not recall any injury to her foot. She has type 2 diabetes mellitus and hypertension. Her medications are insulin, atorvastatin, lisinopril, and daily aspirin. She is 163 cm 5 ft 4 in tall and weighs 95 kg 210 lb; BMI is 36 kg/m2. Temperature is 38.9 C 102.0 F, pulse is 80/min, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 140/95 mm Hg. Examination shows a 3 x 3-cm full-thickness wound over the second and third metatarsal heads of the plantar aspect of the right foot; there is necrotic tissue and eschar but no exposed tendon or bone. The plantar aspect of the right forefoot is tender to palpation. Dorsalis pedis and posterior tibial pulses in the right lower extremity are 1+. Semmes-Weinstein 5.07 monofilament testing shows absent sensation over the right foot. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Arterial Doppler ultrasonography of the lower extremities</br>b. Arteriography of the lower extremities</br>c. Electromyography and nerve conduction studies</br>d. MRI of the right foot</br>e. Triple-phase bone scan</br></br></br><p>Correct answer selection<br /></br>A. Arterial Doppler ultrasonography of the lower extremities (with calculation of an ankle-brachial index)</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Vignette analysis<br /></br>• Long-standing diabetic (neuropathy on monofilament testing) with a plantar forefoot ulcer that has progressed despite off-loading.<br /></br>• Ulcer now full-thickness with necrotic tissue + systemic signs of infection (T = 102 °F).<br /></br>• Dorsalis pedis &amp; posterior tibial pulses only 1 + → suggests possible peripheral arterial disease (PAD) and impaired perfusion.<br /></br>• No exposed bone → osteomyelitis is possible but not the first diagnostic priority.<br /></br>Key diagnostic fork: Will the ulcer heal if we treat the infection, or is there critical limb ischemia that first requires revascularization? The initial, least-invasive way to answer that is a vascular study (ABI via arterial Doppler).</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Clinical critical reasoning<br /></br>1. Every diabetic foot ulcer must be evaluated for (a) infection/osteomyelitis and (b) arterial insufficiency.<br /></br>2. Because healing cannot occur without adequate blood flow, vascular assessment is done first when pulses are diminished.<br /></br>3. Non-invasive arterial Doppler with ABI is the guideline-recommended initial test; it stratifies perfusion and determines need for further imaging or revascularization.</p></br><p>Take-home message: In a diabetic foot ulcer with weak pulses, the first diagnostic step is a non-invasive vascular study (arterial Doppler/ABI). Invasive angiography or imaging for osteomyelitis comes later or if clinically indicated after perfusion is documented.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Pathophysiology review<br /></br>• Neuropathic ulcers arise over pressure points (metatarsal heads) due to loss of protective sensation.<br /></br>• PAD (macrovascular atherosclerosis accelerated by diabetes) decreases tissue oxygenation, delaying wound healing and predisposing to infection and gangrene.<br /></br>• ABI &lt; 0.9 confirms PAD; &lt; 0.4 suggests critical ischemia requiring urgent revascularization.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Treatment / diagnostic cascade<br /></br>1. Non-invasive Doppler → ABI.<br /></br>   • Normal ≥ 0.9: manage infection, debridement, off-loading.<br /></br>   • Abnormal: vascular surgery referral ± formal angiography → percutaneous or surgical revascularization.<br /></br>2. After adequate perfusion is assured, evaluate for deep infection (probe-to-bone, ESR/CRP, plain X-ray → MRI if needed).<br /></br>3. Broad-spectrum antibiotics, surgical debridement, strict glycemic control, pressure off-loading, smoking cessation, statin, antiplatelet, etc.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Why the other choices are incorrect  </p></br><p>B. Arteriography of the lower extremities<br /></br>• Gold standard for mapping lesions before intervention but invasive, nephrotoxic, and not first-line. Perform only after a non-invasive study shows significant PAD.</p></br><p>C. Electromyography &amp; nerve conduction studies<br /></br>• Would confirm diabetic neuropathy, which is already clinically evident (absent monofilament sensation). Results would not change acute management of the ulcer.</p></br><p>D. MRI of the right foot<br /></br>• Best imaging for osteomyelitis or deep abscess, but vascular supply must be evaluated first. MRI is more expensive and less immediately actionable than a Doppler in the setting of weak pulses.</p></br><p>E. Triple-phase bone scan<br /></br>• Less specific for osteomyelitis than MRI and, like MRI, is secondary to determining limb perfusion. False positives common in neuropathic (Charcot) bone.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Core concept<br /></br>Before any diabetic foot ulcer can heal—or invasive imaging/intervention be planned—you must document adequate arterial blood flow non-invasively with arterial Doppler/ABI.</p>
192. A 27-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 6-month history of increasing pain and progressive swelling and stiffness of her ankles. Despite nightly application of ice to her ankles, ibuprofen therapy, and decreased activity during the past 3 months, her pain has worsened, she has had swelling of her ankles throughout the day, and she has had morning stiffness of her ankles. She has sustained no recent trauma to her ankles. She had a severe right ankle sprain in college. Her only other medication is an oral contraceptive. She is an avid tennis player and plays twice weekly. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows swelling and moderate effusion of both ankles. Range of motion of both ankles is full but produces mild pain. X-rays of the ankles are shown. Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient's symptoms?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Gonococcal arthritis</br>b. Gouty arthritis</br>c. Lyme disease</br>d. Osteomyelitis</br>e. Rheumatoid arthritis</br>F. Transient osteoporosis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Rheumatoid arthritis  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Duration: “6-month history” → chronic, not an acute crystal, infectious, or traumatic process.<br /></br>• Laterality &amp; symmetry: “both ankles,” “progressive swelling and stiffness” → symmetric inflammatory pattern typical of RA.<br /></br>• Inflammatory quality: “morning stiffness,” swelling/effusion, pain improved only minimally with NSAID → classic for inflammatory arthritis.<br /></br>• Lack of systemic infection clues: Afebrile, normal vital signs, no leukocytosis, chills, or skin findings → argues against septic (gonococcal) arthritis or osteomyelitis.<br /></br>• Imaging: Although the actual radiograph is not shown here, early RA films of the ankle typically demonstrate only soft-tissue swelling ± peri-articular osteopenia before joint-space loss/erosions develop—consistent with exam description (“moderate effusion,” full ROM but painful).  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Inflammatory arthritis in a young woman with symmetrical involvement and prolonged morning stiffness is rheumatoid arthritis until proven otherwise. The core takeaway: chronic, symmetric inflammatory arthritis with morning stiffness &gt; 30–60 min is RA; septic, crystal, or transient processes are usually unilateral, episodic, or acute.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)<br /></br>• Autoimmune synovitis driven by anti-citrullinated protein antibodies (ACPA) and rheumatoid factor (IgM against IgG Fc).<br /></br>• Persistent synovial inflammation → pannus formation → cartilage destruction and eventual bony erosions.<br /></br>• Typically begins in MCP, PIP, wrists, and MTPs but ankles, knees, elbows common with progression.<br /></br>• Extra-articular: nodules, ILD, pericarditis, anemia of chronic disease.  </p></br><p>Risk factors: female sex, HLA-DR4, smoking.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostics &amp; Treatment Pearls<br /></br>Diagnostics<br /></br>• Labs: ↑ ESR/CRP, positive RF (~80%) and/or anti-CCP (more specific).<br /></br>• Imaging: Early—soft-tissue swelling, juxta-articular osteopenia; Late—joint-space narrowing, marginal erosions.  </p></br><p>Treatment (Step 2-level hierarchy)<br /></br>1. Disease-modifying therapy as soon as diagnosis suspected: methotrexate is first-line.<br /></br>   – Add folic acid supplementation.<br /></br>2. Short-term symptom control: NSAIDs or glucocorticoids (e.g., prednisone) while waiting for DMARD effect.<br /></br>3. Escalation: combination csDMARDs or biologics (TNF-α inhibitors such as etanercept, adalimumab) if inadequate response.<br /></br>4. Screen for TB &amp; hepatitis before biologics.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Gonococcal arthritis<br /></br>• Presentation is typically acute (days), often migratory, can involve tenosynovitis and dermatitis, and usually mono- or oligoarticular. Fevers common. Chronic 6-month symmetric ankle effusions without systemic signs is atypical.  </p></br><p>b. Gouty arthritis<br /></br>• Classically acute, severe monoarthritis (1st MTP “podagra”) in middle-aged men; risk factors include alcohol, diuretics. Radiographs later show “punched-out” erosions with overhanging edges, not simply soft-tissue swelling.  </p></br><p>c. Lyme disease<br /></br>• Mono- or oligoarticular large-joint arthritis (knee most common) months after untreated Borrelia infection, usually unilateral. Requires tick exposure history; not symmetric chronic ankle disease.  </p></br><p>d. Osteomyelitis<br /></br>• Bone infection causes focal bone pain, systemic signs, and lytic changes on imaging, not symmetric joint effusions with full ROM.  </p></br><p>f. Transient osteoporosis<br /></br>• Self-limited painful osteopenia of the hip (most commonly) in middle-aged men or pregnant women; radiographs show diffuse osteopenia, not joint effusion, and condition resolves in 6–12 months without persistent inflammatory findings.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Key Takeaway<br /></br>Chronic, symmetric inflammatory ankle arthritis with prominent morning stiffness in a young woman = rheumatoid arthritis; initiate DMARD therapy early to prevent irreversible joint damage.</p>
193. A previously healthy 24-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 2-week history of fatigue and easy bruising. Her temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 100/min, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 100/60 mm Hg. Examination shows ecchymoses over the lower extremities. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hemoglobin: 9.2 g/dL</br>Leukocyte count: 53,000 /mm3</br>Lymphocytes: 10 percent</br>Myeloblasts: 90 percent</br>Platelet count: 63,000 /mm3</br>Serum</br>Creatinine: 1.9 mg/dL</br>Lactate dehydrogenase: 2,233 U/L</br>A bone marrow biopsy specimen confirms acute myelogenous leukemia. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Hydration and urine alkalinization</br>b. Renal ultrasonography</br>c. Transfusion of platelets</br>d. Transfusion of 2 units of packed red blood cells</br>e. Induction chemotherapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Hydration and urine alkalinization  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key phrases:<br /></br>  – “Acute myelogenous leukemia” confirmed on marrow.<br /></br>  – Very high leukocyte count with 90 % blasts → large tumor burden.<br /></br>  – Creatinine 1.9 mg/dL and LDH 2233 U/L → evidence of cell‐turnover–related renal injury.<br /></br>• Pattern recognition: Newly diagnosed AML + marked leukocytosis + rising creatinine/marked LDH = high risk (and possibly early signs) of tumor lysis syndrome (TLS).  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Patients with bulky, rapidly proliferating hematologic malignancies (ALL, AML, high-grade lymphomas) are at immediate risk of TLS.<br /></br>2. TLS can be spontaneous but is classically precipitated by induction chemotherapy.<br /></br>3. Cardinal prevention: aggressive IV hydration to maintain a high urine flow and urine alkalinization ± xanthine oxidase blockade (allopurinol) or urate oxidase (rasburicase).<br /></br>4.
