These measures must be started BEFORE chemotherapy; otherwise uric-acid nephropathy can worsen the already rising creatinine.<br /></br>Take-home: In a leukemic patient with elevated creatinine/LDH, always think TLS prophylaxis before cytotoxic therapy.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Massive tumor cell lysis releases K⁺, PO₄³⁻, and nucleic acids → uric acid.<br /></br>• Acidic urine favors uric-acid crystal precipitation in renal tubules ⇒ acute kidney injury (AKI) and further electrolyte derangements (hyperK, hyperPO₄, secondary hypoCa).<br /></br>• Hydration dilutes solutes; alkalinization increases uric-acid solubility. Prevention reduces morbidity and permits subsequent chemotherapy.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>1. IV normal saline (2–3 L/m²/day) to keep UOP ≥100 mL/hr.<br /></br>2. Urine alkalinization (IV sodium bicarbonate) TARGET pH 7.0–7.5 (tested concept, although newer guidelines rely more on rasburicase).<br /></br>3. Add allopurinol or rasburicase.<br /></br>4. Dialysis if refractory hyperK or oliguric AKI develops.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Renal ultrasonography<br /></br>• Would look for obstructive uropathy; nothing in history suggests obstruction. Does not address imminent TLS.  </p></br><p>c. Transfusion of platelets<br /></br>• Prophylactic transfusion threshold generally &lt;10–20 k/mm³ (or active bleeding/procedure). Platelets 63 k/mm³ with only mild ecchymoses ≠ urgent transfusion.  </p></br><p>d. Transfusion of 2 units of PRBCs<br /></br>• Hemoglobin 9.2 g/dL in a stable young patient does not mandate transfusion; emphasis is on preventing kidney failure from TLS first.  </p></br><p>e. Induction chemotherapy<br /></br>• Definitive AML therapy, but initiating it now without TLS prophylaxis risks catastrophic AKI, hyperkalemia, and arrhythmia. Supportive measures take priority.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Core Concept<br /></br>Before giving cytotoxic therapy to patients with high-burden leukemias or lymphomas—especially when creatinine or LDH is already elevated—start aggressive IV hydration and urine alkalinization (plus allopurinol/rasburicase) to prevent tumor lysis syndrome.</p>
194. A 20-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 3-year history of bruising without any trauma. He has not had bleeding from his nose or mouth. He has no histo [Y of serious illness and takes no medications. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows ecchymoses in various stages of healing over the upper extremities. Results of a complete blood count are within the reference range. His prothrombin time is 20 seconds INR=2, and partial thromboplastin time is 25 seconds. This patient most likely has a deficiency of which of the following?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Factor V</br>b. Factor VII</br>c. Factor VIII</br>d. Factor IX</br>e. Factor X</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Factor VII  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Bruising without any trauma” over several years → suggests a congenital bleeding-factor deficiency rather than platelet dysfunction (no mucosal bleeding, petechiae, or thrombocytopenia).<br /></br>• Normal CBC → platelet count is normal, so not thrombocytopenia.<br /></br>• PT 20 s, INR = 2 (prolonged) with a normal aPTT (25 s) → isolated abnormality of the extrinsic pathway.<br /></br>• No medications, liver disease, or vitamin K deficiency mentioned → think inherited single-factor deficiency.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Examine coag lab pattern.<br /></br>   – PT evaluates the extrinsic &amp; common pathways (factors I, II, V, VII, X).<br /></br>   – aPTT evaluates intrinsic &amp; common pathways (factors VIII, IX, XI, XII, plus II, V, X).<br /></br>2. Only PT is prolonged → only a factor unique to the extrinsic pathway can be missing, i.e., Factor VII.<br /></br>3. If the common pathway (II, V, X) were deficient, BOTH PT and aPTT would be prolonged.  </p></br><p>Take-home: An isolated prolonged PT with normal aPTT = think Factor VII deficiency (or early vitamin K deficiency/warfarin effect).  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Factor VII is synthesized in the liver and requires vitamin K carboxylation. It complexes with tissue factor to initiate the extrinsic pathway, activating Factor X, which then proceeds down the common pathway to generate thrombin and fibrin. Isolated congenital Factor VII deficiency is autosomal recessive and can present with variable easy bruising and mucosal bleeding, though severe hemarthroses are less common than in hemophilias A/B.  </p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• Confirmatory test: specific Factor VII activity assay (&lt;30 % diagnostic).<br /></br>• Acute bleeding: recombinant activated Factor VII (rFVIIa) or fresh frozen plasma.<br /></br>• Prophylaxis for severe cases: rFVIIa or prothrombin-complex concentrates.<br /></br>• Vitamin K administration has no effect on congenital deficiency but would correct acquired deficiency.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Factor V<br /></br>   – Part of the common pathway: deficiency would prolong BOTH PT and aPTT. Normal PTT here rules it out.<br /></br>c. Factor VIII<br /></br>   – Intrinsic pathway (Hemophilia A). Deficiency prolongs aPTT only; PT remains normal.<br /></br>d. Factor IX<br /></br>   – Intrinsic pathway (Hemophilia B). Also prolongs aPTT only.<br /></br>e. Factor X<br /></br>   – Common pathway. Deficiency prolongs BOTH PT and aPTT.  </p></br><p>Key Differentiator: Only Factor VII lies solely in the PT arm; all others listed affect pathways measured by aPTT as well.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Students often choose a common-pathway factor (V or X) when they see an elevated PT, forgetting that the aPTT would invariably rise with those deficiencies. Always pair the lab abnormality to the clotting cascade branch tested.</p>
195. A 42-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 3-year history of ringing in her ears. The sound gradually intensified during the first year and has been unchanged since that time. The ringing is more noticeable when she is in a quiet environment but otherwise does not fluctuate throughout the day. She reports that her hearing is otherwise normal. She has hypertension treated with lisinopril. She has smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 25 years and drinks one glass of wine daily. Her blood pressure is 130/80 mm Hg; other vital signs are within normal limits. Examination of the external auditory canals shows no abnormalities. Finger-rub testing shows no abnormalities. Audiometry shows mild high-frequency hearing loss bilaterally. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. MRI of the brain and internal auditory canals</br>b. Brain stem auditory evoked response</br>c. Electronystagmography</br>d. MR angiography of the brain</br>e. No further testing is necessary</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. MRI of the brain and internal auditory canals (IACs)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key findings<br /></br>  – 3-year history of constant, non-pulsatile “ringing” (subjective tinnitus)<br /></br>  – Gradual progression, then plateau (suggests an organic, space-occupying process rather than a transient medication or noise exposure)<br /></br>  – Audiometry: mild high-frequency sensorineural hearing loss (SNHL) in BOTH ears<br /></br>  – Age 42 (young for presbycusis)<br /></br>• Why they matter<br /></br>  – Subjective tinnitus + SNHL prompts evaluation for cochlear vs retrocochlear pathology.<br /></br>  – High-frequency loss is the earliest change seen with vestibular (acoustic) schwannoma.<br /></br>  – Schwannoma frequently begins in one ear but can be bilateral (esp. with NF2) or initially appear symmetric on screening audiogram; MRI is required to rule it out.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Step 1 in a tinnitus work-up is always formal audiometry (already done).<br /></br>2. Next step: If audiogram shows any sensorineural loss—particularly asymmetric, unilateral, or unexplained in a patient &lt;60 y—guidelines recommend gadolinium-enhanced MRI of the cerebellopontine angle &amp; IACs to exclude vestibular schwannoma or other retrocochlear lesions.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Persistent subjective tinnitus with SNHL warrants MRI of the brain/IACs; audiogram alone cannot exclude a schwannoma.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Vestibular schwannoma<br /></br>• Benign Schwann-cell tumor on CN VIII within the IAC.<br /></br>• Earliest sign: high-frequency SNHL ± tinnitus; progresses to imbalance or facial numbness.<br /></br>• Diagnosis: contrast MRI (sensitivity &gt;95 %).<br /></br>Other causes of subjective tinnitus: presbycusis, noise trauma, ototoxic meds, Ménière disease—but these are diagnoses of exclusion once schwannoma is ruled out.</p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>After MRI confirmation:<br /></br>• Tumor &lt;2.5 cm &amp; minimal symptoms → Serial MRI (“watchful waiting”).<br /></br>• Larger or symptomatic → Stereotactic radiosurgery (Gamma Knife) or microsurgical excision.<br /></br>If MRI negative → Conservative tinnitus management: hearing aids, sound therapy, CBT.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Brain-stem auditory evoked response<br /></br>• Can screen for retrocochlear pathology but sensitivity is lower than MRI; reserved if MRI is contraindicated.</p></br><p>c. Electronystagmography<br /></br>• Assesses vestibular function in vertigo disorders (e.g., Ménière, vestibular neuritis). Patient has no vertigo.</p></br><p>d. MR angiography<br /></br>• Indicated for pulsatile/objective tinnitus to look for vascular lesions (AVM, carotid stenosis). Tinnitus here is non-pulsatile.</p></br><p>e. No further testing<br /></br>• Appropriate only when tinnitus is clearly benign (e.g., transient, medication-related, or age-related bilateral SNHL in older adults). At age 42 with progressive symptoms and audiometric SNHL, a retrocochlear lesion must be excluded first.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Assuming bilateral high-frequency loss rules out schwannoma—early tumors may be missed on screening audiograms, and bilateral schwannomas occur in NF2. Always image when tinnitus + SNHL occur in a younger patient.</p>
196. An 18-year-old basketball player is brought to the emergency department 1 hour after collapsing during a game. He was successfully resuscitated at the scene. On arrival, he is awake and alert. His temperature is 37.1  C 98.8 F, pulse is 80/min and regular, respirations are 12/min, and blood pressure is 120/80 mm Hg. Examination shows no abnormalities. An ECG shows left ventricular hypertrophy. Echocardiography shows systolic anterior motion of the anterior leaflet of the mitral valve causing a subaortic obstruction. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Adrenergic blocking agent therapy</br>b. Placement of a permanent ventricular pacemaker</br>c. Aortic valve replacement</br>d. Mitral valve anuloplasty</br>e. Surgical excision of the endocardium of the left ventricular free wall</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Adrenergic-blocking agent therapy (e.g., oral β-blocker such as metoprolol or propranolol)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “18-year-old basketball player … collapsed during a game” → young athlete with exertional syncope/sudden arrest → think hypertrophic obstructive cardiomyopathy (HOCM).<br /></br>• “ECG shows left ventricular hypertrophy.”<br /></br>• “Echocardiography shows systolic anterior motion (SAM) of the anterior leaflet of the mitral valve causing a subaortic obstruction.” → Classic echo description of HOCM.</p></br><p>These key phrases clinch the diagnosis; the question is asking for the first management step after resuscitation.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>HOCM obstruction worsens when contractility ↑ or preload ↓ because the hypertrophied septum + SAM narrow the LVOT. Therefore treatment aims to<br /></br>1. Reduce myocardial contractility (negative inotropy)<br /></br>2. Increase diastolic filling time (↓ HR)<br /></br>3. Maintain/increase preload &amp; afterload  </p></br><p>β-blockers accomplish all three: they blunt sympathetic stimulation, slow the heart, and allow more LV filling, thereby decreasing the dynamic gradient.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In a hemodynamically stable HOCM patient, the first-line chronic therapy is a β-adrenergic blocker.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>• Autosomal dominant mutations in sarcomeric proteins (β-myosin heavy chain, myosin-binding protein C, etc.).<br /></br>• Asymmetric septal hypertrophy → narrowed LVOT.<br /></br>• SAM of the mitral valve further occludes the outflow tract during systole → dynamic obstruction, systolic murmur, syncope, risk of ventricular arrhythmias &amp; sudden death.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>First-line:<br /></br>• β-blocker (metoprolol, propranolol).  </p></br><p>Second-line / adjunct if symptoms persist:<br /></br>• Non-dihydropyridine CCB (verapamil) or negative-inotrope disopyramide.  </p></br><p>Refractory / severe obstruction or recurrent syncope despite meds:<br /></br>• Surgical septal myectomy or alcohol septal ablation.<br /></br>• Dual-chamber ICD for primary/secondary prevention of sudden death.  </p></br><p>Supportive: avoid dehydration, extreme exertion; keep preload/afterload up (no vasodilators, no diuretics).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Permanent ventricular pacemaker<br /></br>   • Dual-chamber pacing can widen LVOT and is a second- or third-line intervention for medically refractory cases; not initial therapy.</p></br><p>c. Aortic valve replacement<br /></br>   • LVOT obstruction in HOCM is dynamic and subvalvular; the aortic valve itself is normal. AVR addresses fixed valvular stenosis, not HOCM.</p></br><p>d. Mitral valve annuloplasty<br /></br>   • Used for primary mitral regurgitation due to annular dilation. Although SAM involves the mitral leaflet, the pathology is not annular; annuloplasty does not relieve LVOT obstruction.</p></br><p>e. Surgical excision of the endocardium of the LV free wall (septal myectomy)<br /></br>   • Septal (not free-wall) myectomy is effective for severe, drug-refractory obstruction but is invasive and not first-line after a single event.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Key Takeaway<br /></br>Young athlete + exertional syncope + SAM of mitral valve = HOCM. Initial management is a β-blocker to reduce dynamic LVOT obstruction.</p>
197. A 1-year-old boy is brought to the office because his parents are concerned that his birthmarks have been increasing in size and number during the past 6 months. Growth and dewelopment are appropriate for age. Immunizations are up-to-date. He has no histo [Y of serious illness and receives no medications. Examination shows ten 6- to 10-mm tan-brown maculae over the patient's trunk, axillae, and all extremities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. CT scan of the abdomen</br>b. Echocardiography</br>c. EEG</br>d. Ophthalmologic examination</br>e. Renal ultrasonography</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>d. Ophthalmologic examination</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• Age 1 year with normal growth/development<br /></br>• TEN (≥ 6) sharply demarcated, 6- to 10-mm tan-brown macules on trunk, axillae, extremities → classic café-au-lait macules<br /></br>• Appearing before age 2 and increasing in number/size → strongly suspicious for Neurofibromatosis type 1 (NF1, von Recklinghausen disease)<br /></br>Key “rule-in” features: 6 or more café-au-lait spots &gt;5 mm in a pre-pubertal child.<br /></br>Because only one NIH criterion is met so far, additional screening for another criterion is needed to establish/ exclude NF1. Optic pathway gliomas &amp; Lisch nodules (iris hamartomas) can present in the first 2–3 yrs and are detected by a comprehensive eye exam.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step-by-step thinking:</br>1. Multiple café-au-lait macules → think NF1.<br /></br>2. Diagnostic criteria require ≥ 2 of: ≥ 6 CALM, axillary/inguinal freckling, ≥ 2 Lisch nodules, optic glioma, ≥ 2 neurofibromas or one plexiform neurofibroma, distinctive osseous lesion, or affected 1° relative.<br /></br>3. The child currently has only one criterion (≥ 6 CALM).<br /></br>4. The earliest additional criterion that can be non-invasively evaluated is ocular involvement (Lisch nodules, optic glioma).<br /></br>Therefore, the most appropriate NEXT step is referral for a detailed ophthalmologic examination.</p></br><p>Take-home message: In an infant with multiple café-au-lait spots, screen early with an eye exam to look for optic pathway glioma/Lisch nodules to confirm NF1 and institute vision-saving surveillance.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (NF1)</br>• AD mutation, chromosome 17, neurofibromin (RAS-GAP) tumor-suppressor loss.<br /></br>• Manifestations: café-au-lait macules, axillary/inguinal freckling, Lisch nodules, neurofibromas, optic pathway glioma, learning disabilities, scoliosis, malignant peripheral nerve-sheath tumors, pheochromocytoma, renal artery stenosis.<br /></br>• Optic gliomas arise in ~15% of children, peak &lt;6 yrs; asymptomatic early, so routine eye exams are crucial.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostic &amp; Management Pearls</br>• Baseline &amp; annual ophthalmologic exam from infancy through adolescence.<br /></br>• Annual BP monitoring (renal artery stenosis, pheochromocytoma).<br /></br>• Developmental/behavioral screening, skin exam, spine exam each visit.<br /></br>• Imaging (MRI brain/orbits) only if visual symptoms or abnormal eye exam.<br /></br>• Genetic counseling for family.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. CT scan of the abdomen<br /></br>   – Used to evaluate adrenal or renal masses (pheochromocytoma, Wilms tumor). Pheochromocytoma risk rises in adolescence/adulthood, not infancy; Wilms tumor screening is for Beckwith-Wiedemann or WAGR, not NF1. No abdominal mass or hypertension here.</p></br><p>b. Echocardiography<br /></br>   – Indicated for rhabdomyomas (tuberous sclerosis) or congenital heart disease. NF1 can have vascular lesions, but routine echo is not recommended and there are no cardiac findings.</p></br><p>c. EEG<br /></br>   – Performed for seizure evaluation (e.g., tuberous sclerosis cortical tubers). Child has no seizures or developmental delay; EEG would not establish a second NF1 criterion.</p></br><p>e. Renal ultrasonography<br /></br>   – Screens for renal angiomyolipomas (tuberous sclerosis) or Wilms tumor in BWS/WAGR. NF1 surveillance focuses on BP, not routine renal imaging in asymptomatic infants.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line</br>Multiple café-au-lait spots in an infant → suspect NF1 → confirm with the next easiest NIH criterion: ophthalmologic examination for optic pathway glioma or Lisch nodules.</p>
198. Three hours after undergoing placement of an intravenous line using routine universal precautions and beginning intravenous fluid therapy, a hospitalized 2-year-old girl has the onset of a generalized itching rash. She was admitted to the hospital today because of a 3-day history of fever and diarrhea. As a newborn, she underwent surgical repair of a myelomeningocele and creation of a ventriculoperitoneal shunt. She is not currently receiving any medications. Vital signs 3 hours ago and now are:</br>3 Hours Ago – Temperature: 38  C 100.4  F; Pulse: 110 /min; Respirations: 24 /min; Blood pressure: 105/65 mm Hg</br>Now – Temperature: 37.8  C 100  F; Pulse: 160 /min; Respirations: 40 /min; Blood pressure: 80/50 mm Hg</br>Examination shows diffuse, raised, erythematous lesions with pale centers. The upper and lower extremities appear mottled. Capillary refill time is 5 to 6 seconds. The remainder of the examination, including funduscopic and neurologic examinations, shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is most likely to have prevented this patient's current condition?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Avoidance of latex gloves</br>b. Earlier administration of fluids</br>c. Intravenous administration of a corticosteroid</br>d. Intravenous administration of dopamine</br>e. Revision of the shunt</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>A. Avoidance of latex gloves  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Timeline: Rash, hypotension, and tachycardia begin 3 hours after IV line placement.<br /></br>• Skin findings: “Diffuse, raised, erythematous lesions with pale centers” = urticaria.<br /></br>• Hemodynamics: BP 80/50 mm Hg, cap refill 5–6 s → distributive shock.<br /></br>• Key past history: Myelomeningocele surgically repaired in infancy. Children with spina bifida / myelomeningocele have repeated OR and catheter exposures and develop IgE-mediated latex hypersensitivity at very high rates (up to 60 %).<br /></br>Pattern = acute anaphylaxis (urticaria + hypotension) triggered by latex contact during IV insertion.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Allergen exposure (latex gloves or tourniquet) during IV start.<br /></br>2. Pre-formed IgE on mast cells/basophils cross-links → histamine, tryptase, leukotriene release.<br /></br>3. Results: Vasodilation, ↑vascular permeability (hypotension), pruritic wheals, tachycardia.<br /></br>Take-home: In patients with spina bifida, assume latex allergy; use only non-latex products to prevent peri-procedural anaphylaxis.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Repeated mucosal exposure to latex proteins → class-switching to IgE.<br /></br>• Re-exposure → Type I hypersensitivity (minutes–hours).<br /></br>• Mediators: histamine → urticaria &amp; vasodilation; leukotrienes, prostaglandins → bronchospasm, hypotension.<br /></br>Risk factors: spina bifida, urologic malformations, multiple surgeries, healthcare workers.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Acute management (not asked):<br /></br>• IM epinephrine first-line, airway support, aggressive isotonic fluids, H1/H2 blockers, IV steroids for biphasic reaction.<br /></br>Prevention (asked): absolute latex avoidance—non-latex gloves, catheters, vial stoppers, tourniquets. Confirm with serum IgE or skin prick if history unclear.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Earlier administration of fluids<br /></br>– Could transiently improve BP but does not stop IgE mast-cell degranulation; does not prevent reaction.</p></br><p>C. Intravenous corticosteroid prophylaxis<br /></br>– Steroids reduce late-phase inflammation (4–6 h later); they do not reliably prevent the immediate anaphylactic phase triggered by antigen exposure.</p></br><p>D. Intravenous dopamine<br /></br>– Vasopressor used to treat refractory hypotension once shock occurs; has no preventive role.</p></br><p>E. Revision of the shunt<br /></br>– Shunt infection/malfunction causes fever, vomiting, altered mental status over days, not abrupt urticaria/hypotension within hours of IV placement.</p></br><p>Core concept: Children with spina bifida are presumed latex-allergic; strict latex-free precautions prevent peri-procedural anaphylaxis.</p>
199. A previously healthy 29-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 6-week history of intermittent, increasingly severe upper abdominal pain. He has not had fever, chills, weight loss, or intolerance to fatty food. Over-the-counter famotidine has provided minimal relief. He has smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 10 years. His temperature is 37 C 98.6 F, pulse is 68/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 120/82 mm Hg. Abdominal examination shows epigastric tenderness to palpation. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Test of the stool for occult blood is positive. Esophagogastroduodenoscopy shows a 1.5-cm ulcer along the lesser curvature of the stomach; there is no active bleeding. Biopsy results are shown. Smoking cessation is recommended. In addition to lansoprazole therapy, which of the following is the most appropriate pharmacotherapy?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Amoxicillin and metronidazole</br>b. Bismuth subsalicylate and ciprofloxacin</br>c. Bismuth subsalicylate and tetracycline</br>d. Ciprofloxacin and metronidazole</br>e. Clarithromycin and amoxicillin</br>F. Tetracycline and clarithromycin</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Clarithromycin and amoxicillin  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Young (29-year-old) man with a 6-week history of gnawing epigastric pain + positive stool guaiac + endoscopically proven 1.5-cm ulcer on the lesser curvature of the stomach.<br /></br>• No NSAID use, no systemic illness → most common etiology of solitary gastric/duodenal ulcer in this demographic is Helicobacter pylori infection.<br /></br>• Biopsy “results are shown” (not displayed in prompt), implying organisms were seen—spiral/curved gram-negative rods within the mucous layer, i.e., H pylori.<br /></br>→ Key pattern: gastric/duodenal ulcer in a young patient without NSAID use → think H pylori → eradicate organism.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Stepwise thought process:<br /></br>1. Confirm ulcer (EGD).<br /></br>2. Identify likely cause (H pylori on biopsy).<br /></br>3. Treat with standard eradication regimen = PPI + 2 antibiotics.<br /></br>4. First-line “clarithromycin-based triple therapy” (PPI + clarithromycin + amoxicillin) is recommended in areas with clarithromycin resistance ≤15% and no prior macrolide exposure.<br /></br>The patient is already starting lansoprazole (PPI). Therefore, we must select the antibiotic pair that, when added to a PPI, constitutes triple therapy: clarithromycin + amoxicillin.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: For H pylori ulcers, give a PPI plus two antibiotics—typically clarithromycin + amoxicillin (or metronidazole if penicillin-allergic).  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)<br /></br>• H pylori = microaerophilic, curved gram-negative rod with urease activity → alkalinizes microenvironment → mucosal damage and inflammation → peptic ulcer (duodenal &gt; gastric).<br /></br>• Chronic infection increases risk for MALT lymphoma and distal gastric adenocarcinoma.<br /></br>• Eradication heals ulcer, relieves symptoms, and lowers malignancy risk.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>First-line regimens (10–14 days):<br /></br>1. Clarithromycin-based triple therapy<br /></br>   – Omeprazole/esomeprazole/lansoprazole + clarithromycin 500 mg BID + amoxicillin 1 g BID.<br /></br>   – Substitute metronidazole 500 mg TID if penicillin allergic.<br /></br>2. Bismuth-based quadruple therapy (if macrolide resistance or prior macrolide exposure)<br /></br>   – PPI + bismuth subsalicylate + metronidazole + tetracycline.  </p></br><p>Confirm eradication (urea breath test or stool Ag) ≥4 wks after therapy in ulcers, complicated disease, or MALT lymphoma.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Amoxicillin and metronidazole – Would require addition of clarithromycin OR bismuth/tetracycline for an accepted regimen; dual therapy alone is inadequate and has high resistance/failure.<br /></br>b. Bismuth subsalicylate and ciprofloxacin – Ciprofloxacin is not part of any guideline-approved H pylori regimen.<br /></br>c. Bismuth subsalicylate and tetracycline – Missing metronidazole; needs all three components plus a PPI for quadruple therapy.<br /></br>d. Ciprofloxacin and metronidazole – Non-standard combo; ciprofloxacin is not used, and dual therapy is insufficient.<br /></br>f. Tetracycline and clarithromycin – Never used together because both are protein-synthesis inhibitors with redundant mechanisms and do not constitute a proven regimen.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Choosing metronidazole when the patient is not penicillin-allergic or defaulting to quadruple therapy without evidence of significant macrolide resistance history.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Take-Home Message<br /></br>In a patient with biopsy-proven H pylori peptic ulcer disease, initiate clarithromycin-based triple therapy: PPI (lansoprazole) + clarithromycin + amoxicillin (or metronidazole if penicillin-allergic).</p>
200. A 2-week-old boy is brought to the physician because of a 5-day history of a progressive rash that began at a site on his scalp where an electrode was attached during labor. Despite application of topical antibiotic ointment, the rash has spread to his trunk and extremities. He was born at term following an uncomplicated vaginal delivery. Apgar scores were 8 and 9 at 1 and 5 minutes, respectively. Initial examination showed no abnormalities. He is currently at the 30th percentile for length, 75th percentile for weight, and 25th percentile for head circumference. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows erythematous patches with overlying vesicles containing clear or cloudy fluid over the entire body. Appropriate treatment of this patient is most likely to prevent which of the following acute manifestations of his condition?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Brain abscess</br>b. Chorioretinitis</br>c. Encephalitis</br>d. Mastoiditis</br>e. Ventriculitis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>c. Encephalitis  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “2-week-old,” “progressive rash,” “began at a site on his scalp where an electrode was attached”: Scalp fetal-monitor electrodes create small breaks in the skin that allow maternal HSV-2 (or HSV-1) in the birth canal to inoculate the neonate.<br /></br>• “Erythematous patches with overlying vesicles containing clear or cloudy fluid”: Classic herpetic vesicles on an erythematous base.<br /></br>• “Despite topical antibiotic ointment, the rash has spread”: Bacterial impetigo (eg, Staph aureus) would usually respond to mupirocin; spread despite antibiotics suggests a viral etiology.<br /></br>Pattern: Vesicular rash in a neonate 1–3 weeks old, especially after scalp electrode placement, = neonatal herpes simplex virus infection.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Neonatal HSV presents in three overlapping forms:<br /></br>1. SEM disease (Skin-Eyes-Mouth) – vesicles only<br /></br>2. CNS disease – meningoencephalitis (lethargy, seizures, bulging fontanelle)<br /></br>3. Disseminated disease – sepsis-like picture, hepatitis, DIC  </p></br><p>Untreated SEM disease frequently progresses to CNS ± disseminated disease within days to weeks. Early IV acyclovir halts viral replication and largely prevents the acute complication of HSV encephalitis.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In a neonate with vesicular rash linked to scalp electrode, immediately start IV acyclovir to prevent progression from SEM disease to life-threatening encephalitis.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Maternal genital HSV can shed virions during delivery.<br /></br>• Fetal scalp electrodes = microabrasions → inoculation → local replication in skin → viremia → possible CNS spread via trigeminal/olfactory nerves or blood.<br /></br>• HSV encephalitis in neonates causes temporal-lobe hemorrhagic necrosis, seizures, coma, high mortality.  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>First-line: High-dose IV acyclovir for 14–21 days + ophthalmologic exam + serial CSF PCR.<br /></br>Second-line (rare acyclovir-resistant strains): IV foscarnet.<br /></br>Follow-up: Monthly suppressive oral acyclovir for 6 months to ↓ recurrent lesions and neurodevelopmental sequelae.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Rundown<br /></br>a. Brain abscess – Usually post-bacterial meningitis (Strep viridans, Staph aureus) or cyanotic heart disease; presents weeks–months later with focal neuro deficits, not diffuse vesicular rash.<br /></br>b. Chorioretinitis – Part of classic congenital toxoplasmosis triad (chorioretinitis, hydrocephalus, intracranial calcifications); infection is transplacental, not peripartum, and rash is uncommon.<br /></br>d. Mastoiditis – Suppurative complication of acute otitis media in older infants/children (fever, posterior auricular tenderness), unrelated to vesicular rash or scalp electrodes.<br /></br>e. Ventriculitis – Inflammation of ventricular lining secondary to bacterial meningitis or intraventricular hemorrhage; presents with persistent fever and CSF pleocytosis, not widespread vesicles.</p></br><p>Key Exam Pearl<br /></br>Neonatal vesicles + scalp electrode = think HSV. Prompt IV acyclovir prevents fatal neonatal HSV encephalitis.</p>
